Two lines. One family. The question runs through both.

Series Preface
This  volume  contains  the  complete  chronicle  of  the  Hale  family  of  
Worcestershire, assembled in a single edition for the first time. The ten books were  
composed over the course of 2024 and 2025 by James Marsh-Hale and Eleanor Voss,  
working from the Hale family papers deposited at the Bodleian Library, Oxford,  
under the reference MSS. Hale-Marsh.
The collection begins in 1066 with Ranulf de la Hale — a Norman soldier of no  
particular distinction who crossed the Channel with William's army and was given, in  
the  aftermath  of  the  Conquest,  a  small  parcel  of  confiscated  Saxon  land  in  
Worcestershire. It ends in 2026 with two people working in Oxford and London to  
assemble the record of what that land, and the family that held it, became across  
nine centuries.
What connects the ten books is not a continuous narrative — the archive has  
gaps, the documents are of different kinds, the voices change with the centuries —  
but a continuous question. Ranulf asked it on his deathbed in 1115: whether what he  
had done was sufficient. Every Hale after him asked some version of the same  
question. None of them answered it cleanly. All of them kept asking.
The language of each volume is the language of its period. Brother Eadmer's  
monastic English of 1121 is not the same instrument as John Hale's Tudor English of  
1534, which is not the same instrument as Augusta Hale's Regency English of 1822,  
which is not the same instrument as the contemporary English of the emails and  
journal entries that close the chronicle. The reader who moves through all ten  
volumes will experience the whole history of the English language as well as the  
whole history of one family — and will find, perhaps, that the two histories are not  
entirely separable.
The question is the thread. The language is the costume it wears in each century.  
The family is the body that carries both.
A note on structure: each of the ten books contains its own front matter,  
genealogical table, and glossary. These are preserved in this omnibus edition. The  
reader is therefore free to enter the chronicle at any point without losing the  
apparatus of the individual volumes. The series preface and master timeline at the  
front of this edition are provided for those who prefer to read in sequence.

James Marsh-Hale and Eleanor Voss
Oxford and London, March 2026



✦
HERE BEGINNETH
The Chronicle of the
House of de la Hale
of Worcestershire
Being the True Account of the Coming of Ranulf de la Hale
to the Kingdom of England in the Year of Our Lord 1066,
and of his Descendants unto the Third Generation,
Compiled from Divers Chronicles, Writs, and Testimonies
Set down by Brother Eadmer of Worcester,
Monk of the Cathedral Priory of St. Mary,
in the Year of Our Lord 1121.
Anno Domini MXXI

"Her com se cyng Willelm ongean to Englalande mid mycelum here of 
Normandige."
"Here came King William back to England with a great army from Normandy."
— The Anglo-Saxon Chronicle, entry for 1067
"The Normans are a cunning and revengeful people; their eloquence 
subdues where force fails."
— Ordericus Vitalis, Historia Ecclesiastica, c.1125



The Preface of Brother Eadmer
Being an Account of the Sources from which this Chronicle is Drawn
In the name of God the Father, and of His Son our Lord Jesus Christ, and of 
the Holy Spirit, one God in three persons, I Brother Eadmer, a monk of the 
Cathedral Priory of the Blessed Virgin Mary at Worcester, unworthy servant  
and poor sinner, set down here what I have gathered, by diligent inquiry 
and by the testimony of those who saw and heard and did, of the history of 
the family called de la Hale, who hold their lands at Halecroft in the county 
of Worcester by the grant of King William the Conqueror and by the 
subsequent confirmation of his son King William Rufus, and whose present 
lord, Sir Geoffrey de la Hale, has charged me upon his soul and mine to 
preserve what is known before it is lost.
I write in the year 1121, which is to say in the fifty-fifth year since the 
coming of the Normans to England, and in the twenty-first year of the reign 
of our present sovereign lord King Henry, the Conqueror's youngest son. 
Those who lived through the first years of the Conquest are now old, or 
dead, or speaking with the uncertain memory of age, and the prior of this 
house, Father Oswald, has for some years exhorted the monks of this 
community to preserve in writing what old men tell us of those times, before  
the telling stops.
The sources from which I have drawn are these: first, such entries as 
exist in the annals of this house pertaining to events in this county; second, 
the oral testimony of Edwin the Saxon, who is now past seventy years of age  
and who was a young man in the household of the thegn Leofric of Halecroft  
when the Normans came, and who has spoken to me on six separate 
occasions, with a frankness that I attribute to his age and to his evident 
conviction that there is no longer anything to fear from the truth; third, a 
writ of land grant, in Latin, which Sir Geoffrey holds in his charter chest 
and permitted me to read and to copy; fourth, the testimony of Dame Maud 
de la Hale, Sir Geoffrey's mother, who is sixty years of age and whose 
recollections of her father-in-law Ranulf, though coloured by the natural 
piety of a widow, are of considerable historical interest; and fifth, my own 
observation of the de la Hale family and their tenants over many years of 
pastoral acquaintance.
I have endeavoured to set down what is true, and to mark clearly what 
is uncertain, and to omit what cannot be verified. Where the testimony of 
witnesses conflicts, I have said so. Where the record is silent, I have not 
invented. A chronicle that invents is not a chronicle but a sermon, and I

have had enough experience of sermons to know that they are more 
satisfying to the preacher than to the congregation.
I dedicate this work to the glory of God and to the preservation of 
memory, which is itself, as the blessed Augustine teaches, a form of the 
presence of God in time.
Brother Eadmer of Worcester
The Priory of St Mary, Worcester
Written in the month of October, Anno Domini 1121

I
Of Normandy, and of Ranulf de la Hale, and of  
the Reason He Came to England
Anno Domini 1066
In the duchy of Normandy, in the village of La Hale-sur-Risle in the valley of  
the river Risle, there was born in the year of Our Lord 1040 a boy who was 
given the name Ranulf, being the third son of a man called Godfrey who 
held half a hide of land in that valley by service to his lord, and who was 
himself the son of a Northman — that is to say, a man of the Viking blood 
that had settled in Normandy these hundred and thirty years past and had, 
in that time, learned the French tongue and the French manner of fighting 
but had not, his grandson would later say, entirely relinquished the Viking 
manner of wanting.
The village of La Hale takes its name from the old word for an 
enclosure, a hall, a place set apart — hale in the Norman tongue, which is 
the same word in its root as the Saxon heall that the English used for the 
great hall of their lords, so that when Ranulf de la Hale arrived in England 
and found that the English called such places by a name so close to his own,  
he took it as a sign, which may tell us something about the man that the 
rest of this chronicle will either confirm or complicate.
He was the third son. In the world of 1066, this was a condition of 
singular clarity: the eldest would have the land, the second would have the 
Church or a senior position in a lord's household if fortune served, and the 
third would have what he could take. Ranulf was not, by the testimony of 
those who knew him, a man of resignation. He was, in the phrase that 
Edwin the Saxon used when he described him to me — and Edwin used it 
with a directness that suggested he had spent many years arriving at the 
exact word — a man of appetite. He wanted. He wanted land, and a name 
attached to land, and sons to carry the name, and the particular security 
that only the possession of ground beneath one's feet can confer upon a 
man who has grown up watching others possess it.
It was this appetite that brought him, in the autumn of 1066, across the 
Channel in the fleet of William, Duke of Normandy, who had his own 
appetite, considerably larger, and the military and political genius to satisfy 
it.

✦ ✦ ✦
What we know of Ranulf's early life is sparse. He was, by the time of the  
crossing, twenty-five or twenty-six years of age. He had served in William's 
household forces for at least four years — Edwin, who heard this from the 
man himself, says six — and had distinguished himself sufficiently at the 
siege of Domfront in 1063 to receive a small payment and the personal 
commendation of the duke, which was, in practical terms, the currency 
most likely to produce future reward. He was not a knight. He was a man-
at-arms, which is to say a mounted soldier of some skill but without the 
formal investiture of knighthood and without the lands that knighthood 
required. He was, in the hierarchy of William's army, a man of the middle —  
above the foot soldiers, below the lords, and in precisely the condition of 
motivated ambition that made such men useful to a duke who needed 
fighters willing to cross open water in autumn to take a kingdom.
The crossing was made in September of the year 1066. The fleet 
numbered, by the accounts that survive, somewhere between four hundred 
and seven hundred ships — the chroniclers disagree, as chroniclers always 
disagree about numbers, in direct proportion to the impressiveness of the 
numbers in question. What is not disputed is that it was the largest military 
crossing of the Channel since the Romans, and that the men who made it 
knew, as they looked back at the receding coast of Normandy, that they 
were either going to England or to the bottom of the sea.
[Note by the scribe, Brother Eadmer:]
I have asked Edwin whether Ranulf spoke of 
the crossing to him. He said: "Often. He said 
it was the longest night of his life and he had 
lived through several long nights. He said the 
horses were worse off than the men, being 
unable to understand what was happening to 
them, and that he spent much of the crossing 
steadying his horse by speaking into its ear, 
which is how he learned that comfort offered 
to another creature steadies the offerer as 
much as the receiver. He said he did not 
sleep." — Brother Eadmer.

II
Of the Battle of Hastings, Being the Account of  
Edwin the Saxon Who Saw It from the English  
Side
The fourteenth day of October, Anno Domini 1066
What follows is the testimony of Edwin, son of Godwin, formerly of the 
household of the thegn Leofric of Halecroft, as given by him to Brother 
Eadmer of Worcester in the Priory of St Mary in the years 1119 and 1120. I 
have set it down as nearly as possible in his own words, though I have 
translated from the English tongue into Latin and here back again, and 
some loss is inevitable in any such passage. I note, for the reader's benefit, 
that Edwin is a man of remarkable lucidity for his age, and that his account 
of the battle is consistent, across three separate tellings, in all material 
particulars.
— Brother Eadmer.
    ✦ ✦ ✦
"I was seventeen years old in the year the Normans came. My father was a 
free man, a ceorlisc man as we say, who held two virgates of land from the 
thegn Leofric at Halecroft, and I was in Leofric's service as a household 
man, which meant I carried messages and kept accounts and did whatever 
was asked of me, and when Leofric rode to war I rode with him. This is how 
I came to be at the great battle."
"The English army had already fought at Stamford Bridge in the north, 
against the Norwegian king Harald Hardrada, and the English king Harold 
had beaten the Norwegians so thoroughly that very few of them went home,  
and Harald Hardrada was among those who did not. It was a great victory. 
But the news came south within days that the Normans had landed on the 
southern coast, and so King Harold, who was a man of absolute 
determination and perhaps insufficient patience, turned his army and 
marched it south again before the men had properly rested from the 
northern battle. Leofric said to my father, the night before we left: 'We are 
tired. Harold should rest us. But Harold is Harold, and we march.'"
"The battle was on the fourteenth day of October, on a ridge

called Senlac, which the English call the hoary apple tree hill. The 
Normans came up the slope toward us. We had the high ground, 
which should have been an advantage, and in the morning it was. 
The Norman knights came up the hill and we held the shield wall 
and they broke on it like water on a cliff face. I was in the middle 
of the line with Leofric's men and I could feel the wall through my 
own shield, the pressure of a thousand men all leaning into it at 
once, and the weight of the horses and the spearmen coming 
against it, and for a time the wall held and the Normans fell back 
down the slope."
"I do not know at what hour the shield wall broke. Time in a 
battle is not the time of ordinary life; it stretches and collapses and  
you cannot hold it in your mind. What I know is that at some point 
in the afternoon the Normans pretended to retreat, and some of 
our men — not Leofric's, Leofric kept us firm — broke out of the 
wall to pursue them, and when they pursued, the Normans turned 
and killed them, and the wall was broken, and after that it was a 
different kind of battle."
"The king died in the late afternoon. I did not see it. I heard it 
pass down the line like a wave, the word that Harold was dead, 
and then the line began to come apart, and the dying really began.  
Leofric took an axe-blow to the shoulder in that last hour and did 
not die of it then but died of the wound three weeks later, in great 
pain, which was a worse death than Harold's. I carried him off the 
field on my back. He was not a light man."
"I saw the Normans that day. Not as men, then — they were 
the enemy, they were the killing thing, they were what you tried 
not to die from. It was later that they became men to me. One of 
them in particular."
"After Leofric died I went home to Halecroft, because I had nowhere 
else to go and my father was there. William's army was moving through the 
south and midlands, taking everything, burning what resisted and taking 
what did not. We heard stories. A village burned here, a church robbed 
there, a thegn killed for not submitting fast enough. Leofric's sons had fled 
north. The land at Halecroft had no lord. It was waiting to be given to 
someone, and we all knew it, and knowing it was a particular kind of fear — 
not the fear of a thing happening but the fear of waiting for a thing to 
happen."

"The Norman came in the spring of 1067. He came with two other 
soldiers and a clerk who carried a document, and he rode into the yard at 
Halecroft and looked at the house and the fields and the mill and the wood, 
and my father and the other free men stood in the yard and looked at him, 
and he looked back at us, and then the clerk read the document, which was 
in Latin and which none of us could understand, and the Norman said 
something to the clerk and the clerk said to us, in English that was not quite  
English: 'This man is Ranulf de la Hale. This land is his, by the king's gift. 
You serve him now.'"
"That is how it was done. That is how England changed hands."

III
The Writ of Grant, and of What It Gave and  
What It Did Not Give
Anno Domini 1067
Among the documents preserved in Sir Geoffrey de la Hale's charter chest, 
which he permitted me to examine in the year 1119, is a writ of land grant, 
written in Latin on vellum, much worn at the fold-lines but legible 
throughout. I have copied it here, as exactly as the condition of the 
document permitted, together with my translation into the English tongue.
— Brother Eadmer.
Writ of King William I, Granting Lands at Halecroft
Issued at Winchester, in the year of Our Lord 1067
[In the original Latin:]
Willelmus rex Anglorum omnibus fidelibus suis Francis et Anglis in 
Wigornscira salutem.
Sciatis me dedisse et concessisse Ranulfo de Hala, militi meo, pro 
servitio suo fideli ad Hastingas et alibi, terram que vocatur Halecroft in 
comitatu Wigorniensi, que fuit Leofric thegni, qui contra me pugnavit et 
mortuus est.
Tenebit ipse Ranulfus dictam terram et omnes pertinencias suas sibi et 
heredibus suis de me et heredibus meis per servitium unius militis in 
perpetuum.
Quare volo et firmiter precipio quod ipse et heredes sui bene et in pace 
et honorifice teneant predictam terram cum omnibus libertatibus et 
liberis consuetudinibus suis.
Testibus: Roberto Comite Moritonii. Willelmo Episcopo Londoniensi. 
Datum apud Wintoniam.
[Translation:]
William, King of the English, to all his faithful men, French and

English, in Worcestershire, greeting.
Know that I have given and granted to Ranulf de la Hale, my soldier, 
for his faithful service at Hastings and elsewhere, the land called 
Halecroft in the county of Worcester, which was the land of the thegn 
Leofric, who fought against me and is dead.
The said Ranulf shall hold the said land and all its appurtenances, to 
himself and his heirs, from me and my heirs, by the service of one 
knight, in perpetuity.
Wherefore I will and firmly command that he and his heirs hold the 
aforesaid land well and in peace and honourably, with all its liberties 
and free customs.
Witnessed by: Robert, Count of Mortain. William, Bishop of London. 
Given at Winchester.
[Note by the scribe, Brother Eadmer:]
This document is authentic, as I judge by the 
form of the Latin, the witnesses named, and 
the condition of the seal, which though 
broken retains the impression of King 
William's equestrian figure. I note that the 
grant is made "for faithful service at Hastings 
and elsewhere" — the elsewhere is not 
specified, but Dame Maud tells me her father-
in-law Ranulf said it referred to the harrying 
of certain villages in Kent that resisted the 
Norman advance in the weeks after Hastings. 
He did not speak of this with pride, she said, 
but he did not speak of it with shame either. 
He spoke of it as a soldier speaks of what he 
has done — which is to say, as something that  
happened, and that produced a consequence, 
and that is now over. — Brother Eadmer.
The writ gave Ranulf de la Hale the land. What it did not give him — what 
no writ could give — was the understanding of what he had received.
Halecroft was not empty land. It was a working estate of some two 
hundred and forty acres, comprising arable strips in the open field, a water-
mill on the brook that ran along the eastern boundary, a wood of perhaps 
thirty acres used for pannage and timber, and a settlement of eight families 
— four free men, of whom Edwin's father was one, and four villeins who 
held their strips in return for labour services. There was a hall, which the 
Saxons had built of timber and which would have been, by Norman 
standards, a considerable building of its kind. There were cattle, sheep,

pigs, and geese. There was a granary that was, in the spring of 1067, still 
partly stocked with the previous year's harvest.
All of this was Ranulf's. But none of it spoke French. None of it had 
fought at Hastings or anywhere else in his service. None of it knew who he 
was or what the writ in the clerk's hand said or why it should mean 
anything to them. What they knew was that Leofric was dead and that this 
Norman was standing in the yard, and that between those two facts their 
world had changed in a way they had not chosen and could not undo.
Ranulf knew this too. Edwin told me — and this is the detail that has 
stayed with me most persistently in the years since Edwin told it to me — 
that when the clerk finished reading the writ, and the yard was silent, and 
the English stood in a row looking at him, Ranulf dismounted. He did not 
have to. A Norman lord arriving to take possession of confiscated English 
land had no requirement to dismount, no protocol that insisted upon it. He 
dismounted because, Edwin said, he was a man who understood that a man 
on horseback looking down at men on foot is not in a position to begin 
anything that requires the cooperation of those men.
He stood in the yard. He looked at them. He said something in French. 
The clerk translated: he said that he had no intention of burning anything 
that was still standing, and that he required food and lodging for himself 
and his men, and that he would speak with the heads of each household on 
the following day, one at a time.
Edwin's father said, in English, that food would be found and that the 
hall was available. The clerk translated. Ranulf nodded. He walked to the 
hall and went inside.
"That evening," Edwin told me, "my father said: he is a practical man. I 
do not know yet whether that is better or worse than the other kind. We will  
find out." My father was right on both counts. — Edwin's testimony, as 
recorded by Brother Eadmer."

IV
Of the First Winter, and of the Learning of Two  
Languages, and of the Woman Called Aelswith
Anno Domini 1067–1068
The first winter at Halecroft was, by every account I have gathered, the 
making of whatever the de la Hale family would subsequently become. Not 
because anything dramatic occurred in it — no battle, no great transaction, 
no event that a chronicle would ordinarily think worthy of record — but 
because it was in that winter that Ranulf de la Hale undertook the most 
difficult thing a man of his formation could attempt: he tried to understand 
what he had been given.
He was, by Edwin's account, a man of more intelligence than his station 
suggested. He had grown up in a household that was neither wealthy nor 
connected enough to provide the education that the Norman nobility of 
higher degree received, but he had a practical acuity — a capacity for 
observing how things worked and for adjusting his behaviour accordingly —  
that served him in the absence of formal learning. He had also, which was 
less common among the soldiers of William's army than one might hope, a 
degree of patience with things he did not yet know. Most of the Norman 
knights and men-at-arms who received grants of English land treated the 
English tenants on those lands as subhuman — not from cruelty, precisely, 
but from the particular combination of contempt and incomprehension that 
conquerors direct at the people they have conquered, which is itself a kind 
of defence against the unsettling recognition that the conquered are people.
Ranulf was not wholly free of this. Edwin is too honest a witness to 
pretend that the relationship between the Norman and his English tenants 
in those first years was one of easy equality. There were months of cold 
distance, of misunderstanding that hardened into grievance, of demands 
made in French that were either misunderstood or deliberately 
misunderstood in English, and of English customary rights that Ranulf 
overrode not from malice but from ignorance of their existence. The open-
field system of strip cultivation, the common rights over the wood and the 
waste, the labour services owed and their traditional limits — all of this was 
embodied knowledge that the English carried in their bodies and their 
habits and nowhere written down, and which Ranulf had to learn as a child 
learns a language: by immersion, repetition, and the patient correction of

his errors by people who had more reason to let him make them than to 
help him avoid them.
    ✦ ✦ ✦
The woman called Aelswith was the widow of one of the free men of 
Halecroft, a man named Wulfric who had died of a fever in the autumn of 
1066, which was a year in which the county of Worcester, like most of 
England, was so occupied with the political catastrophe of the Conquest 
that ordinary deaths went almost unnoticed. She was perhaps thirty years 
of age, with two young children, and she held her dead husband's virgate by  
the sufferance of the old thegn Leofric, who had allowed her to keep it in 
exchange for a small annual rent. Whether this arrangement would survive 
under the new lord was, in the first months, uncertain.
Ranulf did not disturb her holding. This was, Edwin says, not 
sentimentality but calculation: he needed the land worked, and a woman 
farming her dead husband's strips with her children and two hired hands 
was more productive to him than the same land in his own hands and 
worked by nobody. The calculation was sound. But its soundness does not 
diminish its consequence.
Aelswith spoke some French. She had acquired it — this is a detail that 
illuminates the complexity of the world into which Ranulf had arrived — 
from her own mother, who had been a servant in the household of a Norman  
merchant in Worcester who had settled in England some years before the 
Conquest, in the period when the Norman influence at the court of Edward 
the Confessor had made such men common enough. She spoke it 
imperfectly, with the particular accent of English mouths around Norman 
vowels, but she spoke it, and this made her, in the village of Halecroft in the  
winter of 1067, uniquely valuable.
Ranulf employed her as an interpreter. She interpreted not only 
language but custom: she told him, in her imperfect French, what the 
labour services were and when they were owed and what the tenants 
expected in return for them. She told him when the common field was to be 
ploughed and when it was to lie fallow. She told him why the mill-dues were  
disputed and what the dispute was and how it had been settled, three years 
before, by a judgment of Leofric that all parties still considered just. She 
told him, with a directness that he later described, in Edwin's account, as 
either courage or recklessness or the freedom of a woman who has nothing 
left to lose, that two of the villeins were planning to leave at Michaelmas 
and that if he wanted to keep them he would need to reduce their week-
work from three days to two.
He reduced the week-work. They stayed. He paid her an additional 
amount in silver for the information. She accepted it without comment,

which was itself, in Ranulf's experience of the women he had known, 
unusual enough to be notable.
[Note by the scribe, Brother Eadmer:]
Dame Maud tells me that her father-in-law 
rarely spoke of Aelswith to his family — not 
from secrecy, she thought, but from a 
reticence about matters he considered private  
that was general to his character. What she 
knew she had gathered from Edwin and from 
fragments. She said: "He was a man who did 
not explain himself. He acted, and the actions 
were their own explanation." I find this a 
useful guide to the reading of what follows. —  
Brother Eadmer.
In the spring of 1068 Ranulf de la Hale married Aelswith of Halecroft. 
The marriage was contracted in the church of St Wulfstan’s at the edge of 
the village, before Father Edric, the Saxon priest of the village,, who 
conducted the ceremony in Latin with the resigned competence of a man 
performing a sacrament for parties of whose personal history and mutual 
intelligibility he prefers to remain ignorant. The witnesses were Edwin's 
father Godwin and a Norman soldier named Gaston who was Ranulf's only 
companion from the crossing and who had been granted, by some prior 
arrangement between them, a small portion of the Halecroft pasture in 
exchange for his own military service.
The marriage was unremarkable by the standards of the Conquest, 
which produced many such unions — Norman men with grants of land and 
no wives, English women with claims to continuity and no protectors — 
though it was, by the standards of Norman social hierarchy, a step 
downward for Ranulf. He was marrying an English widow of villein stock 
who happened to have free status by her dead husband's tenure. His fellow 
Normans in the county, the men of greater grant and greater pretension, 
would not have considered it a suitable match. There is no evidence that 
Ranulf considered their opinion relevant.
Edwin told me — and here I must record that his voice changed when 
he told me this, acquiring a quality I can only describe as careful — that at 
the wedding feast, which was held in Halecroft's hall and which the whole 
village attended, Ranulf stood at the end of the meal and spoke. He spoke in  
French, which the clerk Gaston translated. He said: this land is mine by the 
king's writ. But it was before mine, and will be after mine, and the people 
on it were here before me and will be here after me, and I intend to know 
them, and I ask them to know me, and I do not expect this to be 
accomplished in an evening, but this is the evening on which it begins.

The village was quiet when he said it. Then Edwin's father, who was not 
a man given to ceremonial utterance, raised his cup and said, in English: 
"Well enough. Drink." And they drank.

V
Of the Harrying of the North, and of What It Did  
to Those Who Carried It Out
Anno Domini 1069–1070
In the winter of 1069 and 1070, William the King carried out what is known 
in the chronicles as the Harrying of the North — a campaign of systematic 
destruction across Yorkshire, Durham, and the northern counties, in 
response to a rebellion there that had sought and received support from the  
Danish king Swein. I do not intend here to describe that campaign in full, as  
it lies outside my county and is already recorded in the chronicles of 
Symeon of Durham and in the accounts of Ordericus Vitalis. I intend only to 
record what it did to Ranulf de la Hale, who was among the soldiers called 
to serve in it.
By the terms of his tenure — the service of one knight, as the writ states  
— Ranulf was obliged to render military service to the king on demand, for 
forty days per year at his own cost, and for longer periods at the king's cost.  
The summons came in the autumn of 1069. He left Aelswith at Halecroft, 
with Gaston to manage the estate and Edwin's father to manage the 
tenants, and he rode north with the Worcester levy.
He was gone for four months. He returned in the early spring of 1070. 
Edwin was in the yard when he rode in, and I have asked Edwin to describe 
what he saw.
"He was thinner. That was the first thing. The horse was thin 
too, and the horse had been a good horse when he left. His face 
was — I do not have the word for it in your Latin. In English we 
say haergian, the same root as the harrying itself, which is the 
word for what a wolf does to a sheepfold — he looked haerged. As 
though something had been through him and taken things out."
"He did not speak of the north for some weeks. Aelswith asked 
him and he said: later. She did not ask again, which was, in my 
experience of her, the most difficult thing she could have done, 
because she was not a woman who let things rest. She let this one 
rest, because she understood, I think, what kind of silence it was."
"When he did speak of it — to me, not to her, which tells you

something about what men will say to other men that they will not 
say to their wives — he said: I have burned things I should not 
have burned. He said it without explanation, and I did not ask for 
one. There is a register of that sentence that requires no 
elaboration. In the north they burned everything. The villages, the 
crops, the mills, the stored grain, the livestock. They burned it so 
that no army could live off it, which is a military logic I can follow. 
They also burned it so that no person could live off it, which is a 
different logic, and one that I do not think Ranulf ever made his 
peace with, though he served in it."
"He planted an extra acre of wheat that spring, on land that 
was not under the plough-rotation. Gaston asked why. He said: 
because somewhere there is not enough wheat. He did not explain 
further."
I have set down this account of the Harrying because it seems to me, upon 
reflection, relevant to understanding what Ranulf de la Hale became in the 
subsequent years of his tenure at Halecroft. A man who has done violence 
on a scale he cannot reconcile with the person he wishes to be has two 
options: he can deny it, which some men do, or he can spend the rest of his 
life attempting, in the particular ways available to him, to balance the 
account. Ranulf de la Hale, as the remainder of this chronicle will show, was  
not a man of denial. But he was also not a man of explicit confession or 
public penance. He was a man who planted wheat.

VI
Of the Children of Ranulf and Aelswith, and of  
the Two Languages that Lived in the House
Anno Domini 1068–1087
Ranulf and Aelswith had three children who survived infancy. The eldest 
was a boy, born in 1069 and named William, after the king, which was at 
once a political statement and a personal calculation, as the name William 
in the England of that decade was the most reliable passport a man's son 
could carry. The second was a girl, born in 1071, named Maud, which was 
the Norman form of the English name Matilda and was itself a small 
emblem of the household she was born into — a name that worked in both 
languages, belonging fully to neither. The third was a boy, born in 1075, 
named Leofric.
The naming of the third child Leofric — which is to say, the naming of a 
Norman lord's son after the English thegn from whom the land had been 
taken by conquest — is the detail I have pondered most in the years I have 
spent with this family's history. Dame Maud, who bears the name of her 
aunt, told me that when she asked her father Geoffrey about the name, he 
said only that his grandmother Aelswith had insisted upon it, and that 
Ranulf had agreed without argument. Edwin's account is more 
circumstantial: he says that Aelswith told Ranulf that she would name the 
child Leofric because a land does not forget its dead, and that the least they  
could do was remember him in the one way that costs nothing. Ranulf said: 
then we name him Leofric. And they did.
The house at Halecroft was, in the years of the children's growing, a 
house of two languages. By the mid 1070s Ranulf could conduct the 
manorial court in English, with an accent that Edwin describes as entirely 
his own, owing nothing to Normandy and nothing to Worcestershire and 
therefore something new. Aelswith spoke French well enough for most 
purposes, and the children spoke both with the unconscious fluency of those  
for whom two languages are simply the medium of daily life rather than a 
deliberate achievement.
It was the children's language that was the future. Brother Oswald, who  
taught the eldest boy William his letters, told me — Oswald is now old and 
in this house, and I have spoken with him often — that William de la Hale 
was the first person he had encountered who thought in English and French

simultaneously, without choosing between them, which produced in his 
written Latin a peculiarity that Oswald found initially alarming and 
subsequently charming: a tendency to construct sentences that had the 
logic of French grammar and the word-order of English, held together by 
Latin conjunctions, producing something that was formally incorrect by 
classical standards and somehow more precise than the correct forms 
would have been. Oswald said: he wrote like a man who has two eyes and 
uses both. The depth of field is unusual.
I find this a useful description of the whole de la Hale enterprise at 
Halecroft in those years. A household using both eyes. The Norman eye for 
hierarchy, for the organisation of military obligation, for the documentation 
of rights in writing. The English eye for the land itself, for the customary 
knowledge embedded in practice rather than record, for the particular 
intimacy between people and place that is not loyalty in the abstract but 
something more like familiarity — the knowledge of a field's character, a 
wood's yield, a brook's behaviour in flood.
    ✦ ✦ ✦
FROM THE WORCESTER PRIORY ANNALS, COMPILED BY DIVERS HANDS, 
ENTRIES FOR THE YEARS 1070–1087
1070. This year the king came to Worcester and held his court at 
Easter, and many disputes of land were heard. Ranulf de la Hale 
appeared before the king's commissioners and his title to Halecroft was  
confirmed. He paid one mark of silver for the confirmation.
1074. There was much sickness this year in the county of Worcester, 
and many cattle died. The mill at Halecroft was repaired by Ranulf de 
la Hale at his own expense, which was noted by the prior as a work of 
practical charity, since the mill serves seven villages.
1079. Aelswith, wife of Ranulf de la Hale, died in September after a 
short illness. She was buried in the churchyard of St Wulfstan’s at 
Halecroft. Ranulf gave two silver candlesticks to the church in her 
memory. Father Cynewulf reported that at the burial Ranulf wept, 
which was noted as unusual for a Norman.
1083. William, eldest son of Ranulf de la Hale, was invested as a knight  
at Worcester by the Bishop's deputy. He is reported to be a young man 
of good character who speaks English and French with equal facility.
1087. King William the Conqueror died in September at Rouen, of 
wounds received at the siege of Mantes. His death was received in 
Worcester without great public grief, which the prior noted with

neither approval nor disapproval.

VII
Of the Death of Ranulf de la Hale, and of His  
Testament, and of What Edwin Said When It  
Was Done
Anno Domini 1099
Ranulf de la Hale died in the summer of 1099, in his fifty-ninth year, of a 
fever contracted during a wet spring. He had been ailing for two years 
before this, and had made over the management of the estate to his son 
William in the year 1097, though he lived on at Halecroft until the end. 
Father Cynewulf, who was then very old, came to give him the last rites, 
and it is from Father Cynewulf's account — given to my predecessor at this 
priory, who recorded it, and which I have before me now — that the 
following is drawn.
On the day before he died, Ranulf asked for Edwin. Edwin was by this 
time past fifty years of age and a man of some standing in the village — not 
a holder of land in his own right, but a free man of recognised authority 
whose opinion on matters of custom and usage was sought by William de la 
Hale as regularly as it had been sought by his father. He came to the hall 
and sat by Ranulf's bed. Father Cynewulf remained in the room, at Ranulf's 
request.
What was said between them is recorded by Father Cynewulf in a brief 
note that has the quality, in its abruptness, of a man writing down 
something he was not entirely sure he should be writing down. I will set it 
out as he set it out:
[Note by the scribe, Brother Eadmer:]
"Ranulf asked Edwin if the land was well. 
Edwin said it was. Ranulf said: I took it 
wrongly. Edwin was silent a moment and then  
said: you have held it rightly, since. Ranulf 
said: is that sufficient? Edwin said: I do not 
know. It is what there is. Ranulf said: tell my 
sons I said so. Edwin said he would. That was 
all. — Father Cynewulf, as recorded in this 
house's annals."

The testament of Ranulf de la Hale was read by Father Cynewulf on the day 
after his death. It divided the estate as follows: to William, the eldest, the 
hall, the arable land, and the mill — the substance of the estate, as was 
proper for the eldest son. To the middle child Maud, who had married a 
Norman knight from Hereford, a sum of silver. To the youngest, Leofric, the  
wood and the pasture on the western side of the estate, with the right to 
build there and to hold by the same service to William that William held to 
the king.
The wood that Leofric received was not, in 1099, considered the most 
valuable portion of the estate. Timber and pannage were useful; arable land  
was the real wealth. But the wood had a brook running through it, and a 
spring at its northern end, and over the following generation Leofric de la 
Hale built a house there, and turned the wood's edge to pasture, and made 
of his smaller portion something that would, in time, produce a branch of 
the family distinguished enough from the main line to require its own 
history.
It is from this branch — the woodland branch, as I think of them — that 
the line of Hale eventually proceeds to the Worcester solicitor Robert Hale 
some six centuries hence, and thence to his third son Nathaniel. But that is 
a long story, and I am only its beginning. Let me set down the end of 
Ranulf's, which is sufficient for this volume.
    ✦ ✦ ✦
Edwin lived for eleven more years after Ranulf's death, dying in 1110. In 
one of our last conversations — I was a young monk then, not long in 
orders, and he was willing to speak to me with a freedom he would not have  
extended to the older brothers — I asked him how he judged Ranulf de la 
Hale, now that the man was dead and could not be affected by the 
judgment.
He thought for a considerable time. Edwin's silences were never the 
silences of a man with nothing to say; they were the silences of a man 
taking the question seriously.
"He came as a conqueror," Edwin said at last. "He did not pretend 
otherwise, and I respect that. He did not say: I am your friend, I am your 
benefactor, I come to improve your condition. He said: this land is mine, by 
the king's gift, and you serve me now. And then he dismounted."
"The dismounting," Edwin said, "was the whole of it. Not the writ. Not 
the name. Not the silver he gave my father at that first harvest, or the mill 
repairs, or the wheat he planted, or any of it. The dismounting was the 
thing. Because a man who dismounts is a man who has decided that the 
distance between himself and the people he has been given power over is a

distance worth closing. Not eliminating — he never pretended they were 
equal, and they were not, and the law said they were not. But worth closing.  
Worth the effort."
"He was not a good man," Edwin said. "He was a man who tried. 
Whether trying is enough, in the reckoning that counts, I do not know. The 
priests tell me it is not enough without God's grace, and I believe them, 
because I have seen too many men try and fail. But trying is the beginning 
of something, and the something it began here lasted longer than he did. 
Which is, I suppose, what is meant by a family."
He died the following year, in his sleep, in the house his father had held 
on the same land for fifty years. William de la Hale carried his body to the 
churchyard. He was buried next to his father Godwin, near the wall, in the 
shadow of the yew tree that has stood in the churchyard of St Wulfstan’s at 
Halecroft since before any living man could remember.

VIII
Of William de la Hale, Who Was the Second  
Lord of Halecroft, and of His Son Geoffrey, Who  
Was the Third
Anno Domini 1087–1121
I have now arrived at the generation I know from my own observation as 
well as from the record, for Sir Geoffrey de la Hale is the man who charged 
me with this chronicle and who is my contemporary, and his father William I  
knew as a young man knows an old one — imperfectly, and with more 
curiosity than understanding, though the understanding has come with age.
William de la Hale was, in the judgment of all who knew him, a simpler 
man than his father — which is not a diminishment. He was the product of 
his father's project, and what that project had produced was a man who did 
not have to think about what his father had thought about constantly. 
Ranulf had thought, every day, about the gap between what he was and 
what he had received. William had received the estate as a fact, as a child 
receives its world, and was therefore free to simply inhabit it rather than to 
justify it. He spoke English as naturally as he spoke French, farmed the land  
with the knowledge his English mother had passed to him, administered the  
manorial court with a combination of Norman legal form and English 
customary precedent that the county court in Worcester regarded, when 
disputes came before it, as a surprisingly workable system.
He married, in 1090, a Norman woman from a family of middling rank 
in Gloucestershire, which restored, for the first time since Ranulf's marriage  
to Aelswith, the social standing that Ranulf's choice had compromised. The 
woman's name was also Maud — there were a great many Mauds in 
Norman England at this period, owing to the Empress Maud's prominence 
in the politics of the previous generation — and she was, by Dame Maud my 
contemporary's account of her grandmother-in-law, a woman of determined 
practical intelligence who ran the domestic economy of Halecroft with the 
rigorous efficiency of a woman who understands that comfort is not given 
but created and that its creation requires constant attention.
Their son Geoffrey, who is the present lord, was born in 1093 and 
inherited the estate in 1115, upon his father's death. He has managed it, in 
the six years I have known him as its lord, with the compound competence 
of a man who has received something over three generations of

accumulation and who understands that his responsibility is to pass it on, 
rather than to remake it. He is not his grandfather. He does not need to be. 
Ranulf built something from nothing, by appetite and calculation and a kind 
of rough-edged practical wisdom that is perhaps only available to men who 
have nothing to begin with. Geoffrey holds what was built. The holding is its  
own skill.
    ✦ ✦ ✦
FROM THE DOMESDAY SURVEY OF 1086, AS IT PERTAINS TO THE LANDS 
OF RANULF DE LA HALE IN WORCESTERSHIRE
[The following is extracted from the great survey of King William, 
conducted in 1086, which records the state of all landholdings in England. 
The entry for Halecroft reads as follows, in translation from the Latin:]
Ranulf de la Hale holds Halecroft from the King. There are two 
hides. In the demesne are two ploughs, and eight servants. There are 
four villagers and four smallholders with four ploughs. There is a priest  
and a church, and eight acres of meadow. A wood one league long and 
half a league broad. In the time of King Edward it was worth sixty 
shillings; now it is worth one pound.
[Note: The Domesday value of Halecroft — one pound in 1086, against sixty 
shillings in the time of King Edward the Confessor — indicates an estate under  
good management. The increase is attributed by the commissioners to the 
repair of the mill and the extension of the arable. — Brother Eadmer.]

X
Of the People of Halecroft, and of Their Names,  
and of the World They Inhabited Before and  
After
Anno Domini 1067–1075
I have thus far written of Ranulf and of Edwin, who are the two men whose 
testimony most directly informs this chronicle. But Halecroft was not two 
men. It was a settlement of eight families in the year of the Domesday 
survey, and somewhat more in the years before, when the plague of 1070 
had not yet thinned the village, and I think it a disservice to the record to 
leave those families as nameless as the Domesday commissioners were 
content to leave them. They were not merely ploughs and acres. They had 
names, and histories, and opinions about the Norman who had arrived in 
their yard, and I have gathered what I could of these from Edwin and from 
the older tenants of the present day, who carry in their memories the 
stories their grandparents told.
The free men of Halecroft at the time of the Conquest were four in 
number. Edwin's father Godwin I have already described. The second was a 
man named Ulfric, who held a virgate at the northern end of the estate near  
the brook, and who was, by all accounts, a man of such formidable 
taciturnity that the arrival of a Norman lord who could not speak English 
was, in his case, no particular alteration of circumstances. Ulfric 
communicated in grunts, Edwin said, equally well to Normans and to 
Saxons, and Ranulf, who had dealt with enough soldiers to understand that 
a man of few words is often a man of considerable competence, valued him 
accordingly. He drained the northern field in 1070 and increased its yield by  
a third, and said nothing about it.
The third free man was Eomund, who was the miller, a position of 
considerable importance in a village community where the grinding of grain  
was the critical transformation between harvest and bread. The mill was 
Eomund's livelihood and his identity and his source of authority, and he 
defended all three with the litigious energy of a man who understands that 
the miller's position is always being encroached upon — by tenants who 
grind elsewhere, by the lord who wishes to increase the mill dues, by the 
element of water itself, which is ungovernable and occasionally floods the 
wheel. He had, at the time of the Conquest, an ongoing dispute with Ulfric 
about the diversion of a tributary stream that affected both the mill's water

supply and Ulfric's drainage. This dispute predated the Conquest by three 
years and outlasted it by twenty, and was eventually resolved by William de 
la Hale' son Geoffrey, who ordered both parties to split the cost of a new 
channel and to stop bothering him about it.
The fourth free man was a very old person — sex and precise age both 
uncertain in the record — known only as the Beekeeper, who had held a 
half-virgate at the edge of the wood since before any living person could 
establish when, and who maintained a relationship with the hive-bees of 
Halecroft that the other villagers regarded with a mixture of respect and 
superstition. The Beekeeper lived alone, spoke rarely, and was consulted on  
matters of weather, sickness, and the timing of certain agricultural 
operations with a regularity that suggested their practical knowledge was 
considered more reliable than the calendar. They died in the winter of 1073,  
in extreme old age. Ranulf attended the burial, which Edwin noted with 
quiet surprise.
    ✦ ✦ ✦
The four villein households were in a more complex position than the 
free men. Their holdings were secure only so long as the lord willed it — 
they could not leave, could not sell, could not make decisions about their 
land without the lord's permission, and their labour services — three days a 
week on the demesne, plus additional boon-works at harvest and ploughing 
— were owed to whoever held the lordship, regardless of how that lordship 
had been acquired.
The oldest of the villeins was a woman named Sigefleda, a widow of 
perhaps fifty years, who held her dead husband's half-virgate with two 
grown sons and whose labour services were performed by those sons rather  
than by herself. She was the person who, in the first weeks of Ranulf's 
arrival, brought food to the hall without being asked — not as a gesture of 
welcome, Edwin said, but as a statement of fact: the hall must be fed; 
feeding the hall was what the village did; the person in the hall was 
therefore fed. Ranulf received it with courtesy and said something in 
French. The clerk translated. Sigefleda said, in English, that she hoped he 
liked salt pork because that was what there was. The clerk translated. 
Ranulf said salt pork was excellent. The clerk translated. Sigefleda nodded 
and left.
"It was the first normal exchange he had had since arriving in England,"  
Edwin said. "I could see it in his face. Relief. A transaction that worked. Salt  
pork and a nod. He had been a soldier for ten years and crossed the sea and  
fought a battle and received a writ and ridden into a yard of silent 
strangers. What he needed was someone to give him salt pork and behave 
as though it were ordinary. Sigefleda did that. I do not think she intended it 
as kindness. But it was."

[Note by the scribe, Brother Eadmer:]
I have tried, in speaking with the present 
tenants of Halecroft, to trace the descendants  
of these early families. Ulfric's line persists in 
a man called Wulfgar who holds his great-
grandfather's virgate still, and who looks, in 
the particular set of his jaw, very much as 
Edwin describes Ulfric. Eomund the miller's 
grandson holds the mill. The Beekeeper left 
no descendants, but the hives persist, tended 
by a succession of persons who appear to 
inherit the role without it being formally 
assigned. Sigefleda's sons both died in the 
great sickness of 1087, but a granddaughter 
married one of Ranulf's English ploughmen, 
and their children are among the present 
villeins of the estate. The village, in short, has  
not changed so much as its lord. — Brother 
Eadmer.

XI
Of Aelswith Herself: What She Was, What She  
Knew, and What She Chose
Anno Domini 1067–1079
I have described Aelswith as an interpreter and a wife, which she was. I 
have not yet described her as a person, which she emphatically also was, 
and which the record of her husband and the chronicles of the Conquest 
more generally tend to omit, women being considered, in the administrative  
documents of the period, as objects of transaction rather than as agents of 
history. This is a deficiency of the records rather than of the women, and I 
intend, in so far as the testimony available to me permits, to remedy it here.
What I know of Aelswith directly is this: she was born approximately 
1037, the daughter of a free woman of Worcester and a man whose identity 
is not recorded but who was, her mother told her, a Norman merchant of 
the city, which accounted for the French she had been taught as a child — 
taught not, her mother said, as a gift of sentiment but as a practical tool, in 
the same spirit that one teaches a child the names of plants: some are food, 
some are medicine, some are poison, and it is useful to be able to 
distinguish between them. She had been married to Wulfric of Halecroft at 
the age of seventeen, borne him two children, and been widowed by the 
fever of 1066 at the age of twenty-nine, in the same year the world changed 
around her.
She was, in Edwin's description, a woman of exceptional practical 
intelligence and a quality he struggled to name precisely, settling at last on 
the English word gewittig — which means, in its fullest sense, not merely 
clever but possessed of an understanding that extends beyond immediate 
circumstances to their causes and their probable consequences. She 
understood, from the first days of Ranulf's arrival, that the situation they 
were all in was not a crisis to be endured but a negotiation to be conducted,  
and that the question was not whether the negotiation would happen but 
whether it would happen with or without her participation. She chose 
participation.
"She made herself necessary," Edwin said. "Not by luck. By decision. 
She had French, and the Norman had no English, and she understood that 
this gave her a position that would last exactly as long as the position 
remained valuable — which meant she had to make it more valuable than

merely translating salt pork, which she did by extending its scope. She told 
him things he needed to know before he knew he needed to know them. 
That is a different kind of intelligence from simply answering questions."
    ✦ ✦ ✦
The marriage, when it came, was not, in Aelswith's account of it as 
reported to Edwin, a surprise. She had observed Ranulf for the better part 
of a year and had arrived at conclusions about him that she considered, 
upon the evidence, reliable. She told Edwin the night before the wedding — 
they had been neighbours and friends since childhood, and the friendship 
survived the translation of their respective conditions that the Conquest had  
produced — that Ranulf was a man she could work with, which was a 
different thing from a man she loved, and which was, at her age and in her 
situation, more immediately useful.
"She said: I do not know if I love him. I know that he listens when I 
speak, which is not common in men of any nation. I know that he planted 
wheat when he came back from the north without explaining why, and that 
a man who plants wheat when he does not have to is a man who 
understands something about the world that not all men understand. I know  
that he has never struck a tenant in my presence, which is more than could 
be said of several English lords I have known. These are the facts I have. 
Love may come from facts, if the facts are good enough. I am willing to 
discover whether they are." — Edwin's account of Aelswith, as given to 
Brother Eadmer."
She did, Edwin believed, come to love him. The evidence he offered for 
this was characteristically oblique: that in the years after their marriage she  
never again described him in the calculating terms of that pre-wedding 
conversation, but spoke of him in the way that people speak of those they 
have stopped analysing — as simply present, simply assumed, simply part of  
the structure of the world as they know it. Which is, Edwin said, what love 
actually is, when it is not performing itself for an audience.
She died in September of 1079, of an illness that came on quickly and 
did not go. She was forty-two years old. Ranulf sat by her bed for the three 
days of her dying and did not sleep, and when she died he went into the 
yard and stood there for a long time, and then he went back in and began 
making the arrangements, and from that day forward he was, Edwin said, 
not a different man but a diminished one — as though a portion of his 
interior had been cleared out and left empty, and he had decided to function  
efficiently in the space that remained rather than to fill it with anything that  
was not her.
The two silver candlesticks he gave to the church were, Ranulf told 
Edwin privately, the finest things he owned. He had bought them in

Winchester in 1070, from a silversmith whose work he had admired, with no  
particular purpose in mind. He had kept them for nine years on the shelf 
above the hall's fireplace, where they had stood in a kind of permanent 
readiness, waiting for the occasion that would justify them. He said: I 
always knew they were for her. I did not know in what form.

XII
Of the Manorial Court of Halecroft, and of  
Justice in a World of Two Languages
Anno Domini 1072–1090
Among the obligations of a lord holding land by knight's service was the 
duty to hold a manorial court — to hear the disputes of his tenants, to 
administer the customary law of the estate, to record the transactions of 
land and obligation that constituted the daily legal life of the village. This 
duty Ranulf undertook from the first year of his tenure, though the 
undertaking required, in the early years, a translation at every step that 
slowed proceedings to a pace that tested the patience of all parties.
The manorial court was held four times a year, on the morning after 
each of the four quarter days — Michaelmas, Christmas, Lady Day, and 
midsummer. It was held in the hall, with Ranulf presiding from the high 
end, the clerk Gaston taking the record in Latin, and the tenants presenting 
their cases and their disputes in English, which the clerk translated. In the 
early years this arrangement was imperfect in both directions: things that 
were said in English lost their exact force in the translation, and things that 
Ranulf said in French acquired, in the translation back, a precision or a 
softness they had not originally possessed. Gaston was not a bad translator 
but he was not a neutral one, and both Ranulf and the English tenants were 
aware of this and adjusted accordingly — Ranulf by learning enough English  
to know when the translation was approximate, and the tenants by learning 
enough French to know when the lord's words were softened.
The first significant case heard in the court — significant because it 
established the character of Ranulf's administration more clearly than any 
routine transaction could — was a dispute between Eomund the miller and a  
villein named Saewin over the amount of grain Saewin had brought to the 
mill and the amount of flour he had received in return. This was a dispute as  
old as milling itself: the miller takes his cut of the grain as his fee, and the 
question of whether the cut is fair or fraudulent is one that every village 
with a mill has argued over since the Romans first introduced the 
technology to Britain. Saewin alleged that Eomund had been taking more 
than the customary toll. Eomund alleged that Saewin's grain was wet and 
therefore lighter than it appeared, producing less flour than a dry measure 
of the same size would produce, and that this was not his fault.

Ranulf heard both sides. He asked Eomund to explain the mechanism of 
the mill in detail, which occupied some time and which Ranulf followed with  
the concentration of a man filing technical information. He asked Eomund 
to show him the toll dish — the measure used to take the miller's portion. 
He asked Sigefleda's son, who happened to be present as a witness to an 
unrelated matter, whether the size of the toll dish was consistent with what 
he remembered from before. The son said it looked the same to him. Ranulf 
asked whether Saewin's grain had in fact been wet at the time of grinding, 
and two witnesses confirmed that it had, as there had been a leak in the 
granary roof that winter.
He found for Eomund on the matter of the flour weight — wet grain 
genuinely does produce less flour — but required him to repair the toll dish 
mechanism, which showed signs of having been adjusted at some earlier 
point, and awarded Saewin a small reduction in his next season's mill dues 
as compensation for the defect. Both parties left dissatisfied, which Gaston 
said, privately, was the mark of a just judgment. Ranulf said: it is the mark 
of a judgment both parties will abide by, which is a different criterion and a 
more immediately practical one. Gaston wrote it down.
[Note by the scribe, Brother Eadmer:]
The manorial court records for Halecroft 
survive in part from the year 1074, when 
Ranulf began requiring Gaston to keep a 
proper roll rather than merely notes. These 
rolls are now in Sir Geoffrey's charter chest 
and are among the most informative 
documents I have seen for understanding how  
the Norman administration of English estates 
actually worked in practice — which is to say, 
imperfectly, adaptively, and with rather more 
pragmatic compromise between Norman legal  
form and English customary expectation than 
the official records of the Conquest suggest. 
— Brother Eadmer.
By the time William de la Hale inherited the court in 1097, he 
conducted it entirely in English, which had become by that date the working  
language of the estate in all practical matters, French being reserved for 
correspondence with Norman lords and with the king's administration. This 
shift — from a French-speaking lord translating into English, to an English-
speaking lord who could also write French — was, in the village's daily 
experience, the clearest evidence that something new had come into being 
at Halecroft: not the English world before the Conquest and not the Norman  
world the Conquest had sought to impose, but a third thing, particular to 
this valley and these families and the fifty years of daily friction and 
accommodation that had produced it.



XIII
Of Maud, the Daughter, Who Left, and of  
Leofric, the Youngest Son, Who Stayed
Anno Domini 1089–1105
The three children of Ranulf and Aelswith dispersed, as children do, in the 
directions that the world's forces and their own characters pointed them. 
William I have described as the inheritor — the eldest, the steady one, the 
man who made of his inheritance a settled fact rather than a project. It is 
the other two whose stories illuminate, from their different angles, what 
Halecroft had made of them.
Maud was married in 1089, at the age of eighteen, to a Norman knight 
named Roger de Beaumont who held lands near Hereford. The marriage 
was arranged by William, who was by that point the effective head of the 
family, Ranulf being in failing health. It was a good match by the standards 
of the time — Roger was of a slightly higher social standing than the de la 
Hale family's Norman connexions would normally command, and his 
interest in the match was partly a consequence of Maud's modest but real 
dowry and partly a consequence of her own person, which several witnesses  
describe as formidable. She spoke French with the fluency of someone for 
whom it was a first language, which it was, and she had her mother's 
capacity for assessment and her father's capacity for silence, which 
combined to produce in her, at eighteen, a quality that Roger de Beaumont 
described in a letter to his brother — I have seen this letter; it survived in 
the de Beaumont family papers — as "a woman who knows what she is 
worth, which is more than I do, and has decided, apparently, that I am 
worth her consideration, which I intend to merit."
She left Halecroft in the summer of 1089 on a horse her father gave her.  
Ranulf walked her to the gate of the estate, which was a walk of perhaps a 
quarter of a mile from the hall, and when they reached the gate he put his 
hand on the horse's neck and said something to her in French, quietly, that 
Edwin could not hear. She said something back. He nodded. She rode out.
Edwin asked Ranulf afterward what he had said to her. Ranulf thought 
for a moment and then said: I told her that her mother would have found 
the right words for this and I do not have them, but that the intention 
behind whatever words I manage is the same as whatever her mother would

have said. Edwin reported that Ranulf then walked back to the hall without 
further comment, which was, Edwin said, entirely like him.
Maud bore Roger de Beaumont four children, outlived him by twenty 
years, managed the Hereford estates with remarkable competence through 
a widowhood, and corresponded regularly with her brother William until his  
death. Her letters survive in the de la Hale charter chest and are among the  
most vivid documents in the collection — written in French of the highest 
register, covering everything from the management of her estates to the 
politics of the king's court to the progress of her children's education, and 
containing, at intervals, observations about the people and places of 
Halecroft that suggest she had carried the village in her memory with a 
fidelity that distance had only sharpened.
In one letter, written to William in 1097, she says: I dreamed last night 
of the mill brook in spring, which is the sound I hear in my memory when I 
try to recall being young. I do not know what that tells you about the nature  
of home but I think it tells you something about the nature of memory, 
which is that it does not keep what we decide is important but what it 
decides is important, and the mill brook in spring was apparently more 
important than I knew.
    ✦ ✦ ✦
Leofric, the youngest, was a different matter entirely.
He had been named, as I have recorded, for the dead English thegn, and  
there was in him from boyhood something of the quality that the name 
seemed to require of him — a rootedness, a particular attachment to the 
physical substance of the land, that his older brother William expressed 
through management and his older sister Maud expressed through memory,  
but that Leofric expressed through the most direct means available: he went  
into the wood.
The wood that Ranulf's testament left to Leofric was, as I have said, 
considered the lesser portion. But Leofric had spent his childhood in it. He 
knew every tree in it with the particular knowledge of a child who has 
played in a wood for years — not the botanical or commercial knowledge of 
a forester, but the intimate and irrational knowledge of a person who has a 
relationship with individual trees, who knows which oak has a hollow large 
enough to shelter in, which ash leans at an angle that makes it climbable, 
which spot near the spring is driest in wet weather. He had inherited this 
knowledge, partly, from his mother, who had known the wood from her own  
childhood and had taken him there from before he could walk and explained  
it to him in English, which was the language in which she knew it.

He built his house at the wood's edge, on the slope above the brook, 
between 1100 and 1103, with his own hands and those of two English 
labourers he had known all his life. It was a timber house, well-made, with a  
stone foundation that he quarried himself from an outcrop in the north 
corner of the wood. He planted an orchard on the south-facing slope below 
it. He cleared twenty acres of the wood's edge to pasture and ran cattle on 
it. He married, in 1104, a free English woman named Godiva, daughter of a 
craftsman from the town of Worcester, who brought to the marriage no land  
but a considerable skill in the management of household production — she 
could tan leather, brew ale, spin and weave, and keep accounts in the 
English fashion, with notched sticks, which she taught Leofric and which he 
used alongside the written Latin records that Gaston had taught him in 
childhood.
His house was, within a generation, the nucleus of a smaller settlement 
— two of his labourers'  families built cottages near it, and a fourth family 
came from a village two miles away whose lord had died without heirs and 
whose land had been absorbed into a larger holding, leaving them without a  
place. Leofric gave them a place. He gave them villeinage — which is to say,  
he gave them the right to hold strips in his new cleared pasture in exchange  
for labour service — and they took it, because villeinage in Leofric's wood 
was better than rootlessness on the road.
It was from this woodland settlement, known as the Hale by those who 
lived in it, that the branch of the family which eventually produced the 
Worcester solicitor Robert Hale — Nathaniel's father — descends directly. 
The name simplified over the generations, as names do: de la Hale became 
atte Hale in the thirteenth century, meaning at the hale, at the hall, at the 
enclosure; atte Hale became simply Hale in the fifteenth century, when the 
French preposition fell away along with the last vestiges of Norman social 
distinction; and Hale it remained, carried from Leofric's woodland clearing 
through the generations, losing its article and its ancestry and eventually its  
knowledge of either, until it arrived, in the form of a solicitor's third son 
named Nathaniel, at the point where the name would have to be rebuilt 
from nothing, as Ranulf had built it in the first place.
Ranulf would have recognised the symmetry, I think. It would not have 
surprised him. The world, in his experience, had a tendency to return things  
to their original condition.

XIV
Of the Church at Halecroft, and of Father  
Cynewulf, and of the Negotiations Between the  
Norman Lord and the Saxon God
Anno Domini 1067–1100
I am a monk, and I have written thus far with the restraint that my 
profession requires about the matter that is closest to my profession. It is 
time to write directly about the church at Halecroft, and about what it 
meant in the years this chronicle describes, because the church was not 
merely an institution or a building or a source of sacraments. It was the one 
space in Halecroft that was, from the first day of Ranulf's arrival, explicitly 
neither Norman nor English but something that contained both — a shared 
territory that neither party had created and that therefore, by a logic that I 
think neither party entirely understood, belonged to both.
The church of St Wulfstan’s at Halecroft was old. How old I cannot say 
precisely; Father Cynewulf, who was old himself, said that the oldest 
parishioners of his own youth remembered their grandparents speaking of 
the church as already ancient, which would put its origins at least in the 
ninth century and possibly earlier. It was built of stone, which was unusual 
for a village church of that period, and the stone had weathered to the 
particular grey-brown of old limestone that suggests several centuries of 
English weather. The interior was small — room for perhaps fifty people 
standing — with a single aisle, a wooden chancel screen, and a small 
sacristy that served also as Father Cynewulf's living quarters.
Father Cynewulf had been the priest of Halecroft for thirty years when 
Ranulf arrived, and had every intention of continuing for thirty more, and 
largely did. He was an English priest of the old formation — ordained not by  
formal seminary training, which barely existed in rural England at that 
period, but by a bishop's laying-on of hands following a period of 
apprenticeship to an older priest, and educated in the practical liturgy of 
the parish rather than the theology of the schools. He was not, by the 
standards of the reforming churchmen who were beginning to transform the  
English Church in the aftermath of the Conquest, an impressive figure. He 
spoke no Latin beyond the liturgy; his theology was a mixture of orthodox 
doctrine and the older folk practices of the English countryside that the 
Church had long since officially condemned and practically tolerated; and 
he had, over thirty years in the same village, developed the specific mixture

of personal authority and profound local knowledge that makes a country 
priest either a great asset or a considerable obstacle depending on the 
disposition of the lord he serves.
He served Ranulf with what I can only describe as a magnificent 
pragmatism. When Ranulf asked him, through the clerk, whether the mass 
could be conducted more quickly because it was harvest time and the 
tenants needed to be in the fields, Father Cynewulf said, in English, that 
God's time was not subject to harvest schedules, and then reduced the mass  
by eight minutes through a series of adjustments that he never explicitly 
acknowledged and that technically violated no canonical rule. When Ranulf 
asked him to conduct the blessing of the new mill channel in French, so that  
the Norman guests present could understand it, Father Cynewulf said the 
blessing was in Latin, which neither the Normans nor the English 
understood, and that this was the point of it. When Ranulf asked him to pray  
for the soul of Aelswith at the Sunday mass, he did so, in terms of such 
warmth and particularity that several of the congregation wept, and 
continued to include her name in the prayers of the faithful for the 
remaining twelve years of his ministry at Halecroft.
    ✦ ✦ ✦
The relationship between Ranulf and Father Cynewulf was one of 
mutual incomprehension and mutual respect, which is a combination more 
common in the records of human cooperation than either theologians or 
cynics generally acknowledge. Ranulf did not understand the old priest's 
English theology with its admixture of older belief. Father Cynewulf did not 
understand Ranulf's Norman Catholicism, which had been shaped by the 
reforming spirit of William's church and was considerably more orthodox 
than anything the English rural parishes of the period practiced. Each found  
the other's religion slightly alien and slightly interesting, and each 
concluded, over time, that the alien and interesting thing was not a threat 
but a variant of the same fundamental impulse toward meaning.
I note this because it seems to me one of the small, unremarked 
processes by which the England of the post-Conquest period actually 
formed itself: not through the great political transactions of kings and 
bishops, which the chronicles record, but through the ten thousand local 
negotiations between Norman lords and English priests, between French-
speaking administrators and English-speaking farmers, between a legal 
system being imported from Normandy and a customary practice rooted too  
deep in the land to be simply overwritten. England after 1066 was not 
Norman, and it was not Saxon. It was the friction between them, gradually 
producing heat, and then light, and then something new.
Father Cynewulf died in 1101, having outlasted Ranulf by two years, 
and was buried, at his own request, beneath the floor of the sacristy he had

lived in for forty years. His successor was a younger man, partly Norman-
trained, who spoke both languages competently and conducted the mass 
with a briskness that suggested he had indeed been told about the harvest 
schedules. The congregation adapted. Adaptation was, by 1101, the primary  
skill of the people of Halecroft. They had been practicing it for thirty-five 
years.

XV
Of Ranulf in His Age, and of the Conversations  
He Had with Edwin That Were Not About  
Business
Anno Domini 1090–1099
In the last decade of his life, Ranulf de la Hale was, by Edwin's account, a 
different kind of man from the one who had arrived in the yard at Halecroft 
in the spring of 1067. Not in his essential character — the appetite was still 
there, the practical intelligence, the capacity for silence when silence was 
more useful than speech — but in what he chose to spend his attention on. A  
man of sixty, with his land secure and his children grown and his wife 
buried and his debts of service paid, has a kind of freedom that is not 
available to younger men, and Ranulf used it in a way that Edwin found, at 
first, surprising: he began to ask questions.
Not questions about the estate, or the court, or the management of his 
obligations to the king's administration. Questions about England before the  
Conquest. Questions about what it had been like. He asked Edwin about the  
festivals — the old English celebrations of the year's turning that the 
Church had overlaid with saints' days but not entirely replaced. He asked 
about the governance of the hundred court before the Normans reorganised  
it. He asked about King Harold, which Edwin said was the most surprising 
question of all, coming from a man who had fought against Harold at 
Hastings.
"He asked me: was Harold a good king? I said: he was king for nine 
months. That is not long enough to determine. Ranulf said: from what you 
saw of him. I said: I saw him once, at the muster before Stamford Bridge. 
He was a tall man who sat his horse as though he had been born on it, and 
the men who had served him longest were the ones who most wanted to 
follow him, which is the best evidence of a man's quality in my experience. 
Ranulf was quiet for a moment and then said: yes. It is the same evidence I 
have found reliable."
"He asked me once — this was perhaps two years before he died — 
whether I had forgiven the Normans. I told him I had not, in general, 
because forgiveness in general is a theological category and not something I  
have direct experience of; but that I had, specifically and over thirty years, 
come to a position with respect to him personally that was not resentment,

and that if this was forgiveness I was willing to call it so, though I preferred 
the word accommodation, which is more accurate. He said: accommodation.  
Yes. That is what it is. I have been trying to remember the right word for it 
for thirty years. He seemed, when I said it, genuinely relieved."
    ✦ ✦ ✦
In the summer of 1098, the year before his death, Ranulf did something 
that Edwin did not understand until later. He walked, alone, from Halecroft 
to the church of St Mary at Worcester, which was a journey of 
approximately seven miles, and presented himself at the door of this priory 
and asked to speak with a priest. The priest who came was not my 
predecessor but an older monk whom I knew in my novitiate and who died 
some years ago; but he told the story before he died, and I have it from a 
brother who heard it.
Ranulf told the monk: I have held land in this county for thirty years 
that I took in a war. I have tried to hold it well. I am going to die and I want
