to know whether what I have done is, in the reckoning of the Church, 
sufficient, or whether there is something else required of me. The monk 
asked him: have you confessed your sins? He said: regularly, to my parish 
priest, but I am not speaking of sins. I am speaking of the weight of a 
transaction. The land was taken. I have held it. Is the holding a sufficient 
answer to the taking? The monk said: that is a question for God's judgment, 
not mine. Ranulf said: I thought so. Then can you at least tell me what form 
the question should take in prayer, so that I am asking God the right thing? 
The monk thought for a considerable time and then said: ask for justice. Not  
mercy — any man can ask for mercy. Ask for justice, and mean it, which is 
the harder ask, because justice may not find for you. Ranulf said: yes. That 
is the right question. He thanked the monk, walked home, and was dead 
within the year.

XVI
Of What Halecroft Became, and of the Long  
Work of a Name
Anno Domini 1100–1121
I write this final chapter not as a conclusion — the story of the de la Hale 
family has no conclusion I can reach from 1121, being as it is a story still in 
progress — but as a summation. A weighing of what I have gathered, set 
against what it seems to mean.
In fifty-five years, the estate of Halecroft has passed from a dead 
English thegn to a Norman soldier, from that soldier to his English-Norman 
son, from that son to his grandson, who is the man who asked me to write 
this, and who is himself the father of a boy who asked: who was Ranulf? The  
question was innocent. It was also, I think, the most important question the 
family asks of itself, and will continue to ask, in one form or another, for as 
long as there are people who bear the name.
The hall Ranulf found in 1067 has been rebuilt once and repaired many 
times. The mill stands and grinds. The wood where Leofric built his house 
has grown and been cleared and grown again in the way of managed woods,  
which are cut and replanted on a cycle that outlasts any individual tree or 
any individual family by centuries. The church of St Wulfstan’s is as old and 
as grey as it was when the Normans came, and will outlast everything else 
recorded in this chronicle.
The village has changed. The spoken language of daily life is English, 
with a French word here and there embedded in it like stones in a wall, 
from the fifty years of bilingual administration. The law is Norman in its 
form and English in its application, as it is across England. The food, the 
farming, the festivals — these are English, with Norman additions in the 
great hall and Norman occasions in the calendar. The children of the 
present generation know only one world, which is the world that their 
grandparents'  negotiations produced, and they do not know that it is a 
produced thing rather than a given one. This is, I think, how cultures form: 
not through the decisions of kings and the battles of armies, which history 
records, but through the accumulated negotiations of ordinary people on 
ordinary estates, which history does not.

FROM THE WORCESTERSHIRE PLEA ROLLS, ENTRIES CONCERNING THE 
DE LA HALE FAMILY, YEARS 1097–1121
1097. William de la Hale appears before the sheriff's court to confirm 
his tenure of Halecroft following his father's retirement. The tenure is 
confirmed. He pays the relief — the inheritance fee — of ten shillings. 
Noted as a man who speaks good French and better English and who 
keeps his court rolls in correct Latin.
1103. Leofric, younger son of the late Ranulf de la Hale, brings a 
complaint against a neighbour to the east who has encroached upon 
the boundary of his woodland holding. The boundary is walked by the 
county surveyor. The neighbour is found to have encroached by twenty 
yards. He is required to remove his fence and pay a small penalty. 
Leofric is recorded as satisfied with the judgment.
1115. Geoffrey de la Hale succeeds to Halecroft on the death of his 
father William. He does homage before the sheriff. He is noted as a 
young man of about twenty-two years, well-spoken in both languages, 
who has already demonstrated competence in the management of his 
estate. His tenure is confirmed without dispute.
1119. Geoffrey de la Hale commissions a monk of the Cathedral Priory 
to compile the history of his family for the preservation of the record. 
This is noted as an unusual act for a man of his station, most such 
compilations being reserved for families of greater eminence. The prior  
of Worcester approves the commission, noting that the preservation of 
local history is a work of piety and scholarship equally.
Brother Eadmer continues his conclusion:
When Geoffrey de la Hale commissioned me to write this chronicle, he 
said something that I have thought about at intervals throughout the two 
years of its composition. He said: write what is true, including what is not 
flattering, because a history that flatters is of no use to the people who 
come after, who will need to know what actually happened rather than what  
their predecessors would have wished to have happened.
I have tried to honour this instruction. I have written that Ranulf came 
as a conqueror. I have written that he participated in the Harrying of the 
North and returned changed by it. I have written that the land at Halecroft 
was not given to him but taken, and that the document calling it a gift — the  
king's grant of confiscated property — is a polite and legally accurate 
description of a violent fact. I have written all of this alongside what Ranulf

did with what he had taken, which was, in my judgment, more than the 
minimum that the situation required and less than perfect justice, and 
which produced, over three generations, something worth preserving.
The name de la Hale means, as I have said, of the hall, of the enclosure, 
of the place set apart. In Norman French it is a designation of location: a 
family defined by the place they hold. In the English that the family 
increasingly spoke, the same root — hale, heall — means the same thing, 
with the additional resonance that English gives to old words worn smooth 
by long use: a hall is a place of gathering, of judgment, of warmth in winter,  
of the particular communal life that distinguishes a settlement from a 
collection of isolated people.
Ranulf de la Hale came to England with a name that was, at that point, 
a designation of where he was from. He left it, at his death, as a designation  
of what he had built — which was a place, and a set of obligations to the 
people in it, and a method of holding both that his descendants have 
inherited, modified, and in certain instances betrayed, and from which they 
continue, as I write, to derive their identity.
Whether that is sufficient answer to the question he asked on his 
deathbed — is that sufficient? — I do not know. The question does not admit  
of a final answer, and I think Ranulf knew this before he asked it. He asked 
it anyway, because the asking is what a man of conscience does, even when 
the answer is withheld.
I have finished this chronicle on the fourth day of October, 1121, which 
is the anniversary, within a day, of the battle. Fifty-five years is a long time 
in a human life and a short time in the life of a family. The mill is turning. 
The fields are in their autumn colours. The yew in the churchyard is exactly 
as it has always been.
I commend the souls of all those named in these pages to the mercy of 
God, and this chronicle to the care of whoever comes after.

A Note Upon This Text
Added by the present editor, who compiled the full Hale family archive in 1887
Brother Eadmer's Chronicle was discovered in the manuscript collection of 
Worcester Cathedral Priory in 1872 by the Reverend Thomas Attwater, who 
was then engaged in cataloguing the mediaeval holdings of that institution. 
He published a partial transcription in a volume of county antiquities in 
1875 which received almost no attention, the period being one in which the 
reading public's appetite for Norman estate management was not 
pronounced.
It was brought to my attention in 1884, in connection with my assembly 
of the Hale family papers for the volume containing Sir Nathaniel's Memoirs  
and, subsequently, the correspondence of Mr Edmund Hale. I recognized 
immediately the significance of the chronicle as the founding document of 
the family history it initiates, and I have accordingly placed it at the 
commencement of this archive, before the Memoirs, though it was 
discovered after them.
The reader who has followed the Hale family from this beginning to 
Edmund's end — from Ranulf dismounting in a Worcestershire yard in 1067 
to Edmund sealing his escritoire in Shropshire in 1882 — will observe that 
the family theme announces itself at the very origin. A man who cannot 
undo what he has done. A man who must decide whether to acknowledge 
what he is or to perform something else. The question that Ranulf asked on 
his deathbed — is that sufficient? — is the same question that Edmund Hale 
was still asking, eight centuries later, in a different register, with different 
stakes, and with rather more elaborate vocabulary.
It is, perhaps, the only question that a family ever really asks. The 
names change. The question persists.
Sir Augustus Frere, K.C., F.S.A.
Inner Temple, London, 1887

A Glossary of Words and Terms
The language of Domesday England, and of those who wrote about it
Thegn
An Anglo-Saxon landowner of rank — one step below the nobility, holding land in 
exchange for military and administrative service to the king or a great lord. The 
thegns were the backbone of Saxon England, and the Conquest destroyed their 
class almost entirely, replacing them with Norman knights. Leofric was a thegn; 
Ranulf was the man who replaced him.
Ceorl (ceorlisc)
A free man of the Anglo-Saxon lower class — not a slave, not a noble, but a man 
who held land, paid taxes, and owed military service. Edwin's father was a ceorlisc 
man. In the decades after the Conquest, the distinction between free men and 
villeins was increasingly used by Norman lords to reduce obligations — what had 
been free tenure was reclassified as villein tenure, and customary rights were 
quietly eroded.
Villein
A serf under the feudal system — a person bound to the land, unable to leave 
without the lord's permission, owing fixed labour services in exchange for their 
strips in the open field. Not a slave, but not free. The distinction between villein 
and free man was one of the most important and most contested in medieval 
English law.
Virgate
A unit of land measurement, typically about thirty acres, sufficient to support a 
family. The virgate was the standard unit of peasant tenure in the open-field 
system. Two virgates was a comfortable holding; one virgate was subsistence; a 
fraction of a virgate was poverty.
Pannage
The right to drive pigs into the lord's wood in autumn to feed on fallen acorns and 
beech mast. Pannage rights were among the most valuable and most contested of 
common rights, because oak and beech woods produced, in a good year, enough 
mast to fatten the pigs that were the primary protein source for most English 
peasants.
Amanuensis
A person who takes down dictation — the recorder of another's words. Brother 
Eadmer is, in a sense, the amanuensis of the whole de la Hale story: he takes down

what others tell him, and the gaps between the testimonies are as significant as 
the testimonies themselves.
Rubric
In medieval manuscripts, the headings and decorative initials written in red ink — 
from the Latin rubrica, red earth. Chapter headings were rubrics; they stood out 
from the black text of the body. The visual design of this novel attempts to 
preserve this distinction.
Harrying
From the Old English heregian — to lay waste, to ravage. The Harrying of the 
North in 1069–70 was one of the most devastating acts of deliberate destruction in 
English history: William ordered the systematic burning of crops, livestock, and 
settlements across the northern counties to deny resources to the Danish-backed 
rebellion. Contemporary chroniclers estimated that the resulting famine killed tens  
of thousands. The land was still waste, in places, twenty years later, as the 
Domesday Book records.
Demesne
The portion of a lord's estate farmed directly by him, as opposed to land let to 
tenants. The Domesday entry for Halecroft notes two ploughs in the demesne — 
that is, two plough-teams working the lord's own land. The rest of the arable was 
held by the villeins and free men in strips in the open field.
Writ
A formal document in the name of a king or lord, ordering or granting something. 
The Anglo-Saxons had writs; the Normans used them extensively. The land-grant 
writ is the most important document in the de la Hale story: it is the piece of 
vellum that says, in law, that the conquest is a fact.
Scriptorium
The room in a monastery where manuscripts were copied and composed. Brother 
Eadmer works in the scriptorium of Worcester Priory — surrounded by other 
monks copying psalters and chronicles, in a room that smells of vellum, oak-gall 
ink, and tallow candles.
Vellum
Prepared animal skin — calf, sheep, or goat — used as writing material before 
paper became common. Vellum was expensive and durable. The documents in this 
novel would have been written on it, which is why they survive. The texture and 
colour of aged vellum is the visual register this book attempts to evoke.
Fealty
Loyalty and allegiance owed by a vassal to a lord, formally sworn on the Gospels. 
The entire feudal system rested on fealty: you held your land in exchange for 
loyalty, military service, and a specific set of obligations. Breaking fealty was 
treason. The interesting thing about the de la Hale situation at Halecroft is that the

English tenants owed fealty to Ranulf, who had taken the land from their former 
lord by conquest: fealty, in this case, was not freely given but accepted as the only 
alternative to homelessness.
Chronicle
A historical record arranged by year — from the Greek chronos, time. The Anglo-
Saxon Chronicle, begun in the ninth century, is the most important historical 
document of Anglo-Saxon England. It is written in Old English, which makes it the 
only significant historical text of the period not written in Latin, and which gives it 
a directness and an occasional bluntness that Latin prose tends to smooth away.
Domesday Book
The great survey of England ordered by William the Conqueror in 1085 and 
completed in 1086 — a record of who held what land, how much it was worth, and 
what obligations were attached to it. It was called Domesday because its 
judgments were final, like the Last Judgment. It is the most extraordinary 
administrative document of the medieval world, and it tells us more about England 
in 1086 than we know about most countries for centuries afterward.
EXPLICIT
Hic finit Chronica Domus de la Hale de Wigornscira.
"Here ends the Chronicle of the House of de la Hale of Worcestershire."

THE HALE DYNASTY
BOOK II
The House of Hale
Being a Compilation of Parish Records, Inquest Testimonies,
Manorial Rolls, and Sundry Documents pertaining to the Family
of Hale of Halecroft, in the County of Worcestershire,
gathered and set in order by the hand of one who came after.
Covering the years
1121 — 1400
    ✶ ✶ ✶
Editor's Prefatory Note
The documents gathered in this volume constitute the second part of what the  
collector Sir Augustus Frere — who first assembled the Hale family archive in 1884  
— described in his private correspondence as the dark century of the family's  
keeping. The Norman root, as recorded by Brother Eadmer of Worcester Priory, had  
produced by the middle of the twelfth century a settled English family: no longer de  
la Hale, nor quite yet simply Hale, but something in between — a name in the process  
of becoming what it would remain for nine hundred years thereafter.
What follows is drawn from three principal sources: the manorial rolls of Halecroft,  
preserved  in  part  at  the  Worcester  Record  Office;  the  parish  register  of  St.  
Wulfstan's, Halecroft, which survives from 1214 in a fragmentary state; and a  
collection of testimonies gathered during the inquest of 1351, which concerned itself  
with the disposition of the Hale lands following the deaths — in the space of fourteen  
months — of every male member of the family save one.
That one was a boy of eleven named Thomas Hale. This is, among other things, his  
story.
The language of these documents is the English of their time: Middle English in its  
several registers, ranging from the formal Latinate English of the ecclesiastical  
records to the rougher, more vigorous tongue of the manorial court. The editor has  
preserved  the  original  orthography  where  the  source  documents  survive,  and  
rendered the remainder in the spirit of the age — which is to say, with a directness

and a plainness that the Victorians would have found rather startling, and which the  
modern reader may find, in its own way, not unlike coming home.
— A.F.
London, November 1884

I
What Leofric Built
Halecroft, Worcestershire, 1121–1195
From the chronicle of Brother Eadmer, which closed in the year of Our Lord  
1121, we know this much: Leofric, the younger son of Ranulf de la Hale and his  
English wife Aelswith, walked into the wood to the east of Halecroft in the spring of  
that year, felled what timber he required, built a house at the spring, and called the  
place  the  Hale.  He  was,  by  his  father's  chronicle,  a  man  of  few  words  and  
considerable purpose. He married. He cleared. He farmed.
The records that survive from the subsequent seventy years are thin. The manor  
of Halecroft continued under Leofric's elder brother William, who proved competent  
if uninspired; William's son Richard succeeded him in 1168, and Richard's son  
Edmund in 1191. The elder line of de la Hale persisted, performed its obligations to  
the lords of Worcester, paid its tithes to St. Wulfstan's, and left very little impression  
upon the historical record — which is, in its own way, the mark of a family that has  
succeeded in what it set out to do.
Leofric's line, at the Hale, left rather more.
 Nota Bene — ✦Here the parish register of St. Wulfstan's Halecroft takes up the account, in the 
hand of Father Osbert, who held the living from 1178 to 1214. Father Osbert was not a subtle 
man, but he was thorough.
FROM THE PARISH REGISTER OF ST. WULFSTAN'S, HALECROFT —  
C. 1189
ANNO DOMINI 1189
Item. Baptised this Michaelmas the son of Robert Hale of the Hale and his wife  
Edith, formerly Edith atte Pond of Pershore. The child is called William. Father  
Osbert did perform the sacrament.
Item. Buried this same season one Godwin the elder, villein of the manor, aged by  
estimation fourscore years, who had been a boy at the Conquest and who  
remembered Ranulf de la Hale coming to the gate. He is the last such. God have  
mercy upon his soul.

The entry for Godwin the elder is worth pausing upon. He was, so far as can be  
ascertained, the last living person in Halecroft who had witnessed the arrival of the  
original Norman soldier — the man who had stood at the gate in the autumn of 1066  
and been given salt pork by a widow named Sigefleda. That the parish priest noted  
his passing is either pastoral sentiment or an awareness, however dimly felt, that  
something was ending. The village that Godwin had known — half Norman, half  
English, neither quite at ease with the other — had resolved, across a century, into  
something new. The name above the holding in the wood was now simply Hale, with  
no article, no Norman particle, nothing to mark its origin. A name become English by  
the slow labour of time.
Robert Hale of the Hale — father of the William baptised in 1189 — appears in the  
manorial rolls of 1183 as a free tenant holding twelve acres of woodland and four of  
arable. He owed suit of court twice yearly and a hen at Christmas. He had a plough-
beast, two pigs, and a wife with strong opinions about the mill. He was, in other  
words, a small free farmer of modest but secure standing: neither the poverty of the  
villein class nor the pretension of the lesser gentry, but something comfortably  
between — the English yeoman in his early form.
His son William would be something rather more.
ANNO DOMINI 1214
Item. Died this January the Reverend Osbert, priest of this parish these six-and-
thirty years. He was not much loved but he was always present. God have mercy  
upon his soul.
Item. William Hale of the Hale hath this year purchased from the lord of the  
manor the freehold of such lands as his father Robert held in villeinage, together  
with two further strips of the east field, paying therefore the sum of four shillings  
and a promise of military service in the event of necessity, which necessity hath  
not yet arisen. He is henceforward a freeholder of this parish and oweth no  
further suit of court.
The purchase recorded in 1214 is the first significant act of accumulation in the  
documentary record of the Hale family — the first moment in which a Hale spends

money not merely to survive but to advance. Robert Hale had held twelve acres by  
lease. William Hale now holds fourteen by freehold. The difference between those  
two facts is not merely agricultural; it is the difference between a man who may be  
dispossessed and a man who may not.
William Hale would hold those fourteen acres for fifty-one years. He would add to  
them twice, though modestly. He would serve on the manorial jury in 1231 and again  
in 1244. He would build a second barn. He would send his eldest son Edmund to the  
priory school at Worcester, which cost him a great deal and produced, by his own  
account,  only  a  young  man  with  opinions  about  theology  and  an  unfortunate  
tendency to quote Ovid at the supper table.
He would also live to see the first of the great trials that the fourteenth century  
had in preparation for the family — though not the worst of them.
    ✶ ✶ ✶

II
The Education of Edmund Hale
Worcester and Halecroft, 1235–1280
Edmund Hale, the eldest son of William, was sent to the Priory of St. Mary at  
Worcester in the year 1235, when he was eight years old. He was not sent to become  
a monk — his father was explicit upon this point in a letter to the prior, which  
survives in the Worcester Cathedral Archive — but to learn what a man of property  
ought  to  know:  letters,  numbers,  a  little  Latin,  enough  Scripture  to  satisfy  a  
confessor, and sufficient acquaintance with the law to avoid being cheated by those  
more educated than himself.
He received all of this, and somewhat more besides. The prior of 1235 was a man  
named Brother Cadfael — a Worcestershire man born, spare and astringent, who  
had spent fifteen years in the schools at Paris before deciding that the proper  
business of a monk was not philosophy but literacy, and that the proper business of  
literacy was to make it available to those who would otherwise remain at the mercy  
of those who possessed it.
Edmund was at Worcester for nine years. He returned to Halecroft in 1244 with a  
hand that could write a clear court document, a mind that could follow a legal  
argument, and a disposition — according to his father — that had become inclined  
toward precision and away from comfort. William Hale, who had worked hard all his  
life to own fourteen acres free and clear, looked at his educated son and could not  
quite tell whether he had done the right thing.
 Nota Bene — ✦What follows is a letter from Edmund Hale to his brother Robert, written in the 
year 1251. It is the earliest surviving letter written by a member of the Hale family. The spelling 
is Edmund's own. The Latin phrases are used correctly, which tells us something about Brother 
Cadfael's school.
A LETTER FROM EDMUND HALE TO HIS BROTHER ROBERT —  
BEING THE FEAST OF ST. BARTHOLOMEW, 1251
Robert my brother, I write from Worcester where I am come on the matter of the  
boundary dispute with the lord Warin's men, which matter I do think will come to

some resolution before the month's end if the lord Warin's steward hath the wit to  
see what is plain, which I do not altogether take for granted.
I have seen the deed of 1183 which father spoke of, and it doth set out the eastern  
boundary at the old oak and not at the stream as Warin's man doth claim. The oak  
standeth still, though it is much reduced by time. I have had Father Dunstan — who  
hath the Latin — make a copy of the relevant passage, which I send hereunder.  
Nota bene the phrase in virgata una et dimidia: this is one virgate and a half, and  
not one virgate, as Warin's steward hath represented to the court. Either he cannot  
read or he hath read and chosen not to. I know not which is the worse.
I am in health. The inn is tolerable. Tell mother I shall return before the harvest if  
God wills it. Give my respects to William Carpenter who I hear hath finished the  
new gate, which I hope is better made than the old one, which let in the wind most  
grievously.
Your loving brother, Edmund Hale, this Tuesday. God save you.
The boundary dispute with Lord Warin's estate took three further years to  
resolve, and was settled in the Hale family's favour in 1254. Edmund's grasp of the  
Latin deed was, according to the manorial court record, decisive. He received no fee  
for his service; he had simply gone to Worcester and read the document and told the  
truth of what it said. It was, perhaps, the most valuable thing his father's money had  
ever purchased.
Edmund married in 1248 — a woman named Agnes, the daughter of a prosperous  
Pershore tanner, who brought to the marriage a dowry of three pounds, a loom, and  
a reputation for not suffering fools, which reputation proved thoroughly merited  
across forty years of marriage. They had five children: William, Alice, Robert, Joan,  
and a second Edmund who died at two days old and was buried under the west wall  
of St. Wulfstan's with a small stone that can still be found there, if you know where to  
look.
The eldest, William — William Hale the younger, as the records style him —  
inherited the holding at the Hale upon Edmund's death in 1287. Edmund was sixty  
years old, which was a considerable age. He had spent his life on the same fourteen  
acres his father had purchased, had added perhaps two more, had served as jury  
foreman at the manorial court on seven occasions, had written documents for half  
the village at one time or another, and had been, by all surviving accounts, a man of  
plain virtue and unsentimental competence.

His grandson, the William Hale who appears in the records of 1320, would face  
something that no Hale had faced before, and that no document in the family archive  
had prepared any of them for.
    ✶ ✶ ✶

III
The Famine Years
Halecroft, 1315–1325
The Great Famine came to Worcestershire in the summer of 1315 as it came to all  
of England: with rain. It rained in May and it rained in June and it rained in July. The  
wheat rotted in the field. The hay could not be dried. The peas and beans that  
survived the wet were taken by mildew before they could be stored. By October it  
was clear that there would be nothing like a sufficient harvest, and by January 1316  
people in the towns were dying of hunger — not the peasants yet, who had reserves  
and cunning, but the urban poor who had nothing behind them, and then, as spring  
came on with further rain and a second ruined planting, those with less cunning than  
they had supposed.
The manorial rolls of Halecroft for 1315 to 1322 — seven years — are written in a  
hand that grows progressively more strained. The court was still held twice yearly;  
the suits were still recorded; the tithes and rents were noted as received or as  
outstanding. But the entries that proliferate across these seven years, filling margins  
and adding themselves to the foot of columns never designed for such annotation,  
are the records of death and default: tenants who could not pay their rent, tenants  
who had abandoned their holdings, tenants who were dead.
FROM THE MANORIAL ROLLS OF HALECROFT — MICHAELMAS 1317
Item. Roger atte Brook, villein, holdeth one half-virgate in the west field. Hath  
paid no rent since Easter last. The reeve reporteth that Roger atte Brook hath  
sold his plough-beast in the spring to buy grain and hath nothing more to sell. The  
court ordereth that his rent be forgiven for this term and that the lord's barn shall  
provide one bushel of rye for the sustenance of his household, this to be counted  
as a loan against future rents.
Item. The widow Marjorie Carpenter, free tenant, surrendereth her holding of  
three acres in the south field, she being unable to maintain the same. The said  
holding doth revert to the manor and will be re-let when a suitable tenant can be  
found. The widow Marjorie is permitted to remain in her cottage without charge  
of rent until the following Michaelmas, at which time her situation shall be  
reviewed.

Item. Thomas Smith, villein, is reported dead this Lammas. His son Richard,  
being of age and sound in body, doth claim his father's virgate. Granted, on  
payment of heriot, being the best beast of the said Thomas, which is one very thin  
ox.
William Hale, who held the Hale in these years, fares rather better in the record  
than many of his neighbours, which is to say that his name does not appear among  
those who defaulted on rent or abandoned their holdings or required assistance from  
the lord's barn. Whether this was because his land in the woodland was better  
drained than the open fields, or because the pigs he kept were less susceptible than  
the grain to the persistent wet, or simply because he had reserves and was careful  
with them, the rolls do not say. He appears in 1316 as a juror, and again in 1318, and  
in 1320 as foreman — Edmund his grandfather's office, now his. He had learned,  
somewhere, to read a document.
His wife Margaret is mentioned once in the famine years: in the court record of  
Easter 1318, where she is noted as having provided bread from the Hale's oven to  
the household of Roger atte Brook, the same Roger whose rent had been forgiven the  
previous autumn. There is no ceremony in the recording of this fact. The clerk writes  
it as he would write any other transaction: Margaret wife of William Hale doth give  
bread. It is done. The names are written down.
By 1322, the rains had eased and the harvests had partially recovered. The  
damage had been immense — not merely in deaths, which were considerable, but in  
the fabric of the manorial system, which had revealed itself to be rather more fragile  
than the long centuries of its operation might have suggested. Holdings had reverted  
to the manor that would not be re-let for a generation. Families that had farmed the  
same strips for two hundred years had simply ended, their names extinguished not  
by plague or war but by weather and arithmetic.
The Hales had survived. The Hale had held. The family that would face what  
came next was intact.
What came next was considerably worse than rain.
    ✶ ✶ ✶

IV
The Pestilence
Worcestershire, June–December 1348
The plague reached Bristol in the summer of 1348. It had come from the  
Continent — through France, through the ports of the south — and it moved with a  
speed that no one had prepared for and no one could explain. Within four months of  
its arrival in Bristol it was in Gloucester. By October it was in Worcester. By  
November it had reached Halecroft.
What the plague was, precisely, the people of 1348 did not know. The physicians  
said it was a corruption of the air; the clergy said it was the wrath of God; the  
peasantry said it was the end of the world, and in the particular world they had  
known it was not entirely wrong. The modern understanding — that it was Yersinia  
pestis, carried by fleas on rats, moving through human populations with a fatality  
rate of somewhere between thirty and sixty per cent — would not have helped them.  
What they knew was that a man would be well in the morning and dead before  
nightfall, and that the dead turned black and stank, and that the priest who anointed  
the dying died himself, and that the gravedigger who buried the priest was dead  
before the grave was properly filled.
THE DEATH REGISTER OF ST. WULFSTAN'S HALECROFT —  
AUTUMN 1348
Being a record kept by Father Edmund Croft, priest of this parish, of those departed in 
the pestilence, God have mercy on their souls.
November, the feast of All Saints — Agnes atte Well, widow. John Miller and his wife 
Isabel and their three children, whose names were Hugh, Margaret, and the infant 
called Blessing who had no other name yet given. Simon the tanner.
November, the feast of St. Martin — Richard Hale of the Hale, freeholder, aged four-
and-forty. His wife Maud. Their son Edmund, aged eighteen.
November, the week following — William Hale of the Hale, freeholder, aged six-and-
twenty, son of the said Richard and Maud. His wife Alice. Their daughter Joan, aged

three.
December, the feast of St. Andrew — Thomas Hale of the Hale, freeholder, aged two-
and-twenty. Brother to the said William.
December — Roger the reeve. Margaret wife of the carpenter. The carpenter himself, 
the day following.
December, before Christmas — Father Edmund Croft, priest of this parish and keeper 
of this register. He wrote the above entries in his own hand, and he is gone.
[The remainder of this page is blank. The following entries are in a different, less 
practiced hand, identified by internal evidence as that of a lay brother from the priory 
who came to assist the parish when no priest remained.]
December, after Christmas — Walter Hale, freeholder, aged seventeen, last surviving 
son of Richard Hale. Buried without ceremony there being none left to perform it.
Remaining: Thomas Hale, son of the said William and Alice, aged eleven years. He 
liveth.
There it is: the death of the Hale in fourteen months of recorded time. Richard  
Hale, fifty years old, the freeholder, the juror, the man who knew the east field as  
well as he knew his own hand — gone. His wife Maud — gone. Three sons: Edmund,  
William, Thomas, all gone. William's wife Alice, William's daughter Joan — gone.  
Walter, the youngest, who had escaped the first wave and must have thought himself  
spared, gone at Christmas. The tanner, the miller, the carpenter, the reeve, the  
priest — the fabric of the village, shredded in the space of a season.
What survived was a boy of eleven named Thomas, who had been sent in October  
to stay with his mother's sister in Pershore and who, when he returned to Halecroft  
in the spring of 1349, found that everyone he had known was dead.
TESTIMONY OF BROTHER ANSELM OF WORCESTER PRIORY, GIVEN TO THE  
INQUEST OF 1351:

I came to Halecroft in the March of the year 1349, being sent by the prior to take  
account of what had befallen the parish, for we had received no word from Father  
Croft since November and the silence did itself tell us something of what we would  
find.
I found the village much diminished but not, God be thanked, extinguished. Of the  
some four-and-thirty households that had stood in the autumn, perhaps twenty  
remained, the rest having been emptied either by death or by the flight of those who  
had chosen to risk the road rather than remain. Among those who had remained and  
lived were Oswin the miller's boy, who had survived though his parents had not, and  
old Matilda the beekeeper, who appeared not to have noticed the pestilence at all, or  
if she had, had decided it was none of her affair.
The holding at the Hale was empty. The boy Thomas had not yet returned from  
Pershore, as I have said. I went there and found the house standing and the animals  
still living — someone, perhaps a neighbour, had been tending them — and the deed-
box in its place on the shelf where it had always been, which told me that whoever  
had cared for the animals had known enough not to touch what was not theirs.
I stayed a fortnight. I buried what remained to be buried, which was not much, for  
the snow had been a mercy of a kind. I said masses for the dead. I wrote down their  
names, which I present to this inquest as my testimony. And I waited for the boy.
He came in April. He was eleven years old and he had walked from Pershore and he  
was carrying his mother's cooking pot, which he had taken from the house in  
Pershore so that he would have something to cook in when he got home. He walked  
up to the house at the Hale and put down the cooking pot and looked about him and  
said: where is everyone.
I told him.
He stood a little while in silence. Then he said: I had better see to the pig.
I watched him go into the yard and I thought: this one will not break.
    ✶ ✶ ✶

V
The Inquest of 1351
Worcester, Michaelmas Term
In the autumn of 1351, when Thomas Hale was thirteen years old, the manorial  
court of Halecroft convened an inquest into the disposition of the Hale lands  
following the deaths of their holders. This was routine business: the plague had left  
property questions of this kind all across England, and the law — in its slow,  
grinding, reassuringly impersonal fashion — was working through them one by one.
The question in Thomas's case was straightforward in principle and complicated  
in fact. The land at the Hale had been held by Richard Hale in fee simple — that is,  
outright, with the right to pass it to his heirs. Richard's heirs were dead. The  
question was therefore whether the holding reverted to the manor, or whether it  
passed to Thomas — Richard's grandson by his eldest son William — as the nearest  
surviving male descendant.
There were those who argued that it should revert. The manor had been patient  
during the famine years, had forgiven rents and extended charity, and had not been  
repaid with anything like the loyalty the system presumed. The pestilence had cost  
the manor three capable tenants in the Hale family alone. The holding at the Hale  
was good productive land. There was no reason, argued the lord's steward, why a  
boy  of  eleven  with  no  family  behind  him  should  expect  to  inherit  what  four  
generations of careful work had built.
There was one person who disagreed with this view. That person was not Thomas  
Hale, who was thirteen and had no standing before the court. It was old Matilda the  
beekeeper, who appeared before the inquest uninvited and who, when asked what  
business she had before this court, said: this boy's business, for he hath no tongue  
old enough to speak for himself.
TESTIMONY OF MATILDA OF HALECROFT, FREE WOMAN, GIVEN TO THE 
INQUEST OF 1351:
I have lived in this village since before any man in this room was born and I shall  
likely live here after most of them are gone. I knew Ranulf de la Hale that built the  
hall, though I was young then and he was old. I knew his son Leofric that built the  
Hale in the wood. I knew every man and woman of that family through five

generations to Richard that is dead of the plague and his sons that are dead and his  
son's son Thomas that stands before you now.
You ask whether the boy shall keep his land. I will tell you what the law saith not.  
The law saith whether the title is proper. I will tell you whether the family is proper.
Richard Hale's father William paid his suit of court and his tithes and his heriot  
without fail for forty years. Richard himself served as foreman of this jury seven  
times. His son Edmund could read a document in Latin and did read one before this  
very court and saved the manor from a false claim by Warin's steward, which saving  
the manor appeared not to notice at the time but may now, at its leisure, reflect  
upon.
The boy Thomas walked from Pershore in April last, having heard that his family was  
dead, and his first act upon arriving at his empty house was to see to the pig. I tell  
you this not to amuse the court but because it is the whole of what you need to know  
about whether this family keeps its land. He is eleven years old and he came home  
and he saw to the pig. That is a Hale.
Give him his land. He has earned it by descent and his family hath earned it four  
times over by the labour of their hands and the honesty of their dealings. I have kept  
bees in this village for longer than this manor's memory and I tell you: do not wrong  
this boy.
The inquest found in Thomas Hale's favour. The holding at the Hale — fourteen  
acres freehold, the barn, the house at the spring, and such livestock as had survived  
— was confirmed as his by right of inheritance, to be held in wardship by the prior of  
Worcester until he reached his majority. The lord's steward objected and was  
overruled.
The recording clerk noted at the foot of the inquest roll: This judgment was given  
Michaelmas term, in the twenty-fifth year of the reign of King Edward III. The boy  
Thomas  Hale  did  stand  before  the  court  throughout  the  proceedings  without  
weeping or fidgeting, which the clerk noted because he had not seen its like in a boy  
so young.
 Nota Bene — ✦It is worth noting, for those who will read the chronicle of Edmund Hale five 
centuries hence, that the quality observed by the clerk — composure before a court, in 
circumstances of considerable personal difficulty — appears, without comment, in Edmund's own 
correspondence. The family did not know it was inheriting this. They were merely being 
themselves.

✶ ✶ ✶

VI
Thomas Comes of Age
Halecroft, 1351–1380
Thomas Hale reached his majority in 1357 and took formal possession of his  
inheritance from the prior's wardship. He was nineteen. The prior — not the Cadfael  
of Edmund's youth but a different man, a Shropshire man named Aldhelm, pragmatic  
and not unkind — gave him back the deed-box and the fourteen acres and the  
goodwill of the priory, and told him that if he had difficulty reading the deeds he  
should come back and someone would help him.
Thomas Hale could read the deeds perfectly well. He had learned his letters, as  
his great-grandfather Edmund had done before him, at the priory school — his  
wardship had included education, which the prior had considered the most useful  
thing the estate could offer a boy with no family to teach him. He had also learned, in  
the eight years of his wardship, a number of other things that the priory had not  
specifically intended: how property law worked, how manorial courts could be  
influenced, how the clergy managed their own considerable estates, and how a man  
of  limited  resources  might  leverage  the  reputation  of  his  family's  history  to  
outperform what his acreage alone would suggest he was capable of.
He was, in this, his great-grandfather Edmund's heir in spirit as well as blood.  
Edmund had used literacy as a tool. Thomas used it as a weapon — not aggressively,  
not  dishonestly,  but  with a purposiveness  that  his  neighbours recognised and  
respected and very occasionally resented.
FROM THE MANORIAL ROLLS OF HALECROFT — 1362
Item. Thomas Hale of the Hale doth petition this court for leave to enclose the  
strip of common land lying between his eastern boundary and the mill-stream,  
being approximately one acre in extent and hitherto used as pasture by the  
tenants of the west field. He offereth in exchange the right of way through the  
Hale wood for the driving of cattle to the common pasture beyond, and a promise  
to maintain the mill-stream bank for a distance of forty yards. The court doth  
consider this exchange fair and doth grant the same.

The enclosure of 1362 was Thomas's first significant act of expansion. The strip  
of land he acquired was neither large nor immediately profitable, but it completed  
the boundary of the Hale holding on its eastern side, giving him a coherent and  
defensible parcel where before he had had an irregular one. The right of way he gave  
in exchange cost him nothing and created goodwill. He was twenty-four years old  
and he was already thinking like a man who intended to pass something on.
He married in 1365: a woman named Eleanor, the daughter of a small freeholder  
in the neighbouring village of Claines, who brought to the marriage a sharp wit, a  
knowledge of herb medicine that would prove extremely useful, and a younger  
brother named John who would become, in due course, Thomas's most trusted  
adviser and occasional conscience. They had four children: Richard, born 1367;  
Agnes, born 1369; Edmund, born 1371; and Margaret, born 1374, who survived  
when her brothers Richard and Edmund did not — Richard dying of a fever in 1380,  
Edmund of an accident with a cart the same year.
The losses of 1380 did not break Thomas. They diminished him, visibly, for a  
season — Eleanor reported in a letter to her brother John that her husband had taken  
to sitting with his account books for long hours without appearing to do any  
accounting, which was not like him. But by the following spring he had taken stock,  
as he had learned to do at eleven years old in an empty house, and proceeded  
accordingly.
The son who remained was not Richard or Edmund but John — his youngest, born  
1376, and named, Thomas wrote in the parish register, after his uncle John Claines,  
who had been a good friend to this family and deserved to have his name carry on  
somewhere.
 Nota Bene — ✦Here the manorial rolls give way to a document of a different kind. In 1382, 
Thomas Hale dictated a statement to the prior at Worcester — not a will, not a charter, but what 
the prior described as a remembrance: a private account of what the family had been and what 
he hoped it would become. He was forty-four years old. He dictated it in English, though the prior  
wrote it in a mixture of English and Latin as was his habit. What follows is as close a 
transcription as the surviving copy allows.
A REMEMBRANCE DICTATED BY THOMAS HALE OF THE HALE,  
TO THE PRIOR OF WORCESTER — 1382
My grandfather's grandfather was a Norman that came over with the Conqueror

and was given land here, which land was not his to give but was given nonetheless  
and my family hath held it by the labour of its hands ever since, which I think is as  
good a title as any.
My father and his brothers are dead of the pestilence. My sons Richard and  
Edmund are dead of the common mishaps of life, which do not require a plague to  
be mortal. My son John is six years old and he is the whole of what is left of this  
family on the male side, saving myself.
I have held these lands for twenty-five years from my majority and I have made  
them better than I found them, not by great acts of cleverness but by attending to  
what needed attending to and not attending to what did not. I have dealt honestly  
with the manor and the manor hath dealt tolerably with me. I have served on this  
jury and I have read documents when they needed reading and I have given bread  
when bread was needed and I have not pretended that I was more than I am or less  
than I intend to be.
I wish my son John to know this: we are not a great family. We have never been a  
great family. We are a family that holds its land and keeps its word and comes home  
when it could go elsewhere and sees to the pig. If he can do these things as his  
forebears have done them, he will be a Hale, and that is sufficient, and more than  
sufficient, and I shall rest content.
Written at Worcester this autumn, in the year of Our Lord 1382, being the sixth year  
of the reign of King Richard II. Thomas Hale, his mark.
    ✶ ✶ ✶

VII
John Hale and the Rising
Worcestershire, 1381–1400
The great rising of 1381 — called by later historians the Peasants' Revolt, though  
it was rather more than that and rather different from what the name implies —  
reached Worcestershire in June of that year, a month after its eruption in Kent and  
Essex. The men of Worcester marched on the castle. The men of several surrounding  
manors opened the lord's barns. Documents were burned. Lawyers were threatened,  
and in one recorded case worse than threatened.
The records of Halecroft for 1381 are missing. The manorial rolls for that year —  
Michaelmas term and Easter term both — have been lost, either by accident or  
design. Whether any Hale was among the men who marched or burned documents is  
therefore unrecorded. Thomas Hale's remembrance of the previous year, with its  
emphasis on honest dealing and keeping faith with the manor, suggests a man not  
naturally inclined to revolt. But his remembrance also noted that the land had been  
given to his ancestor without legal right, and that the family held it by the labour of  
its hands rather than by the grace of any lord, which is the language of a man who  
has thought about these things and reached his own conclusions.
What is recorded is that the Hale holding survived 1381 intact, its lands neither  
seized by the manor in reprisal nor abandoned by its holder in the aftermath of the  
rising's failure. Thomas appears in the rolls of 1382 as if nothing had happened. This  
may mean he was entirely uninvolved. It may mean the prior protected him. It may  
mean nothing at all. The missing year is the most eloquent document in the family  
archive.
ANNO DOMINI 1395 — FROM THE REGISTER OF ST. WULFSTAN'S, 
HALECROFT
Item. Died this Whitsuntide Thomas Hale of the Hale, freeholder of this parish,  
aged seven-and-fifty years. He had held his lands through the pestilence and the  
famine's aftermath and the rising, and he had passed them on in better condition  
than he found them. He is buried beside his wife Eleanor, who preceded him by  
three years. His son John, aged nineteen, doth now hold the Hale.
Father Anselm of this parish noteth for the record that Thomas Hale was a man

who attended his obligations and missed no tithe and was twice present at the  
death of a neighbour when no one else could be found, which is not nothing.  
Requiescat in pace.
Thomas had held the Hale for forty-four years from his majority and thirty-eight  
from his remembered self. He had built what the plague had all but destroyed. He  
had seen the England of Richard II, its splendours and its discontents, its great art  
and  its  abrupt  political  violence;  he  had  seen  Richard  deposed  and  Henry  of  
Lancaster crowned Henry IV in 1399, which was the kind of thing that made a small  
freeholder in Worcestershire hold his land a little more carefully and say rather less  
than he thought.
His son John was nineteen in 1395, and he would live to see things that his father  
had not imagined, and he would hold the land through all of them, and his grandson  
— through a daughter, and through a marriage that rerouted the name in ways  
Thomas's  remembrance  had  not  anticipated  —  would  be  alive  when  England  
changed again, under the Tudors, in ways that made every change before them seem  
like a rehearsal.
But that is the business of the next volume.
    ✶ ✶ ✶

VIII
The English That Remained
On Language and the Name, 1121–1400
It is worth pausing, before the chronicle closes, to remark upon what has  
happened to the language across the two and a half centuries this volume covers.  
Brother Eadmer, writing in 1121, composed his chronicle in a mixture of Latin,  
Anglo-Saxon cadence, and the early Norman French constructions that a monastery  
near Worcester would have absorbed. The English he wrote was not yet Middle  
English; it was something between, a tongue in transition, carrying the weight of the  
old language in its rhythms even as new words entered from the north of France and  
the Latin renewed itself from Rome.
By 1251, when Edmund Hale wrote his letter to his brother Robert about the  
boundary dispute, the transition was substantially complete. The English of that  
letter is recognisably the ancestor of the English we use today: its vocabulary is  
broader, its spelling unstable because literacy was still a new skill for the class that  
exercised it, its Latin insertions a matter of habit and precision rather than of  
primary expression. A man writes in the tongue he thinks in, and Edmund thought in  
English.
By 1382, when Thomas Hale dictated his remembrance, the language was  
Chaucer's language — the East Midlands dialect that was becoming, by political  
accident and geographical circumstance, the standard English of the educated  
classes. Thomas did not write Chaucer's verse; he dictated plain prose, the prose of a  
working man who had been taught to read but not to ornament. But the structures of  
that prose — the direct statement, the subordinate clause carrying its weight without  
flourish, the personal address to a reader not yet born — these are the structures of a  
language that had found its form.
The name itself follows the same arc. Ranulf de la Hale — a French construction,  
the particle de la announcing Norman origin and the noun hale, meaning an  
enclosure or a corner of land, announcing its location. By Leofric's generation the  
particle was dropping. By Edmund's generation it was gone. By Thomas's generation  
the name was simply Hale — an English word that had swallowed its French origin so  
completely that no one who heard it would think of Normandy. The family had done  
to its name what England had done to its language: absorbed the foreign element so

thoroughly that it became native, and the origin became a matter of scholarship  
rather than experience.
There is a final document in this collection, and it is the strangest of them all. It  
was found folded inside the deed-box when Thomas's son John came to examine his  
inheritance in 1395. It is written not by a member of the Hale family but by the same  
old woman who had spoken for Thomas before the inquest of 1351: Matilda the  
beekeeper, who must have been, by 1395, of a quite extraordinary age, and who  
appears to have known that she was writing a document that might outlast her  
considerably.
A PAPER FOUND IN THE DEED-BOX OF THOMAS HALE, IN THE  
HAND OF MATILDA OF HALECROFT — DATE UNCERTAIN,  
PERHAPS 1390
To whoever shall read this after I am gone, which will be soon enough:
I have known six generations of this family and I shall not know a seventh, my time  
being finished. I have seen them come from a man who did not speak the language  
of the people he was given to rule over, and I have seen them become, by the slow  
work of years, something that is entirely of this place and entirely of this tongue.
The first of them asked, on his deathbed, whether what he had done was sufficient.  
I do not know whether it was or was not. I know only that he asked, and that the  
asking is what this family does. They ask whether they have done enough. They do  
not always answer, and perhaps there is no answer, but they ask.
The boy Thomas asked it at eleven, standing in an empty yard with a cooking pot,  
though he did not use those words. He said: I had better see to the pig. It is the  
same question.
I am very old and I have kept bees longer than anyone living can remember and I  
have seen more than I will say and I tell you this for nothing: a family that keeps  
asking whether it has done enough will keep the land. A family that stops asking  
will lose it, sooner or later, one way or another.
Keep asking. Keep the land. See to the pig.
Matilda. Her mark.
    ✶ ✶ ✶
