GLOSSARY
Words which the text employs and the reader may wish explained
Virgate A unit of land measurement used in medieval England, typically between  
20 and 40 acres depending on the region and the quality of the soil. In  
Halecroft, a virgate appears to be approximately 28 acres. A half-virgate,  
therefore, was roughly 14 acres — sufficient for a family to sustain itself if  
the land was good and the seasons tolerable.
Fee simple The most complete form of land ownership recognised in English law: held  
absolutely,  without  condition,  and  freely  transmissible  to  heirs.  The  
opposite of copyhold tenure, which was held at the lord's pleasure and  
reverted to the manor upon the holder's death unless formally renewed.
Heriot The payment due to the lord of the manor upon the death of a tenant:  
traditionally the best beast owned by the deceased, or a money equivalent.  
It is a survival of an earlier custom by which the arms and equipment of a  
dead warrior reverted to his lord. By the fourteenth century it had become  
a form of death duty, exacted from people who had no arms and no warrior  
ancestry.
Suit of court The obligation of a tenant to attend the manorial court at stated intervals  
— typically twice yearly, at Michaelmas and Easter. Failure to appear  
without adequate excuse was itself a punishable offence. The suit of court  
was simultaneously an obligation and a right: only those bound to attend  
had the standing to speak.
Nota bene Latin: note well. Used in documents of the period to direct the reader's  
attention to what follows. Edmund Hale's use of it in his letter to his  
brother is the natural speech of a man educated in a priory school, where  
Latin was the primary language of formal expression, and its phrases had  
become part of his thinking.
In virgata una 
et dimidiaLatin: in one virgate and a half. The phrase from the deed of 1183 upon  
which the boundary dispute with Lord Warin's estate turned. Edmund  
Hale's ability to read this phrase, and to distinguish it from the steward's  
misrepresentation of it as simply one virgate, was the product of nine  
years of priory education. It was worth, in practical terms, approximately  
twenty-eight acres of Worcestershire farmland.
The Great 
FamineThe years 1315 to 1322, during which a series of failed harvests — caused  
primarily by exceptionally wet summers — produced widespread famine

across northern Europe. In England, it is estimated that between ten and  
fifteen per cent of the population died. The Great Famine is sometimes  
treated as a prelude to the Black Death of 1348, since it weakened the  
population,  disrupted  the  agricultural  system,  and  demonstrated  the  
fragility of the manorial order upon which English society rested.
Yersinia pestis The bacterium responsible for bubonic plague, identified in 1894 by  
Alexandre Yersin. The plague of 1348 — the Black Death — killed between  
thirty and sixty per cent of the population of Europe in the space of  
approximately  five  years.  In  England,  the  mortality  was  somewhere  
between a third and a half of the total population. The English economy did  
not return to its pre-plague levels of productivity until approximately 1500.
Fee simple / 
wardshipWhen a minor inherited land held in fee simple, the law provided for a  
wardship: an adult guardian, typically appointed by the court or the  
church, who managed the estate until the heir reached his majority. The  
prior of Worcester's wardship of Thomas Hale was an act of genuine  
charity, since the priory had no legal obligation to assume it and received  
no significant benefit from doing so.
The Peasants' 
Revolt, 1381A  widespread  uprising,  primarily  in  southern  and  eastern  England,  
triggered  by  the  imposition  of  a  poll  tax  and  by  the  accumulated  
frustrations of a labouring class whose value had increased dramatically  
since the Black Death but whose legal and economic freedoms had not.  
The revolt was suppressed within weeks, its leaders executed, and its  
immediate demands refused. Its long-term effect on the dismantling of  
villeinage, however, was considerable.
Middle English The form of the English language spoken and written in England from  
approximately 1100 to 1500, representing the transition between Old  
English (Anglo-Saxon) and Early Modern English. It is the language of  
Chaucer's  Canterbury  Tales  (c.  1390),  Langland's  Piers  Plowman  (c.  
1370), and the anonymous Sir Gawain and the Green Knight (c. 1400). The  
spelling was unstandardised; the vocabulary was a rich mixture of Anglo-
Saxon, Old Norse, Norman French, and Latin.
Enclosure The process by which common land — strips of arable field or common  
pasture used by multiple tenants — was consolidated into single holdings,  
typically by purchase or exchange. Thomas Hale's enclosure of 1362 was a  
small-scale private arrangement rather than the large-scale parliamentary  
enclosures of the sixteenth and eighteenth centuries, but it reflects the  
same  underlying  logic:  the  preference  of  the  capable  tenant  for  
consolidated, defensible land over the fragmented strips of the open-field  
system.

A Note on the Language of this Volume
The English of these documents ranges across two and a half centuries and several  
registers of formality. The Latin of the charter and the ploughman's English of the  
manorial court are not the same instrument. The editor has sought to preserve the  
distinction while ensuring that the modern reader is not lost. Where a document  
survives in the original, it has been transcribed with minimal alteration. Where the  
document is a reconstruction from summary evidence, the language aims for the  
spirit of its period rather than a literal fidelity it cannot claim.
The reader who wishes to pursue the language of medieval England further may find  
no better guide than the works of Geoffrey Chaucer (c. 1343–1400), whose English is  
approximately that of the final documents in this volume, and whose ear for the  
texture  of  human  speech  across  social  classes  remains,  six  centuries  later,  
unmatched.
    ✶ ✶ ✶
END OF BOOK II — THE HOUSE OF HALE
The story continues in Book III:
The Hale Inheritance
1485 — 1560

THE HALE DYNASTY
BOOK III
The Hale Inheritance
Being a Collection of Letters, Household Accomptes, Depositions,
and sundrie other Documents pertaining to the Familie of Hale
of Worcestershire, in the Reigns of Henrie the Seventh,
Henrie the Eighth, Edwarde the Sixth, and Marie the First.
Covering the years
1485 — 1560
    ❁ ✚ ❁
Editor's Note
Between the last entry of the House of Hale — Thomas Hale's death in 1395 and the  
wardship of his son John — and the documents gathered in this volume, a gap of  
ninety years intervenes. It is not a silent gap. The Hale family continued in Halecroft;  
the holding in the wood continued to be farmed; the name continued to be written in  
the parish register of St. Wulfstan's. But the documents of that interval are sparse: a  
succession of births and deaths and marriages, the occasional appearance in a  
manorial roll, no letters, no depositions, no occasion of sufficient drama to generate  
the kind of written record that would survive.
By 1485 — the year that Henry Tudor defeated Richard III at Bosworth Field and  
changed the name of the ruling house of England — the Hales of Worcestershire  
were in their sixth generation since Leofric built the house at the spring, and their  
fourteenth since Ranulf de la Hale crossed the Channel with William's army. They  
were, by that date, an unambiguously English family of modest but respectable  
standing:  free  tenants,  literate,  known  in  the  county,  members  of  the  lesser  
yeomanry that formed the backbone of rural England.
What the Tudor period gave them was occasion. The reign of Henry VIII in particular  
— its dissolution of the monasteries, its break with Rome, its extraordinary and rapid  
transformation of the religious landscape that had, in its fundamental Catholic  
structure, endured since Ranulf's time — forced upon every family in England the

question of where they stood. The Hales had spent four centuries carefully not  
standing anywhere in particular. The sixteenth century would not permit this.
The documents that follow are written in the English of their time: early Tudor prose,  
with its unstandardised spelling, its directness about money and obligation, its  
capacity for sudden feeling within formally constrained expression. The reader who  
has  followed this family  from  Brother Eadmer's  chronicle  will  notice  that  the  
language has continued its journey — broader than the Middle English of Thomas  
Hale's remembrance, less ornate than the Victorian English of Edmund Hale six  
generations hence, but carrying in its rhythms the accumulated weight of everything  
that came before.
— A.F.,  London, November 1884

I
What John Hale Left Behind
Halecroft, 1485–1492
John Hale — Thomas's youngest, born 1376, the son who had survived when his  
brothers did not — held the Hale for forty-five years and died in 1440, having seen  
the end of the Lancastrian line, the beginning of the Wars of the Roses, and the  
particular English talent for conducting political catastrophe without allowing it to  
disturb the agricultural calendar. He had married twice: first a woman named Isabel,  
who died without issue in 1408; then a woman named Margaret, who gave him three  
children — Eleanor, William, and Robert — before dying herself in 1421, when the  
youngest was four years old.
William Hale inherited in 1440 and held the Hale until 1478. His son Robert held  
it from 1478 until 1503. Robert's son Thomas — another Thomas, the third of that  
name in the family's record — held it from 1503 until 1531. And Thomas's son John,  
born in 1498, was the man who, in the years between 1532 and his death in 1558,  
faced the full force of what Henry VIII had decided England would become.
But before we reach John, we must account for what his grandfather Robert left  
behind: not merely the land, which was intact and well-maintained, but a document  
that Robert had composed in 1490, five years after Bosworth Field, which tells us a  
good deal about what the Hale family had been thinking as the world changed  
around them.
Nota — The following inventory was discovered among papers associated with the Hale estate 
and is dated by internal evidence to approximately 1490. It is in Robert Hale's hand — identified 
by comparison with his signature on the manorial rolls — and appears to be a private accounting 
of the household's possessions, probably made in anticipation of his son Thomas reaching 
adulthood.
THE INVENTORIE OF ROBERT HALE OF THE HALE IN 
WORCESTERSHIRE, SET DOWNE THIS MICHAELMAS 1490
THE HALE — GOODES & CATTELLS, 1490 Quantitie Valewe
The dwelling house with the barn and outhouse, the 1 —

well, and the garden within the enclosure
Oxen, being the plough beasts, in good health and of 
good age2 xii s.
Kine, milch cows, being sound beasts 4 xvi s.
Swine, being bred on the mast of the wood 6 iii s.
Shepe, being on the common pasture, counted at last 
shearing14 vii s.
The beehives, being in the garden, maintained 
according to custom3 ii s.
Grain in the barn, being wheat and rye from the last 
harvest— xxiv s.
Woollen cloth, being the product of the household loom,  
undyed8 ells iv s.
Pewter vessels, being plates and cups for the table 9 pieces iii s.
Iron pots and a griddle, being for the kitchen 4 pieces ii s.
A chest of oak with lock and key, being for the keeping 
of deeds1 iii s.
Books, being a psalter in English and a copy of the 
statutes pertaining to land tenure2 v s.
The deed of freehold, dated 1214, and all subsequent 
instruments of title—[priceless]
Debt owed to the household by neighbours, recoverable  
at law if necessary—viii s. iiii d.
The inventory is a plain document, but several items in it are worth remark. The  
two books — a psalter in English and a copy of the land tenure statutes — tell us that  
literacy in the Hale family had become, by 1490, not merely a skill but a habit: these  
are not the books of a man who was taught to read and then forgot the purpose of  
reading, but the books of a man who read for use. The psalter is devotional; the  
statutes are practical. Both were necessary, and Robert Hale knew why.
The chest of oak deserves attention also. It has appeared before in this chronicle:  
it is the deed-box that Brother Anselm found on the shelf in 1349, unmolested by  
whoever had tended the animals while Thomas was in Pershore. It is the box in which  
Matilda's final letter was found. It is, in Robert's accounting, the only item he marks  
as priceless — a word that, in the English of 1490, carried its literal meaning: beyond

the reach of price, not to be put in a column of shillings and pence. The deeds  
themselves are the worth of the box. And the box, unbroken through plague and  
famine and rising and the changing of dynasties, is the worth of the family.
Robert Hale died in 1503, leaving the Hale to his son Thomas in good order.  
Thomas held it quietly for twenty-eight years. It was Thomas's son John who would  
have to decide, in the years after 1532, what the family stood for.
    ❁ ✚ ❁

II
The Letters of John Hale
Worcestershire, 1532–1540
John  Hale  was  thirty-four  years  old  in  1532,  when  Henry  VIII  began  the  
parliamentary process that would end in the Act of Supremacy of 1534 and the  
formal break with Rome. He was a free tenant of the manor of Halecroft, as his family  
had been for three centuries. He was literate, as his family had been for three  
centuries. He was a communicant of the parish church of St. Wulfstan's, as his family  
had been since Ranulf de la Hale had first attended mass in a half-finished church in  
1068, unsure whether the Latin words meant what he thought they meant.
What John Hale was not, and had perhaps never been required to be, was a man  
with a theological position. The question of whether the Pope or the King held  
supremacy over the English church had not, until 1532, been a question that a  
yeoman farmer in Worcestershire was expected to answer. It was a question for  
scholars and prelates and politicians. The Hales had always been content to leave  
such questions to people with more education and less to lose.
The letters that follow — six of them, written between 1532 and 1540 — show a  
man being forced, slowly and against his inclination, to have a position.
John Hale of the Hale to his cousin Edmund Hale of Worcester — the feast of 
St. John, 1532
Cosyn Edmund, I wryte to aske what newes you heer from the citie, for we heer in  
the countrie heer little but rumour and contradiccion one tale aginst another. Some  
do say the Kinge will have the Pope cast out of England altogether and that all the  
bishopps  must  swere  to  the  Kinge's  supremacie,  and  others  do  say  this  is  
exaggeracion and the matter will be setled in good order between the King and  
Rome as suche matters have alwaies bin.
I do not troble myself greatly with these questions, cosyn, for I am a farminge man  
and have no opinion about bishops that they are likely to profit from. But the priest  
at St. Wulfstan's doth preach of late with an unquiet aire about him, and the prior's  
men who have alwaies come to collect the tithe have not come this quarter, which I  
do not know how to interpret.
Also I have herd that Cranmer is made Archbishop and that this is a thing of

significaunce, though I confesse I do not fully understand how. If you can explain it  
to me in plaine wordes I should be gladde of it. My wife Margery saieth I should not  
trouble myself about archbishops and she is probablie right, but she also saieth the  
same about the weather and I have found it prudent to consider the weather.
Write when you can. The harvest was tolerable. The pig is well.
Your loving cosyn, John Hale. God save you and the Kinge.
Edmund Hale of Worcester — John's cousin, son of a younger brother who had  
moved to the city to pursue a trade in cloth — does not appear to have replied, or if he  
did the reply has not survived. What does survive is John's second letter, written two  
years  later,  which  suggests  the  situation  in  Worcestershire  had  clarified  
considerably.
John Hale to his cousin Edmund Hale — Candlemasse, 1534
Cosyn, the Act is passed and the Kinge is Supreme Head of the Church of Englande.  
Father Oswin at St. Wulfstan's did read the proclamacion from the pulpit last  
Sunday and his voice did not shake, which I take for a sign that he has made his  
peace with it, or at least his practise. The congregation did sit in silence throughout,  
which I take for a sign that they have not.
I have signed the oath as required. All householders of substance are required to do  
so. I signed it because I am a householder of substance and because the alternative  
to signing it is not something I have examined too closely, there being a man in  
More's position to instruct me about alternatives.
I will tell you what I think, cosyn, since you are my kin and not like to report me to  
anyone who would find it interesting. I think the King is wrong in his theologie but  
right in his practise. I think a church governed from Rome, which is to say governed  
by a foreigner who has never seen an English field and cares nothing for an English  
winter, is a church that serves itself before it serves its people. I also think that a  
king who kills men for agreeing with him is a king who should be watched.
But these are thoughts for a man's own head and not for a letter, so I shall stop. I  
have signed. I keep my land. I say my prayers in the same church I was christened in  
and I intend to continue doing so for as long as God and the Kinge permit me.
Your cosyn who is careful, John Hale.

Nota — The reference to Thomas More — executed in July 1535 for refusing the oath — tells us 
that John Hale was writing with knowledge of what the refusal had cost the most eminent man in 
England who had attempted it. His decision to sign was not ignorance; it was calculation. He was 
thirty-six years old and had a wife and three children.
    ❁ ✚ ❁

III
The Dissolution
Worcestershire, 1538–1542
In 1538, the process known to history as the Dissolution of the Monasteries  
reached Worcestershire. The great priory of St. Mary at Worcester — where Edmund  
Hale had been educated three centuries before, where Brother Cadfael had taught a  
solicitor's grandson to read Latin documents, where Thomas Hale had gone as a boy  
under wardship, where the Hale family had brought its difficult questions and its  
dead for four hundred years — was dissolved, its monks dispersed, its buildings  
handed to the Crown.
The priory school closed. The tithe records that had been kept there since the  
twelfth century were inventoried, some removed to London, some destroyed, some  
simply lost in the confusion of dissolution. Among the papers lost — as Sir Augustus  
Frere  noted  with  evident  frustration  in  his  1884  catalogue  —  were  several  
documents relating to the Hale wardship of 1348 to 1357, and what appears to have  
been a copy of Brother Eadmer's chronicle made at some point in the fifteenth  
century.
John Hale, who had been educated at the priory school in his own youth and who  
owed to it whatever facility with letters he possessed, wrote to his cousin Edmund in  
Worcester on the day the dissolution was announced in the county.
John Hale to Edmund Hale — October, 1538
Cosyn, the priorie is dissolved. I heer it from Father Oswin, who herd it from the  
sherriff's man, who came to the village yesterday with the Kinge's commissioners to  
make an inventorie of such goods as the priorie holdeth in this parish. They have  
taken the tithe barn, which now belongs to a man in London whose name no one  
seems to know.
I walked up to the priorie this morning. I do not know why. The gates were open,  
which they never are, and some of the monks were standing in the yard with their  
possessions in bundles, not knowing where to go. Brother Anselm — not the Anselm  
of my grandfather's chronicle but another, a young man of perhaps five-and-twenty  
— did ask me whether I knew of work for a man who could read Latin and copy  
documents, which was the most helpless question I have ever been asked by a

learned man.
I told him to come to the Hale and he could stay a fortnight until he determined his  
way, which he did, and he is here now and earns his board by teaching my son  
William his letters, which is something at least.
I feel the loss of the priorie more than I expected. It was always there, cosyn. Our  
family has taken its troubles there since before there was writing to record it. There  
is no one to take troubles to now, or at any rate no one in the same place. I suppose  
we must learn to keep our troubles to ourselves.
Your cosyn, John Hale. God save the Kinge.
The dissolved priory lands were sold in 1540 to a London mercer named Aldgate,  
who had no connection to Worcestershire and who visited the property twice in the  
following decade. The tithe barn passed through two further owners before being  
purchased in 1571 by a local family. The priory church itself fell into disrepair and  
was partially demolished for building stone in the 1580s, though part of the nave  
survived and was incorporated into a private house.
Brother Anselm — the young Benedictine who had asked John Hale for work —  
remained at the Hale for three months, not a fortnight. He taught John's son William  
to read and write with rather more facility than the village school had managed, and  
he repaired the deed-box, which had developed a crack in its lid. He then went to  
Worcester and found employment as a copying clerk at the assizes, which was the  
closest available approximation of what he had been trained to do. He corresponded  
with the Hale family for several years afterward, and his letters — practical, slightly  
melancholy, and quietly funny about the law — are among the more charming  
documents in the archive.
FROM A LETTER OF BROTHER ANSELM TO JOHN HALE — Michaelmas, 1541
I am settled at Worcester and find the work tolerable, the hours long, and the  
litigants no better than litigants have ever been. I do copy documents at the assizes  
and I do read Latin for men who cannot and I do find that the law is much the same  
law whether it is administered by the King or the Pope, which is to say that it is the  
instrument of the man who holds the pen rather than the man who stands before the  
court.
I think often of your family's deed-box, which I mended and which I hope continues  
to hold fast. There is something in it that I think worth your knowing: when I was

repairing the lining, I found beneath the lining a folded paper that had been placed  
there, I think, many years ago — perhaps a great many years. The handwriting is old  
and the English is old, not the English we write now. I cannot date it precisely. I have  
made a copy of it and send it hereunder, and have replaced the original beneath the  
lining, thinking that wherever it has been it should stay.
The paper reads, so far as I can make it: To whoever shall read this after I am gone,  
which will be soon enough — and it continues in the manner of a woman's voice,  
speaking of bees and of a family and of keeping faith. I think it is very old. I think you  
should know it exists.
Nota — The paper Brother Anselm found is, of course, Matilda's letter — the document from the 
earlier volume, dated by Sir Augustus Frere to approximately 1390. It had been hidden beneath 
the lining of the deed-box for a hundred and fifty years. John Hale, upon receiving Brother 
Anselm's transcription, wrote at the foot of the letter: I do not know who she was but she was 
right about the pig.
    ❁ ✚ ❁

IV
The New Religion
Halecroft, 1547–1553
Henry VIII died in January 1547, leaving his nine-year-old son Edward on the  
throne and a country in a state of profound theological uncertainty. Henry had  
broken with Rome but had not, in any meaningful sense, embraced Protestant  
doctrine; he had wanted the Pope's authority removed from England, not the Pope's  
religion. The reformers who had been waiting patiently — or, in several cases, not so  
patiently — through the last decade of his reign now found their moment.
Under Edward VI and his regent the Duke of Somerset, England moved rapidly  
toward a Protestant settlement. The mass was abolished. The English Prayer Book  
was  introduced  —  Thomas  Cranmer's  masterpiece  of  ambiguity,  designed  to  
accommodate those who believed in the Real Presence and those who did not,  
though in the end it satisfied neither. Saints were stripped from churches. Stained  
glass was broken. Altars were removed and replaced with tables. Priests who had  
said the mass in Latin for twenty or thirty years were required, overnight, to conduct  
a service in English from a book they had been given a fortnight ago.
Father Oswin of St. Wulfstan's, who had said the mass for thirty-one years and  
who had read the Act of Supremacy from his pulpit without his voice shaking, was  
presented with the new Prayer Book in the spring of 1548. He is reported — in a  
deposition taken by the bishop's visitors in 1549 — to have placed it on the altar,  
opened it, read the first three pages, and then closed it and sat down in the front pew  
for a long time.
John Hale wrote to his son William, who was at that time in Worcester for the  
assizes, on the day Father Oswin sat down.
John Hale to his son William Hale — Spring, 1548
William, I write in haiste. Father Oswin has sat down in the church and will not rise.  
He is not ill. He is not mad. He is, as near as I can tell from watchiing him these past  
two hours, thinking. His curate — the young man from Pershore, Makepeace, a  
reformer of the new sort who doth wear his convictions on his sleeve and his collar  
both — doth stand at the back of the church looking uncertain what to do, which is a  
sensible responce to the situation.

I have sent your mother in with bread and small beer, which Father Oswin has  
accepted, and which is the only communicacion he has permitted. He ate the bread  
with an expression of a man who hath been given a simpler problem than the one he  
is considering and is grateful for it.
I do not know what will become of the new booke. I have read what you sent me of it,  
and it is not an unbeautifull thing — Cranmer can write, I give him that, the English  
is fine English — but it is not the same. I was baptised and married in the old rite.  
Your grandfather was buried in it. I am not certain a man can simply swop one  
religion for another and keep the same soul, though I suppose men have managed  
stranger conversions.
Come home when the assises are done. Bring the new booke if you have a copie. We  
will read it together.
Your father, John Hale. God preserve us all.
Father Oswin eventually rose from the front pew and conducted the new service.  
He continued to conduct it for the remaining five years of his life, until his death in  
1553 — the year that Edward VI died and Mary Tudor came to the throne and  
abolished the Protestant settlement overnight, restoring the mass and beginning the  
prosecution of those who had enforced the reform.
Father Oswin died, as it happened, three weeks before Mary's accession. His  
timing, observed John Hale in a letter to his cousin, was impeccable: he had managed  
to conduct the new service for five years without apostasy, and had then died before  
being required to reconvert. Whether this was luck or Providence or merely the  
body's independent scheduling, John Hale said he was not equipped to judge. He  
buried Father Oswin under the east wall of St. Wulfstan's and said the old mass over  
him, which he had learned from memory and which was, strictly speaking, already  
illegal again under the new regime. Nobody objected.
A BOOKE OF QUESTION AND ANSWERE CONCERNING THE NEWE  
BOOKE OF COMMON PRAYER — BEING CIRCULATED IN  
WORCESTERSHIRE, 1549
Q. Is it lawful for a Christian man to pray in the English tongue?
A. It is not onely lawful but commanded by scripture, for the Apostle Paul  
doth say that he had rather speak five words with understanding than ten

thousand in an unknown tongue.
Q. Is it lawful to remove the images of saintes from the churches?
A. Where images are worshipped, they are idols, and idols are an  
abomination. Where they are not worshipped but are kept for remembrance,  
learned men differ. The Kinge's commissioners have not differed.
Q. What is to become of the priests who refuse the new order?
A. They are to be admonished. If they persist, they are to be removed from  
their livings. This hath begun already in severall counties.
Q. What is a man to do who knoweth not where his conscience standeth in these  
matters?
A. He is to read the scripture, which is now in English, and to pray, and to  
attend his church, and to wait upon the Lord. The Lord hath not, so far,  
provided a simpler answer.
John Hale, by his own account in a letter to his cousin dated 1549, had attended  
six different meetings at which the above pamphlet — or something like it — had  
been discussed. He had spoken at none of them. He had listened, he wrote, very  
carefully, and had gone home and read the parts of the new Prayer Book that  
pertained to the burial of the dead, which was the part he expected to require most  
urgently in the near term.
He was forty-nine years old. He had watched England change its religion twice in  
his adult lifetime and expected, with the grim attentiveness of a farming man who  
reads the sky, to watch it change again.
    ❁ ✚ ❁

V
William Hale and the Monastery Lands
Worcestershire, 1540–1555
John Hale's son William was born in 1522 and was educated — partly at the  
village school, partly by Brother Anselm during his brief residence at the Hale, partly  
by his own considerable appetite for reading, which had led him by the age of twenty  
to a facility with documents that surprised those who had expected a farmer's son.
William Hale was not content to be a farmer. He was not discontented with his  
father's life — he had enough judgment to understand its value — but he was alert to  
what the dissolution of the monasteries had created: an enormous quantity of land,  
suddenly available, at prices that were in many cases far below their long-term  
agricultural  value,  because  the  Crown needed money quickly  and the  London  
buyers, though wealthy, did not always know what they were buying.
In 1545, aged twenty-three, William Hale purchased from a London mercer the  
lease of twelve acres of former priory land adjoining the eastern boundary of the  
Hale holding. He paid four pounds, which was every penny he had saved and  
borrowed besides. The transaction is recorded in a document now held at the  
Worcestershire Record Office, and it is the first time in the family's documented  
history that a Hale has purchased land not from a feudal lord but from a commercial  
transaction in a competitive market.
He was buying from a man who did not know the land. He was buying land that  
had been farmed by monks for three hundred years and was therefore in good order.  
He was buying at the price of a man who needed cash now, not of a man who  
understood what twelve acres of well-maintained Worcester farmland was worth in  
twenty years.
His father, writing to him the following week, said: You have done either a very  
clever thing or a very foolish one, and I cannot tell which. I hope you can.
❀
INDENTURE OF LEASE BETWEEN RICHARD ALDGATE OF  
LONDON, MERCER, AND WILLIAM HALE OF HALECROFT,  
WORCESTERSHIRE, YEOMAN — BEING THE 12TH DAY OF

MARCH, ANNO 37 HENRY VIII, 1545
THIS  INDENTURE  WITNESSETH  that  the  said  Richard  Aldgate,  for  and  in  
consideration of the summe of Four Pounds lawful money of England to him in  
hand paid by the said William Hale before the ensealing and deliverie hereof, the  
receipt whereof is hereby acknowledged, hath demised, granted, and to ferme  
letten, and by these presents doth demise, grant, and to ferme let, unto the said  
William Hale, his executors and assigns,
ALL that parcell of land lying and being in the parish of Halecroft in the countie of  
Worcester, containing by estimation twelve acres be the same more or less,  
formerly parcel of the possessions of the late Priorie of St. Marie at Worcester, now  
dissolved, bounded to the north by the millstream, to the east by the King's  
highway, to the south by the land of the said William Hale called the Hale, and to  
the west by the common pasture of Halecroft;
TO HAVE AND TO HOLD the said parcell of land unto the said William Hale, his  
executors and assigns, for the terme of twentie and one years, yeilding and paying  
therefore yearly unto the said Richard Aldgate, his heirs and assigns, the annual  
rent of six shillings and eight pence at the feast of Michaelmas.
IN WITNESS whereof the parties to these presents have hereunto set their hands  
and seals. Given the day and year first above written.
Signed: Richard Aldgate. His mark. — William Hale. His mark.
[Endorsed in a later hand, identified as that of Sir Augustus Frere: This lease was  
converted to a freehold purchase in 1568 by William Hale's son John, for the sum of  
nine pounds. The twelve acres so acquired remained in the family until 1891, when  
they were sold as part of the general disposition of the Hale estate. — A.F.]
William Hale's calculation proved correct. The twelve acres, farmed in addition  
to the original holding, made the Hale one of the more substantial family farms in the  
parish by the mid-1550s. More significantly, the transaction established William as a  
man who could read a legal document, understood what a lease meant, and was not  
intimidated by men who lived in London. These were qualities that would serve him  
in the years ahead, when the ability to navigate the machinery of an increasingly  
bureaucratic state became the primary skill of the English yeoman class.

John Hale died in 1558 — the same year as Mary Tudor, which John, had he  
survived to know it, would have regarded as unremarkable coincidence but would  
have noted with his characteristic dry precision. William Hale inherited the Hale and  
its newly enlarged acreage and the deed-box with Matilda's letter under the lining,  
and he set himself to the business of holding what his family had always held: the  
land, the name, the habit of keeping faith.
    ❁ ✚ ❁

VI
The Religion Question
Halecroft, 1553–1558
The five years of Mary Tudor's reign — 1553 to 1558 — constituted the most  
dangerous period the Hale family had faced since the plague of 1348. The danger  
was of a different kind: not random and biological but specific and institutional,  
operating through the machinery of the law, requiring active participation or active  
resistance,  and  offering  no  middle  ground  to  those  who  had  spent  a  lifetime  
occupying it.
Mary's policy of returning England to Rome and prosecuting those who had  
enforced  or  actively  supported  the  Protestant  settlement  was  not  —  in  
Worcestershire, at least — applied with the indiscriminate brutality that history has  
sometimes  attributed  to  it.  The  burning  of  Protestants  that  gave  Mary  her  
posthumous reputation was concentrated in London and the south-east; in the rural  
Midlands, the enforcement was more a matter of pressure and penalty than of fire.  
Churches returned to the old rite. Married priests were required to separate from  
their wives. The new Prayer Books were collected and burned. Those who had  
profited most visibly from the dissolution — the men who had bought priory lands  
cheaply and were known to have done so — were examined.
William Hale was examined in 1555.
DEPOSITION OF WILLIAM HALE OF HALECROFT, TAKEN BEFORE THE 
BISHOP'S VISITORS AT WORCESTER — Midsummer Term, 1555
William Hale of Halecroft in the countie of Worcester, yeoman, being duly sworn,  
doth depose and saith as followeth:
That he did in the year 1545 purchase the lease of twelve acres of former priory land  
in Halecroft from one Richard Aldgate of London, mercer. That he did pay the sum of  
four pounds for the said lease. That he doth hold the said land and doth farm the  
same. That he doth not know and hath never known the said Richard Aldgate  
personally, the transaction being conducted through a solicitor at Worcester.
Being asked whether he considered himself to have profited improperly from the  
suppression of religious houses, he saith: I purchased a lawful lease from a lawful  
owner at a lawful price and I farm the land honestly, which I believe God and the law  
both approve. I did not dissolve the priory. I did not make the decision. I bought

twelve acres of good Worcester farmland and I have improved it. If this is a sin, it is a  
very common one and I await instruction as to its penance.
Being asked whether he attended the mass regularly, he saith: I do attend my parish  
church of St. Wulfstan's Halecroft regularly, as I have done since my christening,  
and I receive the sacrament as administered by Father Makepeace of that parish.  
Whether Father Makepeace doth administer the same according to the rite of Rome  
or according to the rite of the late King Edward's prayerbook or according to some  
private arrangement of his own devising I am not entirely certain, but I attend and I  
receive and I pray, and I commend the question of his precise theological practice to  
those with more authority in such matters than a farming man.
Being asked whether he had any books of heretical content in his possession, he  
saith: I have the psalter in English that belonged to my grandfather, one book of  
common prayer, and a copy of the land tenure statutes. He doth not believe any of  
these to be heretical, though he is willing to submit them to examination.
The visitors doth find no cause for further proceeding against the said William Hale.  
He is admonished to attend his church diligently and to render to Caesar the things  
that are Caesar's.
The examination of 1555 is the most complete picture we have of William Hale as  
a man: measured, precise, drily funny at the moments when drily funny was the only  
available mode of defence, and utterly unintimidated by the formal machinery of  
institutional authority. He had learned something that neither Brother Eadmer's  
chronicle nor Matilda's letter had explicitly taught: that the way to survive an  
institution that wishes to trouble you is not to fight it, and not to placate it, but to give  
it, very precisely, what it has asked for and nothing more. The bishop's visitors asked  
whether he had profited improperly. He told them what he had paid, what he had  
received, and what he thought of it. They asked about the mass. He told them he  
attended it. They asked about heretical books. He described his shelf.
They went away. The land remained.
Mary Tudor died in November 1558, the same month as John Hale. Elizabeth I  
succeeded. England changed its religion for the fourth time in twenty-six years.  
William Hale, who had watched all four changes and survived two examinations and  
one dissolution, went home and fed the pigs and wrote a letter to his son John, who  
was fifteen years old, telling him that it would probably be safe to take down the  
crucifix from the parlour wall now, but not to throw it away, because one never  
entirely knew.
    ❁ ✚ ❁



VII
What William Left His Son
Halecroft, 1558–1580
William Hale outlived both Mary Tudor and his own father by a considerable  
margin, dying in 1580 at the age of fifty-eight: old, in the terms of the sixteenth  
century, but not exceptionally so for a farming man who had eaten well, worked  
steadily, and avoided the more dramatic forms of political engagement. He left  
behind an estate that was, by any measure of the family's history, in its strongest  
condition since Ranulf de la Hale had received his original grant of confiscated Saxon  
land five centuries before.
The holding now comprised the original fourteen acres of the Hale, the twelve  
acres of former priory land purchased in 1545, and a further four acres acquired in  
1562 from a neighbour who needed ready money. Thirty acres in total, all freehold,  
all in good heart. The farmhouse had been extended twice. There was a second barn.  
The deed-box held not just the original instruments of title but a small but growing  
collection  of  documents  pertaining  to  each  transaction  —  leases,  indentures,  
receipts, correspondence — arranged in chronological order, which was William's  
innovation. He was, in this, the first Hale since Edmund the educated to think of the  
archive as an archive: not merely a repository of the deeds one might need at law,  
but a record of the family's history and decisions, worth preserving for its own sake.
He wrote a letter to his son John shortly before his death, which John preserved,  
and which John's son preserved, and which came eventually to Sir Augustus Frere in  
1884. It is the last document in this volume, and it is worth quoting at length,  
because it is the clearest statement of what the Hale family understood itself to be,  
and to owe, and to require of those who came after.
William Hale to his son John Hale — Being the winter of 1579, William being 
then fifty-seven years of age
John, I write this to be read after I am gone, and not before, for I find it easier to say  
certain things to a man who cannot argue with them.
You will inherit thirty acres of good Worcester farmland and a house that is in better  
repair than the one I was born in, and a set of documents in the deed-box that will  
tell you everything the law requires to know about what you hold and why you hold

it. You will also inherit a name that has been in this parish since before anyone living  
can remember, and which means something here, though I cannot tell you exactly  
what it means because the meaning of a name is not a thing that can be written in a  
deed.
I have watched England change its religion four times in my lifetime. I signed an  
oath to a king I thought was wrong. I attended a church whose priest I was not  
always certain was saying the same thing in his heart that he was saying with his  
mouth. I purchased land from the dissolution that some men would say I should not  
have purchased. I was examined by bishops twice and I told them the truth both  
times, which is not quite the same as telling them everything, and they found no  
cause to proceed against me, which is the best result a man of middling station can  
reasonably expect from a bishop.
I have not been a saint. I have not been a coward. I have been a man who held his  
land and kept his name clean and tried to understand what was required of him well  
enough to do it without being destroyed by it. It is not a heroic account and I do not  
offer it as one. But the land is good and the deeds are in order and the deed-box is  
sound.
Your grandfather's grandfather left a remembrance in 1382 that said we are a  
family that holds its land and keeps its word and comes home when it could go  
elsewhere and sees to the pig. I have read that remembrance many times and I have  
tried to live by it. I give it to you as I received it: as instruction rather than advice,  
which means you may disregard it if you judge it necessary, but not without  
thought.
One thing further. At the bottom of the deed-box, beneath the lining, there is a  
folded paper. It was put there by a woman whose name was Matilda, long ago. I do  
not know what she was to this family exactly. She seems to have been the kind of  
person a family needs without quite being able to define why. Read it. Then put it  
back.
See to the pig, John.
Your father, William Hale. God save you and keep you. Written at the Hale in  
Worcestershire, being the forty-sixth year of the reign of Queen Elizabeth the First.
John Hale, who received this letter in the winter of 1580, was twenty-two years  
old. He had grown up in a world that had stabilised — Elizabeth's settlement was  
holding, the religious convulsions of his father's youth had quieted into a durable if  
ambiguous Protestantism, and the England of 1580 was recognisably the England of

the following century. He would live until 1631 — a full fifty years more — and he  
would see things his father had not imagined: the defeat of the Armada, the death of  
Elizabeth, the coming of the Stuarts, the beginning of the tensions that would, in his  
grandsons' time, produce the Civil War.
But that is the matter of the next volume.
John read his father's letter. He read Matilda's paper beneath the lining. He put it  
back. He saw to the pig.
    ❁ ✚ ❁

VIII
On the English of the Sixteenth Century
A Note on the Language of this Volume
The documents gathered in this volume represent a period of extraordinary  
change in the English language, which mirrors with uncomfortable precision the  
change taking place in the English church and the English state. The English of John  
Hale's letter of 1532 — with its unstandardised spelling, its Latin still present in  
formal expression, its direct and digressive syntax — is a different instrument from  
the English of William Hale's letter of 1579, which is closer in rhythm and vocabulary  
to the language of Shakespeare (born 1564, in Stratford, which is not forty miles  
from Halecroft) than to the language of Chaucer.
The change was driven partly by the printing press, which arrived in England in  
1476 and had, by the 1530s, produced a sufficiently large body of printed English to  
begin  exerting  pressure  toward  standardisation.  It  was  driven  partly  by  the  
Reformation itself, which made the English of the Bible and the Prayer Book a daily  
encounter for every church-going English person — which is to say, every English  
person. And it was driven partly by the expansion of the legal and commercial world,  
which required documents that could be read and understood by parties who had no  
shared local dialect and no Latin.
William Tyndale, whose translation of the New Testament appeared in 1526 and  
was immediately banned and burned, had written the most widely distributed  
English prose of the sixteenth century — which is to say, the most widely distributed  
English prose ever written to that date. The rhythms of his translation — its short  
clauses, its preference for Anglo-Saxon vocabulary over Latinate circumlocution, its  
directness of address — were the rhythms that Cranmer used in the Prayer Book,  
and that educated Englishmen used in their private correspondence, and that the  
Hale family used in their letters without knowing they were using them.
John Hale, writing in 1534 that he had signed the oath because the alternative  
was not something he had examined too closely, there being a man in More's position  
to instruct him about alternatives, was using the same verbal economy that Tyndale  
had brought to the Gospels: concrete, direct, the meaning not concealed behind  
formality but working through it. He was not an educated man in any formal sense.  
But he was a literate man in a literate age, and the language he had absorbed was the  
finest vernacular English that had ever been written.

The reader who has followed this family from Brother Eadmer's careful Latin-
inflected English to John Hale's vigorous Tudor prose has traced, in miniature, the  
whole journey of the English language from its medieval roots to the vernacular  
confidence of the Elizabethan age. The next volume — set in the Civil War and  
Restoration — will find the language at its most morally and politically charged: the  
English of Milton, of Bunyan, of the New Model Army's declarations, of the King  
James Bible in its first generation of use. It is English that knows it matters, because  
the people who write it are, for the first time in history, certain that what they write  
may change the world.
The Hales will have to decide, in that world, what they believe. The Tudor century  
had permitted them the luxury of performing belief without quite committing to it.  
The seventeenth century would not be so accommodating.
    ❁ ✚ ❁
