GLOSSARY
Words and matters which the text employs and the reader may wish explained
Act of 
Supremacy, 1534The parliamentary act that declared Henry VIII to be the Supreme Head  
of the Church of England, replacing the Pope as the ultimate religious  
authority in England. It required all subjects of substance to swear an  
oath  acknowledging  this  supremacy.  Those  who  refused  —  most  
famously Thomas More and Bishop John Fisher — were executed for  
treason.
The Dissolution 
of the 
MonasteriesThe process by which Henry VIII, between 1536 and 1541, closed all the  
monastic houses of England, seized their lands and assets, and sold or  
granted them to private individuals. It was the largest transfer of  
property in England since the Norman Conquest. It enriched the Crown,  
created a new class of landowners, destroyed irreplaceable collections  
of manuscripts and archives, and permanently altered the physical and  
social landscape of rural England.
Fee farm / 
indentureA fee farm was a form of perpetual lease at a fixed rent, commonly used  
for monastic and Crown lands in the sixteenth century. An indenture was  
the formal document recording such an agreement — its name derives  
from the practice of cutting the document in two along an indented  
(jagged) line, each party keeping one copy, the fit of the two pieces  
serving as proof of authenticity.
Thomas More 
(1478–1535)Lord Chancellor of England, humanist, and author of Utopia. He refused  
to swear the oath required by the Act of Supremacy, believing it to  
contradict his religious convictions, and was executed for treason in July  
1535. His death was the clearest demonstration available to any literate  
Englishman of what the refusal of the oath could cost.
Thomas Cranmer 
(1489–1556)Archbishop of Canterbury under Henry VIII and Edward VI, and the  
principal author of the Book of Common Prayer (1549 and 1552). His  
prose is among the finest in the English language. Under Mary Tudor, he  
was burned at the stake for heresy in 1556, after recanting and then  
withdrawing his recantation at the moment of execution.
The Book of 
Common PrayerThe liturgical book introduced under Edward VI in 1549 (revised 1552),  
containing the order of service for all Church of England rites: morning  
and evening prayer, the Eucharist, baptism, marriage, burial. Written  
principally by Cranmer, it was intended to be comprehensible to all

English-speaking Christians regardless of theological position. Its prose  
— particularly the burial service, the collects, and the marriage rite — is  
among the founding texts of modern English literature.
Marian 
persecutionThe prosecution of Protestants under Mary I (reigned 1553–1558),  
which resulted in approximately 280 executions by burning, earning  
Mary the posthumous epithet 'Bloody Mary.' The prosecutions were  
concentrated in London and the south-east; in rural counties such as  
Worcestershire, enforcement was typically limited to examination, fine,  
and the requirement to reconform.
Yeoman In Tudor and early modern England, a yeoman was a free man who  
owned and farmed his own land, below the rank of gentleman but above  
the level of tenant farmer or labourer. The yeoman class formed the  
productive backbone of rural England and provided the bulk of its  
military and administrative manpower. The term implied not merely an  
economic status but a moral one: stability, reliability, rooted connection  
to a specific place.
Unstandardised 
spellingUntil  the  late  seventeenth  century,  English  spelling  was  not  
standardised: a single word might be spelled several different ways by  
the  same  writer  in  the  same  document,  according  to  phonetic  
preference, regional dialect, or simple habit. The standardisation of  
spelling was driven partly by the printing press and partly by the spread  
of  grammar-school  education.  John  Hale's  letters  retain  this  
unstandardised  quality  throughout:  he  writes  cosyn  and  cousin  
interchangeably, and his spelling of common words varies from letter to  
letter.
    ❁ ✚ ❁
END OF BOOK III — THE HALE INHERITANCE
The story continues in Book IV:
The Divided House
1620 — 1665

THE HALE DYNASTY
BOOK IV
The Divided House
Being a Collection of Letters, Petitions, Field Dispatches, Depositions,
Pamphlets, and Sundry other Documents pertaining to the Familie of Hale
of Worcestershire, during the Wars of the Three Kingdoms,
the Interregnum, and the Restoration of His Majesty King Charles the Second.
Covering the years
1620 — 1665
    ✠ ✠ ✠
Editor's Note
John Hale the Third — the man to whom William Hale wrote his final letter in 1579,  
bidding him see to the pig — held the Hale until his death in 1631. He had lived  
through fifty years of Elizabethan and early Jacobean stability, the longest settled  
period England had known since the reign of Henry VIII, and he had watched the  
tensions accumulate: between Parliament and Crown, between the increasingly  
confident Puritan wing of the English church and the ceremonialism that Charles I  
and  his  Archbishop  Laud  were  attempting  to  reimpose,  between  the  ancient  
prerogatives of the monarchy and the equally ancient liberties of the subject.
He died before the breaking point. His son Robert Hale, born 1595, would not be so  
fortunate. Robert Hale was forty-seven years old when Charles I raised his standard  
at Nottingham in August 1642 and the Civil War began. He had three sons: John,  
born 1620, who would fight for Parliament; Thomas, born 1624, who would fight for  
the King; and William, born 1628, who was fourteen years old in 1642 and who would  
spend the war in Worcestershire watching his family divide and trying to hold what  
remained of it together.
The documents gathered in this volume are the record of a family in the act of  
breaking, and then — slowly, painfully, across twenty years and more — in the act of  
mending. The English in which they are written is the most morally charged of any  
period in this chronicle: it is the English of the King James Bible, of Milton's great  
pamphlets, of the New Model Army's declarations and the Leveller petitions. It is

English that has found its full power and is deploying that power in the service of  
positions  whose  incompatibility  will  cost  approximately  two  hundred  thousand  
English lives before the matter is settled.
It is also the English of two brothers who have chosen different sides and who  
continue, across the duration of the war and for a decade after it, to write each other  
letters. The letters are the most remarkable documents in the family archive. They  
are what this volume is about.
— A.F.,  London, November 1884

I
Before the Breaking
Halecroft, 1620–1642
John Hale the Third died in the spring of 1631, aged seventy-three. He was buried  
in the churchyard of St. Wulfstan's, which he had attended all his life, in the same  
ground as his father and his grandfather and every generation of the family since  
Thomas Hale had been confirmed in his inheritance by the manorial court of 1351.  
The Reverend Bartholomew Marsh — a mild man, competent, who had held the living  
since  1608  and  who  had  opinions  about  the  Calvinist  tendencies  of  the  new  
Archbishop Abbot but kept them largely to himself — conducted the service.
His son Robert inherited thirty acres, a farmhouse, a second barn, the oak deed-
box with its accumulated instruments of title, and the particular kind of unspoken  
anxiety that sits in a household when a patriarch has died and the world outside has  
begun to make alarming noises. The noises in 1631 were not yet the noise of war —  
that was eleven years away — but they were the noise of a constitutional crisis  
building with the slow certainty of a tide: the King governing without Parliament, the  
Forced Loan, the imprisonment of those who refused it, the Three Resolutions of the  
Commons in 1629, Laud's innovations in the church, the ship money. Every year a  
new grievance. Every year a sharper answer.
Robert Hale was a careful man, in the family tradition. He had been educated at  
the grammar school in Worcester and had read law for two years at Gray's Inn in  
London before deciding that London was expensive and uncomfortable and that  
Worcestershire suited him better. He had returned to the Hale in 1618, married a  
woman named Anne Fellowes in 1619, and had begun the process of settling into his  
inheritance with the methodical attentiveness that the Hale family had always  
brought to its land.
His three sons grew up in the 1630s as the crisis deepened. John, the eldest, went  
to Oxford in 1638 and came back in 1640 with a copy of Prynne's Histrio-Mastix and  
strong opinions about bishops. Thomas, the middle son, stayed in Worcestershire  
and worked the farm and read his Bible and developed equally strong opinions about  
the authority of the King, though he expressed them rather more quietly than his  
brother. William, the youngest, who was twelve years old when the war began,  
watched both of them and said very little.

Robert Hale to his brother Edmund Hale of Worcester — Michaelmas, 1640
Brother Edmund, I write with a heavy heart and an uncertain mind. John is come  
back from Oxford and I scarce know him. He is full of the Parliament men and their  
arguments against the bishops and the ship money, and he speaks of the King's  
prerogative in a manner that would have been considered seditious ten years past  
and which I confess I do not entirely know how to answer, for some of his arguments  
are not without force.
Thomas, who hath been here all the while and hath not been to Oxford, doth hold the  
contrary opinions with equal vehemence. He saith the King hath the right of his  
crown from God and that those who oppose it do oppose God's order. He quotes  
Scripture at John, and John quotes Scripture back at him, and I sit at the head of the  
table between them and think of my grandfather's letter in which he said he sat  
between his sons and could not tell which had the right of it.
I have not said this to either of them, for it would not help. But I confess to you,  
brother, that I do not know which of them is right. I know only that if this matter  
comes to blows — which God forbid, but which I begin to think God may not forbid —  
I shall lose either a son who fights for the Parliament or a son who fights for the  
King, and I cannot see which loss I could the better bear.
How doth it stand in Worcester? Write when you can.
Your loving brother, Robert Hale. God preserve the peace.
The peace was not preserved. Charles I raised his standard at Nottingham in  
August 1642. The war began. John Hale, twenty-two years old, rode to Gloucester to  
join the Parliamentary forces. Thomas Hale, eighteen years old, rode in the opposite  
direction to join the King's forces assembling at Worcester.
Robert watched both of them go from the gate of the Hale and then went inside  
and sat for a long time with the deed-box on the table in front of him.
William, who was fourteen, brought him bread.
    ✠ ✠ ✠

II
The Parliament Son
Gloucestershire and Worcestershire, 1642–1645
John Hale served with the Parliamentary forces for three years. He was not a  
soldier by nature — he was a farmer's son who had read too much law and too much  
Scripture and had arrived at the conclusion that both Scripture and law were on the  
side of the Parliament — but he was competent and steady and he survived, which  
was not something that could be said of all the men he served beside.
He wrote to his father and to his brother Thomas throughout the war. The letters  
to Robert are formal, careful, aware of what a letter intercepted might cost. The  
letters to Thomas — of which eleven survive — are something else entirely. They are  
the letters of two men who love each other and have chosen opposite sides of a  
question that each believes to be the most important question ever put to an English  
conscience, and who are attempting, across the width of the war, to maintain the  
connection without abandoning the conviction.
They are, in the judgment of Sir Augustus Frere, who discovered them in 1884  
and  spent  three  pages  of  his  catalogue  describing  them  with  unconcealed  
excitement, the most remarkable documents in the entire Hale archive.
John Hale, with the Parliamentary Forces at Gloucester, to his brother Thomas 
Hale at the Hale in Worcestershire — October, 1642
Thomas, I know not whether this letter will reach you, nor whether you would wish to  
receive a letter from the side you do not serve. But I write because you are my brother  
and because I find that I cannot not write, which is perhaps a sentence that would  
trouble you as much as the war itself.
I have been at Gloucester these six weeks. I will not tell you more than that, for I know  
not whose hands this may pass through. I am well. I have not been in a battle yet, though  
I have heard the guns at a distance and the sound is nothing like I expected it to be. It is  
not a heroic sound. It is a practical sound, like a very large and very indifferent machine.
I do not think you are wrong to serve the King, Thomas. I think you are wrong about the  
King, which is a different thing. I think the King is a man who believes himself above the  
law, and I think no man is above the law, and I think this is a point upon which the  
Scripture is clearer than the divines who serve the King would have it be. But I do not  
think you are wrong to believe otherwise. I think you believe it honestly, which is more

than I can say for some who serve on my side.
I think of the land. I think of father. I think of William, who is fifteen and who I pray is not  
fool enough to follow either of us.
Write to me if you can. Write to me if you cannot. The fact of the writing is sufficient.
Your brother who is in disagreement with you but not in enmity, John Hale.
Thomas Hale's reply has not survived — it was presumably destroyed or lost in  
the chaos of the war's progress — but John's next letter, written three months later,  
suggests that Thomas did write back and that what he wrote was characteristic of  
him.
John Hale to Thomas Hale — January, 1643
Thomas, you say the King hath the right of his crown from God and that Parliament doth  
overstep its commission. I have considered this carefully, for you are not a man who  
speaks without thought.
I say this: God doth not give a king the right to levy taxes without consent, to imprison  
men without charge, to dissolve Parliament when it displeases him and govern without it  
for eleven years. These are not rights derived from God. They are habits derived from the  
absence of anyone powerful enough to prevent them. The Parliament is now powerful  
enough to prevent them, and it doth so, and I think it is right.
I say also this: I have stood in a field in Gloucestershire and watched men die for both  
positions. The men who die for the King do not die less honestly than the men who die for  
the Parliament. I have found this difficult to reconcile with my conviction that the  
Parliament is right. I have not yet reconciled it.
Are you well? Is the Hale standing? Is father keeping his health?
Your brother, who is cold and uncertain but still standing. John.
Nota — The battle of Worcester in September 1651 — at which Cromwell destroyed the last 
Royalist army under the young Charles II — was fought partly in and around Halecroft. The 
damage to the parish is recorded in the churchwardens' accounts: several buildings burned, the 
mill wheel destroyed, two men of the village killed. The Hale itself survived undamaged. Robert 
Hale, then fifty-six, apparently negotiated with the Parliamentary commander for the protection 
of his property by presenting the deed-box and demonstrating, with his customary methodical 
precision, that the holding was freehold and not subject to the confiscation order applying to 
Royalist estates.
    ✠ ✠ ✠



III
The Royalist Son
Worcester and Oxford, 1642–1646
Thomas Hale served with the King's forces from 1642 to the fall of Oxford in June  
1646, when the last Royalist garrison surrendered and the first Civil War ended. He  
was not captured. He was not executed. He walked away from Oxford in the general  
dispersal and made his way back to Worcestershire on foot, arriving at the Hale in  
July 1646 thin and worn and, by his own account in a letter written several years  
later, in a state that he could not then put a name to but which he would now call  
defeated — not merely in battle but in the conviction that had carried him to battle.
He was twenty-two years old. His father was fifty-one. His brother John was still  
with the Parliamentary forces, though the first war was over. His brother William  
was eighteen and had spent the war farming and watching and saying nothing, and  
had grown, in those four years, from a boy into something quite different.
⚔
COMMISSION OF ARRAY FOR THE COUNTY OF WORCESTER,  
ISSUED BY HIS MAJESTY KING CHARLES I, AUGUST 1642
CHARLES, by the Grace of God King of England, Scotland, France and Ireland,  
Defender of the Faith &c. To all Commissioners, Noblemen, Gentlemen, and  
Freeholders of our County of Worcester, greeting.
Whereas we have been necessitated by the unlawful and rebellious actions of  
certain Members of our Parliament and their confederates to raise an Army for the  
defence of our Royal Person, the maintenance of the true Protestant Religion, the  
Laws of England, and the liberty and peace of our subjects against those who  
would subvert the same:
We do hereby will and require you to array and put in readiness all men between  
the ages of sixteen and sixty years, and to cause them to be mustered, trained, and  
armed according to the ancient custom of this Realm, and to lead them at our  
command for the purpose of suppressing the said rebellion.

Given at our Court at Nottingham this 22nd day of August, in the eighteenth year  
of our reign.
[Endorsed in Robert Hale's hand: Received the 28th of August. Thomas went the  
following morning. God preserve him.]
Thomas Hale's letters from the Royalist side are fewer and more fragmentary  
than his brother's — partly because he was less inclined to put things on paper,  
partly because the Royalist side was, by 1644, increasingly on the back foot and its  
communications accordingly disrupted. Three letters survive. The first, from Oxford  
in 1643, is careful and formal — he knew the letters might be read by others. The  
second, from 1645, is quite different.
Thomas Hale to his brother John Hale — Oxford, March 1645
John. I will not argue theology with you anymore. I am too tired for it and I think it is  
not the point.
The point, as I now understand it after three years of fighting for it, is this: some  
things ought not to be decided by argument. Some things are settled, and the order  
of them is not arrived at by men sitting in a chamber in London and voting upon it. A  
king is a king. A church is a church. The order of these things was old when England  
was young, and men who vote to overturn it do not know what they are overturning,  
and I think when they have done it they will wish they had not.
I do not say this with confidence. I say it as a man who has watched a great deal of  
dying and who is no longer certain that the dying is proportionate to the question  
that occasions it. But I am here and I have sworn and I will not go back.
How is father? How is the Hale? I think of it more than I expected to.
Your brother Thomas. God save the King.
Thomas arrived home in July 1646 to find his father aged considerably, his  
brother William in full command of the practical operations of the farm, and a letter  
from John waiting for him on the table, which his father had received two weeks  
before and had not opened because he thought Thomas should be the one to open it.
Thomas opened it. He read it for a long time. He put it down without saying  
anything. Then he went out to the yard and saw to the pig, which had not been seen  
to that morning.

His father watched him from the window and did not say anything either.
    ✠ ✠ ✠

IV
The Interregnum
Halecroft, 1649–1658
Charles I was executed on the 30th of January, 1649. Robert Hale heard the news  
four days later, from a man who had ridden from Worcester with it. He wrote in the  
margin of his household account book, in the space beside the record of a purchase  
of nails: The King is dead. January 1649. God have mercy on us all. He did not write  
anything else that day.
The Commonwealth, and then the Protectorate of Oliver Cromwell, lasted until  
1660. For the Hale family, these eleven years were a period of profound and largely  
silent adjustment. John Hale, who had fought for Parliament and whose side had  
won, found that victory did not feel as he had expected it to feel. Thomas Hale, who  
had fought for the King and whose side had lost, found that defeat did not feel as bad  
as  he  had  expected  —  partly  because  the  Royalist  estates  were  subject  to  
sequestration only if their owners had been active in arms after a certain date, and  
Thomas had walked away from Oxford before that date, and had Robert Hale's deed-
box and his talent for precise presentation to thank for the fact that the Hale was  
never sequestered.
William Hale — youngest son, the one who had watched from the gate and said  
nothing and brought his father bread — emerged from the Civil War as the most  
capable farmer in the parish and the most completely undocumented member of his  
generation. He had no political position on record. He had served in no army. He had  
signed no petition and written no letter that has survived. He had held the Hale  
together while his brothers were away, kept his father's health from failing entirely,  
maintained the relations with the manor and the parish that needed maintaining, and  
grown the farm by three acres in the process.
He was, in this, the most purely Hale of his generation: the one who came home  
when he could have gone elsewhere, kept faith with the land, and was quiet about it.
A DECLARATION OF THE ARMY, CONCERNING THE PRESENT STATE OF  
THE COMMONWEALTH OF ENGLAND — BEING CIRCULATED IN  
WORCESTERSHIRE, 1651

We the Officers and Soldiers of the Army of the Commonwealth of England, do  
hereby declare our resolution to maintain and defend the Freedoms of the  
People of England against all tyranny and oppression, whether of King,  
Prelate, or any other power whatsoever that shall seek to impose upon the  
conscience or the estate of any freeborn Englishman.
We do affirm that no man hath a right to govern another without that other's  
consent, and that all just government doth derive its authority from the  
people governed, and that a Parliament freely elected and honestly  
constituted is the proper instrument of that authority.
We do further affirm that the liberty of conscience in matters of religion  
is the birthright of every English Christian, and that no church, court, or  
commission hath the right to compel any man to worship against his  
conscience or to punish him for worshipping according to it.
God is with us. The work is not yet finished.
[Found in the Hale deed-box, in John Hale's hand — apparently copied from a  
printed original. No annotation survives to indicate what John thought of it  
by 1651.]
John Hale had come home in 1648, a year before the execution. He had served for  
six years and he was twenty-eight years old and he was done. He was not done with  
his convictions — he believed what he had always believed about Parliament and the  
law and the rights of the subject — but he was done with believing that war was an  
adequate expression of those convictions, because he had seen too much of what war  
actually expressed.
He  farmed.  He  attended  the  Independent  meeting  house  that  had  been  
established in Worcester in 1646, which was as close as he could come to the religion  
he had arrived at in the years of fighting. He corresponded with his brother Thomas,  
who still attended St. Wulfstan's and still read his Bible in a different direction, and  
they continued their argument by letter across the decade of the Interregnum with a  
mutual respect that had been, if anything, deepened by the fact that they had both  
survived the war and both come home and both concluded that what they had been  
fighting for was better argued for in a letter than expressed with a pike.
John Hale to Thomas Hale — Midsummer, 1653
Thomas, Cromwell hath dissolved Parliament and named himself Lord Protector. I

do not know what to make of it. I fought for Parliament. I did not fight for a Lord  
Protector who governs without Parliament. I confess to you that I feel the ground  
shifting under the argument I have maintained these eleven years, and it is an  
uncomfortable sensation.
I was wrong about some things. I do not know yet which things. I know that I was  
right that the King was above the law in ways the law could not permit. I do not  
know that I was right about what should replace him. I thought Parliament. Perhaps  
I was too simple.
You will resist the impulse to say that you told me so. You always resist that impulse,  
which is one of the reasons I have written to you every year for eleven years even  
when we were at war. Most men would not resist it. You are not most men.
How is the farm? How is father? Write soon.
Your brother who is reassessing, John.
Nota — Robert Hale died in 1657, aged sixty-two. His death came three years before the 
Restoration, which he had spent his last years believing was inevitable. His account book entry 
for the day of the King's execution — The King is dead. God have mercy on us all — was his only 
recorded comment on the political events of the Civil War. He had managed, across fifteen years 
of the most politically dangerous period in English history, to keep both his sons alive and his 
land intact and his position unrecorded. It was, in its way, the most Hale achievement of the 
dynasty.
    ✠ ✠ ✠

V
The Restoration
Worcester and Halecroft, 1660–1665
Charles II entered London on the 29th of May, 1660 — his thirtieth birthday — to  
the sound of bells and the sight of streets strewn with flowers. England had decided,  
with the pragmatism that had always characterised its political settlements, that  
eleven years of republic was enough, and that a king — even a king who had spent  
most of the previous decade in exile and who was known to hold opinions about  
religion that made the English nervous — was preferable to the alternatives that had  
been tried.
Thomas Hale, in Halecroft, heard the news on the first of June and wrote to his  
brother John the same afternoon. The letter is the shortest in the collection and, in  
the judgment of anyone who has read all eleven of the letters exchanged between the  
two brothers, the most carefully composed.
Thomas Hale to his brother John Hale — 1st June, 1660
John. The King is restored. I do not say this to cause you pain and I do not say it in  
triumph. I say it because it is true and because you should hear it first from me  
rather than from someone who would say it differently.
I have thought for twenty years that the Parliament exceeded its commission and  
that what followed from that excess was what always follows from excess. I have not  
thought that a king has the right to be above the law. These two thoughts have sat  
uneasily beside each other for a long time and I do not think the Restoration  
resolves them. I think it merely postpones the argument to a generation that is not  
us.
You were right about some things. I was right about some things. England has had a  
king killed and a republic tried and another king restored and what it has to show  
for it is — what? The land is still here. The church is still there. The people are still  
the people. I do not know whether this is a triumph or a failure and I am not certain  
there is a difference.
Come home when you can. I should like to talk to you in the same room rather than  
on paper. It has been too long.
Your brother, who has been right and wrong in equal measure and is glad you are

alive. Thomas.
John Hale came home in the autumn of 1660. He and Thomas sat together in the  
farmhouse at the Hale — their father was three years dead, William ran the farm, the  
house was Thomas's — and they talked, presumably, for a long time. No record of  
what was said survives, which is appropriate.
What does survive is a joint letter, written in both their hands on the same sheet  
and sent to their cousin in Worcester the following week, which concerns itself  
entirely with a boundary dispute about the mill stream, and which is the most  
deliberate piece of studied normalcy in the archive: two men who have spent  
eighteen years on opposite sides of the most destructive war in English history,  
jointly and equably addressing a question about water rights, as if the question of  
water rights were the only question that had ever mattered and they had both known  
it all along.
    ✨ ✨✰
DECLARATION OF THE RESTORATION OF HIS SACRED  
MAJESTY KING CHARLES THE SECOND — AS READ FROM THE  
PULPIT OF ST. WULFSTAN'S, HALECROFT, JUNE 1660
Whereas it hath pleased Almighty God to move the hearts of the People of this  
Nation to a sense of their duty to God and to His Majesty, and to a desire to return  
to that happy constitution of King, Lords and Commons which is the birthright of  
every Englishman:
And whereas His Majesty King Charles the Second doth hereby declare his most  
gracious  intention  to  govern  by  the  laws  of  England,  to  maintain  the  true  
Protestant Religion, to grant a general pardon for all offences committed during  
the  late  unhappy  Wars,  and  to  confirm  all  lawful  possessions  of  lands  and  
properties as they stood before the same:
Let all loyal subjects give thanks to God for this merciful Providence, and let all  
men of good will apply themselves to the healing of this divided Nation.
[The Reverend Edmund Clarke read this declaration from the pulpit with evident  
relief. In the margin of his copy, in his own hand: Thanks be to God. Let it hold.]

The Restoration did hold — at least through the decade that remains to this  
volume. Charles II proved, in the event, a more intelligent king than his father and a  
less scrupulous one, which in the circumstances of 1660 was probably the right  
combination. The religious settlement was imperfect and left significant numbers of  
Nonconformists, including John Hale, unable to worship as they wished without civil  
penalty. But it held together, and England did not return to war, and the Hale family  
— its lands intact, its brothers both alive, its archive undamaged — entered the  
1660s with something that several generations of its members had not possessed: a  
reasonable expectation that the immediate future would not require them to choose.
John Hale died in 1668. Thomas Hale died in 1672. William Hale, the youngest,  
who had kept the Hale together while his brothers fought and who had never put his  
name on a document that expressed a political position, died in 1689 — the year of  
the Glorious Revolution, another change of king accomplished without war, which  
William would have appreciated if he had survived to hear of it. He missed it by three  
months.
His son Robert — another Robert, the fourth of that name — was twenty-six years  
old in 1689, and he would be the father of Nathaniel, and Nathaniel would be the  
subject of the next book in this series: a man who had heard all his life about the Civil  
War and the Restoration and the family that had survived both, and who would take  
that inheritance and do something his forebears had never quite managed to do. He  
would leave Worcestershire.
    ✠ ✠ ✠

VI
What the Brothers Left Behind
A Reading of the Letters, 1642–1660
Sir Augustus Frere, writing in his 1884 catalogue, described the eleven letters  
exchanged between John and Thomas Hale as the most valuable documents in the  
family archive, and he was not wrong, though his reasons were primarily historical  
rather than literary. To the historical scholar, the letters offer a rare thing: a  
sustained private exchange across the duration of the Civil War between two  
brothers on opposite sides, neither of whom is a public figure, neither of whom  
writes with any awareness that his letters might ever be read by anyone other than  
his brother.
To the literary reader — or to anyone who has followed this family from Ranulf de  
la Hale's deathbed question in 1115 to this point — the letters offer something else.  
They offer the fullest picture we have, anywhere in the archive, of what the Hale  
family's particular quality of mind actually looks like in practice, rather than being  
described by a monk or implied by an inventory.
That quality of mind is this: a capacity to hold two incompatible positions  
simultaneously without either abandoning one's own or dismissing the other's, and  
to maintain that double holding across years of violent disagreement — holding it not  
as a clever trick or a diplomatic posture but as a genuine intellectual condition,  
arrived at by taking both positions seriously enough to see their force.
John Hale believed Parliament was right. Thomas Hale believed the King was  
right. Both beliefs were sincerely held. Both beliefs were tested by events. Both men  
survived to find that their beliefs had not been entirely vindicated by the outcomes to  
which they had contributed. Both men were honest enough to say so. Both men  
continued, after saying so, to believe roughly what they had believed before, because  
the alternative — to have no position, to hold nothing, to be the kind of man who  
waits to see which way the thing falls and then agrees with it — was not a Hale  
alternative.
Matilda, in her letter of 1390, had said: a family that keeps asking whether it has  
done enough will keep the land. A family that stops asking will lose it, sooner or later,  
one way or another. She was speaking of a different kind of question — the question  
of sufficient action, of enough-ness, of whether one had done what was required. But

the letters of John and Thomas suggest another version of the same principle: a  
family that keeps asking what is true, that refuses to settle for a convenient answer,  
that maintains the difficulty of the question even when the question is costing it  
something — that family also keeps something. It keeps its integrity. Which may, in  
the long run, be the deed-box within the deed-box.
The Civil War generation of Hales asked harder questions than any generation  
before them, and answered them less satisfactorily, and was more honest about the  
insufficiency of the answers. They were the best generation of Hales. They were also  
the most expensive, in every sense, and the one that came closest to destroying what  
five centuries of quiet accumulation had built.
They did not destroy it. The land held. The letters survived. And somewhere  
beneath the lining of the deed-box, Matilda's paper waited for the next person to put  
their hand in far enough to find it.
    ✠ ✠ ✠

VII
On the English of the Civil War
A Note on the Language of this Volume
The English of the Civil War period is the most formally powerful English that has  
ever been written, and it is so for a specific historical reason: the King James Bible  
was published in 1611, thirty years before the war began, and by 1642 it had been  
heard in every church in England every Sunday for a generation. Its cadences — the  
parallelism, the falling close, the preference for monosyllabic Anglo-Saxon over  
Latinate abstraction — had become part of the interior life of every literate English  
person, including those who disagreed violently with each other about the uses to  
which they were putting it.
John Milton, who was thirty-four when the war began, had already published  
Comus and Lycidas; he was ten years from Paradise Lost. His pamphlets of the 1640s  
— Areopagitica, The Tenure of Kings and Magistrates, the Doctrine and Discipline of  
Divorce — deployed the resources of the English language in the service of political  
argument with a confidence that no English writer before him had achieved. His  
sentences are long because the argument requires length; his vocabulary is exact  
because the argument requires exactness; his tone is morally urgent because he  
believed, with the full force of his extraordinary intelligence, that what England  
decided in the 1640s would determine the condition of the human soul for centuries  
to come. He was not wrong about the importance of the question. He was, at times,  
wrong about the answer. He was honest enough, in his later work, to know this.
The language of John and Thomas Hale's letters is not Milton's language — they  
are farmers, not poets, and their sentences reflect the plain practical English of men  
who have learned to write from reading the Bible and attending the courts. But the  
biblical cadence is there: in John's that way to survive an institution that wishes to  
trouble you is not to fight it, and not to placate it, but to give it very precisely what it  
has asked for — there is the triadic structure of the Psalms, the falling close, the  
moral precision of a man who has heard Cranmer's Prayer Book all his life. He did  
not know he was writing that way. He was.
Thomas's letters are different in register: briefer, more declarative, less inclined  
to the subordinate clause. He is the more Tyndale of the two brothers — the short  
sentence, the concrete noun, the direct address. Some things ought not to be

decided by argument. Some things are settled. He learned that English, too, from the  
Bible. A different part of the Bible, perhaps, but the same book.
The next volume — set in the early Georgian period — will find the language  
transformed again. Nathaniel Hale dictating his memoirs in 1743 is writing in the  
English of Swift and Defoe: blunt, sardonic, willing to be honest about money and  
appetite in ways the Civil War generation would have found somewhat alarming. The  
long moral urgency of the seventeenth century has released, in the eighteenth, into  
something lighter and more knowing and less certain of its own righteousness. This  
is not entirely a loss.
The Hale who made that transition — Robert Hale the Fourth, Nathaniel's father,  
who was born in 1663 and died in 1710 — left no documents of his own in the archive.  
He is known to us only through the brief references Nathaniel makes in his memoir,  
describing him as a man of good sense and no ambition, which coming from  
Nathaniel is not quite the praise it appears. Between the Civil War generation and  
the Georgian memoir, there is a gap of a generation: a man who kept his head down  
in the aftermath of great events and accumulated quietly, as his family had always  
accumulated, and who produced a son who would not keep his head down at all.
    ✠ ✠ ✠
