GLOSSARY
Words and events which the text employs and the reader may wish explained
The Wars of the 
Three KingdomsThe collective term for the series of conflicts fought between 1639 and  
1651 across England, Scotland, and Ireland, encompassing the Scottish  
Bishops' Wars, the Irish Rebellion of 1641, the English Civil War (1642–
1646), the Second Civil War (1648), and the final campaign ending at the  
Battle of Worcester (1651). Approximately two hundred thousand people  
died  in  the  English  conflict  alone  —  around  four  per  cent  of  the  
population — a proportionate death toll higher than that of the First  
World War.
Commission of 
ArrayThe mechanism by which Charles I raised his army in 1642, ordering the  
Lord Lieutenants of each county to muster the able-bodied men of their  
counties for the King's service. It was based on a medieval precedent  
and was of questionable legality by 1642, since Parliament had not  
authorised it. Parliament responded with its own equivalent, the Militia  
Ordinance, also of questionable legality. Both sides spent the early  
months of the war in a constitutional grey area that neither wished to  
acknowledge.
Sequestration The process by which Parliament seized the estates of those who had  
actively fought for the King after a certain date. Sequestered estates  
were  managed  by  county  sequestration  committees  and  could  be  
compounded  for  —  that  is,  recovered  by  the  original  owner  upon  
payment of a fine proportional to the estate's value. The threat of  
sequestration was one of the primary instruments by which Parliament  
controlled Royalist sympathisers in the aftermath of the war.
The Interregnum The period between the execution of Charles I in January 1649 and the  
Restoration  of  Charles  II  in  May  1660.  England  was  governed  
successively as a Commonwealth (a republic governed by Parliament), a  
Protectorate under Oliver Cromwell (1653–1658), and briefly a second  
Protectorate under his son Richard Cromwell (1658–1659), before the  
Army invited Charles II to return.
The King James 
BibleThe authorised translation of the Bible into English, commissioned by  
King James I and published in 1611. Its translators drew on the work of  
William Tyndale, Miles Coverdale, and others, and produced a text of  
extraordinary literary power. By 1642 it had been read aloud in English  
churches for thirty years and had become part of the mental furniture of

every literate English person. Its influence on the prose of the Civil War  
period — on both sides — is pervasive and unmistakable.
Nonconformist / 
DissenterTerms for those Protestants who refused to conform to the restored  
Church  of  England  after  1660,  including  Independents  
(Congregationalists), Presbyterians, Baptists, and Quakers. The Act of  
Uniformity  of  1662  and  the  subsequent  Clarendon  Code  imposed  
substantial civil disabilities on Nonconformists, including prohibition  
from  holding  public  office  and,  in  some  periods,  imprisonment  for  
holding unauthorised religious meetings. John Hale's attendance at the  
Independent meeting house placed him in this category.
Areopagitica 
(1644)John Milton's prose pamphlet arguing against the censorship of printed  
books, written in response to a Parliamentary order requiring pre-
publication licensing. It is among the most eloquent defences of freedom  
of expression ever written in English, and is notable for being addressed  
to the very Parliament it is criticising — a rhetorical confidence that  
could only have been attempted in the mid-1640s, when Parliament  
needed the support of men like Milton.
The Glorious 
Revolution 
(1688)The deposition of James II — a Catholic king whose attempts to advance  
the cause of his religion alarmed both the political classes and the  
general population — and his replacement by the Protestant William of  
Orange and his wife Mary II, who were invited to take the throne by a  
group of leading politicians. It was accomplished without significant  
bloodshed, which is why it is called glorious. It settled, for practical  
purposes, the constitutional questions that the Civil War had raised and  
failed to resolve: Parliament was supreme; the Crown ruled by law; the  
rights of the subject were protected by statute.
    ✠ ✠ ✠
END OF BOOK IV — THE DIVIDED HOUSE
The story continues in Book V:
The Memoirs of Sir Nathaniel Hale
1671 — 1744

✦ ✦ ✦
THE MEMOIRS OF
Sir Nathaniel Hale,
Baronet,
Late of Hale Court, Worcestershire, and of Threadneedle Street, London;
formerly of His Majesty's Commission of the Peace for the County of Worcester;
and late Proprietor of Shares in the South Sea Company.
Dictated to Mr Josiah Crump, Secretary,
from Rooms in Southwark, during the Winter of 1743–44,
Being an Account of a Life Lived, for the Greater Part, at the Expense of Others.
Published by J. Dodsley, Pall Mall, MDCCXLIV.

"Every Man is not only himself; there have been many Diogenes, and as 
many Timons, though but few of that name; men are liv'd over again, the 
World is now as it was in Ages past."
— Sir Thomas Browne, Religio Medici, 1643
"When a Man knows he is to be hang'd in a Fortnight, it concentrates his 
Mind wonderfully."
— A saying of the period, later attributed to Dr Samuel Johnson



A Note by the Secretary
I am Josiah Crump, and I have been secretary to Sir Nathaniel Hale for the 
better part of eleven years, during which time I have served him in the 
capacities of amanuensis, clerk, occasional creditor, and — upon one 
regrettable evening in the winter of 1738 — as second in a dispute arising 
from a card game in Covent Garden, the details of which need not be 
recorded here, though I will note that the matter was resolved without 
bloodshed and to the general satisfaction of all parties except the 
gentleman who had cheated.
What follows is Sir Nathaniel's account of his own life, dictated to me 
over the course of seventeen evenings between the fourteenth of November,  
1743, and the twenty-second of January, 1744. I have transcribed it 
faithfully and without correction, which has required considerable self-
discipline on my part, as the reader will discover. Sir Nathaniel's 
relationship with chronological sequence is, to employ the most charitable 
formulation available, creative; his relationship with certain well-known 
historical facts is, in several instances, entirely his own; and his opinions 
upon the characters of persons living and deceased are of a frankness that 
would, in a younger man, be considered imprudent, and which in a man of 
seventy-two may perhaps be received with the charitable construction that 
age and destitution together permit.
He dictated lying upon a daybed with a glass of Madeira within reach, 
which he replenished at intervals that bore no apparent relation to the 
emptying of the previous glass. The Madeira was of indifferent quality. He 
did not appear to notice. He has, I think, reached the condition of 
equanimity toward which philosophers have always aspired and which only 
the thoroughly ruined ever actually achieve.
I commend these Memoirs to the public as a document of 
unimpeachable authenticity upon the question of what ambition, applied 
without restraint of scruple, produces in a single human life. Whether the 
life they describe is a cautionary tale or a success story, I leave to the 
reader to determine. Sir Nathaniel himself, when I posed this question to 
him directly, considered for a moment, reached for his glass, and said: 
"Both, Crump. And neither. Which is to say, a life."
J. Crump, Secretary
Southwark, February 1744

CHAPTER I
In Which the Reader is Introduced to My  
Origins, My Father's Profession, and the  
Particular Poverty of a Solicitor's Third  
Son
"In the beginning was the Word; and the Word was, in my father's case, a bill of  
costs."
— N. Hale, Mem.
I was born upon the fourteenth of March, in the Year of Our Lord Sixteen 
Hundred and Seventy-One, in a house in Worcester that smelled of tallow 
candles and legal documents and the particular anxiety of a family that 
possessed sufficient gentility to be ashamed of its circumstances but 
insufficient income to transcend them. My father, Mr Robert Hale, was a 
solicitor of the middling sort — competent, honest, and therefore not rich —  
who spent forty years serving the legal needs of the Worcestershire gentry 
with a conscientiousness that they rewarded with moderate fees and, upon 
his death, a modest epitaph which I composed myself and which described 
him as a man of sterling probity, which he was, and which served him very 
poorly in the world.
I had two brothers elder than myself: James, who was five years my 
senior and had the good fortune to be the eldest, and therefore inherited 
such property as our father left; and William, who was three years my 
senior and had the compensating good fortune to die of a fever at the age of  
sixteen, thereby removing himself from the competition for whatever 
remained. I mention William without callousness; he was a pleasant boy of 
no particular distinction, and his chief contribution to my biography was to 
demonstrate, by his early departure from it, that Providence operates upon 
principles which are entirely its own and which bear no discernible relation 
to merit.
The condition of a solicitor's third son in the England of 1685 was, in its 
essentials, the following: you were educated sufficiently to know what you 
lacked; you were connected, by your father's profession, to the gentry, but 
not of it; you had a name but no estate; a coat but not a coat of arms; 
manners but not a manor. You were, in the phrase of the age, a gentleman's  
poor relation — a category that managed, with considerable efficiency, to 
combine the disadvantages of both the classes between which it was 
suspended, and the advantages of neither.

My father, perceiving this condition with the analytical clarity that he 
brought to all situations except his own finances, determined that what I 
required was a trade that wore the appearance of a profession. He had 
himself entertained hopes of the law, which hopes had produced a solicitor's  
practice rather than a barrister's eminence, and he was acute enough to 
understand that the distinction between those two things was less a matter 
of talent than of connexion, and that connexion was precisely what he could 
not supply. He therefore secured for me, at the age of fourteen, a position 
as clerk to a merchant of the City named Alderman Josiah Pott — a man of 
immense girth, immense prosperity, and a moral architecture that was, 
upon close inspection, rather more flexible than his public reputation 
suggested.
It was Alderman Pott who first showed me, by the daily example of his 
operations, the central truth of commercial life in the England of Queen 
Anne: to wit, that money does not flow toward virtue, nor away from vice, 
but rather toward those who understand the difference between the 
appearance of one and the reality of the other, and who are prepared to 
manage both simultaneously. This is not a cynical observation. It is, as near 
as I can determine, a description of fact.
❧
I will say one thing in defence of my father, whose memory I honour 
despite the inconveniences his honesty created for me: he was, alone among  
all the persons I have known in the course of a long and instructive life, 
entirely what he appeared to be. Not because he lacked the imagination to 
be otherwise, but because he had made a deliberate choice — I saw him 
make it, or rather I saw the settled consequence of his having made it some 
years before I arrived in the world — to live exactly as narrowly as his 
means permitted and exactly as honestly as his profession required, and to 
regard the gap between what he was and what he might contrive to appear 
as a gap too costly to maintain.
I respected this. I did not emulate it. A man can respect a principle and 
still conclude, upon the evidence of his own circumstances, that the 
principle, however admirable in the abstract, is not one that he can afford to  
keep.
My father died in 1699, owing no man a penny, possessed of a house 
worth two hundred pounds and a library worth forty, and leaving to James 
the house, to me the library, and to our joint account the memory of a man 
who had been, in every material sense, insufficient to the world he 
inhabited. I sold the library for thirty-one pounds and invested the sum with 
Alderman Pott, who was by that point employing me not as a clerk but as 
what he was pleased to call his agent of special enquiry, a designation

which covered a variety of functions that I shall describe in due course, and 
which paid, in consequence of their variety, rather better than clerkship.

CHAPTER II
Concerning Alderman Pott, the Nature of  
His Enterprises, and My Introduction to  
the System by Which Respectable Men  
Conduct Disreputable Business
"Trade is the great leveller of morals, as the sea is the great leveller of men."
— N. Hale, Mem.
Alderman Josiah Pott was, in the years I served him, one of the wealthiest 
men in the City of London, and one of the most completely forgotten by 
history, which I attribute not to the insignificance of his operations but to 
the thoroughness with which he ensured that the significant parts of those 
operations appeared, on any document likely to survive his death, as 
entirely someone else's.
His legitimate trade was in cloth: Flemish wool, Indian calico, and the 
silks and muslins of the Levant, which he imported through agents in 
Antwerp, Smyrna, and Surat, and distributed to merchants and drapers 
throughout England at prices that were, in consequence of his scale and his 
connexions, consistently lower than his competitors could match and 
consistently higher than his suppliers could resist. This was, in itself, a 
perfectly creditable enterprise, and one upon which the Alderman might 
have founded a fortune and a reputation simultaneously, had he not been 
constitutionally incapable of limiting himself to it.
The less legitimate portions of his trade I will describe with the 
directness that the passage of forty years and the dissolution of all relevant 
parties makes available to me. Pott held interests, through a series of 
holding arrangements of such labyrinthine complexity that even I, who 
constructed several of them, required a full hour of reflection to trace them 
accurately, in three ships engaged in the Atlantic trade; which is to say, in 
the transportation of enslaved persons from the coast of Guinea to the 
plantations of the West Indies. He was not unusual in this. Half the 
aldermen of the City of London held similar interests, concealed behind 
similar arrangements, and regarded with similar equanimity. I record it not 
to indict Pott specifically — he was, in the moral economy of his age, 
unremarkable — but because the elision of such facts from the memoirs of 
men of his station is itself a form of the very deception that I am, in these 
pages, attempting to anatomise.
[Here Sir Nathaniel paused and

requested his glass be refilled. He 
then said, to no one in particular: 
"Put it all in, Crump. The whole of 
it. I am too old to be respectable 
and too poor to be sued." — J.C.]
My own role in Pott's operations was, in its beginnings, entirely 
mundane. I kept his accounts. I conducted his correspondence with the 
Flemish factors. I translated, with the imperfect French that my father's 
library had provided me, certain documents from Antwerp whose precise 
nature I did not inquire into and whose content I have since had reason to 
suspect concerned matters that the English law of that period regarded 
with a strictness that varied in direct proportion to the social eminence of 
the men involved.
What elevated me, in Pott's estimation, from a competent clerk to an 
indispensable agent was an incident in the autumn of 1695, when I was 
four-and-twenty years of age, which I shall describe plainly. A merchant 
named Harrowgate, who had contracted a substantial debt to Pott upon 
certain securities that were, upon examination, not quite what they had 
been represented to be, fell into financial difficulty and made application to 
Pott for an extension of terms. Pott sent me to negotiate. I negotiated. I 
returned with terms more favourable than Pott had anticipated, and with 
certain information about Harrowgate's other creditors that rendered those 
terms, in the medium term, even more valuable than they appeared. Pott 
asked me how I had obtained the information. I told him. He looked at me 
for a long moment over his considerable belly and said: "Hale, you are 
entirely without scruple." I accepted this as the compliment it was intended 
to be.
He was not, strictly, correct. I had then, and retain still, a species of 
scruple — but it is a scruple of method rather than of object, which is to say 
that I have always been unwilling to obtain by crude or violent means what 
can be obtained by intelligence, patience, and the careful management of 
other people's self-interest. This is not virtue. But it is, I submit, a discipline;  
and a discipline, however morally neutral its application, produces in the 
character that exercises it a quality of self-command that is not entirely 
without value.

CHAPTER III
Of My Marriage to Miss Dorothea Fenwick,  
and the Domestic Arrangements Which  
Followed
"A wife of good family and a husband of good prospects is an alliance; whether  
it becomes a marriage depends upon what happens after."
— N. Hale, Mem.
I married Dorothea Fenwick in the spring of 1702, when I was one-and-
thirty and she was nineteen, and I will say at once — lest the reader form, 
from the disparity of our ages, the ungenerous inference that the connexion 
was purely one of calculation — that the calculation was, in this instance, 
accompanied by a genuine and lasting affection which I did not, at the time 
of the marriage, fully understand myself, and which I have had, in the forty 
years since, occasion to understand considerably better.
Dorothea was the younger daughter of Sir Francis Fenwick of Fenwick 
Park, Cumberland — a baronet of ancient title and catastrophically modern 
debt, whose estates were encumbered to a degree that would, within five 
years of our marriage, require my personal intervention to prevent their 
sale. This intervention I performed willingly, not merely because the 
Fenwick estates were a useful security but because Dorothea loved her 
father with the particular fierce loyalty of a woman who perceives clearly 
the deficiencies of a person and loves them the more precisely on that 
account; and because I had, by that time, arrived at the understanding that 
Dorothea's happiness was a matter which I had, without quite intending to, 
placed rather higher in my own accounting than I had placed most other 
things.
She was not deceived in me, at the outset. I mean by this something 
specific: she did not harbour, about my character, the illusions that younger  
or less intelligent women might have cultivated. She had observed me in the  
eighteen months of our courtship with the steady, particular attention of a 
woman who intends to know what she is doing, and she had arrived, I think,  
at an accurate assessment — not flattering, but not dismissive either — of 
the kind of man I was. She married me in full possession of that assessment.  
I have always regarded this as the most significant honour anyone has done 
me.
❧

"Nathaniel," she said to me, upon the occasion of our first serious 
quarrel, which occurred approximately four months after our wedding and 
concerned a financial transaction that I had completed without informing 
her of its nature until it was concluded — "Nathaniel, I am not a person to 
whom things are done. I am a person who does things. If you intend to treat 
me as the former, you will find the arrangement considerably less 
comfortable than you anticipate."
I had been, until that moment, disposed to regard the communication of 
my business affairs to my wife as a courtesy rather than a necessity. I 
revised this view. Not because she demanded it, but because her 
observation was accurate, and accuracy, in my experience, is the most 
compelling form of argument.
We had, in the years that followed, a marriage that I can describe with 
confidence as the best thing I have been party to. Not without friction — we 
were both of us persons of determined character, and determined 
characters in close proximity produce, as the physical philosophers might 
observe, considerable heat — but of a quality that made the friction 
productive rather than merely painful. Dorothea had opinions upon my 
operations. She expressed them. Some I heeded; some I did not; but I 
always heard them, and they were always worth hearing, and the occasions 
upon which I failed to heed them and was subsequently proved wrong are 
among the most instructive episodes of my career, which is saying 
something.
Hale Court, Worcestershire.
The 3rd of September, 1719.
My dear Husband, —
I have receiv'd your Letter of the 28th ultimo, in which you tell me 
that you have purchas'd further Shares in the South Sea Company to the  
value of Three Thousand Pounds, and that the Prospects of the Venture 
are, in your Estimation, without Parallel in the history of English 
Commerce. I have read this Letter with the Attention it deserves, which 
is to say very carefully and with a degree of Disquiet that I think it my 
Conjugal Duty to communicate to you plainly.
The Disquiet is not, as you will anticipate, the ordinary domestic 
Anxiety about Money, with which I have long since made my Peace. It is,  
rather, the particular Unease that I experience when I observe you in 
the Act of believing something because you wish it to be true, rather 
than because the Evidence compels it. You have been to this Place 
before, Husband — not in the Matter of the South Sea Company, but in 
the matter of Pott's Levant venture in '09, and in the matter of the

Mercer Street properties, and upon each occasion the Confidence 
preceded the Catastrophe with a regularity that I should have thought, 
by now, instructive.
I do not tell you to sell your Shares. I am not a Woman who tells her 
Husband what to do with Money that is, in law, entirely his own. I tell 
you only that I observe in your present Correspondence a Quality of 
Certainty that, in my Experience of twenty years, is not your natural 
register, and that when you write in a Register that is not your own, it is 
generally because you are performing something — and the Question 
that presents itself to me, as it always does, is: for whose Benefit is the 
Performance?
Your children and I are well. The harvest at the home farm is 
adequate. Your son Francis has taken to reading your lawbooks, which I 
take as either an encouraging Sign of Intelligence or a Warning of 
Disposition. I have not yet determined which.
Your faithful and apprehensive Wife,
Dorothea Hale

CHAPTER IV
Being a Full and Unvarnished Account of  
the South Sea Bubble, My Part In It, and  
the Education Which Its Bursting Afforded  
Me
"The South Sea Company was the greatest single proof, in the history of  
England, that there is no limit to the credulity of intelligent men when the  
object of that credulity promises them ten per cent per quarter."
— N. Hale, Mem.
I shall describe the South Sea affair with the directness it deserves, which is  
more directness than it has generally received from those who have written 
about it, most of whom were either participants unwilling to acknowledge 
the extent of their participation, or observers sufficiently remote from 
events to mistake the spectacle for the substance.
The South Sea Company was incorporated by Act of Parliament in 1711, 
ostensibly for the purpose of trading with the Spanish colonies of South 
America. The trade itself was, from the outset, a chimera — the Spanish 
were not disposed to allow English merchants unrestricted access to their 
colonial markets, and the terms of the Asiento, which gave the Company a 
limited right to supply enslaved persons to those markets, were not the 
basis of the commercial empire that the Company's prospectus implied. This  
was known, in broad outline, to anyone who troubled to inquire. Very few 
troubled to inquire.
What the Company actually traded in was expectation. This is not a 
metaphor. It sold shares in the prospect of future profits which it had no 
specific reason to believe it would generate, to purchasers who had no 
specific reason to believe they would receive them, at prices that increased 
in direct proportion to the number of other purchasers who had committed 
themselves and therefore had an interest in maintaining the price. It was, in  
the strictest technical sense, a fraud of collective construction — not the 
fraud of a single villain but the fraud of an entire society that had 
simultaneously decided, for reasons that were not irrational given the 
information available, that the rules of arithmetic had been temporarily 
suspended in favour of a more convenient arrangement.
THE SOUTH SEA COMPANY

A Notice to Proprietors of Stock. June, 1720.
The Court of Directors of the South Sea Company takes Satisfaction 
in communicating
to its Proprietors that the current Price of South Sea Stock stands 
at ONE THOUSAND
AND FIFTY POUNDS per Share, representing an Increase of Eight 
Hundred and Forty
Pounds upon the Issue Price of the First Subscription.
The Directors are pleas'd to advise that a further Subscription 
shall be open'd on
the Fourteenth instant, at a Price of ONE THOUSAND POUNDS per Share,  
payable
by Instalments of Ten per Cent per Quarter.
Persons desirous of Subscribing should make early Application, as 
the Directors
anticipate that Demand will, as heretofore, considerably exceed 
Supply.
By Order of the Court of Directors,
J. Knight, Cashier. Threadneedle Street, London. 9th June, 1720.
[This Broadside preserv'd among the papers of Sir N. Hale, with the 
marginal annotation:
"I bought at 800. I sold at 400. I am, by this calculation, what is 
vulgarly call'd ruin'd."]
I was not, by the standard of many, among the worst casualties of the 
Bubble. I had purchased, in the first enthusiasm of 1719, shares to the value  
of twelve thousand pounds, representing very nearly three-quarters of my 
liquid capital at that time. The Hale Court estate, which I had acquired in 
1708 through a sequence of purchases that I shall describe in the following 
chapter, was not encumbered, as I had resolved upon its acquisition never 
to mortgage land; this resolution, which I had maintained against 
considerable temptation at several points, was the single act of financial 
discipline in my life for which I am most grateful, as it preserved the 
material foundation upon which everything that followed was constructed.

The shares were purchased at an average price of approximately eight 
hundred pounds. They were sold, in October of 1720, at three hundred and 
ninety. The arithmetic of this transaction produced, net of the Fenwick 
estate debt that I had also engaged on the Company's security, a loss of 
approximately nine thousand pounds over eighteen months. I record this 
figure without the remorse that the occasion perhaps calls for, not because I  
felt no remorse — I felt a great deal, and directed most of it inwardly — but 
because remorse, as I have observed in the ruin of many men, is the least 
productive of all the responses available to a person in financial extremity, 
and the one most likely to preclude the energetic action that extremity 
requires.
❧
Dorothea said nothing when I told her. She sat for a moment in the 
morning room at Hale Court with her hands folded in her lap and looked out  
of the window at the park, which was in its October colours and very fine, 
and then she said: "Are we to lose the house?"
"No," I said. "The house is secure. We lose the London properties and 
the Threadneedle Street counting-house, and certain other investments of 
which I shall give you the full account presently. But not the house."
"Then," she said, "we have sufficient to begin again."
It was not a consolation. It was a statement of programme. There is a 
quality of mind that assesses a situation and extracts from it, with the 
minimum of sentiment, the precise quantity of forward motion that it 
permits; Dorothea possessed this quality to a degree that I have 
encountered in perhaps three other persons in my life, and which I regard 
as the most practically useful of all human faculties. She rose from her 
chair, pressed my hand briefly — it was not our habit to be demonstrative; 
we expressed affection through the quality of our attention to each other 
rather than through its physical exhibition — and went to call for tea. We 
spent the following four hours reviewing the accounts. I told her everything.  
We did not speak of it again as a catastrophe; we spoke of it as an 
inventory.

CHAPTER V
How I Acquired a Baronetcy, and at What  
Price, and Whether I Consider It to Have  
Been Worth the Sum
"A title without an estate is a coat without a lining. A title with an estate is a  
coat with a lining. Either may keep out the cold, depending upon the weather  
and the quality of the cloth."
— N. Hale, Mem.
The baronetcy was purchased in 1726, six years after the Bubble, at a price 
of fifteen hundred pounds paid to an intermediary whose connexion with the  
Lord Chamberlain's office I did not inquire into, as certain transactions 
proceed most smoothly in the absence of detailed understanding. The title 
was, in the language of the patent, granted in recognition of services to the 
Crown in the matter of the Worcester Militia Subscription — a matter in 
which I had indeed involved myself, somewhat hastily and at my secretary's 
suggestion, in the autumn of 1725, when it became apparent that the 
opportunity for the purchase of a lesser distinction was likely to present 
itself and that a preceding record of public service, however compressed in 
its construction, would materially facilitate the transaction.
This is not how the creation of baronetcies is officially described. It is, 
however, how several dozen of them were arranged in the decade following 
the accession of the first George, when the new dynasty was in need of 
revenue and the commercial classes were in need of respectability, and the 
intersection of these two requirements produced a market that was, by the 
standards of any other market, admirably efficient.
Was it worth fifteen hundred pounds? Let me consider this with the 
candour that my present circumstances, which include an absence of any 
social position worth protecting, make available to me.
The title produced, in the immediate term, the following material 
advantages: admission to two clubs to which I had previously been unable 
to obtain membership; the elevation of my son Francis to a social circle that  
would, within four years, produce a marriage to the daughter of a 
Derbyshire viscount of adequate property; an improvement in the terms 
offered by my principal bankers at Child's; and an invitation to a dinner at 
which I met the man who became my most profitable client for the 
subsequent twelve years. Against these advantages, I set the fifteen 
hundred pounds, the residual discomfort of a transaction that did not, upon 
any honest examination, reflect well upon the system that made it possible,

and the mild but persistent irritation of being addressed as Sir Nathaniel by  
persons who had, prior to the transaction, addressed me as Hale or, in 
moments of social condescension, as my dear Hale.
The advantages outweighed the costs. I write this without pride, which 
would be absurd, and without excessive shame, which would be 
disproportionate. The world as it is requires navigation as it is; the 
navigator who insists upon the world as it ought to be generally finds 
himself on the rocks.
Letters Patent Creating the Baronetcy of Hale
Granted at the Court of St. James's, in the Thirteenth Year of the Reign of  
His Majesty King George I, 1726.
GEORGE, by the Grace of God, King of Great Britain, France, and 
Ireland, Defender of the Faith, &c., To all Lords Spiritual and Temporal, 
and all other Our faithful Subjects to whom these Presents shall come, 
Greeting.
KNOW YE that We, reposing especial Trust and Confidence in the 
Loyalty, Courage, and fidelity of Our trusty and well-beloved NATHANIEL  
HALE, Esquire, of Hale Court in the County of Worcester, and in 
consideration of his Services rendered to Us in the matter of the 
Worcester Militia and in divers other Matters tending to Our Royal 
Interest and the publick Good of Our Kingdom, do hereby Create and 
Constitute the said NATHANIEL HALE a Baronet of Our United Kingdom 
of Great Britain and Ireland, to have and to hold the said Dignity of 
Baronet unto him the said NATHANIEL HALE and the Heirs Male of his 
Body lawfully begotten, with all and singular the Rights, Privileges, Pre-
eminences, Immunities, and Advantages to the Degree of Baronet 
belonging and appertaining.
Given under Our Sign Manual at Our Court at St. James's the ninth day of 
April in the Year of Our Lord One Thousand Seven Hundred and Twenty-Six.
By His Majesty's Command: [Seal] G.R.

CHAPTER VI
Concerning My Son Francis, His Marriage,  
His Disposition, and the Use He Has Made  
of the Name I Built for Him
"Every generation inherits the consequences of the preceding one's behaviour,  
and believes itself to have earned what it received."
— N. Hale, Mem.
My son Francis was born in 1705 and is now, at the time of these Memoirs, 
thirty-nine years of age, and a man of considerable property and excellent 
reputation in Worcestershire and in those portions of London society to 
which his marriage has given him access. He is, by all accounts — and 
accounts reach me even in Southwark, through the medium of Crump and 
through the occasional letter that Francis is good enough to send, when the 
exigencies of his own affairs permit him the leisure — a gentleman of 
impeccable character. I say this without irony, which is remarkable, 
because irony has been my natural medium for the better part of seven 
decades.
He is genuinely impeccable. He is honest in his dealings, faithful in his 
marriage, attentive to his tenants, regular in his religious observance, and 
regarded in his county as a man whose word is his bond and whose conduct 
is, in all its visible aspects, above reproach. His children are well-bred. His 
house is well-managed. His account at the bank is, I understand, in 
satisfactory condition.
He has not visited me in Southwark. I do not hold this against him, as 
the visit would be difficult to explain to the persons of quality whose opinion  
he has reason to value. I understand the calculation. I made similar 
calculations myself, at various points, though the object of my calculation 
was advancement rather than preservation of reputation. The mechanics 
are the same. The direction merely differs.
What I find interesting — and I mean interesting in the precise rather 
than the ironic sense — is that Francis's respectability is, as nearly as I can 
determine, genuine. He has taken the raw material of the name I 
constructed — with all the transactions, accommodations, and strategic 
infidelities that its construction required — and he has made of it something  
that requires, now, no management at all, because the substance has 
caught up with the surface. The edifice, as I built it, required constant 
maintenance; in Francis's keeping, it has become, apparently, the thing it 
was always pretending to be.

I do not know whether to regard this as success or irony or both. I 
incline toward both. His son — my grandson, who is seventeen and whom I 
have met upon three occasions, the last of which was four years ago — is 
named Robert, after my father, which Francis arranged without consulting 
me and which I discovered through Crump. I was, upon receiving this 
intelligence, affected in a manner that I found somewhat surprising in a 
man of my age and my general orientation toward sentiment.
Robert. My grandson. He will have, if Francis's stewardship and his own  
conduct permit, the thing that I spent my life constructing: a name, an 
estate, and the luxury — which I never possessed and which I am not 
certain I would have known what to do with — of not having to think about 
either.
❧
Whether this constitutes a dynasty, a vindication, or merely the natural 
progression of acquired respectability across generations, I leave to the 
philosophers. What I observe, from the vantage of my Southwark rooms and  
my Madeira of indifferent vintage, is something simpler: that the gap 
between what a man is and what he appears to be is not fixed. That if the 
appearance is maintained with sufficient discipline over sufficient time, the 
reality gradually conforms to it. Not always. Not without cost. But often 
enough to make the project something other than pure deception.
My father was entirely himself, and it cost him everything. I was 
emphatically not myself, for thirty years, and it cost me most things but not 
everything. Francis, by some alchemy I do not fully understand, appears to 
be entirely himself in a self that resembles the one I performed — as though  
the performance, repeated across a generation, has acquired the density of 
truth.
I do not know what to make of this. I find, at seventy-two, that I am more  
interested in questions I cannot answer than I was at forty, when I had the 
confidence of a man who believed he understood the mechanism. The 
mechanism, it turns out, is considerably more complex than I apprehended. 
This is, I think, what is meant by wisdom; and it arrives, as Crump will 
confirm from personal observation, at a very inconvenient time.

CHAPTER VII
Being the Conclusion of These Memoirs,  
in Which the Author Reflects Upon What  
He Has Been, What He Has Done, and  
What, Upon the Balance of the Account,  
Remains
"A man's life, examined at its end, resembles nothing so much as an account-
book: full of entries, not all of them legible, and always, at the bottom, a balance  
which is never quite what one anticipated."
— N. Hale, Mem.
It is the twenty-first of January, 1744. I am, as I have been for the better 
part of a year, resident in three rooms in Southwark, for which I pay four 
shillings a week to a widow named Mrs Catchpole who asks no questions 
and provides, on Tuesdays and Fridays, a dinner of adequate quality. Crump  
informs me that the rooms are better than they appear, which I take to 
mean that they are not as dangerous as they look, which is a description 
that has applied, at various periods, to several aspects of my life and which I  
have come to regard as acceptable.
I have been a solicitor's clerk. I have been a merchant's agent. I have 
been a creditor of small men and a debtor of large ones. I have been a land-
owner, a company promoter, a militia subscriber, and a baronet. I have 
been, successively and sometimes simultaneously, prosperous, comfortable, 
stretched, encumbered, and ruined. I have been a husband and a father and  
a grandfather, though the last is a category of which I have made imperfect 
use. I have been, at various points, on the right side and the wrong side of 
the law, which is not the same as being on the right side and the wrong side  
of what is actually right and wrong, a distinction that the law is not always 
careful to observe.
What have I been, underneath all of this? This is the question that 
occasions, in a man who has spent his life in the construction and 
maintenance of surfaces, the longest and most uncomfortable pause.
I have been, I think, a man of genuine intelligence and deficient patience  
— deficient not in the management of affairs, where I have displayed a 
patience of considerable endurance, but in the longer patience required to 
allow a character to form honestly, rather than constructing one 
deliberately and inhabiting it by act of will. I made myself, in the manner of 
a man who lacks the resources to wait for the world to make him, and the

making produced something that worked — that functioned, that advanced, 
that survived the South Sea ruin and the loss of the London properties and 
two changes of monarch and Dorothea's death in '35 — but that was always,  
underneath the function, a performance.
Dorothea knew this, I think, from the beginning. She married the 
performance in full knowledge of its nature, which is either the most loving 
thing a woman can do or the most alarming thing a wife can say about her 
husband, depending upon one's view of marriage. I incline, after forty years 
of reflection, toward the former interpretation. She was a woman who 
understood that the thing performed, if performed with sufficient conviction  
and sufficient time, acquires a reality of its own; that there is no fixed 
essence beneath the performance which the performance conceals, but only  
the accumulating weight of all the acts and choices and maintained 
consistencies, which is to say: the performance is the man.
My father would disagree with this. He believed in the fixed essence, the  
inalienable self that exists prior to all performance and which all 
performance either reveals or obscures. This belief was, I think, the source 
of both his virtue and his poverty — he could not make himself other than 
he was, and what he was, was insufficient to the world's requirements. I 
made myself other than I was, and found, in the making, something that 
was, if not my father's kind of virtue, at least a different kind of sufficiency.
Francis's children will have a name. They will not know what the name 
cost. They will not know about Alderman Pott's Levant arrangements, nor 
about the baronetcy's purchase price, nor about the years between 1720 
and 1726 when the name was a creditor's jest. They will bear the name as a 
given fact, like the colour of their eyes, and they will build upon it in their 
own way, and their children will build further, and at some point — three 
generations hence, perhaps four — there will be a Hale who is so 
thoroughly what the name implies, so genuinely and unselfconsciously 
respectable, that the distance between what he is and what he appears to 
be will have closed entirely.
I think of this Hale occasionally. I cannot picture him clearly. But I 
imagine him in a library somewhere — London, perhaps, or Worcester — 
surrounded by legal documents, possessed of an excellent reputation, 
married to a woman of good family and considerable perception. I imagine 
him performing rectitude with such thorough conviction that he has entirely  
forgotten it is a performance.
He will not know that the performance began here, in these rooms, in 
the mouth of an old man dictating to a long-suffering secretary on winter 
evenings, with Madeira of indifferent quality and the sounds of Southwark 
coming through the shuttered window.

I find I am glad he will not know. And I find, equally, that I am glad I 
have set it down.
Nathaniel Hale, Bart.
Southwark, January 21st, 1744.

Secretary's Postscript
Sir Nathaniel Hale, first Baronet of that name, died upon the fourth day of 
March, 1744, in his rooms in Southwark, aged seventy-two years. He died, 
as he had lived the latter portion of his life, in a condition of reduced but not  
indigent circumstances, and with a lucidity of mind that did not diminish 
until the final two days of his illness.
He died as he had spoken: directly, without undue sentiment, and in full 
possession of his irony.
His son Francis, second Baronet, attended the funeral. He did not, to my  
knowledge, read these Memoirs prior to their publication, having indicated 
to me, upon the occasion of his father's death, that he considered it best not  
to, as the knowledge of how a thing is made is not always necessary to the 
proper use of it. I thought this a very Hale thing to say, and told him so. He 
looked at me with his father's grey eyes and said nothing, which was also a 
very Hale thing to do.
I have prepared these Memoirs for publication in the belief — which Sir 
Nathaniel shared, and which was one of the few matters upon which we 
were in complete agreement from the beginning — that the honest account 
of a dishonest career is, paradoxically, among the most morally instructive 
documents a society can possess. It is instructive not because it tells us 
what not to do — we know, in the abstract, what not to do — but because it 
describes, with a particularity that abstract prohibition cannot approach, 
the texture of the doing of it: the small decisions, the defensible 
accommodations, the habitual elisions by which a man of intelligence and 
appetite navigates a world that rewards the performance of virtue more 
reliably than it rewards virtue itself.
Whether the world is thereby indicted, or the man, or both, I leave, as 
Sir Nathaniel left everything he could not resolve, to the reader.
Josiah Crump
Southwark, February 1744

A Glossary of the Difficult
Words, phrases, and constructions in these Memoirs that reward the labour of inquiry
Amanuensis
A person who writes from dictation or copies manuscripts — from the Latin manus 
(hand) and servus (servant). Crump is Sir Nathaniel's amanuensis; by describing 
himself this way, he is being self-deprecating but also precise. The amanuensis 
takes down exactly what is said without altering it, which is why Crump's role is 
both humble and significant: he is the instrument through which Nathaniel's voice 
reaches us.
Probity
Complete and confirmed integrity; uprightness. Nathaniel describes his father's 
epitaph as celebrating his "sterling probity." Sterling here means of the purest 
quality, as in sterling silver. The word choice is deliberate irony: probity, which 
sounds like property, is the only thing the father left that was worth anything — 
and it couldn't be spent.
Chimera
From Greek mythology, a fire-breathing monster composed of parts of different 
animals; used to mean an illusion or fabrication — something hoped for that has no  
basis in reality. The South Sea Company's trade with Spanish colonies was a 
chimera: it was always an impossibility, but it was marketed as a certainty.
Asiento
From Spanish, meaning agreement or contract. The Asiento de Negros was the 
agreement between the British and Spanish crowns granting Britain the right to 
supply enslaved African people to Spanish colonies in the Americas. It was the 
nominal commercial basis of the South Sea Company. The word appears here 
without ceremony, which is deliberate: Nathaniel uses it with the same matter-of-
factness with which his era used it, and this very matter-of-factness is part of what 
the novel is asking you to notice.
Encumbered
In legal and financial language, burdened with a mortgage, debt, or other legal 
claim. "Estates encumbered to a degree" means the Fenwick family's lands were 
mortgaged — borrowed against — until they were worth very little in practice. This  
is the aristocracy's specific kind of ruin: all title, no money.
Labyrinthine

Resembling a labyrinth — intricate, tortuous, and difficult to trace. From the 
mythological labyrinth of Crete. Nathaniel's financial arrangements were 
labyrinthine by design: complexity protects concealment.
Conjunctural
Relating to a particular combination of circumstances. Not used explicitly in the 
text, but the entire memoir is conjunctural in its reasoning: Nathaniel understands 
every decision as a response to circumstances, never as an expression of pure 
character — which is itself a philosophical position, and not an uninteresting one.
Equanimity
Mental calmness and composure, especially in difficult situations. Crump observes 
that Nathaniel has achieved equanimity in his old age. This is distinguished from 
happiness: equanimity is what happens when you have stopped fighting your 
circumstances and started thinking about them.
Elision
The omission of a sound or passage; more broadly, the leaving-out of something 
that would be uncomfortable to include. The memoirs are explicitly about the 
elision of inconvenient facts from respectable accounts of a life — which is the 
same process by which reputations are maintained and histories are written.
Palimpsest
A manuscript on which the original writing has been partially erased to make room  
for new writing, but where traces of the original remain. The Hale family name is a  
palimpsest: later generations write their respectability over the earlier 
transactions, but traces remain for anyone who looks closely. This novel is the act 
of looking closely.
Divers
An archaic form of "diverse" or "various" — meaning several, of different kinds. 
"Divers other Matters" in the Letters Patent is legal formula meaning "various 
other things" — the vagueness is deliberate, as royal patents do not enumerate the 
precise nature of the services being rewarded.
Impecunious
Having little or no money; poor. Not used explicitly, but the condition of the third 
son — and later of Nathaniel in Southwark — is impecunious: money is insufficient 
to the demands of the position being maintained.
Amour propre
Self-love, self-respect; from the French. The concept runs through the entire 
memoir: Nathaniel's career is, among other things, the sustained maintenance of 
amour propre in the face of circumstances that would have crushed a man with 
less. The question the memoir raises is whether amour propre based on a 
performance is real amour propre or merely its shadow.

Sinecure
A position that requires little or no work but provides a salary or income. Several 
of the offices described in the memoir are, in effect, sinecures — positions held for 
their social and financial value rather than their function. The baronetcy itself is, in  
one sense, a purchased sinecure of identity.
Inalienable
Not transferable to another; unable to be taken away or denied. Nathaniel's father 
believed in the inalienable self — the fixed essence beneath all performance. 
Nathaniel does not share this belief, and the memoir is, among other things, an 
argument against it.
FINIS
London: J. Dodsley, Pall Mall, MDCCXLIV.

THE HALE DYNASTY
BOOK VI
The Hale Entail
Being a Collection of Letters, Estate Accounts, Solicitors' Memoranda,
Diary Entries, Newspaper Extracts, and Sundry Documents pertaining
to the Hale Family of Worcestershire and London,
in the Reigns of George the Third and George the Fourth.
Covering the years
1795 — 1835
    ❧ ❧ ❧
Editor's Prefatory Note
The gap between Book V of this series — the Memoirs of Sir Nathaniel Hale, which  
close in 1744 with their narrator dying in hired rooms in Southwark — and the  
documents gathered in the present volume spans fifty years. They are not an  
uneventful fifty years, either in the life of England or in the life of the Hale family. But  
they are years that left very little paper behind.
What can be established from the surviving records is this: Nathaniel Hale's son  
Francis, born 1710, inherited the baronetcy and the London connections his father  
had spent a lifetime acquiring. Francis was a more cautious man than Nathaniel —  
he had, according to his son's later account, a strong disinclination toward any  
transaction that was not already concluded in its favour, which is perhaps the  
natural temperament of a man who grew up listening to his father's stories about the  
South Sea Bubble. He invested prudently. He increased the family's holdings in the  
funds. He did not, so far as can be determined, do anything particularly memorable.
Francis's son Edward, born 1745, inherited in 1778 and set about the business of  
converting the family's London commercial prosperity into something that looked  
rather more like landed gentry than his grandfather had managed. He purchased an  
estate in Shropshire in 1782 — Harton Hall, two hundred and twelve acres, a  
comfortable house, a farm, a small park — and settled there with his wife Eleanor  
and their four children: George, born 1770; Charles, born 1772; Augusta, born 1775;  
and the youngest, Robert, born 1780.

The estate came with an entail. The entail — a legal instrument that restricted the  
inheritance to the male line, preventing the land from being sold or settled in any  
other direction — was the instrument that would, in the years between 1810 and  
1830, come close to undoing everything the family had spent six centuries building.  
It would do so not through any dramatic catastrophe but through the most ordinary  
of mechanisms: the failure of the male line, the pressure of debt, and the particular  
English legal construction that preferred the claims of a distant male cousin to the  
competence of a capable woman.
That capable woman was Augusta Hale. This volume is, in large part, her story.
— A.F.,  London, November 1884

I
The State of the Estate
Harton Hall, Shropshire, 1795–1805
Edward Hale died in November 1795, aged fifty. He had held Harton Hall for  
thirteen years, long enough to become attached to it and not quite long enough to  
pay off the mortgage with which its purchase had been financed. He left behind an  
estate of two hundred and twelve acres, an entailed house and park, a farm  
generating approximately three hundred and fifty pounds per annum, a mortgaged  
debt of four thousand pounds at five per cent interest, and four children whose  
prospects divided very sharply along the line of sex.
George, the eldest son, inherited the estate, the title, the debt, and the entail. He  
was twenty-five years old. He had been educated at Shrewsbury school and had  
spent two years at Oxford before concluding that Oxford was disagreeable and  
returning to Shropshire, where he had since been farming with a conscientiousness  
that his father had found reassuring and his younger brother Charles had found  
rather too conscientious for comfort, since Charles had no estate and no title and no  
particular aptitude  for anything except  spending money,  which  he  did with a  
dedication that Edward Hale had found considerably less reassuring.
Augusta, the elder daughter, was twenty years old in 1795. She had received the  
education that a gentleman's daughter of the period received: French, a little Italian,  
music, drawing, and whatever she had managed to acquire by reading her brothers'  
books when they were not paying attention, which was a great deal more than the  
formal curriculum had provided. She had, by the time of her father's death, a clearer  
understanding of the Harton Hall accounts than either George or Charles, and had  
on two occasions caught an error in the estate's quarterly reckoning that the  
solicitor had missed.
The solicitor — a man named Fellowes, of the firm Fellowes and Marsh in  
Shrewsbury — had the grace to thank her on both occasions, and the sense to note in  
his private correspondence that Miss Hale would have made an excellent man of  
business.
HARTON HALL ESTATE — ANNUAL 
ACCOUNT, MICHAELMAS 1795Annual Balance

Timber sales, South Wood — £48
Kitchen garden produce sold at market — £22
Interest on funds (Consols, held at Coutts) — £85
TOTAL INCOME £495
