5%)£200 (£200)
Wages: housekeeper, cook, two maids, coachman, 
three farm hands£142 (£142)
Repairs and maintenance, house and farm buildings — (£67)
George Hale, personal allowance £80 (£80)
Charles Hale, allowance (paid quarterly) £120 (£120)
Augusta Hale, allowance £30 (£30)
Rates, tithes, and parish assessments — (£38)
TOTAL EXPENDITURE (£677)
Annual deficit, to be met from capital (£182)
The account reproduced above is the first that Augusta Hale checked on her own  
authority, in the month following her father's death, without being asked to do so.  
She found it depressing but not surprising. The estate had been running at a deficit  
for three years; she had suspected it from the conversation she had overheard  
between her father and Mr. Fellowes in 1793, though neither man had thought it  
necessary to inform her of the fact.
She wrote to Mr. Fellowes the following week, not as a supplicant but as the  
person who had been managing the household accounts since her mother's death in  
1792 and who therefore had relevant information that the formal estate account did  
not capture. Mr. Fellowes replied promptly and with rather more respect than the  
conventions of 1795 strictly required him to extend to a twenty-year-old woman with  
no legal standing in the affairs he was managing.
Augusta Hale to Mr. Fellowes of Fellowes and Marsh, Shrewsbury — 
December, 1795

Dear Mr. Fellowes, I write regarding the estate account for the Michaelmas  
quarter, which I have examined with some care and which I fear presents a picture  
rather more favourable than the actual circumstances of the household warrant.
The  account  does  not  include  two  items  of  which  I  am  aware:  a  debt  of  
approximately sixty pounds owed to the butcher and the draper in Shrewsbury,  
which my father had been servicing in small payments but had not settled, and a  
further sum of perhaps forty pounds owed to a supplier of seed corn who has  
written twice this year. I mention these not to alarm you but because I think a  
complete account should include them, and because the decision about how to  
address them will be more easily made if they are acknowledged.
I am also concerned about Charles's allowance. I do not say this to make difficulties  
for my brother, whom I love, but to note that an allowance of one hundred and  
twenty pounds per annum paid to a young man in London who has demonstrated no  
capacity for limiting his expenditure to his income is an allowance that will not  
remain at one hundred and twenty pounds. My father's correspondence, which I  
have been sorting since his death, contains three letters from Charles requesting  
advances against future allowances. None of these advances are reflected in the  
estate accounts.
I should be grateful if we could speak at your earliest convenience. I am aware that  
the management of the estate is George's responsibility and not mine, but I believe  
George would be better placed to manage it with accurate information than  
without, and I confess I am not certain that he has all the information he needs.
I remain, Sir, your obedient servant, Augusta Hale.
Mr.  Fellowes  replied  two  days  later,  confirmed  both  debts,  expressed  his  
appreciation of her thoroughness, and agreed to arrange a meeting at Harton Hall  
the following week at which both George and Augusta would be present. George,  
informed of the meeting, asked what it was about. Augusta told him. George said he  
had supposed things were tolerably in order. Augusta said she did not think they  
were entirely in order and thought he should understand them before they became  
less in order. George said he would attend the meeting.
Mr. Fellowes attended with his junior partner, a man named Alderton who would  
later become relevant to events, and the meeting lasted three hours. Augusta spoke  
for approximately half of it. George, to his credit, listened.
    ❧ ❧ ❧



II
The Entail Explained
Shrewsbury and Harton Hall, 1806
George Hale managed the estate for eleven years after their father's death with  
a dogged competence that slowly reduced the deficit and a personal life that  
remained, until his thirty-sixth year, resolutely and to his family's increasing concern  
unmarried. This was not, as far as Augusta could determine, the result of any  
disinclination  toward  marriage  in  principle;  it  was  the  result  of  a  specific  
disinclination toward the expense of maintaining a wife and the further expense of  
educating children, given the estate's finances. George had done his arithmetic and  
reached a conclusion that was prudent, solitary, and somewhat dispiriting.
In the spring of 1806, George contracted pneumonia following a week spent  
supervising the drainage of the lower field in unseasonable rain. He was ill for three  
weeks, during which Augusta managed the household and the estate with the  
assistance of Fellowes and Alderton. He recovered, but the recovery was slow and  
incomplete, and it left him with a persistent cough and a newly urgent awareness  
that if he died without issue, the entail would carry the estate to Charles.
Charles, who had in the intervening years accumulated debts in London that  
were not precisely known but were not, by any estimate, small, and who had  
demonstrated  a  consistent  and  total  inability  to  operate  within  any  financial  
constraint whatsoever, was the last person in England to whom George Hale wished  
to leave Harton Hall.
It was at this point that Augusta sat down with Mr. Fellowes and asked him to  
explain, slowly and in plain terms, exactly what the entail said and what it meant.
❧
MEMORANDUM ON THE ENTAIL OF HARTON HALL,  
PREPARED BY FELLOWES AND MARSH, SOLICITORS,  
SHREWSBURY — APRIL, 1806

At the request of Miss Augusta Hale, we set out below our understanding of the  
entail as it affects the estate of Harton Hall, in the county of Shropshire.
The estate of Harton Hall was purchased by Sir Edward Hale in 1782, subject to a  
pre-existing entail in tail male strict settlement, which provides that the estate  
shall pass to the nearest male heir in the direct line, failing which to the nearest  
male heir in the collateral line, with remainder to any surviving daughters only in  
the complete absence of any male heir whatsoever.
In plain terms: the estate passes from Sir George Hale to his sons, if he has sons;  
failing sons, to Mr. Charles Hale; failing Mr. Charles Hale, to any sons of Mr.  
Charles Hale; and so on through the male line to the most distant collateral male  
descendant before it would pass to any female heir.
The estate cannot be sold, mortgaged beyond its current encumbrance, or settled  
in any other direction without a Private Act of Parliament to bar the entail, which  
is a process both costly and uncertain in its outcome.
We note, with respect, that Miss Hale's question about whether the entail might  
be broken by mutual agreement between the life tenant (Sir George) and the heir  
presumptive (Mr. Charles) is technically correct: a tenant in tail and the next  
remainder man may, by a legal process called a common recovery, dock the entail  
and vest the fee simple in the tenant in tail absolutely. This would require Mr.  
Charles Hale's co-operation and legal advice of the highest quality. It would also  
require Mr. Charles Hale to give up his reversionary interest, which we would not  
anticipate him doing without very considerable inducement.
We hope this is of assistance. We are, of course, available to discuss the matter  
further at any time convenient to Miss Hale and Sir George.
Augusta read the memorandum three times. Then she walked to the window and  
stood looking at the park for some time. Then she wrote to Charles.
Augusta Hale to her brother Charles Hale, London — April, 1806
Charles, I write on a matter of some delicacy and I ask you to read this letter  
carefully before you reply, and not to reply until you have read it at least twice.
You know, I think, the state of the entail on Harton Hall. You stand to inherit it if  
George dies without male issue, which at present he may well do, he being  
unmarried and his health this spring having been indifferent. I do not say this to

distress you or to create expectations, but because you should understand your  
position clearly.
I write to propose the following. George is willing — I have spoken to him — to seek  
legal advice about the possibility of docking the entail if you would agree to  
participate in the necessary legal process. In exchange for your co-operation in this,  
and for your agreement to relinquish your reversionary interest in the estate,  
George would undertake to settle on you the sum of one thousand five hundred  
pounds, which represents a compromise between what the estate can raise and  
what your co-operation is worth to us. The estate would then be his to dispose of as  
he thinks fit, unencumbered by the entail.
I will tell you plainly what I think. I think you will never manage this estate. I say  
this without unkindness — I know your qualities and I value them, and the  
management of a Shropshire farm is not among them, nor should it be. If you inherit  
Harton Hall the entail will have achieved nothing except the transfer of a modestly  
profitable estate to a man who will make it unprofitable and ultimately lose it, and  
neither you nor the estate will have been served by the process. One thousand five  
hundred pounds, wisely invested, could change the shape of your life. I know it is  
not the inheritance. I ask you to consider whether it is not the better thing.
Your affectionate sister, who remains, as ever, your ally, Augusta.
Charles did not reply for three weeks. When he did reply, the letter was short and  
contained a number that was rather larger than one thousand five hundred pounds.  
Augusta wrote back with a number that was slightly larger than one thousand five  
hundred pounds. They corresponded in this manner for six weeks before arriving at  
a figure of two thousand pounds, which was approximately what Augusta had  
expected all along and rather more than the estate could comfortably raise at once.
George, informed of the negotiated sum, said he supposed Augusta had handled  
it well. Augusta said she thought they had all handled it tolerably. Mr. Fellowes,  
informed of the outcome, wrote a letter in which the restraint of his professional  
language barely contained what appeared to be genuine admiration.
    ❧ ❧ ❧

III
Charles in London
London and Shropshire, 1806–1812
Charles Hale took his two thousand pounds and returned to London with the  
expression of a man who considered he had been rather cleverly managed but was  
not entirely sure by whom. He was thirty-four years old and had the manner —  
handsome,  easy,  slightly  too  well-dressed  for  his  income  —  of  a  man  whose  
intelligence had always found its most natural expression in the avoidance of difficult  
things.
He was not a bad man. Augusta's letters about him are invariably affectionate, in  
the particular way that affection survives a clear-eyed understanding of someone's  
weaknesses. He was funny, occasionally perceptive, genuinely kind in the easy way  
of people who have never been required to be kind in the hard way, and capable of  
occasional surprising acts of generosity toward people who had no claim on him.  
What he was not capable of was sustained application to anything that did not  
immediately  interest  him,  and  Shropshire  agriculture  had  never  immediately  
interested him, and two thousand pounds — however wisely Augusta advised him to  
invest it — was not, in London in 1806, as much money as it might have been.
He did invest some of it, wisely enough, in Consols. He spent some of it less  
wisely. He made a friend named Carrington — a man in a not entirely different  
position to his own, with connections in the City and an appetite for risk — and  
through Carrington he became involved in a scheme that had something to do with  
the provisioning of the army in the Peninsula and something to do with contracts for  
woollen cloth, the details of which shifted each time Charles described them but  
which appeared, by 1809, to be going rather well.
Augusta, receiving Charles's accounts of the Carrington scheme in his letters,  
noted several discrepancies between the scheme as described in November and the  
scheme  as  described  in  March  and  wrote  him  a  careful  letter  asking  which  
description was accurate. Charles replied that both were accurate and that he was  
simply describing different aspects of the same arrangement. Augusta wrote back  
that she would be glad to understand the accounts, if he could send them. Charles  
replied that the accounts were with Carrington. Augusta did not reply to this.

Augusta Hale — her private journal, March 1809
I have written to Charles three times about the Carrington scheme and received  
three answers that are, individually, perfectly coherent and, collectively, impossible  
to reconcile. I do not know whether this is because Charles does not understand the  
scheme himself — which is possible — or because he understands it rather too well  
and does not wish me to understand it — which is also possible. I have decided to  
assume the former, for the sake of my own composure.
George is better this spring. He has begun to speak of Miss Caldwell in Ludlow in  
terms that suggest he considers the subject less remote than he previously did. I  
hope he will act on this with more expedition than he has previously brought to the  
question. He is thirty-nine years old and Miss Caldwell is, by all accounts, a sensible  
woman who would not object to an estate in Shropshire if the master of it were  
adequately presented.
I have been reading Mr. Cobbett's Political Register. I do not agree with everything  
he says, but I find his directness about the disproportion between the condition of  
the labouring poor and the condition of those who own their labour rather bracing.  
My father would have considered him seditious. I consider him observant.
George Hale did, in the event, act with rather more expedition than Augusta had  
expected. He called on Miss Caldwell in April 1809, proposed to her in May, and  
married her in September. She was twenty-eight years old, the daughter of a Ludlow  
solicitor, and possessed precisely the qualities that George had been avoiding for  
fifteen  years  on  the  grounds  of  expense:  good  sense,  good  humour,  and  an  
unromantic clarity about the difference between what they had and what they might  
reasonably hope for.
Her name was Eleanor. Augusta liked her immediately and said so, which was  
not something Augusta said carelessly.
Eleanor Hale — née Caldwell — became, within a year of her marriage, as  
familiar with the Harton Hall accounts as her sister-in-law. This was not because  
Augusta pressed the information on her, but because Eleanor asked for it, going  
through the ledgers with the same methodical interest that Augusta had brought to  
them fourteen years earlier. The two women worked together on the estate's  
finances  for  the  next  twenty  years,  and  their  correspondence  —  of  which  a  
substantial portion survives — constitutes one of the most remarkable records of  
domestic economic management in the period.

❧ ❧ ❧

IV
The War Years and the Debt
Shropshire, 1810–1820
The years of the Napoleonic Wars brought to Harton Hall what they brought to  
most agricultural estates in England: sharply rising grain prices, which increased  
the estate's income; sharply rising costs, which consumed most of the increase; and  
a general atmosphere of national crisis that made the management of a Shropshire  
estate feel both urgently necessary and oddly irrelevant, depending on the week.
George and Eleanor's first child, a daughter named Margaret, was born in 1810.  
Their second, a son named Edward, was born in 1812 and died at three days old.  
Their third, another daughter named Harriet, was born in 1814. Their fourth was a  
son, named George after his father, born in 1816, who lived and thrived and  
constituted, in his parents' careful private accounting, the resolution of the question  
that had threatened to reopen the matter of the entail.
The birth of George the younger was the occasion of a letter from Augusta to  
Charles that contained, for the first time in their extensive correspondence, a  
sentence that could be read as gloating. Augusta immediately recognised it as such,  
crossed it out, and replaced it with a sentence expressing her genuine happiness at  
the birth of a healthy nephew. Both sentences were preserved in Charles's papers,  
the crossing-out being imperfect, and both appear in the archive.
Augusta Hale to Charles Hale — October, 1816
Dear Charles, George and Eleanor have a son, born the twelfth of this month and  
named George Edward Hale. He is, Eleanor writes, a very good-humoured child,  
which I take to be the most useful quality a person can begin life with. George is in a  
state of happiness that I have not seen in him for some years and which is pleasant  
to behold.
I trust you will join me in wishing the family all health and happiness. I also trust —  
and I say this as your sister who has had occasion to think about these matters  
rather more than most — that the arrival of an heir will occasion in you the same  
quiet satisfaction that it occasions in me: the sense that a thing which was in danger  
of going wrong has been prevented from doing so, largely by the application of

considerable effort on the part of several people over a considerable number of  
years.
I will not say more than that. You know what I mean. I believe you know also that  
the two thousand pounds was the right thing, and I hope you have found it so.
Your affectionate sister, Augusta.
Charles's reply, which arrived two weeks later, said only: Yes. I know what you  
mean. I am glad the boy is well. The two thousand pounds was largely the right thing,  
though not entirely in the way you intended. I will explain when I see you, which I  
hope will be at Christmas if the roads are not impossible.
Charles came for Christmas in 1816. The explanation of what had become of the  
two thousand pounds occupied the better part of an evening and involved the  
Carrington scheme, its eventual and not entirely unexpected failure in 1811, a period  
of considerable difficulty, and a recovery that depended substantially on the very  
Consols investment that Augusta had originally recommended. Charles had, in other  
words, spent several years arriving back at the starting position.
Augusta listened to all of this with the expression that Eleanor later described as  
her translating face: the face she wore when converting what someone was actually  
saying into what they meant.
When Charles had finished she said: So you are comfortable now. Charles said he  
was tolerably comfortable. Augusta said she was glad of it. They drank their tea and  
moved on to other subjects, and the financial history of the two thousand pounds was  
never discussed again.
THE SHREWSBURY CHRONICLE
Saturday, 22nd June, 1815
THE GREAT VICTORY AT WATERLOO — NAPOLEON DECISIVELY DEFEATED
We have the honour to announce to our readers the most complete and glorious  
victory obtained by the Allied Forces under the command of His Grace the Duke of  
Wellington over the army of the usurper Bonaparte on the field of Waterloo in  
Belgium, on the eighteenth instant.
The battle, which lasted from eleven o'clock in the morning until near ten at  
night, resulted in the total defeat and dispersal of the French army, with  
immense loss of cannon, standards, and prisoners. The Duke of Wellington, in his

dispatch to Lord Bathurst, describes the action as the most desperate he has  
ever been engaged in, and states that the co-operation of the Prussian forces  
under Field-Marshal Blucher was decisive in its outcome.
The casualties on the British side are reported to be severe. The full lists are  
not yet available, but it is understood that several regiments suffered greatly,  
particularly in the final assault on the French position.
This newspaper joins with all loyal subjects in giving thanks to Almighty God  
for this deliverance, and trusts that the conclusion of twenty-two years of war  
will bring to the agriculture and trade of this county and kingdom that settled  
prosperity which the uncertain times have too long denied.
Editor's note — The copy of the Shrewsbury Chronicle above was found folded inside the deed-
box at Harton Hall when Sir Augustus Frere examined the archive in 1884. In the margin, in a 
hand identified as Augusta Hale's, the following annotation appears: George is not on the list. 
Eleanor wept. I did not. Twenty-two years.
The end of the Napoleonic Wars brought to Harton Hall, as to much of English  
agriculture, a period of adjustment rather than celebration. The wartime grain  
prices that had underpinned the estate's gradual recovery fell sharply as European  
harvests recovered and the artificial premium of wartime demand disappeared. By  
1818, the estate was again running at a deficit, though a smaller one than in 1795. By  
1820 it was breaking even, more or less, which was the best that could be said for  
most of the agricultural estates of Shropshire.
Augusta was forty-five years old in 1820. She had never married — not for want  
of opportunity; there had been two proposals, which she had declined, one in 1802  
and one in 1809, both from men who were in various ways admirable and both of  
whom she had declined because she could see, with the clear-eyed precision that she  
brought to everything, that the marriage would be less than the sum of its parts. She  
lived at Harton Hall, managed what needed managing, corresponded extensively  
with Eleanor about the estate and with a wider circle of friends and acquaintances  
about  everything  else,  and  was  by  general  agreement  the  most  intellectually  
formidable person in a radius of fifteen miles.
    ❧ ❧ ❧

V
The Question of Augusta
Harton Hall, 1820–1828
The question of what Augusta Hale was to do with the rest of her life presented  
itself with increasing urgency as she entered her fifties. It was not a question that  
she herself raised — she was not a woman much given to self-pity, and she had  
constructed a life of considerable intellectual and practical richness within the  
constraints available to her — but it was a question that George raised, increasingly,  
as the decade progressed and the estate's finances stabilised and it became possible  
to think about the future without the immediate pressure of deficit accounting.
The question was, specifically, this: Harton Hall was George's to dispose of now  
that the entail had been broken, and he intended to leave it to his son George, which  
was entirely proper and appropriate. Augusta had no legal claim on it. Her allowance  
— thirty pounds per annum, unchanged since 1795 — was, by the 1820s, a sum that  
barely covered her personal expenses in a house that was not hers and whose  
upkeep she had managed for twenty-five years without payment. George was aware  
of this. Eleanor, who understood money rather better than George did, was more  
acutely aware of it.
The solution that George proposed, and that Augusta accepted with a precision  
that indicated she had already thought it through and arrived at the same conclusion  
by a different route, was this: that the allowance be increased to seventy-five pounds  
per annum, and that the house in which Augusta's great-grandmother had lived — a  
cottage on the estate boundary, four rooms, a garden, rented at present for twelve  
pounds per annum — be made over to Augusta for her lifetime, rent-free, with a right  
of first refusal to purchase it at a favourable price should the estate ever be sold.
Augusta accepted these terms. She also accepted, without being asked, the  
ongoing management of the estate accounts, which Eleanor had begun and which  
Augusta had been doing in parallel since 1810, each woman checking the other's  
work with a collaborative precision that Mr. Alderton — Fellowes having retired in  
1815 — found both extremely helpful and very slightly alarming.
Augusta Hale — her private journal, March 1822

I am forty-seven years old and I live in a cottage that is not mine on an estate that is  
not mine and I have thirty pounds a year and the management of two hundred and  
twelve acres which is not my management because the estate is not mine. I am  
aware of the comic dimensions of this situation and I would be able to appreciate  
them more fully if they were not also my life.
I do not say this in self-pity. I say it because I think it is worth saying plainly to  
oneself, from time to time, rather than allowing the daily management of the  
situation to become a substitute for an assessment of it. My situation is what it is. I  
did not make it. The law made it, and the law was not made by women, and it does  
not much concern itself with women except in the negative: what they may not  
inherit, what they may not own, what they may not decide.
Nevertheless. The cottage is comfortable. The garden is better than I expected.  
Eleanor is my friend in a way that I did not anticipate when she first arrived here and  
which I value more than I am generally able to say. George is George: steady, kind,  
limited in imagination, and constitutionally unable to perceive the irony of the  
situation, which is perhaps a mercy.
Young George is twelve years old and asked me last week what an entail was. I told  
him. He thought about it for some time and then said: that seems rather unfair to the  
daughters. I said: yes, it does rather. He said: would it not be simpler to leave the  
estate to whoever is best suited to manage it? I said: yes, it would. He said: why do  
they not do that then? I said: I do not entirely know, and the answer involves the  
history of property law in England, which I will explain when you are somewhat  
older. He said: I should like that. I thought: you are, at twelve, more satisfactory  
than most men I have known at forty.
Young George Hale — George Edward, as the family called him to distinguish  
him  from  his  father  —  grew  up  at  Harton  Hall  in  the  particular  educational  
environment that Augusta had constructed around him without quite intending to: a  
household in which two women of considerable intelligence were engaged in the  
practical management of a real estate, debating its accounts and its decisions in  
terms that did not condescend to him, expecting him to understand the reasoning  
and not merely the conclusions.
He went to Rugby in 1822 and to Oxford in 1826. He came back to Harton Hall in  
1828 with a degree in classics, an interest in the law of property, and a somewhat  
more sophisticated understanding of the entail question than his twelve-year-old self  
had been offered. He also came back with an acquaintance — made in his first year at

Oxford — who would become relevant to the family's history in ways that none of  
them could have anticipated in 1828.
The acquaintance's name was Alderton. Henry Alderton: the son of the same Mr.  
Alderton who had been Fellowes's junior partner, and who now ran Fellowes and  
Marsh alone. He was, by all accounts and by the evidence of the correspondence he  
subsequently generated, an exceptionally able solicitor and a thoroughly decent  
man, and he had the additional quality — uncommon in the period — of finding  
women's intelligence interesting rather than threatening.
He met Augusta Hale for the first time in the autumn of 1828, at Harton Hall,  
when he came to discuss the estate accounts with George. Augusta was present at  
the meeting, as she always was. He addressed her with the same respect he  
extended to George, which was unusual. She noticed that it was unusual. He noticed  
that she noticed. The meeting lasted four hours and covered everything that needed  
covering, and at the end of it Henry Alderton rode back to Shrewsbury thinking, by  
his own later account, that he had seldom spent four hours more usefully.
    ❧ ❧ ❧

VI
Henry Alderton
Shrewsbury and Harton Hall, 1828–1832
Henry Alderton was thirty-two years old when he met Augusta Hale. He was the  
son of a solicitor, the partner of a solicitor's firm, and the grandson — on his mother's  
side — of a man who had made a reasonable amount of money in the West India trade  
and spent it largely on his children's education. He had been at Rugby before Oxford,  
which is how young George had known him, and he had come back from Oxford with  
the kind of legal intelligence that finds its expression not in argument but in  
arrangement: the quiet art of setting things in order so that the argument never  
needs to happen.
He was not handsome in the way that the period required handsomeness to be;  
he was something more useful, which was competent: competent in his person, his  
dress, his conversation, and his work, without any of the excess in any direction that  
the Regency encouraged and that Augusta Hale had always found rather wearing.  
He was also funny, in a way that Augusta did not immediately identify because it was  
delivered so drily that it was possible to miss it on the first pass.
The letters between them — which begin in December 1828, ostensibly on  
matters of estate business, and which very gradually cease to be ostensibly about  
matters of estate business — are the most intimate documents in the Hale archive  
since the Civil War brothers' correspondence, and for similar reasons: they are  
letters written by two people of considerable intelligence who have found in each  
other something they were not expecting to find, and who are navigating the  
discovery with the mutual wariness of people who have been managing without such  
discoveries for a long time.
Henry Alderton to Augusta Hale — December, 1828
Dear Miss Hale, I write to confirm the arrangement we discussed regarding the  
timber lease on the South Wood, which I have now drafted and which I enclose for  
your consideration and Sir George's approval. The terms are, I believe, as we  
agreed: twenty years, renewable, at a rent that reflects the current market rate for  
mature oak of that quality while preserving the right of the estate to direct the

management of the standing timber.
I also write — and I hope you will permit this slight departure from the strictly  
professional — to say that our conversation on Tuesday regarding the Question of  
the Settled Estates Acts was among the most interesting I have had this year, which  
is not an entirely professional observation. Your analysis of the disproportion  
between the legal intent of the strict settlement and its actual effect on the  
management of estates the size of Harton Hall was, if I may say so, rather more  
sophisticated than most of what is published on the subject, which says something  
either  about  the  published  literature  or  about  the  legal  education  currently  
available to women, and I suspect rather more about the latter.
I would be glad to continue the conversation, either on a subsequent visit to Harton  
Hall or — if you are ever in Shrewsbury on other business — at our offices.
I remain, Miss Hale, your obedient servant, Henry Alderton.
Augusta Hale to Henry Alderton — December, 1828
Dear Mr. Alderton, I am pleased to confirm that the terms of the timber lease are  
satisfactory. I have shown it to Sir George, who read it with the attention it deserves  
— which is to say, he said it looked perfectly in order and signed it.
Your observation about the literature on settled estates is kind, and I will receive it  
as such rather than examining it too closely. What I would say in response is that  
the disproportion you identify is not, in my experience, the result of any particular  
malice on the part of the legislature. It is the result of the legislature's habitual  
failure to consider whether the people most directly affected by its arrangements  
have any interest in their design. This is a large subject and perhaps not one for a  
letter about a timber lease.
I would, however, be glad to continue the conversation. I am in Shrewsbury the  
third week of January on a matter unconnected with the estate. If you are free on  
the Wednesday of that week I should be happy to call at your offices.
I remain, Mr. Alderton, yours faithfully, Augusta Hale.
The meeting on the Wednesday of the third week of January lasted considerably  
longer  than  either  party  had  scheduled.  By  March,  the  correspondence  had  
increased  in  frequency  and  had  almost  entirely  ceased  to  discuss  the  estate  
accounts. By June, Henry Alderton had called at Harton Hall twice on his own

initiative rather than on the estate's, and Augusta had introduced him to Eleanor  
with a level of deliberateness that Eleanor immediately understood and George did  
not.
George, when informed by Eleanor in August that his sister and his solicitor  
appeared to be developing an attachment, was surprised. Eleanor said she did not  
know why he was surprised. George said he supposed he had thought Augusta — he  
did not finish the sentence. Eleanor said: what? George said: settled. Eleanor said:  
she is settled. She is also fifty-three years old and has been managing this estate for  
twenty-eight years without payment or recognition, and if she has found a person  
whose company she values I should think the appropriate response was pleasure  
rather than surprise.
George said he was pleased. He was. He was also, though he did not say this,  
slightly unnerved by the possibility that Augusta might leave Harton Hall, which had  
not occurred to him as a possibility before and which he now recognised as the thing  
he had been unconsciously assuming would not happen.
    ❧ ❧ ❧

VII
The Settlement
Harton Hall and Shrewsbury, 1832–1835
Henry Alderton proposed to Augusta Hale in the spring of 1832. He was thirty-
six. She was fifty-seven. The gap was twenty-one years, which was rather more than  
the  conventions  of  the  period  considered  normal,  though  not  more  than  they  
considered legal. Augusta, who had by this point spent four years receiving the  
attentions of a man who made no secret of the fact that he found her mind more  
interesting than any drawing-room accomplishment she might have offered him in  
1800, said yes.
She said yes with the particular quality of decisiveness that she brought to  
everything: not the breathless assent of a young woman who has been waiting for  
permission to begin her life, but the considered agreement of a woman who has been  
living her life for fifty-seven years and has identified, with the same precision she  
brought to an estate account, exactly what she is gaining and what she is accepting.  
She was gaining a companion and an intellectual equal and a house that was her  
own. She was accepting the social exposure of an irregular match and the occasional  
awkwardness of an age difference that would sometimes be remarked upon. She had  
done the arithmetic.
MEMORANDUM OF MARRIAGE SETTLEMENT — HENRY ALDERTON,  
SOLICITOR, AND MISS AUGUSTA HALE — PREPARED BY  
CROWTHER AND SONS, SOLICITORS, SHREWSBURY, ACTING FOR  
MISS HALE — JUNE, 1832
The following sets out the principal terms of the proposed marriage settlement  
between  Mr.  Henry  Alderton  and  Miss  Augusta  Hale,  agreed  between  the  
respective solicitors following negotiation.
Miss Hale brings to the marriage: her personal effects; the cottage at Harton Hall  
and its contents, held under a life interest conferred by Sir George Hale in 1820;  
her  personal  capital  of  approximately  four  hundred  and  twenty  pounds,  
representing  accumulated  savings  from  her  allowance  and  sundry  small

inheritances; and the goodwill of the estate management arrangements at Harton  
Hall, which have been conducted by Miss Hale for thirty-seven years and which, by  
mutual agreement between Sir George Hale and Mr. Alderton, will continue under  
the same informal arrangement after the marriage.
Mr. Alderton brings to the marriage: his interest in the firm of Fellowes and Marsh  
(now Alderton and Co.); his personal capital, the amount of which is not disclosed  
herein but is stated to be adequate for the maintenance of a household of modest  
comfort; and the property at Number 14 Belmont, Shrewsbury, which is his own  
freehold.
It is agreed that Miss Hale's personal capital shall be settled on herself absolutely,  
not passing under her husband's control, by means of a trust deed to be executed  
simultaneously with the marriage settlement. This arrangement, proposed by Mr.  
Alderton and consented to by Miss Hale, is noted here as unusual but not, in the  
opinion of Crowther and Sons, unprecedented.
Mr. Alderton has expressed, in correspondence with Crowther and Sons, the view  
that a woman of Miss Hale's capacity who has managed a considerable estate  
without legal authority for thirty-seven years ought at minimum to be permitted to  
manage her own capital, and that any settlement which failed to reflect this would  
be, in his word, absurd. Crowther and Sons record this view without comment, as  
it is not their place to comment on a client's settled convictions.
They were married in September 1832 at the church of St. Mary's in Shrewsbury  
— not St. Wulfstan's in Halecroft, where every Hale since the dissolution had been  
married, but in the town where Augusta had chosen to make her new life. George  
gave her away. Eleanor wept. Charles, who had come from London for the occasion  
and was thinner and slightly greyer but not essentially different, made a speech that  
was entirely characteristic: funny, warm, and slightly too long, containing one story  
that nobody present could verify and that Augusta later said was not quite what had  
happened but was better than what had happened and therefore preferable.
Young  George  Edward,  who  was  sixteen,  watched  everything  with  the  
attentiveness he had learned from his aunt and said very little, which was also  
something he had learned from his aunt.
Augusta Alderton, née Hale — her private journal, September 1832

I am married. I am fifty-seven years old and I am married and I live in a house that is  
mine and I have a husband who thinks I am more interesting than a drawing-room  
accomplishment, which is not the most romantic thing that has ever been said of a  
woman but is, I believe, rather more durable.
I have been thinking of Matilda. I have read her letter, which was with the papers I  
brought from Harton Hall, and I have thought about what she said: keep asking  
whether you have done enough. I think I have asked. I am not certain I have  
answered. I think that may be the point.
The house is comfortable. The garden requires attention. Henry is asleep in the  
chair by the fire because it has been a very long day. I am content.
Augusta Alderton continued to manage the Harton Hall accounts until 1839,  
when George Edward took over from his father and the arrangement naturally  
concluded. She lived at Number 14 Belmont until Henry Alderton's death in 1847,  
and then in the cottage at Harton Hall — which passed to her outright on George's  
death in 1848 — until her own death in 1858, aged eighty-three. She outlived her  
brother Charles, her brother George, her sister-in-law Eleanor, and a great many  
people who had considered themselves the important persons in the room when she  
was present.
She left behind, at her death, the private journal she had kept since 1802 and the  
full run of her correspondence with Henry Alderton, which she had preserved with  
the same methodical care she had brought to the estate accounts. Sir Augustus  
Frere, examining these papers in 1884, wrote in his catalogue that Augusta Hale —  
latterly Augusta Alderton — was, of all the Hales he had encountered in the archive,  
the one whose intelligence would have been most recognisable to the present age,  
and that the accident of her sex and her period had cost her whatever she would have  
been given the full range of the century's possibilities. This is a fair judgment. What  
it perhaps underestimates is what she actually was, given the possibilities she had.
    ❧ ❧ ❧

VIII
On the English of the Regency
A Note on the Language of this Volume
The English of the early nineteenth century is the most lethal instrument of irony  
the language has ever produced, and it achieved this distinction by a process of  
deliberate refinement. Jane Austen — born 1775, the same year as Augusta Hale, in  
Hampshire rather than Shropshire, and therefore occupying a remarkably similar  
social position — spent her working life developing the techniques by which an  
English sentence could simultaneously say two things: what its speaker chose to  
present to the world, and what the world actually was. This is the English that  
Augusta Hale writes. She learned it from the same sources Austen did: from the  
letter-writing conventions of the eighteenth century, from Samuel Johnson's prose,  
from the everyday English of a woman who had spent her life managing the gap  
between what was said and what was meant.
When Augusta writes to Mr. Fellowes in 1795 that the account presents a picture  
rather more favourable than the circumstances of the household warrant, she is  
being precise and polite and she is also being devastating. When she writes to  
Charles that the two thousand pounds was the right thing, though not entirely in the  
way you intended, she is forgiving him and reminding him of the debt of gratitude  
simultaneously, in a single subordinate clause. When she writes in her diary that she  
is fifty-seven years old and married and has a husband who thinks she is more  
interesting than a drawing-room accomplishment, and notes that this is not the most  
romantic thing that has ever been said of a woman but is rather more durable — she  
is performing the Regency's characteristic trick of using understatement as a form of  
emotional intensity. The sentence feels light. It is not light.
This is the English that William Hazlitt was writing — his essays, his criticism, his  
long dissections of personality and motive — at the same time Augusta was writing  
her letters. It is the English that Thomas De Quincey was writing, and that Sydney  
Smith was writing, and that the Edinburgh Review was writing. It is English that  
knows its own power and holds that power lightly, which is perhaps the most  
sophisticated trick available to any instrument of expression.
It is also the English that will, a generation later, produce the Victorian manner:  
the ornamentation, the periodic sentence, the elaborate subordinate clause. Edmund

Hale — Augusta's great-great-nephew, the subject of Book VII — writes letters that  
are a direct and lineal descendant of Augusta's, but with all the lightness weighed  
down. The irony has not disappeared; it has become more elaborate, more defended,  
more conscious of itself. The Regency's English trusted that the reader would  
understand. The Victorian's English could not quite trust this, and supplied more  
structure, more subordination, more apparatus. The century's anxiety about its own  
certainties — about religion, about class, about the performance of respectability —  
is written into the grammar.
Augusta  Hale  never  had  those  anxieties.  Or  rather,  she  had  rather  more  
fundamental anxieties — about money, about the law, about what a woman was  
permitted to be — and found the Victorian preoccupations with respectability a  
somewhat distant concern from her cottage in Shropshire. She was born twelve  
years before the French Revolution and died seven years after the Great Exhibition,  
and she watched the entire transformation of the English world and its English  
language from a position of considerable attentiveness and very limited power, and  
she wrote about all of it with a precision that, had it been written by a man, would  
have been called formidable.
It was written by a woman. It is still formidable. The archive does not care about  
the difference.
    ❧ ❧ ❧

IX
The Deed-Box
Harton Hall, 1858
When Augusta Hale died in the spring of 1858, at the age of eighty-three, her  
effects at the Harton Hall cottage were catalogued by Mr. William Alderton —  
Henry's nephew, who had taken over the firm — and the relevant portions of the  
archive were transferred to young George Edward Hale, now fifty-two, who was by  
that point the effective head of the Hale family, his father having been dead for ten  
years.
Among the papers transferred was the deed-box. It had been at Harton Hall since  
Edward Hale had brought it from Worcester when he purchased the estate in 1782,  
and it had sat through sixty-seven years of Hale family management as the repository  
of everything that the family needed to prove about itself at law: the freehold  
instruments, the entail documents, the copy of the 1545 lease to William Hale from  
Richard Aldgate, the 1214 purchase deed, a fragment — very old, the vellum cracked  
— of what appeared to be a much earlier document in a hand that nobody at Harton  
Hall could read.
George Edward, cataloguing the papers with Mr. Alderton's assistance, reached  
into the deed-box to remove the documents and felt something under the lining on  
the left side: a slight stiffness, as if something had been folded there. He mentioned  
it to Mr. Alderton, who said he had noticed it before — his father had mentioned it —  
but had not disturbed it, since it was clearly not a contemporary document and its  
removal might damage it.
George Edward, who had been taught by his aunt to be thorough and careful and  
to look at what was there rather than what one expected to find, cut the lining with a  
small knife and removed the folded paper.
It was in very poor condition — fragile, brown, the ink faded to the edge of  
legibility. He spent two hours reading it, letter by letter, with a magnifying glass. The  
English was old; he could not date it precisely, though he thought it was not the  
English of his grandfather's time or his great-grandfather's. He copied it out in full,  
carefully, and placed the original back in the lining and repaired the tear.

What the paper said — its beginning, which is legible, and its ending, which is  
also legible, though the middle is damaged by water — George Edward copied as  
follows:
Copied in the hand of George Edward Hale, from a very old paper found in the  
deed-box lining — 1858
To whoever shall read this after I am gone, which will be soon enough:
I have lived in this village since before any man in this room was born... [the paper  
is damaged here, several lines illegible] ...and I have kept bees longer than anyone  
living can remember...
[further damage: approximately twenty lines missing]
...a family that keeps asking whether it has done enough will keep the land. A family  
that stops asking will lose it, sooner or later, one way or another.
Keep asking. Keep the land. See to the pig.
Matilda. Her mark.
Transcribed faithfully, this 14th of April 1858, at Harton Hall, by George Edward  
Hale.
George Edward wrote beneath his transcription: I do not know who Matilda was.  
But I think she knew this family rather better than it knew itself. I shall add this  
paper to the archive.
He did. It is there still.
Sir Augustus Frere, finding it in 1884, immediately recognised the handwriting  
from his research into the medieval parish records. He identified the paper as almost  
certainly the work of the woman referred to in the 1351 inquest records and in the  
marginal notation of Thomas Hale's 1382 remembrance. He spent three pages of his  
catalogue describing his excitement at the discovery, and then wrote, at the bottom  
of the third page, in smaller letters than he had been using: She was right, of course.  
She generally was.
    ❧ ❧ ❧

GLOSSARY
Words, laws, and events which the text employs and the reader may wish explained
Entail / strict 
settlementA  legal  arrangement  by  which  land  is  tied  to  a  specific  line  of  
inheritance, typically the male line, and cannot be sold, mortgaged, or  
devised in any other direction by the current holder. The holder of an  
entailed estate is called a tenant in tail; they may use the estate's income  
but cannot dispose of the capital. The entail could be broken only by a  
legal process involving both the current tenant in tail and the next heir in  
remainder — or, from 1833, more easily under the Fines and Recoveries  
Act. The entail was the primary mechanism by which the English landed  
class maintained the concentration of land across generations, and it is  
the central legal fact around which the plots of most Regency-period  
novels — including those of Jane Austen — are constructed.
Consols Short for Consolidated Annuities: a form of British government bond  
introduced in 1751 that paid a fixed annual interest rate (typically three  
per cent) and had no repayment date, being in theory perpetual. They  
were considered the safest possible investment in the period and were  
widely  held  by  the  professional  and  upper-middle  classes  as  the  
foundation of a secure income. To 'invest in the Consols' was the  
Regency period's equivalent of putting money in the bank.
The Settled 
Estates ActsA series of statutes from the mid-eighteenth century governing the  
management and investment of land subject to strict settlement. They  
gave courts of equity the power to authorise certain actions — drainage,  
timber-cutting,  leasing  —  that  the  strict  letter  of  the  entail  would  
otherwise have prohibited, on the grounds that the estate as a whole was  
better  served  by  permitting  them.  Augusta  Hale's  analysis  of  the  
disproportion between the acts' intention and their effect in practice  
reflects a genuine legal debate of the period.
Common recovery  
/ FineTwo ancient legal fictions by which a tenant in tail and the next heir  
could jointly dock (i.e., break) an entail and convert the tenant's interest  
from fee tail to fee simple absolute, enabling the estate to be sold or  
devised freely. Both required the willing co-operation of the heir in  
remainder  —  hence  Augusta's  negotiation  with  Charles,  whose  
agreement was legally necessary and financially purchasable.
Marriage 
settlementA formal legal arrangement made prior to marriage, by which property  
belonging to each party was allocated, protected, or transferred. Under

the common law of the period, a woman's property passed automatically  
to her husband on marriage unless specifically protected by a trust  
established  in  the  settlement.  The  arrangement  Henry  Alderton  
proposed — settling Augusta's own capital on herself absolutely — was  
unusual precisely because it prevented his own legal acquisition of her  
assets, which the law would otherwise have permitted without any  
particular formality.
The Napoleonic 
Wars (1803–
1815)The series of conflicts between France under Napoleon Bonaparte and  
the various coalitions of European powers, ending with Napoleon's  
defeat at Waterloo in June 1815. For English agriculture, the wars  
created an artificial premium on grain prices (the Continental System  
disrupted European food supply, driving up prices in England) and a  
general atmosphere of patriotic urgency that made economic complaint  
seem unpatriotic. The peace, when it came, brought a sharp fall in grain  
prices  that  devastated  many  agricultural  estates  and  contributed  
directly to the political pressures that produced the Reform Act of 1832.
Jane Austen 
(1775–1817)Novelist,  Hampshire.  Contemporary  with  Augusta  Hale  within  two  
months of birth. Author of Sense and Sensibility, Pride and Prejudice,  
Mansfield Park, Emma, Northanger Abbey, and Persuasion. Her novels  
constitute the most precise account available of the social and economic  
world that Augusta Hale inhabited, though Austen set her work in  
Hampshire and Sussex rather than Shropshire, and her protagonists  
have  rather  more  opportunity  for  romantic  drama  than  Augusta's  
situation  provided.  The  entail  of  Longbourn  estate  in  Pride  and  
Prejudice, which threatens to deprive the Bennet daughters of their  
home in favour of a male cousin, is exactly the legal mechanism that  
Augusta resolved at Harton Hall in 1806 — and which Austen's Mrs.  
Bennet discusses at length, though with rather less analytical precision  
than Augusta brought to it.
The Reform Act 
of 1832The  Great  Reform  Act:  parliamentary  legislation  that  substantially  
reformed the electoral system of England and Wales, abolishing the  
rotten  boroughs  (constituencies  with  tiny  electorates  controlled  by  
single landowners), extending the franchise to include most middle-class  
men who owned property above a certain value, and redistributing  
parliamentary seats to reflect the new industrial cities. It was the first  
major step in the gradual democratisation of the British political system  
and the beginning of the end for the unreformed power of the landed  
gentry of which the Hale family was a minor example.

❧ ❧ ❧
END OF BOOK VI — THE HALE ENTAIL
The story continues in Book VII:
The Correspondence of Mr Edmund Hale
1868 — 1882

THE CORRESPONDENCE OF
Mr Edmund Hale
Being a Collection of Letters, Diary Entries, Legal Documents,
Newspaper Cuttings, and One Extraordinary Confession,
Arranged and Presented in Chronological Order Without Alteration
Edited and Introduced by
Sir Augustus Frere, K.C., F.S.A.
of the Inner Temple, London
London: Chapman & Hall, 1887

"The great corrupter of public man is the ego — looking at the world 
through the keyhole of ambition."
— Lord Acton, letter to Bishop Creighton, 1887
"Respectability is the first refuge of the scoundrel; the second is piety; the 
third, and most impregnable, is reputation."
— Anon., Moral Aphorisms, 1849



Editor's Preface
Being a brief account of the assembly of these documents and their provenance
It is with a particular and not entirely uncomplicated species of reluctance 
that I place before the public the papers which constitute this volume. That 
such a collection should be assembled at all is owing not to any prurient 
appetite for scandal upon my part, nor to the commercial calculations of my 
publisher, but to a conviction — arrived at slowly, and contested internally 
at every stage of the inquiry — that the documents herein contained possess  
a moral and instructive significance which, withheld from the general 
reader, would be a disservice both to the advancement of public 
understanding and to the memory of those individuals whose lives were 
most materially affected by the events they describe.
Edmund Rathbone Hale was born in the year 1841, the second son of a 
Worcestershire solicitor of modest but respectable station. He was educated  
at Winchester and subsequently at Balliol College, Oxford, where he read 
Greats with considerable distinction, proceeding to a first-class degree in 
the Hilary term of 1863. His subsequent entry into the legal profession, his 
rapid advancement within the chambers of Messrs. Fairweather and Sons of  
Lincoln's Inn, and his eventual appointment as a junior partner of that 
venerable establishment by the year 1871 — all this, in its broad outline, is 
a matter of public record and requires no extended rehearsal here. He held,  
by inheritance, the baronetcy created for his great-great-grandfather Sir 
Nathaniel Hale in 1726; he did not use the title in professional life, 
preferring the plain designation of Mr, as was not uncommon among 
barristers who wished their reputation to rest upon their work rather than 
their station.
What the public record does not contain — what no public record, by its 
very nature, is designed to accommodate — is the interior man. The man 
who composed, in the privacy of his study, the letters which follow. The man  
whose diary, recovered from a sealed escritoire in the Hale family estate at 
Thornfield, Shropshire, in the months following his disappearance in the 
autumn of 1882, presents a portrait so dissonant with the public figure as to  
occasion, in the reader unprepared for the encounter, something 
approaching genuine vertigo.
I have arranged these papers chronologically, from the earliest 
surviving letter of 1868 to the extraordinary document of October 1882, 
which the reader will find at the conclusion of this volume. I have added

footnotes only where the historical record requires elucidation, or where a 
document's significance may not be immediately apparent to the reader 
unacquainted with the principals. I have suppressed nothing. I have 
embellished nothing. The hand of the editor has been applied solely in the 
service of clarity and proportion.
What the reader makes of Edmund Hale — whether he is to be regarded  
as villain or victim, architect of his own destruction or creature of a society 
whose exacting codes of respectability made honesty an act of social suicide  
— I leave entirely to the individual conscience. I confess that my own 
judgment, after two years of intimate acquaintance with these papers, has 
not resolved itself into any comfortable verdict. I suspect it never will.
Augustus Frere
Inner Temple, London
January, 1887

PART THE FIRST
The Ascent
1868 – 1874
— i —
14 Grosvenor Square, London W.
12th March, 1868
My Dear Whitmore,
You will, I trust, pardon the egregious delay in my reply to 
your last, which has sat upon my desk these three weeks in a 
condition of accusatory reproach that I confess I have found it 
easier to avert my eyes from than to remedy. The defect is, as 
ever, not of affection but of time — or rather, of the peculiar 
tyranny which the profession of the law visits upon those who have  
elected to pursue it with any degree of seriousness. Fairweather 
keeps me at the grindstone from eight in the morning until the 
candles are quite spent, and I am beginning to suspect that the 
man sleeps in his chambers, if he sleeps at all.
But to your question. You ask whether I have made the 
acquaintance of the Viscount Ashbourne and whether the 
connexion might be serviceable. I have, and it may be. Ashbourne 
is a man of considerable property and considerably less 
intelligence than he supposes himself to possess — a combination 
which, in my experience, is among the most profitable of 
arrangements for those who deal with such individuals in a 
professional capacity. He has, moreover, a daughter, Miss 
Adelaide Ashbourne, who is accounted both handsome and 
sensible, and who appears to possess the singular virtue, among 
young women of her station, of not saying everything she thinks. I 
have been introduced. The introduction went, I believe, well.
Do not, my dear Whitmore, allow your imagination to construct  
any romantic architecture upon so slender a foundation. I am five-
and-twenty, a junior man of no independent income and uncertain 
prospects, and Miss Adelaide Ashbourne is the only daughter of a 
viscount. These are facts; and facts, as our old Balliol tutor was

used to observe, are the only truly incorruptible currency.
Nevertheless, I intend to be present at Lady Hartley's musical 
evening next Thursday, where I understand the Ashbournes are 
also expected. It is not romantic architecture; it is professional 
strategy. There is, I have discovered, a great deal of overlap 
between the two.
Your most faithful, if tardy, correspondent,
Edmund R. Hale
— ii —
Private Memoranda of E.R.H. — Thursday, 19th March, 1868
Lady Hartley's. Adelaide Ashbourne. Green silk. She plays Schumann  
with precision and without feeling, which is precisely how she 
converses. I find this quality, which others might consider a defect, 
to be in fact a considerable relief after the suffocating emotionalism 
of Miss Pemberton (whom I continue to see on Wednesdays, from 
necessity rather than inclination, as her brother controls three 
valuable briefs which Fairweather is particularly anxious to retain).
The Viscount Ashbourne spoke to me for twenty minutes about the 
Irish Question, during which interval I maintained an expression of 
concentrated interest whilst thinking principally about the Merton 
estate conveyancing and whether Fairweather has noticed that I 
have been conducting rather more of it than is strictly within my 
remit as junior man. He has not noticed. He rarely notices anything 
that does not present itself in the form of a billable hour.
I spoke with Miss Adelaide for seven minutes, during which she said 
four things of substance and I said five. I consider this ratio 
satisfactory. She has very steady grey eyes and the particular quality 
of attention that suggests she is listening to what one says rather 
than constructing one's own next observation upon the skeleton of it.  
This quality is, in my experience, exceedingly rare.
I shall see her again. This requires arranging, but the arrangement 
presents no insuperable difficulty, as I have observed that Lady 
Hartley holds these musical evenings on the first and third Thursday 
of each month, and that the Ashbournes are regular attendants. 
Information of this kind is the foundation upon which all subsequent 
architecture must rest.

— iii —
Fairweather & Sons, Solicitors
4 Lincoln's Inn Fields, London W.C.
3rd November, 1869
Miss Adelaide Ashbourne,
Ashbourne Hall, Derbyshire.
Dear Miss Ashbourne, — I write in the full cognisance of the 
impropriety of addressing you directly without the formal sanction 
of your father, and I beg you to receive this letter in the spirit of 
sincerity in which it is composed rather than to censure its 
irregularity. I am sensible that the correct and approved avenue of  
the present communication would pass through the Viscount, and 
that in bypassing that avenue I expose myself to an imputation of 
want of respect both for your father and for the conventions of our 
society. I can only plead, in mitigation, that what I wish to say to 
you appears, in the most literal and unmetaphorical sense, 
impossible to say through any other medium.
I have had the honour of your company upon eleven separate 
occasions during the past year and a half. I have, upon each of 
those occasions, observed you with an attention that I hope I have 
succeeded in rendering unobtrusive, and I have arrived, in 
consequence of that observation, at a conclusion which I offer not 
as a declaration of romantic sentiment — for I am not, as you may 
have perceived, a man whose interior landscape is readily 
topographical — but as a statement of reasoned conviction: that 
you are, of all the persons of my acquaintance, the one whose 
company I find most consistently necessary to my intellectual and 
personal wellbeing.
I am aware that I offer you, at present, very little in material 
terms. My prospects are sound but not yet realised; my income is 
adequate but not distinguished. What I offer, in place of immediate  
advantage, is the assurance of absolute fidelity of purpose — which  
is to say, that if you will permit me to address your father with a 
view to a formal understanding between us, I shall dedicate myself  
without reservation to ensuring that the material circumstances of 
your life, under my care, shall be everything that your station and 
your deserts require.
I am, Miss Ashbourne, with the deepest respect and the most 
earnest solicitude for your happiness,

Your most obedient servant,
Edmund Rathbone Hale
— iv —
Ashbourne Hall, Derbyshire.
7th November, 1869
Mr Hale, —
Your letter has been read. It has been read twice, and then a 
third time, and I find that my opinions of it have altered materially 
with each successive reading — which is, I think, a 
recommendation rather than a defect, as opinions that do not alter  
under renewed consideration are generally opinions that have not 
been seriously entertained to begin with.
You are, Mr Hale, the most peculiar suitor I have encountered,  
which is not quite the same as saying the most unwelcome. Most 
men who have written to me upon this subject — and several have,  
though none so directly — have offered me poetry, or sentiment, or  
elaborate comparisons between my eyes and astronomical 
phenomena. You have offered me an assurance of material fidelity 
of purpose. I find, upon reflection, that I prefer it.
You may speak to my father. I make no further commitment 
than this. My father will make his own enquiries, as fathers do, 
and I shall make mine, and the result of those enquiries will 
determine the next step, if any. What I will tell you, because I think  
you are the sort of man who values candour above consolation, is 
that I am not opposed to the question being put.
I am, however, a woman who observes carefully and forms 
opinions slowly and retains them persistently. I mention this not as  
a warning, but as information which seems to me relevant to any 
undertaking of the kind you propose.
Adelaide Ashbourne

PART THE SECOND
The Edifice
1870 – 1877
— v —
MEMORANDUM OF MARRIAGE SETTLEMENT
Between the Viscount Ashbourne of Ashbourne Hall, Derbyshire, and Edmund  
Rathbone Hale, Esq., of Lincoln's Inn Fields, London W.C.
Whereas it has been agreed between the parties above-named that a 
marriage shall be solemnised between the said Edmund Rathbone Hale 
and Miss Adelaide Constance Ashbourne, only daughter of the said 
Viscount, the following terms and conditions of settlement have been 
mutually consented to and are herewith set down for the avoidance of 
future doubt or dispute:
FIRSTLY: The Viscount Ashbourne shall settle upon his daughter, in 
anticipation of the said marriage, the sum of eight thousand pounds, to be  
held in trust by Messrs. Fairweather and Sons of Lincoln's Inn as trustees 
during the lifetime of both parties to the marriage, the income therefrom 
to be payable to Mrs Adelaide Hale for her sole and separate use.
SECONDLY: The said Edmund Rathbone Hale shall, upon the 
occasion of his marriage, take up residence at No. 7 Cavendish Square, 
London W., the lease of which premises has been secured by arrangement  
with the Ashbourne trustees, and shall maintain the said premises in a 
condition befitting the station of a man of the professional classes.
THIRDLY: The said Edmund Rathbone Hale has represented, and 
does hereby represent, that he is a man of good character, unencumbered  
by debts of honour or obligation, and free of any prior entanglement of an 
affective or contractual nature. This representation is made as a material 
inducement to the present settlement, and the Viscount Ashbourne has 
relied upon it accordingly.
Signed and witnessed at Lincoln's Inn Fields, London, this 14th day of March,  
in the year of Our Lord Eighteen Hundred and Seventy.
Signatures: Ashbourne [seal] — E.R. Hale — Jas. Fairweather (Witness)
— vi —

Private Memoranda of E.R.H. — Saturday, 12th March, 1870. (Two days before 
the signing.)
I have written, in the Settlement, that I am free of any prior 
entanglement of an affective or contractual nature. Fairweather read  
the clause aloud to me this afternoon and looked at me steadily over 
his spectacles for a moment longer than the occasion strictly 
required. I returned his gaze without flinching. He said nothing 
further. He never does.
Clara's address is 11 Camberwell New Road. I have not visited since 
the fourteenth of January. I have written once, briefly, to the effect 
that the exigencies of my professional situation make a continuation 
of our present arrangement impossible and that the enclosed sum 
represents, with my sincerest regrets, a final settlement of all 
obligations between us. She has not replied. Her silence is, I choose 
to believe, a form of dignity rather than of grief. It may be both. The 
distinction, in the present circumstances, is one I cannot afford to 
examine.
The Settlement will be signed on Friday. The wedding is in June. I am  
advancing. The direction is the only thing that matters now; the 
means are already behind me.
— vii —
7 Cavendish Square, London W.
3rd October, 1871
My Dear Whitmore,
It gives me a satisfaction of no common order to inform you 
that Fairweather offered me the junior partnership last Tuesday, 
and that I accepted it with a composure that I cannot pretend I 
entirely felt. Fourteen months of marriage; junior partner at thirty:  
these are the coordinates of a life proceeding, by any reasonable 
calculation, according to plan.
Adelaide is well and sends her regards, though she does not 
know I am writing to you and would, I think, disapprove of the 
term, as she has formed the view that what passes between us in 
correspondence is insufficiently formal for a woman of her station 
to commend. She is very probably right. She generally is. This

quality, which I admired at a distance, I find, in close domestic 
proximity, to be simultaneously her most admirable and her most 
— I shall not say uncomfortable — her most exacting 
characteristic.
She has also, I should tell you, a perception that borders upon 
the uncanny. Last week she made an observation about one of my 
clients — a remark so precisely accurate, and delivered with so 
little apparent effort, that I found myself in the unsettling position 
of understanding my own case less well than my wife does. I 
cannot decide whether this is a comfort or a warning.
Ever yours,
Edmund
— viii —
THE TIMES, LONDON.   Wednesday, 4th June, 1873.
APPOINTMENTS AND DISTINCTIONS.
We are pleased to record the appointment of MR EDMUND RATHBONE HALE, 
of Fairweather & Sons,
Lincoln's Inn, as legal counsel to the Marchioness of Trentham in the  
matter of the Trentham
Estate dispute, which has occupied the Chancery Division of the High 
Court for the better part
of three years. Mr Hale, who is known in legal circles for the 
exceptional acuity of his
analytical faculties and the particular elegance of his written 
pleadings, is regarded by
several of his seniors at the Bar as among the more formidable minds 
of his generation.
Mr Hale, who resides at Cavendish Square with his wife, the former 
Miss Adelaide Ashbourne,
daughter of Viscount Ashbourne of Derbyshire, has declined to make 
any comment upon the
appointment, which is consistent with the notable reticence that has 
characterised his
professional conduct throughout a career of which his many admirers 
entertain the highest
expectations.

[Cutting preserved among the papers of E. Hale, Esq.]

PART THE THIRD
The Fracture
1877 – 1881
— ix —
11 Camberwell New Road, London S.E.
17th January, 1877
Mr Hale. —
I write to you after seven years of silence, which I hope you 
will grant is a sufficient demonstration that I am not a woman of 
vindictive or mercenary disposition. I write because there is a 
matter which I have deliberated upon at very great length, and 
upon which I have arrived, after considerable struggle, at the 
conclusion that you must be informed.
When you sent me the enclosed sum in January of 1870 and 
indicated that our connexion was to be severed, you were not, as 
you appeared to believe, settling all obligations. There was at that 
time an obligation of which you were unaware, and which I elected  
— in the interests of your advancement, as I then understood it to 
serve — to discharge in silence and alone. That obligation has a 
name. His name is Thomas. He is six years old. He has your eyes 
and your facility with numbers and your particular manner of 
tilting his head when he is in the act of forming a conclusion.
I do not write to you from any expectation of a resumption of 
our former intimacy. I write because Thomas is now of an age at 
which the question of his father becomes, in the natural course of 
things, insistent; and because I have determined that a lie told to a  
child about his origins is the particular species of dishonesty that a  
mother does not make peace with. I thought you should know 
before he asks. So that you should have the opportunity, if you 
possess the fortitude, to decide what you wish to do.
I ask nothing for myself. I ask, for him, only the consideration 
that your conscience can furnish.
Clara Marsh

— x —
Private Memoranda of E.R.H. — Friday, 20th January, 1877
I have spent three days with Clara's letter in the bottom drawer of 
my escritoire, which is the piece of furniture in my study that has a 
lock, and I have told Adelaide that the lock is stiff, which is true in 
the sense that the lock has not been used for some time and the 
mechanism is not entirely smooth, and false in every other sense.
Thomas. Six years old. His eyes are, she says, mine. I have been 
attempting to conceive of this and find that the attempt produces a 
disorientation of a peculiarly visceral kind — not guilt, precisely, or 
not only guilt, but something more fundamental: the recognition that 
the architecture of my life, which I have constructed with such 
meticulous care over the past decade, is built upon a substratum that  
was never declared to the surveyor. Adelaide signed the Settlement 
on the understanding that I was unencumbered. The Settlement is a 
legal document. I am a lawyer. I comprehend, with a precision that I 
find distinctly unpleasant, precisely what this means.
There are, as I identify them, three possible courses of action. The 
first: to acknowledge Thomas, inform Adelaide, and accept the 
consequences — the dissolution of my marriage, almost certainly, 
and the attendant professional ruin, for Ashbourne's connexions are 
woven into my practice as thoroughly as the warp threads are woven 
into a cloth; to pull one is to alter the other. The second: to make 
provision for Thomas in secret, to visit Clara, to arrange a financial 
settlement that buys the silence without admitting the obligation. 
The third — and it is this third course that I observe myself, with a 
cold and clinical detachment, already gravitating toward — is to do 
nothing, to acknowledge nothing, and to trust that Clara's seven-year  
silence was indicative of a permanent disposition rather than a 
temporary strategy.
I am not, I think, a wicked man. But I have observed, in the course of 
my professional life, that the distance between a good man and a 
man who does wicked things is, in practice, considerably shorter 
than moral philosophy suggests. The gap is bridged not by a single 
dramatic act of corruption but by a sequence of small, entirely 
defensible decisions, each of which, viewed in isolation, admits of a 
respectable construction.
— xi —

7 Cavendish Square, London W.
24th January, 1877
Miss Marsh, —
I received your letter. I have given its contents the most 
serious and sustained consideration of which I am capable. I write 
now in the full and painful awareness of the inadequacy of any 
written communication to the weight of what you have disclosed, 
and I ask you to read what follows in the spirit of a man who finds 
himself in the presence of an obligation that he did not know he 
had incurred but which he recognises, now that it is made known 
to him, as incontestable.
I wish to make provision for Thomas. I am not in a position, at 
the present time, to do more than this — the reasons for which I 
shall not rehearse here, as they will be, I think, apparent to you, 
and as I am unwilling to insult your intelligence with an account of  
obstacles which, while genuinely material, are also, in the ledger 
of moral obligation, entirely subordinate to the fact of a child who 
did not ask to be born and who is owed, at the very minimum, the 
security that his circumstances presently deny him.
I shall arrange for a payment of one hundred and fifty pounds 
per annum to be made to an account in your name at Messrs. 
Courts & Co., Strand. The payment will be made quarterly, without  
fail, through my own solicitors — a different firm, I should tell you, 
from my own employer, as you will understand why I consider it 
necessary to maintain this separation. There will be no letter with 
the payment. There will be no further communication from me 
unless you initiate it. If you should require more, you must write to  
me; I shall do what I can.
I am, Miss Marsh, sensible of the degree to which this 
response falls short of what the situation demands. I am also 
unable, at the present juncture, to offer anything more substantial 
without consequences that I am not in a position to accept. I offer 
this not as a justification — there is none that would withstand 
examination — but as the most accurate account of the truth that I  
am capable of furnishing.
E.R. Hale
— xii —

Ashbourne Hall, Derbyshire
(Written during my visit to my father, February 1877)
Edmund, —
I am writing from Derbyshire, where I have been these ten 
days, and I find that the distance between us is producing in me a 
quality of reflection that proximity has, in recent months, rendered  
difficult. I shall be direct, as I believe you have come to expect 
from me, and as I have found obliquity, in our dealings with one 
another, to be a currency that neither of us spends well.
You are concealing something. I do not know what it is. I have 
had this knowledge for approximately three weeks now — it 
arrived, as such knowledge tends to, not through any single 
observable evidence but through an accumulation of small details: 
the lock on the escritoire, which you told me was stiff and which I 
find, upon examining it in your absence, yields quite readily to the 
key; the particular quality of your attention at dinner, which has 
been, since January, that of a man whose mind is elsewhere; and 
the letter you posted by your own hand on the twenty-fourth of last  
month, rather than sending it by the servant, which you have not 
done since the early months of our marriage.
I am not writing to demand a confession. I am writing to tell 
you that I am not without perception, and that whatever it is you 
are carrying, you are carrying it less efficiently than you suppose. 
The performance, Edmund, has developed a visible seam.
Adelaide

PART THE FOURTH
The Unravelling
1881 – 1882
— xiii —
THE PALL MALL GAZETTE.   Thursday, 14th April, 1881.
PROCEEDINGS IN CHANCERY: THE MERTON ESTATE MATTER.
The hearing in the matter of Merton v. Hale & Anor, which has 
occupied the attention of the
Chancery Division for the better part of four months, resumed 
yesterday before Mr Justice
Collingwood. Mr Edmund Hale, the respondent, appeared in person, 
having instructed no counsel
for his defence — a decision that has occasioned considerable comment  
among those members of
the legal profession who have followed the case with the interest 
that it amply merits.
The substance of the allegations against Mr Hale, which we refrain 
from summarising in detail
pending the conclusion of proceedings, concerns the management of 
certain trust funds of which
Mr Hale has been a trustee since 1873, and whether the management 
thereof has been conducted
in the exclusive interests of the beneficiaries, as the law requires,  
or in partial furtherance
of interests of a more personal and less avowable character.
Mr Hale, who has hitherto enjoyed a reputation in legal circles of 
the highest order, was
observed by those present to maintain throughout the proceedings an 
air of the most complete
composure. Whether this composure represents the equanimity of an 
innocent man or the
