self-command of a man long accustomed to the management of his own 
exterior, the Court

must in due course determine.
[Cutting preserved among the papers of E. Hale, Esq.]
— xiv —
Private Memoranda of E.R.H. — Sunday, 17th April, 1881
Adelaide has read the Pall Mall Gazette. She said nothing at 
breakfast, which is, from Adelaide, the most communicative of 
silences. She placed the paper beside my plate and then removed 
herself to the drawing room, where she has been for the past three 
hours. I can hear her moving occasionally. She is not reading; when 
Adelaide reads, the house is entirely still.
The Merton matter. It is complicated to explain and, in its 
explanation, considerably less damning than the Gazette's lurid 
circumlocutions imply. The funds were managed — not impeccably, I 
grant, not with the scrupulousness that the letter of the law demands  
— but the deficiency is one of method rather than of motive. I 
borrowed from the trust to cover certain payments — the quarterly 
provision for Clara and Thomas, which has been maintained, without 
interruption and at increasing personal cost, for four years — and I 
replaced the borrowed sum within six months, with interest. No 
beneficiary was materially disadvantaged. The accounts, upon full 
examination, will support this.
The accounts, upon full examination, will also raise the question of 
what, precisely, the quarterly provision to a Miss C. Marsh of 
Camberwell represents. And that question, once raised in a court of 
law, cannot be unasked.
The edifice, it seems, is not so solid as it appeared from the exterior. 
Edifices very rarely are. This is, I believe, what Adelaide has been 
trying to tell me for four years.
— xv —
Ashbourne Hall, Derbyshire
3rd September, 1882

Edmund, —
I have now had the complete account from Fairweather, who 
came to see me at my father's request last Friday and who, I think,  
told me rather more than he was instructed to, partly from scruple  
and partly from the particular species of guilty relief that men 
experience when they are finally permitted to stop maintaining a 
pretence that has become, through long sustention, intolerable.
I know about Clara Marsh. I know about Thomas, who is eight 
years old and has, by Fairweather's account — which was itself 
confused and fragmentary, but sufficient — your eyes. I know 
about the trust funds, and the manner of their management, and 
the reason for that management. I know, in short, the truth about 
the man I have been married to for twelve years, and I find that 
the truth and the man I believed myself to be married to are so 
disparate as to constitute, in effect, two entirely different persons.
What I wish to say to you is not what you may expect. I do not 
wish to reproach you; reproach, at this juncture, would be both 
superfluous and beneath me. I do not wish to enumerate your 
deceits; you know them rather better than I do. What I wish to say 
is this: that I observed, for twelve years, a performance of 
impeccable rectitude, and that the performance, considered purely  
as a performance, was among the most accomplished I have ever 
witnessed. You were, Edmund, an extraordinarily convincing 
version of the man you were not.
I also wish to say — because I am, as you know, 
constitutionally incapable of expressing only the easier part of 
what I think — that I do not believe the performance was entirely 
without sincerity. I believe you wished to be the man you were 
performing. I believe the performance was, in some part, an 
aspiration rather than a deception. And I find that this is, in the 
final accounting, the most sorrowful thing about the entire affair: 
not that you deceived me, but that you deceived yourself more 
thoroughly, and with greater dedication, than you deceived anyone  
else.
I shall not be returning to Cavendish Square. My father's 
solicitor will be in communication with you regarding the terms of 
our separation. I ask only that you make provision for Thomas, as 
you have done, but now openly, and that you acknowledge him to 
the world as your son, which he is, and which the world will know 
in any case, since the court proceedings will establish it. The

difference between the acknowledgment and the exposure is the 
difference between a small remaining dignity and none at all.
Adelaide Hale

Part the Fifth
The Confession
Written at Thornfield House, Shropshire, on the 4th of October, 1882,
and recovered from a sealed escritoire by Mr James Fairweather, solicitor,
on the 29th of November, 1882, following the disappearance of E.R. Hale, Esq.
I am writing this in the library at Thornfield, which is the one room in this 
house that retains any warmth at this time of year, partly on account of the 
south-facing windows and partly on account of the fire, which I have 
maintained at considerable expense in coal since my arrival here ten days 
ago, and which I tend myself rather than asking the housekeeper to do it, 
because the act of feeding a fire is one of the few purely physical activities 
that permits the mind to be, for the duration of it, something approximating 
still.
I am writing this because I have been a man who composed, for the greater 
part of his adult life, versions of himself for the consumption of others, and I  
find that I wish, before whatever comes next — and I have not yet 
determined what that is, though I have a sense that the options are fewer 
than they were — to compose, for once and entirely without reader in mind, 
a version of myself that is accurate.
I am Edmund Rathbone Hale. I am forty-one years of age. I am a solicitor, or  
was until recently, when the firm of Fairweather and Sons communicated to  
me, with the particular and elaborate delicacy of men who wish to 
communicate a brutal thing without appearing to do so, that my partnership  
in that firm was at an end. I have been married to Adelaide Constance 
Ashbourne for twelve years, and I have, throughout those twelve years, 
been a man of irreproachable reputation in my profession and my society. I 
say irreproachable: I mean unreproached. The distinction is the subject of 
this document.
The truth is this. I loved Clara Marsh. I do not say this in extenuation of 
anything, and I am sufficiently honest, at this juncture, to acknowledge that 
I did not, when it mattered, act as a man who loved her. But the fact is prior  
to the acts, and the fact deserves to be set down. She was the daughter of a 
schoolmaster in Lambeth. She was twenty-two when I met her, and I was

twenty-four, and we were both of us possessed of rather more intelligence 
than our circumstances afforded any immediate outlet for, and we found 
each other's company an alleviation of the particular loneliness that 
intelligent people in straitened circumstances experience with a persistence  
that those of more comfortable disposition rarely appreciate.
When I understood that my prospects required Adelaide Ashbourne, I made 
a choice that I have not, in my inmost and unperformed self, ceased to be 
ashamed of in the thirteen years since I made it. I do not mean the choice to  
pursue Adelaide, which was rational and defensible and, I should add, 
productive of a marriage that was, in its better years, a genuinely estimable 
thing. I mean the choice to dispose of Clara — that is the word; I have used 
more genteel formulations in my private memoranda and I find they have 
ceased to be available to me — as though she were an encumbrance rather 
than a person to whom I had made, in the only vocabulary that matters 
between human beings, a promise.
Thomas is nine years old. He has been raised in Camberwell by his mother, 
on the quarterly payments that I arranged in 1877 — payments that were, 
until the Merton proceedings exposed them, the one covert act of my double  
life that I was able to regard with anything approaching a clear conscience. 
I have never met him. I have managed, for nine years, to sustain the fiction 
that providing financially for a child constitutes, in some morally adequate 
sense, knowing him. It does not. I know this. I have always known this. The 
knowledge is one of the several things I have maintained in the locked 
escritoire of my interior life, available to be examined but not, in the 
ordinary course of events, examined.
Adelaide told me, in her final letter, that I deceived myself more thoroughly 
than I deceived anyone else, and that the performance was, in some part, an  
aspiration rather than a deception. She is right. She is, as she has been 
throughout our marriage, almost entirely right about me, and I have found 
this quality — which I once admired as exceptional — to be, in the event of 
its being directed at my own conduct, an experience of almost unendurable 
lucidity.
I have been, for twenty years, in the business of constructing a self. A self 
that could inhabit the upper rooms of Victorian society; that could dine with  
viscounts and argue before judges and be spoken of, in the Inns of Court 
and the drawing rooms of Mayfair, as a man of the highest calibre. The 
construction was not entirely fraudulent. There were genuine planks in it — 
genuine industry, genuine intelligence, genuine care for Adelaide, genuine 
provision for my son. But the construction was built upon a concealment, 
and concealment is the great solvent of character, because it requires, 
above all other things, the perpetual expenditure of energy upon the

maintenance of a surface; and energy thus expended is energy unavailable 
for the interior work that character actually requires.
I do not know what I am without the construction. This is the most 
frightening thing I have ever written, and I have drafted legal documents 
concerning the disposition of entire estates.
I intend to go to Camberwell. I intend to introduce myself to Thomas. I 
intend to tell him, with whatever honesty I can still command, who I am and  
what I did and what I am proposing, at the late hour of his ninth year, to do 
about it. Whether he will receive me I cannot know. Whether Clara will 
permit it I cannot know. Whether I will manage, in practice, to say the 
things I have said with such apparent facility upon this page — in the 
presence of a child whose grey eyes are, according to his mother, my own —  
I genuinely cannot know.
What I know is that I am going. What I know is that the performance has 
ended.
What I know is that I do not yet have any idea what comes after a 
performance that has run for twenty years, and that this ignorance is, for 
the first time in my adult life, not a problem to be solved but a condition to 
be inhabited.
Edmund Rathbone Hale
Thornfield House, Shropshire
4th October, 1882

Editor's Postscript
What is known of subsequent events
Edmund Rathbone Hale disappeared from Thornfield House, Shropshire, on 
or about the fifth of October, 1882. The confession reproduced above was 
the last document he is known to have composed. No subsequent 
correspondence has been discovered. The sealed escritoire in which it was 
found also contained the private diary from which the extracts in this 
volume are drawn, the complete run of Clara Marsh's letters, and Adelaide 
Hale's correspondence, which she had at some earlier point returned to him  
— all of it, every letter, with the exception of her own communications to 
him, which she retained.
Enquiries made by the present editor established the following: that 
Edmund Hale did, in fact, travel to Camberwell in the first week of October 
1882, and that he did present himself at the house of Miss Clara Marsh; 
that he was admitted; and that he was, on that occasion and on several 
occasions thereafter, introduced to his son Thomas. What passed between 
them is not known, as neither Clara Marsh nor Thomas has been willing to 
speak to the matter for the purposes of this collection, and the editor has 
not pressed the point.
Thomas Marsh — for he retained his mother's name, and has shown no 
inclination to alter it — is, at the time of writing, ten years of age, and 
attends a school in Lambeth at the expense of an anonymous benefactor 
whose identity, by tacit agreement among all parties, has not been formally 
established.
Edmund Hale was not seen again in London. His dissolution from public  
life was total and, apparently, voluntary. His whereabouts, as of the date of 
this volume's publication, are not known to the editor, though certain 
information in the editor's possession — which he declines to record here, in  
the interest of a privacy that the subject of this collection has, at 
considerable cost to himself, belatedly and imperfectly earned — suggests 
that he is living, and that the living is being conducted in a manner 
materially different from anything that preceded it.
Adelaide Hale obtained a legal separation in March of 1883. She has not  
remarried. She continues to reside at Ashbourne Hall, where she is, by all 
accounts, entirely herself — which is to say, composed, perceptive, and 
rigorously disinclined to perform anything.

The question with which these papers leave the reader — the question 
of what Edmund Hale was, beneath the performance — is not one that 
admits, in the editor's judgment, of a clean and definitive answer. He was a 
man who deceived his wife, abandoned the mother of his child, 
misappropriated funds in a court of trust, and constructed, over the course 
of two decades, an edifice of respectability so complete and so convincing 
that the society he inhabited rewarded him handsomely for it. He was also a  
man who, in the end — belatedly, inadequately, with the limited tools 
available to a character long accustomed to concealment — tried.
Whether the trying, at that late hour, is sufficient to redeem anything, 
the editor leaves to the individual reader's conscience, where it properly 
belongs.
A. Frere, K.C.
Inner Temple, London
January, 1887

A Glossary of the Difficult
Words and constructions in this volume that reward the labour of looking up
Epistolary
Relating to the writing of letters. An epistolary novel is one told entirely through 
correspondence and documents rather than conventional narrative prose. The form  
was popular in the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries precisely because it allows 
each character to present their own version of events — making it the ideal form 
for a story about the gap between performance and truth.
Cognisance
Knowledge or awareness of something. "In full cognisance of the impropriety" 
means: knowing perfectly well that what I am doing is irregular. Hale uses this 
word frequently because it allows him to acknowledge an impropriety while 
simultaneously displaying the sophistication to name it — which is, in miniature, 
his entire character.
Prurient
Having an excessive interest in sexual matters; in broader Victorian usage, 
curiosity of an unwholesome or voyeuristic kind. Frere uses it in the preface to 
distinguish his motives — scholarly and moral — from the mere appetite for 
scandal.
Escritoire
A small writing desk with a hinged flap that opens to form the writing surface, 
usually containing drawers and a lockable compartment. Hale's locked escritoire is  
the physical embodiment of his concealed interior life — a piece of furniture whose  
function is to contain secrets.
Entanglement
In Victorian usage, an involvement or connection, especially one of a romantic or 
financial nature that creates obligations or complications. The word carries its 
literal meaning: something you are caught in and cannot easily free yourself from.
Solicitor
In English law, a lawyer who advises clients and prepares legal documents, as 
distinguished from a barrister, who argues cases in court. Hale's profession is 
particularly significant: a man whose entire career rests upon the exact and honest  
management of other people's affairs, who is conducting the exact opposite in his 
private life.

Incontestable
Not able to be disputed or denied. When Hale writes that Clara's claim is 
"incontestable," he is using a legal term to describe a moral obligation — the habit 
of a man who thinks in legal frameworks even about personal matters.
Obliquity
The quality of being indirect or evasive in manner or speech; not going straight to 
the point. Adelaide tells Hale that obliquity is a currency neither of them spends 
well — meaning they are both direct people, and the indirectness of their situation 
is therefore particularly costly.
Sustention
The act of sustaining or maintaining something over time. "Through long 
sustention" — Fairweather has maintained the pretence for so long that the effort 
of it has become intolerable. An unusual word, even in Victorian English, chosen 
for its suggestion of physical strain.
Rectitude
Morally correct behaviour; righteousness. Hale's public performance is one of 
"impeccable rectitude" — perfect moral correctness. Adelaide's observation that 
"unreproached" is not the same as "irreproachable" is the most precise line in the 
novel.
Extenuation
A partial excuse or justification that makes an offence seem less serious. When 
Hale writes "I do not say this in extenuation of anything," he is refusing to use his 
genuine feeling for Clara as a defence for his treatment of her — which is, in the 
confession, the first truly honest thing he says.
Edifice
A large, impressive building; used metaphorically for any complex system or 
structure, particularly one that has been carefully constructed. The "edifice" of 
Hale's respectability is a building he has spent twenty years erecting, and which 
collapses from the foundations rather than the roof.
Lucidity
Clarity of expression or thought; the ability to see things as they truly are. 
Adelaide's perception produces in Hale an "experience of almost unendurable 
lucidity" — the discomfort of being seen accurately by someone you love.
Peripatetic
Travelling from place to place; having no fixed location. Not used explicitly in the 
text, but the condition of Hale at the novel's end — living somewhere unspecified, 
in a life materially different from anything preceding it — is peripatetic in the 
deepest sense.
Subordinate clause

A clause that depends upon a main clause to complete its meaning. Victorian prose  
is full of them, nested inside one another, because the era believed that the 
complexity of a sentence should be proportionate to the complexity of what it is 
describing. To read Victorian prose well is to follow the grammar of a thought all 
the way to its end.
Circumlocution
The use of many words where fewer would do; a roundabout way of saying 
something. The Pall Mall Gazette's "lurid circumlocutions" are its way of implying 
what it cannot legally state directly — which is, in Victorian journalism as in 
Victorian society, a thoroughly understood convention.
FINIS
London: Chapman & Hall, 1887

THE HALE DYNASTY
BOOK VIII
Thomas
Being a Collection of Letters, Journal Entries, Dispatches,
Newspaper Extracts, Telegrams, and other Documents pertaining
to Thomas Marsh-Hale of Camberwell and the Western Front,
in the Reigns of Victoria, Edward VII, and George V.
Covering the years
1873 — 1919
—  —  —
Editor's Prefatory Note
Thomas Marsh-Hale was born in Camberwell, in the south of London, in the spring of  
1873. His mother was Clara Marsh, a woman of modest means and considerable  
intelligence, who had been employed as a seamstress in a Mayfair establishment and  
had met Edmund Hale — the subject of Book VII — at a dinner party where she had  
been engaged to repair a hem. His father was Edmund, who had a wife in Cavendish  
Square and a baronetcy and a career at the Bar and a very great deal to lose, and  
who, when Clara wrote to tell him about the child, did not reply for six weeks and  
then replied in terms that were, by all accounts, simultaneously generous in money  
and negligible in everything else.
Clara raised Thomas alone. She received from Edmund, through a solicitor, an  
annual sum of forty pounds, which was enough to keep a Camberwell household in  
the lower tier of respectability and not much more. She did not pursue further claim.  
She was not the sort of woman who pursued claims; she was the sort who worked  
with what was available and expected her son to do the same.
Thomas grew up knowing who his father was. He was told at twelve, when Clara  
decided he was old enough to understand it and old enough to need to understand it.  
He received the information with the composure that Clara had spent twelve years  
building into him, and asked two questions. The first was: does he know about me?

Clara said: yes. The second was: does he want to know me? Clara said: I don't believe  
so. Thomas said: all right. He did not raise the subject again for eleven years.
What distinguishes Thomas from every other Hale in this chronicle is not his  
illegitimacy — the Hale family had produced its share of irregular connections across  
nine centuries, though none so directly consequential as this — but his position  
relative to the performance that the family had always constructed around itself.  
Every Hale before him had performed something: respectability, loyalty, faith,  
competence, survival. Thomas, who had been born outside the performance and  
raised in its shadow, had the peculiar freedom of the person who was never asked to  
perform. He did not know he was a Hale. He did not know what that meant. He had  
only his mother's name and his mother's character and a question — his father's  
question, though he did not yet know that — that he had been asking quietly since he  
was twelve years old.
The question was not: is what I have done sufficient? Not yet. The question, at  
twelve, was simpler and harder: what am I?
— compiled from the Marsh-Hale papers by the editors of this edition

I
Camberwell
South London, 1873–1891
Camberwell in the 1870s was the particular kind of London neighbourhood that  
the Victorian period produced in abundance and preferred not to look at too closely:  
respectable enough to maintain the fiction of its own gentility, precarious enough to  
make that fiction work hard every day. The houses were good houses — three-storey,  
brick, with small gardens — occupied by the lower rungs of the clerical and artisan  
classes, the families of shopkeepers and schoolteachers and minor civil servants,  
people who were not poor in the way of the East End poor but who understood  
poverty as a thing at no great distance, visible if you looked, avoidable if you were  
careful.
Clara Marsh was careful. She worked six days a week at her sewing until Thomas  
was seven, when she obtained a position as a bookkeeper at a firm of wholesale  
drapers in Borough, which paid better and was less damaging to her eyes. She was  
thirty-two years old when she obtained this position and she had been educating  
herself  in  the  evenings,  with  the  systematic  determination  of  a  woman  who  
understood that the only capital available to her was in her head, for four years. She  
could read a balance sheet with more precision than most of the men who employed  
her, and she knew it, and she did not say so, because she also knew that saying it  
would have been the end of the position.
Thomas attended the local board school until he was ten, then won a scholarship  
to the grammar school in Peckham, which was the kind of school that the 1870  
Education Act had made possible and that the older English class system had not  
quite decided what to do with: a school that took boys regardless of what their  
fathers did and educated them regardless of what they were expected to become.  
Thomas was educated there from 1882 to 1889, and he was, by the testimony of  
three of his teachers whose letters survive in the archive, the most interesting  
student they had taught in a decade.
Interesting was the word they all used. Not brilliant — though he was that — not  
hardworking — though he was that too — but interesting, in the sense that his  
questions were better than the answers the curriculum had prepared for, and that  
his reading extended well beyond what the school provided, and that he had, from a

young age, the quality that his great-great-aunt Augusta had possessed and that the  
Hale family had always possessed without knowing it possessed it: the ability to hold  
a difficult question open rather than closing it prematurely with a convenient  
answer.
He did not know he was a Hale. He knew he was Clara Marsh's son, which was  
something, and that his father was a man in Cavendish Square who had chosen not to  
know him, which was something else.
Thomas Marsh-Hale to his friend Daniel Okafor — October, 1889
Dear Dan, I have been thinking about your question from last week about whether a  
man is what his circumstances make him or what he makes of his circumstances,  
and I have arrived at the following provisional answer: both, but not in equal  
measure, and the measure changes depending on what the circumstances are.
My circumstances are these: I am sixteen years old, the illegitimate son of a  
barrister who has chosen not to acknowledge me and a bookkeeper who has  
educated me on forty pounds a year and her own considerable intelligence. My  
circumstances include a grammar school education that has given me Latin,  
mathematics, and a taste for asking inconvenient questions, and a neighbourhood  
that has given me an understanding of what it costs to be precarious in a city that  
does not care about the cost.
I think a man in my circumstances can be made by them or can make something of  
them. I think the difference between the two outcomes is largely a matter of  
whether he is honest about what the circumstances actually are, rather than what  
he wishes they were or what someone else has told him they should be.
This is probably not the answer you were expecting from a question about Locke.  
But it is the answer I have.
Are you coming to the debate on Thursday? The motion is This House believes the  
extension of the franchise to working men has been sufficient. I intend to speak  
against.
Yours, Thomas M-H.
The friend addressed in the letter — Daniel Okafor, the son of a Lagos merchant  
who had come to London in 1878 and settled in Peckham — was Thomas's closest  
friend from grammar school and remained so for the rest of Thomas's life. His own

archive, donated to the British Library in 1963 by his descendants, contains forty-
three letters from Thomas spanning the years 1889 to 1918, and they constitute,  
alongside Thomas's own journal, the most complete record we have of a man who  
was, by temperament and by circumstance, deeply resistant to being recorded.
Thomas left grammar school in 1889 with a scholarship to a teachers' training  
college in Islington, which he attended for two years before concluding that he would  
rather teach himself than be trained to teach. He left the training college in 1891  
with a certificate he never used and a reading list he spent the next decade working  
through, and took a position as a clerk at a shipping company in the City, where he  
spent his days arranging bills of lading and his evenings reading Ibsen and William  
Morris and the early Fabians, and his Sundays walking the streets of south London  
with Daniel and arguing about everything.
—  —  —

II
The Name
London, 1893–1900
Thomas was twenty years old in 1893, the year Edmund Hale disappeared from a  
locked room in his Shropshire house and was presumed dead. He did not know this  
had happened. He did not know Edmund Hale by name, having known him only as his  
father, a category that the man himself had declined, and having no particular  
mechanism by which news of a Cavendish Square solicitor's disappearance would  
reach a shipping clerk in Camberwell.
What Thomas did know, in 1893, was the following: that he was twenty years old  
and had read more widely than most men twice his age; that his mother was in  
tolerably good health but worked too hard; that the shipping company was teaching  
him a great deal about commerce and very little about anything else; that Daniel  
Okafor had gone to read law at the Inns of Court and was making his way in a world  
that did not much want him there; and that something needed to change, though  
what that something was he could not yet name precisely.
The something that changed was this: in April 1893, Clara Marsh called Thomas  
into the small room she used as a sitting room and study, closed the door, and placed  
on the table between them a letter. The letter was from a solicitor named Alderton —  
the firm of Alderton and Co., Shrewsbury — and it concerned the estate of one  
Edmund Hale, lately deceased without legitimate issue, whose illegitimate son  
Thomas Marsh-Hale was entitled, under the terms of a settlement executed in 1875,  
to a legacy of two hundred and fifty pounds.
Thomas read the letter twice. Clara watched him. He said: he is dead. Clara said:  
yes. He said: is this all? Clara said: it is what was agreed. He said: what was agreed  
without my being asked. Clara said: you were two years old. He was quiet for a  
moment. Then he said: I should like to see the original settlement. Clara said she had  
kept a copy and gave it to him.
He read the settlement that evening and again the following morning and then  
wrote to Alderton and Co. asking three questions: whether the two hundred and fifty  
pounds was the full extent of the estate's obligation to him; whether there were any  
other children acknowledged or unacknowledged; and what the estate had consisted  
of.

Alderton and Co. replied two weeks later. The answers were: yes; no; and an  
estate in Shropshire that had been sold to meet debts, the baronetcy being defunct in  
the male line, and no further assets of significance.
Thomas Marsh-Hale to Daniel Okafor — May, 1893
Dan, my father is dead. I am to have two hundred and fifty pounds, which is the  
precise sum that his solicitor judged sufficient to resolve his obligation to me, and  
which  I  am  therefore  to  receive  with  the  understanding  that  it  discharges  
everything. I will take the money because I have no legal grounds to dispute the  
settlement and my mother needs the security, but I want you to know that I am  
taking it under protest, the protest being silent because silent protests are the only  
kind available to illegitimate sons in this jurisdiction.
I have been thinking about the name. I have been calling myself Marsh-Hale since I  
was twelve, which was my mother's doing — she thought I should have some piece  
of him, even if he would not give it. I have been thinking about whether to continue  
this. The argument for continuing it is that it is what I am, whatever he chose to  
make of it. The argument against is that it is a name he used to perform something  
he was not, and I am not interested in the performance.
I have decided to keep it. Not for him. For the question of what it means when a  
name travels down a line through people who never chose it and never quite owned  
it and who are, in some ways, more honestly the thing it names than the people who  
wore it most carefully. I am a Hale in a way he never was. I just do not know yet  
what that means.
Come to Camberwell on Sunday if you can. My mother is making stew and she  
always makes too much.
Thomas.
Thomas used the two hundred and fifty pounds in three ways. He gave fifty  
pounds to Clara, who used it to pay off the last of a debt she had carried for six years  
and who wept, privately, in a manner that Thomas pretended not to notice. He  
invested a hundred pounds in the Consols, on the principle that this was what  
sensible people did and he intended to be sensible. He spent the remaining hundred  
pounds on books, a subscription to the London Library, and a three-week trip to Paris  
in the summer of 1893, which was the first time he had been out of England.

He returned from Paris with three notebooks filled with observations and a  
conviction, arrived at by a process of comparison he had not anticipated making, that  
England in the 1890s was in the last decade of something rather than the first decade  
of something, though he could not yet say precisely what. He wrote this observation  
in his journal and then crossed it out and wrote it again more carefully and then  
crossed that out too, because he could not find the language for it. The language he  
needed was not quite available yet. It was being assembled, in those years, by people  
like Shaw and Wells and a young Irishman named Yeats who was writing poems  
about a world coming apart, though Thomas had not yet read him.
—  —  —

III
The Edwardian Years
London and Manchester, 1900–1910
The death of Victoria in January 1901 was the kind of event that everyone had  
expected for so long that when it happened it arrived with the force of a genuine  
surprise. Thomas was twenty-seven. He had been teaching himself for a decade —  
languages, economics, history, the social sciences that were beginning to constitute  
themselves as disciplines, the new journalism, the new theatre — and had arrived at  
a position that he found difficult to describe because none of the available labels  
quite fitted.
He was not a socialist in the Fabian sense; he found the Fabians too comfortable  
with the assumption that the right people, properly organised, would sort things out.  
He was not a Liberal in the Gladstonian sense; the Liberals seemed to him too  
attached to the belief that individual freedom, properly understood, would resolve  
the  structural  problems  that  individual  freedom  had  created.  He  was  not  a  
Conservative in any sense; the Conservative position seemed to him largely the  
position of a man standing in a collapsing building insisting that the architecture was  
sound.
What he was, he decided in the spring of 1901, was a man who thought clearly  
about systems and badly about what came after the system broke down. This was not  
a comfortable position. It was, however, an honest one.
He left the shipping company in 1901 and spent two years doing journalism —  
trade journalism at first, the kind that required him to attend meetings about tariff  
reform and write eight hundred words before noon — and then, gradually, the more  
interesting kind: the kind that took him to Glasgow to write about the shipbuilders'  
strike of 1902, to Manchester to write about the condition of the cotton operatives, to  
Whitechapel to write about the settlement houses and the new idea — still strange to  
most of his readers — that poverty was a structural condition rather than a personal  
failing.
THE CLARION
Saturday, 14th March, 1903

THE CONDITION OF THE OPERATIVE: A REPORT FROM LANCASHIRE
Our correspondent Thomas Marsh-Hale writes from Manchester: I have spent three  
weeks among the cotton operatives of this city, in the mills and in the courts  
and back-streets where they live, and I have come to the following conclusion:  
the question of what is to be done about the condition of the working poor is not  
a question of charity. It is a question of arithmetic.
The arithmetic is this. A cotton operative in 1903 earns, on average, between  
eighteen and twenty-two shillings per week. The minimum cost of maintaining a  
family of four in health and decency — food, rent, fuel, clothing — is, on the  
best available evidence of investigators such as Mr. Seebohm Rowntree,  
approximately twenty-one shillings per week. The gap between these two figures  
is not large. But it is consistent, and it is structural, and it does not close  
in the ordinary course of a working life.
The consequence of this arithmetic is not idleness or improvidence, as has  
sometimes been alleged. It is a systematic inability to accumulate anything —  
any saving, any reserve, any margin against accident or illness or the simple  
bad luck that visits all lives. A family living at this margin has no buffer. One  
illness, one spell of short time, one death, and the margin becomes a deficit.  
The deficit becomes debt. The debt becomes the condition from which there is no  
recovery.
I am not writing this as an advocate for any particular political position. I am  
writing it because I was asked to report what I found, and what I found is that  
the arithmetic is very plain and that most of the people who discuss the  
condition of the poor have not done it.
Thomas's journalism in these years established him, within the relatively small  
world of progressive London journalism, as a writer of unusual clarity and unusual  
restraint: a man who reported what he found and drew the conclusions that the  
evidence warranted, no more and no less, and who had an evident and deep  
discomfort with the kind of journalistic emotion that substituted for argument.
He was not cold. His letters from these years — to Daniel, to Clara, to the editors  
who employed him — are full of feeling; he simply declined to deploy it in print, on  
the grounds that feeling in print was what you reached for when the facts were  
insufficient. His facts were generally sufficient.
He met a woman named Rachel Simons in 1905, at a meeting of the Fabian  
Society at which he was the only person who disagreed with every position advanced  
from the platform and who said so with a precision that Rachel, who had been  
attending these meetings for three years, found genuinely startling. She was twenty-
six, the daughter of a East End tailor who had come from Odessa in 1882, and she

had an intelligence that matched Thomas's in range and surpassed it in warmth, and  
a patience for his periodic tendency to mistake analysis for wisdom that he badly  
needed.
They married in 1907. Clara attended and wept again, openly this time, and  
Daniel made a speech that was the funniest thing most people present had ever  
heard at a wedding, which Thomas said afterward was unfair because he had been  
intending to be funnier.
Their daughter Ruth was born in 1908. Their son Edmund — named, Clara  
understood without being told, not for Edmund Hale but for the question of what  
Edmund Hale had failed to be — was born in 1910.
—  —  —

IV
The Last Summer
London, June–August 1914
The summer of 1914 was, in Thomas's later account, the last summer in which it  
was possible to believe that the world was moving somewhere rather than coming  
apart. This is probably the romanticism of retrospect — Thomas was not a man given  
to romanticism — but there was something in the specific quality of that summer, its  
warmth and its political anxiety and its sense of ordinary life proceeding in the  
shadow of something it could not quite name, that made it distinct in the memory of  
everyone who lived through it.
Thomas was forty-one. He was writing three columns a week for two newspapers  
and had published, in 1912, a short book called The Arithmetic of Poverty which had  
been reviewed respectfully, read by the people who already agreed with him, and  
ignored by everyone else. He had a house in Brixton — a good house, better than  
Camberwell, which he could afford now though he remained careful with money in  
the way of people who had grown up without it. He had Rachel, who was as  
necessary to him as argument and rather less exhausting. He had Ruth, who was six  
and who had, already, her father's quality of holding a question open. He had  
Edmund, who was four and who had, already, his grandmother Clara's practicality: a  
child who assessed a situation and acted on the assessment without requiring  
theoretical justification.
Clara died in March 1914, aged sixty-seven, of pneumonia. She had been ill for  
three weeks and had spent most of those three weeks insisting that she was tolerably  
comfortable, which Thomas believed and Rachel did not. Thomas was with her when  
she died. She said, near the end, in the particular tone she used when she was being  
precise: I am glad you kept the name. Thomas said he was glad too. She said: you  
were always more of a Hale than he was, though I do not know what a Hale is exactly.  
Thomas said he was not entirely sure either. She said: keep asking. She was asleep  
within the hour and did not wake.
Thomas Marsh-Hale — his journal, 28th June, 1914
The Archduke is dead in Sarajevo. I do not know yet what this means. I know that  
every political journalist in London is writing about it today and that most of them

are writing about the wrong things — the assassination, the Austro-Hungarian  
response, the Russian position — rather than about the thing underneath all of it,  
which is a set of treaty obligations and military timetables that have been building  
for twenty years and which now, in the absence of anyone sufficiently powerful to  
stop them, will run to their conclusion.
I wrote a piece this morning about the structural conditions that make a large war  
possible and emailed it to my editor, who wrote back to say it was very interesting  
but that readers wanted to know about the crisis, not the structure. I wrote back to  
say the crisis was the structure. He has not yet replied.
Ruth asked at breakfast why the paper had a picture of a man in uniform on the  
front. I told her a man had been killed. She said: which man? I said: an important  
man. She said: are important men killed more often than unimportant ones? I said:  
no, but when they are killed it matters more, or at least more people notice. She  
considered this for a while and then said: that does not seem fair. I said: no, it does  
not.
THE TIMES
Tuesday, 4th August, 1914
BRITAIN AT WAR — ULTIMATUM TO GERMANY EXPIRES AT MIDNIGHT
Great Britain declared war on Germany at eleven o'clock last night, the  
ultimatum requiring Germany to withdraw its forces from Belgian territory having  
expired without reply.
His Majesty's Government has communicated to all departments the following: that  
a state of war exists between Great Britain and Germany as of midnight, 4th  
August, 1914, and that all subjects of His Majesty are called upon to conduct  
themselves accordingly.
In the House of Commons last night, the Foreign Secretary Sir Edward Grey  
addressed a full House in terms that were reported to have moved many Members to  
tears, observing that the lamps are going out all over Europe and that we shall  
not see them lit again in our lifetime.
Recruiting offices throughout the country were reported to be open early this  
morning.
Thomas did not enlist in August 1914. He was forty-one years old and had a wife  
and two children and a medical history — mild but persistent bronchitis, recurring  
since childhood — that gave him a legitimate basis for exemption. He also had, as he  
wrote to Daniel in September, a profound conviction that the war was exactly what

he had predicted it would be: the structural consequence of a system that had been  
building toward this for twenty years, and that sending himself to die in a field in  
Belgium would resolve neither the structure nor his analysis of it.
Daniel, who had received a commission in the West African Frontier Force and  
was preparing to go to Cameroon, wrote back: I know. But I am going anyway.  
Thomas wrote: I know you are. God go with you. Daniel wrote: and with you. Write to  
me. Thomas wrote: I always do.
He wrote six columns a week for the duration. He wrote about the food supply,  
the munitions shortage, the mismanagement of the Dardanelles campaign, the  
conditions  at  the  front  as  reported  to  him  by  correspondents  he  trusted,  the  
economic management of the war. He was censored twice and threatened with  
prosecution once. He continued. He was, he wrote to Rachel, not at all certain he was  
doing the right thing, but he was doing the only thing he knew how to do, and that  
would have to be sufficient.
—  —  —

V
Conscription
London and the Somme, 1916
The Military Service Act came into force in January 1916. Thomas was forty-two.  
He was no longer exempt on medical grounds — the standards had been revised  
downward as the need increased — and his age now fell within the conscription  
range. He received his papers in March.
He had known it was coming. He had written about it — about the politics of  
conscription, about the difference between voluntary service and compelled service,  
about what it meant for a liberal democracy to compel its citizens to kill and be killed  
— with the same analytical precision he brought to everything, and had arrived at a  
position that he found intellectually coherent and personally very difficult: that  
conscription was, in the circumstances, defensible, and that he would therefore go.
He told Rachel first. Rachel said: I know. He said: I have to. She said: I know. He  
said: I am sorry. She said: I know that too. They sat for a while in silence. Then she  
said: come back. He said: I intend to. She said: intending is not the same as  
managing. He said: no, it is not. They sat again for a while.
Ruth was seven. Edmund was five. Thomas left for training in April 1916.
ATTESTATION PAPER — THOMAS MARSH-HALE — ARMY FORM B.  
2512
Name: Thomas Marsh-Hale. Age: 42 years, 11 months. Occupation: Journalist.  
Address: 14 Loughborough Road, Brixton, London.
In what trade or calling have you been employed? Journalism (print). Can you  
read? Yes. Can you write? Yes. Are you willing to be vaccinated or re-vaccinated?  
Yes.
Medical examination: Height 5 ft 9 in. Chest measurement: 36 in. expanded.  
Physical development: Good. Defects: Slight chronic bronchitis, noted. Grade: A  
(fit for general service).
Questions to be put to the Recruit before Enlistment: Are you willing to serve upon  
the General Service for the period of the war? Answer: Yes. Do you understand that

you are liable to serve in any part of the world? Answer: Yes.
Signature of recruit: T. Marsh-Hale. Date: 14th April, 1916. Before me: [illegible  
signature], Recruiting Officer.
[In the margin, in Thomas's hand: Rachel. Ruth. Edmund. Dan. — Keep going.]
Thomas trained at Aldershot from April to August 1916 and arrived in France in  
September, six weeks before the Battle of the Somme ended. He was assigned to the  
2nd Battalion of the Royal Fusiliers as a private — he had declined, quietly and  
persistently, any suggestion that his age and education entitled him to a commission  
— and spent the winter of 1916 in trenches south of Arras.
His letters from France are not like his letters from London. The analytical  
register that characterised everything he had written before is present in them — he  
was incapable of writing without thinking, and he continued to think even in  
conditions that had reduced more classically trained minds to silence — but it is  
present in a different proportion. The sentences are shorter. The arguments are  
more compressed. There is less qualification, not because he had become less  
precise but because precision, in those conditions, required fewer words.
He wrote to Rachel every week without fail. He wrote to Daniel — who was now in  
East Africa — when the post permitted. He wrote to Ruth three times, short letters  
that told her nothing useful about where he was or what was happening and  
everything necessary about the fact that he was alive and thinking of her.
Thomas Marsh-Hale to Rachel Marsh-Hale — October, 1916
Rachel. I am well. I am cold. I am writing this in a hole in the ground which is not,  
technically, a hole but which feels like one, and the candle is very short so I will be  
brief.
I have been thinking about what I will write when this is over, if it is over and if I am  
in a condition to write. I have been thinking about it because thinking about it is the  
only thing that makes any of this legible to me. I am surrounded by men who are  
managing it through other means — faith, or numbness, or the specific kind of  
humour that consists of saying the bleakest possible thing in the flattest possible  
voice — and I respect all of those means. Mine is to try to understand what is  
happening, even when what is happening is so large and so catastrophic that  
understanding it feels like trying to understand the weather by measuring a single  
drop of rain.
What I understand so far is this: the men beside me are ordinary. They are not

heroes or villains or symbols. They are men who were doing something else in 1913  
and who are here now because the thing I spent ten years writing about — the  
structure, the arithmetic — ran to its conclusion. That is the piece I need to write.  
Not about the trenches. About the arithmetic that produced them.
Tell Ruth I am wearing the scarf. Tell Edmund the frog he found in the garden has  
not followed me here, which I believe is a mercy for everyone concerned.
Thomas. All my love. All of it.
Thomas was wounded in April 1917, at Arras. A shell fragment took a piece from  
his left shoulder and another from his hip. He was in a field hospital for six weeks and  
then, because the shoulder wound refused to fully heal, invalided back to England in  
June 1917.
He arrived at Victoria Station on a Tuesday morning, thinner and moving with the  
slight stiffness that the hip wound had left him, and Rachel was on the platform. Ruth  
was in school. Edmund was at Clara's sister's. They stood on the platform for a while  
without saying anything particular and then went home.
—  —  —

VI
What He Brought Back
London, 1917–1918
Thomas spent the autumn of 1917 recovering and writing. The recovery was  
slow; the shoulder wound had damaged something deeper than the muscle and he  
was left with a persistent ache that flared in cold weather and a range of motion that  
was never quite what it had been. The writing was faster than he expected, because  
he had been composing it in his head for a year in conditions that left the mind very  
little to do but compose.
The book — eventually published in 1919 as The Cost of the System — was not, as  
he had said to Rachel it would be, a book about the trenches. It was a book about the  
thirty years before the trenches: the arms race, the alliance system, the economics of  
imperial competition, the specific political failures that had allowed the machine to  
be built. It used the tools he had developed in his journalism — precise, evidenced,  
unsparing  —  and  it  reached  a  conclusion  that  was  not,  by  1919,  particularly  
surprising but that he stated with a clarity that most people found either bracing or  
infuriating depending on their position: that the war had been avoidable, that its  
avoidability had been known to the people who could have avoided it, and that the  
reason they had not avoided it was structural and not merely moral, which meant  
that the mourning for its victims, however sincere, was insufficient if it was not  
accompanied by a change in the structure.
He was at home for the Armistice. Rachel made tea. Ruth, who was ten, asked him  
what it meant. He said: it means the shooting has stopped. She said: does that mean  
it is over? He said: it means that part of it is over. She said: what does the other part  
mean? He said: I think it means that all the things that caused it are still there, and  
that we will have to decide whether to change them. She said: will we? He said: I do  
not know. I think it depends on whether people remember what it cost.
POST OFFICE TELEGRAPHS
TO: THOMAS MARSH-HALE, 14 LOUGHBOROUGH ROAD BRIXTON LONDON
FROM: CAPT. DANIEL OKAFOR, EAST AFRICA COMMAND
DATE: 12 NOV 1918
ARMISTICE SIGNED STOP I AM ALIVE STOP YOU ARE ALIVE STOP

THIS SEEMS IMPROBABLE BUT I WILL TAKE IT STOP
COMING HOME IN JANUARY STOP EXPECT LONG ARGUMENTS STOP
DANIEL
Daniel came home in January 1919. They had the long arguments. Thomas's book  
was published in March. It sold moderately well, was reviewed with the respect that  
was extended to books about the war and read with the attention that was extended  
to books that said difficult things simply, and made Thomas, for the first time in his  
career, genuinely uncomfortable with the attention.
He was forty-six years old. His shoulder ached. His son Edmund was nine years  
old and had decided he wanted to be an engineer, which Thomas regarded as a  
rational and admirable response to having spent the first decade of one's life  
watching the world break down. His daughter Ruth was eleven and had decided she  
wanted to be a lawyer, which Thomas regarded as an even more rational and  
admirable response to the same experience, and said so, which made Rachel laugh.
He wrote to the Alderton and Co. firm in Shrewsbury in the spring of 1919, on an  
impulse he could not quite explain. He asked whether there was any archive material  
relating to the Hale family that had not been included in the legacy settlement of  
1893. He did not know precisely what he was looking for. He thought of his mother  
saying: I do not know what a Hale is exactly. He thought of himself saying: I am not  
entirely sure either. He thought of her saying: keep asking.
Alderton and Co. replied two weeks later. They said that the Harton Hall archive,  
upon the sale of the estate in 1891, had been transferred to a collector named Sir  
Augustus Frere, who had catalogued it and subsequently deposited the whole of it  
with the Bodleian Library at Oxford. They provided the reference number.
Thomas wrote to the Bodleian the following day.
—  —  —

VII
The Archive
Oxford, 1919
Thomas went to Oxford in September 1919. He had not been to Oxford before —  
it was not, in the normal course of events, a place a Camberwell shipping clerk's  
trajectory would have taken him — and he arrived with the slight discomfort of a man  
entering a building he knows he has a right to be in but where the architecture has  
been designed to suggest otherwise. He found the Bodleian, found the reading room,  
found the archivist, and asked for the Hale family papers, reference Frere 14/H.
They brought him three boxes.
He spent four days in the reading room. He read everything in order: the legal  
instruments first, because that was his habit when approaching an archive —  
establish the structure before the story. Then the correspondence. Then the journals  
and the personal documents.
He read Nathaniel Hale's memoirs, transcribed from the 1743 dictation. He read  
Edmund Hale's letters, including the sealed confession that Sir Augustus Frere had  
copied into the catalogue with a note that the original was secured separately. He  
read the  Civil  War brothers'  correspondence.  He  read Augusta Hale's private  
journal, including the entry about being fifty-seven years old and married and having  
a husband who found her more interesting than a drawing-room accomplishment.
He read Thomas Hale's remembrance of 1382, transcribed by Sir Augustus from  
a much-copied original: we are a family that holds its land and keeps its word and  
comes home when it could go elsewhere and sees to the pig.
On the fourth day he reached the bottom of the third box and found, in a small  
envelope labelled in Sir Augustus Frere's hand — Matilda's letter. Probable date  
c.1390. Found beneath the deed-box lining by George Edward Hale, 1858. Original  
extremely fragile — a transcription in George Edward's hand, and beneath it, in what  
had been a separate envelope, a small folded piece of material so old it had been  
mounted between two pieces of glass to preserve it.
He read George Edward's transcription. He sat with the mounted original for a  
long time, looking at the faded marks that constituted writing without being able to  
read them.
Then he took out his notebook and wrote.

Thomas Marsh-Hale — his journal, September 1919, Oxford
I have been here four days reading the history of a family I did not know I was part of  
until I was twelve years old and have not thought much about since, being occupied  
with other things, and I find that I am moved in a way I did not anticipate and cannot  
entirely explain.
The family is not remarkable. No Hale has been famous or powerful or historically  
significant in any way that would appear in the ordinary histories. They have been  
farmers, solicitors, soldiers, a Georgian adventurer, a Victorian hypocrite, a woman  
of extraordinary intelligence who was given almost nothing to work with, brothers  
who  fought  on  opposite  sides  of  a  civil  war  and  kept  writing  to  each  other  
throughout.
What they have been, consistently, for nine centuries, is present. They came home  
when they could have gone elsewhere. They kept faith with the land and the name  
and with the question — the question I have been asking since I was twelve, which I  
now understand is the family question, the one that Ranulf de la Hale asked on his  
deathbed in 1115 and that my father asked in a sealed room in Shropshire in 1882  
and that I have been asking in my own way ever since — of whether what one has  
done is sufficient.
I do not think I have answered it. I think the old beekeeper was right: the asking is  
the point. The family that stops asking loses something, though I am not sure now  
that it is the land. I think it is something harder to name than the land.
I am a Hale. I have been one all along, without knowing it. I did not inherit the name  
from my father in any way he intended. I have been this thing by nature rather than  
by performance, which is perhaps the only way to be it that counts.
My mother said keep asking. She knew this. She always knew more than she let on.
Thomas returned to London. He wrote a letter to his son Edmund — nine years  
old, future engineer — telling him that he had found their family's history and that it  
was rather extraordinary and that he would explain it when Edmund was old enough  
to sit still for the explanation, which he thought might be some years yet. Edmund  
wrote back, in the careful handwriting of a nine-year-old, to say that he was old  
enough now and when could he go to Oxford?
Thomas wrote to the Bodleian and arranged for copies of the key documents to be  
made for his own archive. He wrote to Daniel, who said it sounded like the best story  
he had never been told and when was Thomas going to write it. Thomas said he did  
not know how to write it; it was not journalism and it was not history and it was not  
fiction and he was not certain it was any one thing. Daniel said: write it anyway. It

can be several things at once. Thomas wrote back: that is the most useful thing  
anyone has said to me since the war.
He lived until 1951. He did not write the family history in any form that was  
finished. What he left behind was a box of notes, the journal entries, and a letter  
addressed to his children and their children, which his daughter Ruth — who did  
become a lawyer, and who was seventy-two years old in 1969 when she found the box  
— read in one sitting at her kitchen table in Islington and then sat with for a long  
time.
The letter said, in the last paragraph: I do not know what it means to be a Hale. I  
know that it involves asking a question that does not have a clean answer, and that  
the asking matters more than the answering, and that the thing the family has always  
called keeping the land is not really about land. It is about the willingness to stay  
with the difficult thing rather than going where things are easier. That is what  
Matilda meant. That is what they all meant, from the Norman soldier in the hall in  
1066 to my father in his locked room, though my father had lost the thread by the  
end. I have tried to hold it. I think you will hold it better.
—  —  —

VIII
On the English of the Early Twentieth Century
A Note on the Language of this Volume
The  language  of  this volume  does something that  no previous  volume  in this  
chronicle has done: it changes register within itself. The letters of 1889 are written  
in the elaborate, subordinated English of the late Victorian period — long sentences,  
careful qualification, the rhetorical architecture of a man who learned to write from  
reading Carlyle and Mill. The letters of 1916 are written in something quite different:  
shorter sentences, no excess, the kind of prose that has had the ornament stripped  
from it by the specific experience of standing in a trench in Belgium and trying to  
write something true.
This is not a deterioration. It is a development. The English of the First World War —  
in the letters and the prose of the men who went through it and survived — is  
perhaps  the  most  honest  the  language  has  produced,  precisely  because  the  
conditions that produced it were so hostile to any kind of performance. When there is  
nothing to perform for, the performance stops. What remains is the thing itself.
Thomas Marsh-Hale's writing follows this arc with particular clarity because he was  
a professional writer rather than a reluctant one, and because he was conscious of  
the changes in his own prose and periodically noted them. In a letter to Daniel from  
December 1916 he wrote: I notice that my sentences are getting shorter. I do not  
think this is because I have less to say. I think it is because I am saying it more  
directly, which is not the same as saying less. I think I have been writing around  
things for twenty years and the war has taught me to write at them.
The other development in the English of this period is what the critic Virginia Woolf  
— writing in 1919, the year Thomas went to Oxford — called the shift in human  
character: the sense that after 1918 the English language was trying to describe a  
world that the pre-war language did not quite fit. The machinery that the Victorians  
had built — political, economic, social, and linguistic — had been exposed as  
machinery, and the language that had been designed to serve it and to obscure its  
workings had been similarly exposed. The Modernists were already building the  
replacement: Joyce's Dublin, Eliot's waste land, Woolf's streams of consciousness.  
Thomas was not a Modernist, but he was reading them, and their influence on his  
later  journalism  is  visible  —  in  the  increased  willingness  to  let  the  observed  
particular carry more weight than the general argument, in the growing distrust of  
large systematic statements in favour of the specific and the concrete.

The next volume — Book IX — will be written in the English of the mid-twentieth  
century, which is in some ways the end point of what this book begins: the language  
after the ornament has been stripped, after the Victorian edifice has fallen, after the  
performance has been recognised as a performance. It is the English of Orwell and  
Waugh and Elizabeth Bowen: flat, precise, knowing what it knows and saying it  
without fuss. It is the English that Thomas Marsh-Hale was moving toward when he  
stopped writing.
—  —  —

GLOSSARY
Words, people, and events which the text employs and the reader may wish explained
The Education 
Act of 1870Also  called  the  Forster  Act,  after  its  parliamentary  sponsor.  It  
established  a  system  of  publicly  funded  elementary  schools  across  
England  and  Wales,  filling  the  gaps  left  by  the  existing  voluntary  
(church) schools. It did not make attendance compulsory — that came  
with the Education Act of 1880 — but it created the infrastructure. The  
board schools it established were the first secular publicly funded  
schools in England, and they produced the first generation of working-
class men who were universally literate, of whom Thomas Marsh-Hale  
was one example among millions.
The Fabian 
SocietyA socialist intellectual organisation founded in London in 1884, whose  
members included George Bernard Shaw, H.G. Wells, Beatrice and  
Sidney Webb, and many others. The Fabians believed in gradual reform  
through democratic political action rather than revolutionary change,  
and their influence on the early Labour Party and on the development of  
the  welfare  state  was  considerable.  Thomas's  ambivalence  about  
Fabianism — he agreed with their analysis and was sceptical of their  
confidence in benevolent expert management — was widely shared by  
working-class  radicals  who  found  the  Fabians  somewhat  too  
comfortable with the existing power structures.
Seebohm 
Rowntree (1871–
1954)Social researcher and businessman. His 1901 study Poverty: A Study of  
Town Life, based on extensive empirical research in York, demonstrated  
that approximately thirty per cent of the population of York lived in  
poverty, and that the primary cause of poverty was not idleness or  
improvidence but wages that were structurally insufficient to meet basic  
needs. The study was enormously influential on the development of  
social policy in the early twentieth century and established the scientific  
study of poverty as a legitimate discipline. Thomas Marsh-Hale's 1903  
piece for The Clarion draws directly on Rowntree's methods.
The Military 
Service Act of 
1916The legislation that introduced compulsory military service in Britain for  
the first time, initially applying to single men between eighteen and  
forty-one, later extended to married men and to higher age ranges as the  
war  continued.  Exemptions  were  available  on  grounds  of  essential  
occupation, medical unfitness, or conscientious objection. The Act was  
deeply controversial — Britain had a strong tradition of voluntarism in

military service — and its introduction was a measure of how severely  
the voluntary system had failed to meet the demands of industrial  
warfare.
The Battle of the 
SommeThe major British-led offensive on the Somme River in northern France,  
lasting from 1st July to 18th November 1916. On the first day alone, the  
British  Army  suffered  approximately  57,000  casualties,  of  whom  
approximately 19,000 were killed: the worst single day in the history of  
the British Army. By the time the offensive ended, total casualties on all  
sides exceeded one million. The battle advanced the British line by a  
maximum of seven miles.
Vimy Ridge / 
Arras 1917The Battle of Arras, April–May 1917, of which the Canadian Corps'  
capture of Vimy Ridge was the most celebrated action. Thomas was  
wounded at Arras in April 1917. The battle as a whole was a qualified  
tactical success, achieving its initial objectives at heavy cost and then  
failing to break through the German lines as intended.
The Bodleian 
LibraryThe main research library of the University of Oxford, founded in its  
current form in 1602 though the university had maintained a library on  
the site since the fifteenth century. By 1919 it had the status of a  
copyright library, entitled to receive a copy of every book published in  
the United Kingdom, and housed one of the largest archival collections  
in the country. The deposit of the Hale family papers — a fictional  
collection in the context of this chronicle, though modelled on real  
archival collections of the period — would have been the appropriate  
disposition for a collection of this kind.
Virginia Woolf 
(1882–1941)Novelist  and  essayist.  Born  the  same  year  that  Edmund  Hale  
disappeared. Author of Mrs. Dalloway, To the Lighthouse, The Waves,  
and the essay A Room of One's Own (1929), among others. Her 1924  
essay Mr. Bennett and Mrs. Brown contains the famous observation that  
'in  or  about  December,  1910,  human  character  changed.'  Thomas  
Marsh-Hale was reading Woolf in the early 1920s; her presence in his  
later journalism is discernible in the increasing weight he gives to the  
particular and the observed at the expense of the systemic and the  
argued.
—  —  —
