THE HALE DYNASTY
BOOK IX
The Name
Being a Collection of Letters, Civil Service Memoranda,
Wartime Notices, Newspaper Extracts, Journal Entries,
and Sundry Documents pertaining to Edmund Marsh-Hale
of London and the Ministry of Supply.
Covering the years
1933 — 1960
———
Editor's Prefatory Note
Edmund Marsh-Hale was born in Brixton on the fourteenth of August, 1910. He was  
the second and younger child of Thomas Marsh-Hale — his sister Ruth, born 1908,  
was the elder — and only son — journalist, veteran, the man who found the family  
archive in a reading room in Oxford in 1919 — and Rachel Simons, whose practicality  
had always been the ground beneath Thomas's analysis. He grew up in a household  
where clear thinking was the primary value and obscurity was treated as either a  
failure of intelligence or a failure of nerve, neither of which was acceptable.
He chose engineering because he wanted to work with things whose properties  
could be verified. Steel has a yield strength that is either sufficient or not sufficient;  
concrete cures to a compressive strength that either meets the specification or falls  
short. There is, in structural engineering, a directness between what you calculate  
and what happens in the world that Edmund found deeply satisfying and that his  
father — who had spent twenty years calculating the arithmetic of poverty and  
watching the arithmetic be ignored — found quietly admirable.
The years this volume covers are the years in which England itself was tested for  
structural sufficiency. The Depression, the approach of war, the war itself, the Blitz,  
the austerity of reconstruction — each one a loading condition, each one producing a  
result that could be measured or at least honestly observed. Edmund Marsh-Hale  
spent all of these years in his professional capacity doing what structural engineers

do: calculating what the structure could bear, reporting the result accurately, and  
proposing what needed to change if the result was insufficient.
The language of this volume is the language Thomas had been moving toward for  
thirty years and which Edmund simply inhabited from the beginning: flat, precise,  
direct. Orwell called it the plain style and wrote a manifesto for it in 1946. Edmund  
had been practising it since 1933 without having read the manifesto, because it was  
the only style available to a man who had been taught that decoration was what you  
reached for when the evidence was insufficient.
His evidence was generally sufficient. He did not reach for decoration. This made  
him, in the words of his Ministry colleague Howard Brent, the most useful engineer  
in the building and occasionally the most inconvenient man in the room. The  
distinction was, in Edmund's judgment, not a distinction at all.
— compiled from the Marsh-Hale papers, deposited 1974

I
The Engineering of Things
London and the Midlands, 1933–1938
Edmund took his degree from Imperial College in June 1933. He had been the  
best student in his year at the mathematics of structural analysis, and the second  
best  at  the  drawings,  which  he  had  found  harder  —  not  because  his  spatial  
understanding  was  poor  but  because  drawing  required  a  patience  with  the  
approximate that the mathematics did not, and Edmund had a strong preference for  
the exact. His tutor, a man named Hartley who had designed three railway bridges  
and who found Edmund's precision more promising than irritating, told him at his  
final assessment that he had the making of a very good engineer provided he learned  
to work with what the materials actually were rather than what the specification  
stated they should be. Edmund thanked him and said he would try. He spent the next  
forty years trying, which is what good engineering is.
England in 1933 was not a good country to be beginning a career in. The  
Depression had reduced construction activity by approximately a third from its 1929  
peak, and the third it had reduced it by was precisely the third that employed young  
graduate engineers on new work rather than the maintenance and repair of old  
work. Edmund found his first position — a junior engineer at a small consultancy  
called  Bramley  and  Sons  in  Holborn  —  only  after  four  months  of  systematic  
searching, which he conducted with the same methodical attention he brought to a  
structural calculation: one approach, one result, revise the approach, try again.
Bramley and Sons had survived the Depression by doing whatever structural  
work was available, regardless of scale or glamour, which meant that Edmund spent  
his  first  two  years  checking  the  load-bearing  capacity  of  existing  floors  in  
commercial buildings, visiting factories to assess the structural implications of new  
machinery, and writing reports that said either this is adequate or this is not  
adequate, which was precisely the kind of work that his tutor had prescribed and  
that Edmund found genuinely interesting in the way that close attention to real  
things is always genuinely interesting.
He was good at it. What he discovered, in those first two years, was that the gap  
between what a structure was designed to do and what it actually did under the  
specific conditions of its actual use was almost always larger than the design had  
assumed, and that most of the structural failures he was called to assess were not the

result  of  bad  calculation  but  of  the  accumulation  of  small  deviations  from  
specification — the floor joist that was two per cent undersize, the concrete mix that  
had been poured slightly wet on a cold day, the column that bore a load three per  
cent higher than the plan had shown because a wall had been moved during  
construction  and  nobody  had  updated  the  drawings.  His  reports  on  these  
assessments  were  precise,  comprehensive,  and  occasionally  unwelcome  to  the  
building owners who had commissioned them, who had expected to hear that  
everything was fine.
Everything was usually fine, in the sense that the building was not about to fall  
down. It was not always fine in the sense that the building was in the condition its  
owners believed it to be in, which is a different matter and one that Edmund  
considered worth distinguishing.
Edmund Marsh-Hale to his father Thomas Marsh-Hale — February, 1934
Father, I want to tell you about something I have noticed in the work here that I  
think you will recognise from a different angle. The buildings I am assessing are all,  
in some degree, performing adequately. None of them has collapsed. None of them  
is in immediate danger of collapse. But almost all of them have accumulated, over  
their  working  lives,  a  set  of  small  deficiencies  —  minor  under-specifications,  
gradual deterioration of fixings, incremental additions of load — that the original  
calculations did not account for, because the original calculations were made for  
the building as designed rather than the building as it actually came to exist.
This is not negligence. It is the ordinary condition of things in use. But it means that  
the building's actual capacity — what it can actually bear, today, in its actual  
condition — is somewhat less than its designed capacity, and nobody has measured  
the difference. They have trusted the original calculation and stopped there.
I think you would say this is the arithmetic problem in a different register. The  
structure is performing. The performance is being reported as adequate on the  
basis of the original design rather than the current reality. The gap between the  
reported condition and the actual condition accumulates quietly until something  
happens.
I do not have a solution. I have an accurate description. I think that is where you  
always start.
Edmund.

Thomas's reply — he was sixty years old, still writing, still clear — said: yes. That  
is exactly the arithmetic problem. The question is always who benefits from not  
measuring the gap. Edmund wrote back: in buildings, usually the owner. Thomas:  
and in wages, usually the employer. Edmund: yes. I thought it was probably the same  
principle.
Edmund moved to a larger firm in 1936 and spent the following eighteen months  
in  Birmingham  and  Coventry,  overseeing  the  concrete  pouring  for  two  new  
manufacturing plants. He was based in Birmingham for the duration, in lodgings  
near the city centre, and he spent his evenings in the city's public library reading and  
his weekends walking the industrial districts with the systematic attention of a man  
who has learned to look at what is actually there. What was actually there — the  
back-to-back terraces, the smoke-darkened brick, the men going into and coming out  
of the factories in their work clothes with the particular expression of sustained  
fatigue that is different from the expression of acute fatigue — confirmed everything  
Thomas had written in 1903 and in 1912 and in The Cost of the System, which  
Edmund had read three times and which had taught him, as much as anything else,  
what it meant to look at a thing and say plainly what you saw.
He came back to London in the autumn of 1937. He was twenty-seven years old,  
thin, precise, and had opinions about the condition of the industrial Midlands that he  
had written in his notebook and had not said aloud to anyone, there being no one in  
the firm who was particularly interested in hearing them. He said them to Thomas  
over dinner on the first Saturday of his return. Thomas listened without interrupting,  
which was his habit when someone was saying something worth hearing, and at the  
end said: write it down. Edmund said: I have written it down. Thomas: then keep it.  
You will need it later. Edmund did not know at the time what later meant.
In October 1938, twelve months before the war, Edmund was recruited into the  
Ministry of Supply as a technical officer in the industrial capacity division. He was  
twenty-eight years old. The salary was six hundred pounds per annum, which was  
more than he had expected and which meant, practically, that he could afford a flat  
of his own in London rather than lodgings, which he had been in one form or another  
since 1933. He took a flat in Finsbury Park and began, for the first time in his  
working life, to use his full capacity for the work in front of him.
———

II
Before the War
Ministry of Supply, London, 1938–1939
The industrial capacity division of the Ministry of Supply was, in the autumn of  
1938, a relatively small operation staffed by a mixture of engineers, economists, and  
civil servants who had been assembled with varying degrees of urgency and varying  
levels of technical qualification to answer a question that nobody in government had  
yet formally admitted was being asked: if Britain went to war with Germany, what  
could British industry produce, and how quickly, and what would need to be built or  
converted to produce the rest?
Edmund's function was to know the answer to the first part of this question for  
the manufacturing facilities relevant to his portfolio, which in the early months was  
primarily the production of artillery shells and small arms ammunition. He visited  
factories. He assessed their capacity. He tested the accuracy of the production  
figures the factories reported against what the physical configuration of the plant,  
the available labour, and the speed of the machinery actually permitted. He found,  
systematically and without surprise, that the reported figures were optimistic, that  
the optimism was not dishonest but structural — the figures had been compiled by  
people  who  believed  their  equipment  ran  at  specification  and  their  workers  
maintained their maximum pace — and that the accurate figures were considerably  
lower.
He wrote reports that said so. His section head, a man named Graves who was a  
competent administrator and an experienced political operator, read these reports  
with the expression of a man who had been given information he had already  
calculated would be inconvenient and who was now deciding what to do with it. He  
passed the reports upward, with a covering note that Edmund was not shown but  
that Edmund could infer from the questions that came back down: are these figures  
conservative? No, they are accurate. Can they be improved in the short term? Some  
of them, with specific investments. Which ones, on what timeline, at what cost?  
Edmund wrote those reports too.
He understood what was happening around him. The government was preparing  
for a war it could not yet publicly acknowledge was coming, using an industrial base  
that was not configured for war production, and was managing the gap between  
what it knew and what it was saying with the specific kind of institutional caution

that produces, in the short term, a degree of stability and, in the longer term, a crisis  
that is larger than it needed to be because nobody said the accurate thing early  
enough.
He was aware that he was one of the people saying the accurate thing early  
enough. He was also aware, with the realism of a man who had grown up in a  
household that understood the relationship between accurate analysis and actual  
power, that saying the accurate thing was necessary and not sufficient. You could not  
compel an institution to act on an accurate report. You could only make sure the  
report existed, was on record, and was undeniable when the moment came to consult  
it.
Edmund Marsh-Hale — his journal, 12th March, 1939
Six months in. I have now assessed forty-three factories in nine counties. I have  
written twenty-seven reports. Eleven of them have had their figures revised before  
being passed upward. In each case the revision was upward. In each case I was  
consulted about the revision. In each case I said the revised figure was not accurate.  
In nine cases the revision was retained in the final report. In two cases my original  
figure was restored.
I do not raise this as a complaint. I raise it as a description of the mechanism. The  
mechanism produces a picture that is more reassuring than the accurate picture.  
The reassuring picture will eventually be tested against reality. When it is tested, the  
gap between the picture and the reality will need to be closed quickly and at  
considerable cost. This is predictable. It is not, from my position, preventable.
What is preventable, and what I am attempting, is for my own section's figures to be  
accurate and documented at every stage, so that when the gap needs closing, the  
baseline is not in dispute. If I cannot prevent the optimism, I can at least ensure that  
my section is not its source.
Father would have something to say about this. Something precise and slightly bitter  
and ultimately constructive. I think I know what it would be. I think it would be: do  
the work. Keep the record. That is what there is.
Thomas came to the Ministry canteen for lunch in May 1939, which was the kind  
of arrangement that required considerable organisation on both sides and produced,  
when it happened, the particular quality of conversation that Edmund had been  
having with his father since he was nine years old: precise, unsentimental, reaching  
toward honest conclusions rather than comfortable ones. Thomas ate his sandwich  
and listened to Edmund's account of the production figures and the revised figures

and the gap between them. Then he said: what are the factories actually capable of in  
wartime conditions?
Edmund said: I don't know exactly. Somewhere between my figures and the  
revised figures. Closer to mine. Thomas: and the plan is based on the revised figures.  
Edmund: yes. Thomas: so the plan is wrong. Edmund: the plan is optimistic. Thomas:  
same thing, with better manners. Edmund: yes.
Thomas was quiet for a moment, eating his sandwich. Then he said: your  
grandfather would have found this familiar. Edmund: which grandfather? Thomas:  
the Norman one. Edmund: I'm not sure what a Norman soldier in 1066 has to do with  
shell production in 1939. Thomas: he was given something that wasn't configured for  
the purpose it was needed for, and he had to make it work anyway, and he did.  
Edmund: that's a very long way to say keep going. Thomas: it usually is.
———

III
The War Begins
London, September 1939–May 1940
The war began on the third of September 1939, at eleven in the morning, in the  
voice of Neville Chamberlain on the wireless. Edmund heard it in his flat in Finsbury  
Park, sitting in his only armchair with a cup of tea that had gone cold while he was  
listening. Chamberlain sounded tired, which was accurate. He sounded regretful,  
which was also accurate. He sounded like a man who had used every available means  
to prevent the thing that was now happening and had run out of available means,  
which Edmund thought was the most accurate description of the previous twelve  
months in British foreign policy.
He finished his cold tea, washed the cup, put on his coat, and walked to the  
Ministry. The duty officer at the desk nodded at him as he came in and said: expected  
you'd be in. Edmund said: where else would I be? The duty officer said: fair point.  
Edmund went upstairs and sat at his desk and began reviewing the production  
figures for the third quarter, which needed to be accurate, because they would  
shortly be the baseline against which everything else was measured.
The first eight months of the war — the period that newspapers later called the  
Phoney War, because the expected immediate catastrophe had not immediately  
materialised — were, from Edmund's perspective in the industrial capacity division,  
anything but phoney. They were the months in which the gap between the plan's  
assumptions and the industrial reality was being quietly measured and quietly  
acknowledged and quietly built into a revised plan that said, more clearly than  
anything that had been said publicly, that the original plan had been wrong and that  
the correction would require substantial effort and considerable time and more  
investment than had been originally allocated.
Edmund wrote three major reports in this period. Each one was longer than the  
last and more precisely documented. Each one had a section headed Current  
Constraint, which listed the specific bottleneck — the material shortage, the tooling  
limitation, the labour skill gap — that was preventing actual output from meeting  
planned  output.  Each  one  had  a  section  headed  Proposed  Resolution,  which  
described what would need to happen, at what cost, on what timeline, to close the  
gap. Each one was read by people above his section head's level, which Edmund

knew because the questions that came back were more technically sophisticated  
than the questions Graves asked.
THE TIMES
Monday, 4th September, 1939
BRITAIN AND FRANCE DECLARE WAR ON GERMANY
Great Britain is at war with Germany. The ultimatum demanding the withdrawal of  
German forces from Polish territory having received no reply, His Majesty's  
Government declared a state of war as of eleven o'clock yesterday morning,  
Sunday the third of September.
The Prime Minister, Mr. Neville Chamberlain, broadcast to the nation at eleven-
fifteen. France declared war simultaneously. The Dominions of Australia and New  
Zealand have signalled their immediate support.
The Cabinet met yesterday afternoon and again this morning. Mr. Winston  
Churchill has been appointed First Lord of the Admiralty. His appointment is  
widely interpreted as a signal of the seriousness of the Government's intent.
Recruiting offices throughout the country report orderly queues. Air raid  
precautions have been activated in all major cities. Gas masks are available  
from ARP posts. The blackout begins tonight at sunset.
Edmund was not called up. His work at the Ministry was designated as a reserved  
occupation — essential war work — and his specific technical knowledge was  
considered sufficiently valuable to the industrial capacity programme that replacing  
him with a less experienced officer was not considered a viable option. He was aware  
of this. He was also aware, in the particular way of men who have read their fathers'  
letters from the front, that the reserved occupation was not a neutral fact but a  
choice  with  consequences  that  ran  in  two  directions  simultaneously:  the  
consequence of staying and being useful, and the consequence of not going and  
knowing what went without you.
He did not agonise over this. His father had spent two years in France and had  
come back changed by it in ways that Edmund had been able to observe throughout  
his childhood, and the observation had produced in him a clarity about what the  
experience cost that prevented him from romanticising it. He was not, he told Ruth in  
a letter of October 1939, afraid to go. He was clear-eyed about what going would  
mean and equally clear-eyed about what staying and being useful would mean. He  
had been told to stay. He would stay. He would be as useful as possible. That was the  
arithmetic of the situation.

Ruth wrote back: it is the right decision. Not because you are afraid, which I  
know you are not, but because you are needed where you are and you know it.  
Father's generation went because they had no other way to be useful. You have one.  
Use it. Edmund kept the letter in his desk drawer for the duration.
———

IV
The Blitz
London, September 1940–May 1941
The Blitz began on the seventh of September, 1940. The Luftwaffe's shift from  
attacking RAF airfields to attacking London was, from the strategic perspective of  
the Battle of Britain, a German error that effectively conceded air superiority to  
Fighter  Command.  From  the  perspective  of  the  people  living  in  London,  this  
strategic  error  expressed  itself  as  fifty-seven  consecutive  nights  of  bombing,  
followed by sporadic but sustained raids through the following spring.
Edmund experienced the Blitz from three positions simultaneously. The first was  
his flat in Finsbury Park, which was close enough to the East End's heaviest bombing  
to make the raids audible and the sky over the city visible from his bedroom window  
as a flickering orange that was unlike any light he had seen before. The second was  
the Ministry shelter in the basement, where he slept four nights in seven on a camp  
bed with seven colleagues and one small stove, and where the work of the industrial  
capacity division continued largely uninterrupted because the work did not allow for  
interruption. The third position — the one he found hardest — was the one that  
required him to visit, in the weeks after a raid, the industrial facilities that had been  
damaged or destroyed, to assess what was lost and what could be salvaged and how  
the production figures needed to be revised.
He was good at this assessment. He was not unmoved by it. The factories he  
visited were not abstractions; they were the specific buildings whose floor loads he  
had calculated and whose production figures he had measured, and the workers in  
them  were  the  men  and  women  whose  faces  he  had  been  looking  at  since  
Birmingham. Standing in a bombed factory was not like standing in a bombed house,  
but it was not entirely unlike it either, and Edmund's reports from these assessments  
have a quality that his earlier reports do not: a precision that is doing extra work, the  
way precision always does when it is being used to manage something that precision  
alone cannot manage.
AIR RAID DAMAGE ASSESSMENT — MINISTRY OF SUPPLY, INDUSTRIAL CAPACITY  
DIVISION — OCTOBER, 1940

Site: Weston Engineering, Silvertown, East London. Date of raid: 7th-8th  
September 1940. Date of assessment: 14th September 1940. Assessing officer:  
E. Marsh-Hale.
Structural  damage:  Main  production  floor  —  roof  destroyed  over  
approximately 60% of area. Structural frame intact. Secondary production  
bay — wall collapse on north elevation, structural frame compromised at  
bays 4 and 5. Requires demolition of bays 4 and 5 before any reoccupation.
Plant and machinery: Two of four primary lathes destroyed. One overhead  
crane system destroyed. Press equipment: three of six units damaged, two  
beyond repair. One of two grinding lines intact.
Production capacity loss: Approximately 65% of pre-raid output. Recovery  
timeline: Partial recovery (40-50% of pre-raid) within 8-10 weeks with  
priority allocation of roofing materials and replacement machinery. Full  
recovery not possible at this site without reconstruction of structural  
bays 4 and 5: minimum 6 months.
Recommendation: Prioritise repair of structurally sound bays. Relocate  
production from bays 4 and 5 to undamaged facilities. Do not include this  
site  in  production  plans  at  pre-raid  capacity  until  structural  
reconstruction is complete.
Workers injured in raid: 7. Workers killed: 2. Their names were Arthur  
Croft and Mary Hennessy.
The names at the bottom of the assessment report were Edmund's addition. The  
standard form did not include them. He added them to every report he wrote that  
involved a facility where workers had been killed, not as a gesture — he was not a  
man much given to gestures — but because he considered it accurate to record the  
full cost of what had happened, and the full cost included people.
His section head Graves noticed the names on the third or fourth report and  
mentioned it, in the mild way of a man raising a procedural point rather than  
objecting to a principle. Edmund said: they were killed at the facility. The facility  
report should record it. Graves said: it is not standard form. Edmund said: I know. Is

there an objection to it? Graves said: no, I suppose not. Edmund said: then I will  
continue. He continued.
Thomas and Rachel were living in Brixton throughout the Blitz. Edmund visited  
every two weeks, taking the tube to Brixton station and walking the familiar streets  
that were changed now in the specific way of streets where things have been  
removed: gaps between houses where there had been houses, rubble cleared to a flat  
tidiness that was worse somehow than the rubble itself, the neighbourhood's fabric  
altered in ways that were not dramatic but were final.
Thomas was seventy years old. He was still writing, though more slowly. Rachel  
had refused to be evacuated, which Edmund understood and Ruth thought was  
foolish and which was not, in the end, a question that admitted of argument, because  
it was Rachel's decision and Rachel had always been the person in the family whose  
decisions were made once and not revisited.
She had, in those months, taken to cooking Sunday dinners for the neighbours on  
the street whose gas or electricity was intermittent, which was most of them. She did  
this with the same uncomplicated practicality she had brought to everything else,  
and Edmund, arriving on Sunday evenings to find the kitchen full of neighbours  
eating stew, thought of Sigefleda bringing salt pork to the hall in 1067, which he had  
read about in Thomas's copy of the archive and which was, he thought, the same  
instinct expressing itself eight centuries apart: when the structure fails, feed people.
Edmund Marsh-Hale — his journal, 15th November, 1940
The building three doors down from the flat went last night. I heard it go. The sound  
is not like I expected it to be from the descriptions. It is not a bang in the  
conventional sense. It is a concussion, a pressure, followed by a secondary sound  
that is the structure collapsing, which is the sound I recognise and which is worse  
than the first sound because I know exactly what it means.
I went out this morning. The building is gone. The two families in it — I knew their  
faces, not their names — were in the shelter. They are unhurt. The building is not.  
The first floor joists are lying in the street at an angle that tells me the east wall went  
first, which means the bomb was close to the east side. The party wall with number  
twenty-seven is standing but will need assessment before anyone goes near it.
I wrote a note to the borough engineer this morning with my preliminary assessment  
of the party wall. I do not know whether he received it or whether there is anyone at  
the borough engineer's office in a position to act on it. I know the party wall is  
dangerous. I have written it down. That is what there is.

Mother made dinner on Sunday. Eight people I have never met, eating stew in the  
kitchen. The street three doors down gone. The kitchen warm and full.
———

V
Rachel
Brixton, November 1941
Rachel Simons Marsh-Hale died on the seventeenth of November, 1941. She was  
sixty-two years old. She had been, for the previous three months, exactly what she  
had been for the previous sixty-two years: direct, warm, practical, and firmly of the  
view  that  the  arrangements  of  the  world,  however  inconvenient,  were  best  
addressed through attention and effort rather than complaint.
She died on a Monday night in a raid that hit Brixton at approximately half past  
eleven. Thomas had tried, as he had tried on every raid night since September 1940,  
to persuade her to go to the shelter on Acre Lane, which was two streets away and  
which had been reinforced in the summer. Rachel had said, as she had said on every  
raid night since September 1940, that she was too old to sleep on a cot and that  
nothing was going to fall on Brixton, which had been true for fourteen months and  
was not true on the seventeenth of November, 1941.
The house on Effra Road was hit at the rear. The kitchen and the back bedroom  
were destroyed. The front of the house was structurally intact. Thomas was in the  
front sitting room. Rachel was in the kitchen, making the cocoa that she made every  
night before bed because it was what she had always done and she saw no particular  
reason why a German bombing campaign should change it. Thomas was unhurt.  
Rachel was not found immediately; the ARP warden found her an hour later when the  
fire was out.
Thomas wrote to Edmund the following morning. The letter was six sentences.  
The first sentence said: Rachel is gone. The second said: she was in the kitchen. The  
third said: I was not with her at the end. The fourth said: the house is standing except  
at the back. The fifth said: I do not know what else to say. The sixth said: come when  
you can.
Edmund took the first train he could get and was at Effra Road by the early  
afternoon. Thomas was sitting in the front sitting room in his coat — the heating was  
off, the gas main being damaged — with the expression that Edmund had never seen  
on his father's face before and which he recognised, with the recognition of a  
structural engineer confronted with a failed component, as the expression of a man  
whose load-bearing element has been removed.

They did not talk much that afternoon. Edmund made tea on the small spirit stove  
he brought with him. He assessed the structural damage at the rear of the house —  
the kitchen wall would need rebuilding; the rear ceiling joist had come down but the  
main structure was sound — and told Thomas it was safe to stay if he wanted to stay.  
Thomas said he wanted to stay. Edmund stayed for two days, sleeping on the sofa,  
and then went back to the Ministry because the work was there and he could not do  
it from Brixton and Thomas told him to go, in the voice he used when he was  
managing something too large for ordinary speech.
Ruth came the following weekend and every weekend for the next month. She  
and Thomas ate whatever was available under the ration and talked, Ruth told  
Edmund in a letter, about Rachel — about specific things she had done, specific  
things she had said, the particular way she had of making a situation manageable by  
addressing it directly without diminishing it. They talked about her the way Thomas  
had written about the men beside him in France: as ordinary people doing what was  
required of them, not heroes, not symbols, just present and real and now gone.
Edmund Marsh-Hale to his sister Ruth Marsh-Hale — December, 1941
Ruth, I have been thinking about what to say about mother and have not been able  
to find the right words, which is unusual for me because I generally have words for  
things even if they are the wrong words. What I have instead is a series of images.  
The kitchen on Sunday evenings with the neighbours. The way she listened when  
Father was working something out — not impatiently, not deferentially, but with  
attention, the attention of someone who knows the value of what they are hearing.  
The look on her face when I told her about the Birmingham factories in 1936, the  
particular quality of unsurprised recognition, as if I were describing something she  
had always known.
She knew the arithmetic before he did, he said in the dedication. I think that is true.  
I think she knew it in a different register — not as analysis but as experience. She  
had lived the arithmetic for thirty years before he articulated it. The articulation  
was his. The knowledge was already hers.
I think the hardest thing about losing someone who has been a ground — a person  
you have always known was there, who made the other things possible by being  
present — is that the absence is not dramatic. It is structural. Something that was  
supporting everything else is no longer there, and everything above it is slightly  
altered in its position, and you keep noticing it, not in any one moment but in the  
ordinary sequence of moments that makes up a day.

Keep going over to Father. I will come as often as I can.
Edmund.
Thomas Marsh-Hale died in October 1942, eleven months after Rachel. He had  
been  ill  since  the  winter,  with  the  lung  complaint  that  had  troubled  him  
intermittently since the trenches and which had worsened in the cold and damp of  
the damaged Brixton house. He was sixty-nine years old. His last book — a short  
collection of essays on the structural lessons of the current war, written between  
January and July 1942 — was published posthumously in 1943, dedicated: To Rachel  
Simons, who knew the arithmetic before I did.
Edmund received the dedication copy in March 1943 and sat with it for a while.  
He thought it was the correct dedication. He thought his father had chosen the  
plainest possible words for the most important thing, which was what Thomas had  
always done and which was, Edmund thought, the quality he had most tried to  
inherit.
He put the book on the shelf beside The Cost of the System and went back to  
work.
———

VI
Dorothy
Ministry of Supply, London, 1943
Dorothy Price arrived in Edmund's section in February 1943. She had been at the  
Ministry of Aircraft Production since 1941 as a draughtswoman — one of several  
hundred women who had entered the technical drawing offices of the war ministries  
when the men who previously held those positions had been required elsewhere —  
and she had been transferred to the Ministry of Supply as part of a reorganisation  
that was, depending on who you asked, either a sensible rationalisation of technical  
resources or an attempt by the Ministry of Aircraft Production to transfer its most  
inconvenient employee to a department that might find her less so.
What made Dorothy Price inconvenient was not her work, which was excellent,  
but her habit of noticing when the work of others was wrong and saying so, directly  
and without the diplomatic preparation that the drawing office culture of the period  
had evolved to cushion such observations. She had, in two years at the Ministry of  
Aircraft Production, corrected twelve drawings submitted by engineers who were  
her seniors and been correct in eleven of those corrections. The twelfth was a  
judgment call that went against her on the technical committee, which she had  
accepted without apparent rancour but had noted in writing as disputed, and which  
turned out, six months later, to have been a case where her judgment was correct  
and the committee's was not.
She was twenty-six years old, the daughter of a Birmingham ironfounder who had  
taught her technical drawing from the age of eight because he had no sons and she  
had the aptitude and he was not a man who believed in wasting aptitude. She had a  
city and guilds certificate in technical drawing and a private reading in structural  
mechanics that was, as Edmund discovered over the course of their first month  
working together, more thorough than that of several of his graduate engineers.
Edmund noticed her work before he noticed her. The first drawing she submitted  
to his section — a detail drawing for a revised ammunition storage bay, showing the  
structural implications of increasing the bay's load capacity — was accurate in its  
structural calculations, precise in its notation, and contained a detail at the corner of  
the main bearing wall that he had not specified but that was correct and that he had,  
he admitted to himself, not thought of. He called her in to discuss it.

He asked about the corner detail. She said: the original specification would have  
produced a stress concentration at that point under the revised loading. I added the  
gusset plate to distribute it. He said: the specification didn't call for it. She said: no,  
but the physics did. He looked at her for a moment. She looked back at him with the  
expression of a woman who has had this conversation many times and knows exactly  
how it goes. He said: you're right. She said: I know. He said: good. Make sure you  
note the variation from specification in the drawing revision record. She said: I  
already have.
He reported to his section head Graves that the new draughtswoman was  
exceptional and that he intended to give her the revised factory layout series, which  
was the most technically demanding drawing work in the section's current portfolio.  
Graves said he supposed that was Edmund's call. Edmund said: it is. Graves said:  
very well. Edmund gave her the series. She completed it three days ahead of the  
schedule Edmund had set, which he had set with four days' padding specifically  
because he was not yet certain how fast she worked.
Edmund Marsh-Hale — his journal, April 1943
I have been having lunch with Dorothy Price in the Ministry canteen on Tuesdays  
and Thursdays for the past six weeks. I started it as a professional arrangement —  
she is the best technical draughtsperson in the section and there is work to discuss  
— and it has remained professional in content while becoming something else in  
quality.
What the something else is I am not entirely sure how to characterise. She is twenty-
six and I am thirty-two and she is, as I have said in several reports, an exceptionally  
capable technical professional. She is also funny in the way I have not encountered  
in a professional context before: not performing funniness, which is the kind most  
common in offices, but observing something accurately and then saying what she  
has observed in fewer words than the observation requires, which is the condition of  
genuine humour.
She asked me last week what my father did. I told her. She said: did he ever find out  
whether  the  arithmetic  was  fixed?  I  said:  he  thought  it  was  partly  fixed  and  
inadequately fixed and that the fixing required constant attention because it tends to  
revert. She said: like a structure settling. I said: exactly like a structure settling. She  
said: good analogy. I said: it is his analogy, approximately. She said: he sounds like  
someone worth knowing.
I told her he died last year. She said: I am sorry. And then she said something that I  
have been thinking about since: she said, it is harder when the person was also a

mind you relied on, because you lose the person and you also lose the conversation. I  
said: yes. That is exactly it. She said: I know. My father is still alive but I think about  
this.
They talked, over the Tuesday and Thursday lunches of spring and summer 1943,  
about everything the work touched and then beyond the work. About Birmingham  
and what he had seen there and what she had grown up seeing from a different angle  
— the daughter of the ironfounder rather than the engineer assessing the floor.  
About the war and what it was making and unmaking. About Thomas's writing, which  
she read and which she said was the clearest description of the condition of working  
people she had encountered, which was high praise from a woman who had read a  
great deal on the subject.
About the family history, which Edmund told her in installments, not as a  
performance but as the thing it was: a set of facts about a set of people who had been  
asking the same question in different centuries and had not answered it and had kept  
asking. She listened to all of it with the complete attention of someone who is not  
merely being polite but is actually processing what they are hearing. At the end she  
said: that is rather a lot of history to be carrying. He said: yes, I know. She said: is it a  
burden or a compass? He said: I think it is whichever one you choose to make it. She  
said: good answer.
They were married in October 1943. Ruth was the witness. Daniel Okafor sent a  
letter from Lagos that Edmund read at the small reception afterward. Daniel was  
seventy-three years old and had survived two wars and the loss of more people than  
he had expected to lose and he wrote with the warmth of a man who has been  
keeping faith with someone across forty years of letters and is glad the faith was  
kept. He said, among other things: your father would have liked her very much.  
Edmund said, reading it aloud: yes. He would have said she had the same quality  
Augusta had. The ability to make things work with what was actually available,  
rather than what should have been available.
Dorothy, who had just learned about Augusta, said: I will take that. Edmund said:  
I thought you would.
———

VII
The War Ends
London and Harlow, 1944–1950
The last year of the war — 1944 to May 1945 — was, from Edmund's position in  
the Ministry, the year of the full application of everything the previous five years had  
been building toward. The production figures that his section had been calculating  
since 1938, the corrected baselines and the revised timelines and the specific  
investments in specific bottlenecks — all of it was now running at a pace that the  
original optimistic plans had imagined and that his accurate plans had described as  
reachable by a different route, at a different cost, on a different schedule.
He did not take satisfaction in the gap between the optimistic plan and what had  
actually happened. He took satisfaction in the fact that the accurate plan had been  
followed, more or less, and that the production was there. That was enough. He was  
not a man who needed the institutional version of an apology, and he had not  
expected one. He had needed to be right and useful, and he had been both, and that  
was sufficient.
Their son James was born on the third of August, 1945. Edmund was at the  
hospital. Dorothy was in considerable discomfort and said so, in terms that Edmund  
considered entirely accurate and not in need of editorial comment, and he sat beside  
her and held her hand and said nothing except what was asked of him, which was the  
correct response and which Dorothy confirmed afterward was the correct response,  
which Edmund appreciated because he had not been entirely certain.
Hiroshima was the sixth of August, three days later. Edmund heard it on the  
wireless. He sat for a long time without speaking. Dorothy asked what he was  
thinking. He said: I am thinking about what the engineering of that device required.  
And what it means that we were capable of it. She said: what does it mean? He said: I  
think it means the distance between what we can build and what we should build has  
become very large, and that we do not yet have an engineering discipline for  
managing that distance. Dorothy said: I think that is a political problem, not an  
engineering one. Edmund said: yes. But the engineers made it. She said: yes. They  
did.
He took a week of leave before returning to the Ministry to begin the process of  
winding down the wartime production programme and assessing what the industrial  
capacity of Britain looked like in a peacetime configuration. The assessment was

sobering. The war had produced extraordinary feats of production and had done so  
by consuming the underlying capital stock of the country's industry, deferring  
maintenance,  running  machinery  beyond  its  rated  service  life,  exhausting  the  
workforce.  The  country  that  had  won  the  war  was,  in  structural  terms,  in  
considerably worse condition than it had been in 1939. It had been loaded beyond its  
rated capacity for six years and it was showing the deflection.
MINISTRY OF SUPPLY — INTERNAL MEMORANDUM
REF:  MS/4/1941/EH/047
FROM: E. Marsh-Hale, Section Head, Industrial Capacity (Ordnance)
TO:   Deputy Director, Production Planning
RE:   Tank production constraints — Midlands factories, Q1 1941
This memorandum summarises the findings of the section's assessment of tank  
production capacity at the seven principal Midlands facilities, conducted  
January-February 1941.
Current combined output: 47 units per week across all facilities.
Theoretical maximum under existing configuration: 68 units per week.
Actual constraint: Not production floor capacity but steel alloy supply.  
Current alloy specification requires 18% chrome-vanadium steel available  
from three suppliers only, one of which (Sheffield, Hadfield's) is  
operating at 73% of pre-bombing capacity following October damage. Until  
that facility is restored or the specification is revised downward,  
theoretical maximum is not achievable.
Recommended actions: (1) Expedite repair programme at Hadfield's —  
estimated six weeks with priority allocation. (2) Commission assessment of  
whether modified alloy specification reducing chrome content by 4% would  
maintain adequate armour performance — if so, expands supplier base to nine  
facilities. (3) Do not report theoretical maximum to planners as achievable  
until  constraint is resolved. Reporting  an unachievable figure as  
achievable creates planning errors downstream that are more damaging than  
an accurate lower figure.
The third recommendation is the most important. I am aware it produces a  
less favourable picture. The less favourable picture is the accurate one. —  
E. Marsh-Hale, March 1941.

Edmund left the Ministry of Supply in the spring of 1946 and spent six months at  
home with Dorothy and James. James was nine months old. Edmund, who had not  
previously spent a great deal of time with babies, found him interesting in the way he  
found most things interesting: there were observable patterns, the patterns had an  
internal logic, and the logic became clearer the more carefully you attended to it.  
Dorothy said: he is not a structural calculation. Edmund said: no, but the principle of  
attention applies to everything. Dorothy said: I suppose it does. James looked at both  
of them with the expression of a person who cannot yet speak but is listening  
carefully.
During those six months Edmund also wrote the memorandum on housing policy  
and structural engineering in the postwar context that eventually reached William  
Holford at the Ministry of Town and Country Planning and produced, in October  
1946, his appointment as Principal Engineer in the New Towns Directorate. The  
memorandum argued, in the flat precise style he had been developing for fifteen  
years, that the housing crisis in postwar Britain was a structural problem in the  
engineering sense as well as the social sense: the existing urban housing stock was  
under-maintained, over-occupied, and in many cases structurally inadequate by any  
standard that had been applied in the previous thirty years, and that the market, left  
to itself, would address these problems neither quickly enough nor comprehensively  
enough to meet the need.
The New Towns were the answer. Edmund did not design them — he was an  
engineer, not an architect or a planner — but he provided the structural foundation,  
literally, on which the designs could be built, and he did so with the same methodical  
honesty he had brought to every other structure he had worked on. The buildings at  
Harlow would bear the loads they were designed to bear. He made sure of it. He kept  
the records. The records are still there.
———

VIII
Reconstruction and the Ration
Harlow and London, 1946–1954
The New Towns programme was the largest planned construction effort in  
British history since the Victorian railways. Edmund worked on Harlow from 1946 to  
1957, which meant moving the family — Dorothy, James, and by 1948 the daily  
reality of austerity Britain — to a house on the edge of a town that was being built  
around them.
Harlow in 1947 was a market town of approximately four thousand people on the  
eastern edge of Essex, surrounded by farmland, with a railway station on the  
Liverpool Street line and a long history of being precisely nothing in particular. By  
1957 it would be a planned town of approximately eighty thousand, with schools,  
shopping precincts, a market, a bus station, an industrial zone, and twenty thousand  
houses built to standards that no private developer of the period would have  
voluntarily met.
Edmund's specific work was the structural engineering of the public buildings:  
the schools, the community centre, the library, the health centre. Each building was  
an exercise in the precise question he had been asking since Bramley and Sons: what  
does this structure need to do, and is it capable of doing it? The answer, for each  
building, was found in the same sequence: understand the load, calculate the  
members, specify the materials, supervise the construction, verify the result. The  
buildings he worked on at Harlow are still standing. Several of them are listed. He  
did not know this would happen. He did not build them to be listed. He built them to  
be sound, which turned out to be the more durable quality.

Dorothy noted the ration book entries with the same precision she brought to  
drawing — each entry dated, each allocation recorded, the running calculation of  
what remained done weekly on the inside back cover in the small neat hand that was  
her professional handwriting translated into domestic use. She was not resentful of  
the rationing. She was systematic about it, which was the appropriate response to a  
constraint you had not chosen and could not remove but could manage with more or  
less efficiency.
James grew up in a house on the edge of a town being built, with a mother who  
drew things and a father who calculated whether the things would stand. He was a  
serious child in the way that children raised by serious people often are — not  
solemn, not joyless, but attentive, the kind of child who watches how things work  
before he attempts to operate them. He asked questions that were, his teachers  
reported, better than the curriculum had prepared them to answer, which was  
something his grandmother Rachel  had apparently  said about  his grandfather  
Thomas and which Edmund found privately satisfying without making too much of it.
Rationing  ended  in  1954.  The  Festival  of  Britain  had  been  in  1951.  The  
Coronation was 1953. England was slowly, carefully, finding its way back to some  
version of ordinary life, without quite being certain what ordinary life was supposed  
to feel like after twenty years that had included a depression and a war and a blitz  
and an austerity and a welfare state built almost from nothing, which was either the  
most impressive or the most chaotic thing a country had done in the twentieth  
century, depending on your angle.

Edmund's angle was engineering. He thought it was impressive. He thought it  
was also fragile in the specific way of structures that have been built quickly under  
pressure without all the time that careful design requires, and that it would need  
maintaining with more diligence than it would receive. He wrote this in his private  
journal rather than in a Ministry memorandum, because he was no longer at the  
Ministry and because the observation was political rather than technical and he was  
not a politician.
He was also, by 1954, a father with a nine-year-old son who had just asked him  
what an entail was, having encountered the word in a school project on history, and  
to whom Edmund had given a brief and accurate explanation, followed by the offer of  
a more complete one involving the whole archive if James could sit still long enough.  
James said he could sit still for quite a long time. Dorothy said she would make tea.
———

IX
James and the Archive
London and Harlow, 1954–1960
Edmund  spread  the  photostat  copies  on  the  kitchen  table  on  a  Saturday  
afternoon in the summer of 1954. James was nine years old. Dorothy sat at the far  
end of the table with her tea and did not interfere, which was the correct decision  
and which James later said he appreciated, because it meant the conversation was  
between him and his father and he could ask the questions that actually occurred to  
him rather than the questions that seemed appropriate for an audience.
Edmund  went  through  the  documents  in  order.  He  started  with  the  1214  
purchase  deed  —  the  freehold  acquisition  that  William  Hale  had  made  at  
Michaelmas, the first deliberate act of accumulation in the family's record — and  
worked forward: the 1351 inquest, Thomas Hale's remembrance of 1382, the Civil  
War brothers' letters, Augusta's journal, his own grandfather's Oxford journal of  
1919. He explained each document. He explained the connections. He explained, as  
clearly as he could, what the pattern was.
James  listened  with  the  complete  attention  of  a  child  who  has  not  been  
condescended to, which is a different quality from the attention of a child who is  
being polite. His questions were direct and specific. What did Ranulf actually say on  
his deathbed? What did it mean that the brothers stayed in contact during the war?  
Why did Augusta know more about the estate than George? What happened after  
Thomas went to Oxford — did he do anything with what he found?
Edmund answered each question. To the last one he said: he wrote about it in his  
journal. He did not write the family history, though Daniel thought he should. He left  
notes. He left the journal. He left a letter addressed to you and your children, or to  
whoever came after, which is sitting in a box in Ruth's flat in Islington and which I  
will take you to read when you are a little older. James said: why not now? Edmund  
said: because there is a great deal of context required to understand it properly, and  
we have just spent three hours on the context and you are nine years old and I think  
that is probably enough context for one Saturday.
James accepted this without visible resentment, which Edmund noted. He had his  
mother's pragmatism about what was available and what was not. He finished his  
lemonade and looked at the documents still spread on the table and said: so we have  
been the same family the whole time. Edmund said: yes. James said: and Matilda

knew that before any of us did. Edmund said: she seems to have known something  
about the pattern, yes. James said: what happened to the pig?
Edmund looked at him. James said: the one they kept seeing to. Edmund said: I  
don't know. I think it was probably a different pig each time. James said: that makes  
sense. You wouldn't keep the same pig for nine hundred years. Dorothy came in from  
the kitchen with more tea and said that was the most sensible observation she had  
heard about the whole archive. Edmund said: I thought so too. James said: can I read  
the letter in Ruth's box? Edmund said: when you're older. James said: how old?  
Edmund said: old enough to sit still for all of it. James said: I can sit still for a long  
time. Edmund said: I know. I noticed.
Edmund Marsh-Hale to his sister Ruth Marsh-Hale — December, 1956
Ruth, I want to tell you about something James said last week. He is eleven now, and  
his history class had been asked to write an essay on their family history. He showed  
me the essay. It was four pages, accurate as far as I could check it, and it ended with  
the following sentence: my family has been in England since 1066 when a Norman  
soldier was given some land he had not earned by a king who had not owned it, and  
they have been trying to deserve it ever since.
I read it twice. Then I asked him where it came from. He said: it is just what  
happened, isn't it? I said yes. He said: I thought I should say it plainly. I said yes. You  
should.
Father would have liked it. Grandfather would have liked it. I think Matilda would  
have liked it most, because it is the plainest version of the thing she said in her  
letter, which was written in approximately 1390 and which we still have, which is  
itself an argument for keeping things.
He asked me recently whether the name will carry on. I said: that depends on you.  
He said: I think it will. I asked him why. He said: because the pig always needs  
seeing to. I said: yes. It does.
Edmund.
The 1950s settled into themselves. Rationing had ended. Suez was 1956 — the  
moment that England discovered, if it had not known before, that the imperial self-
image and the actual capacity of the country had diverged to an extent that could no  
longer  be  managed  by  confidence.  Edmund  followed  Suez  from  his  desk  in  
Clerkenwell — he had left the New Towns Directorate in 1957 and set up a small

structural engineering practice with two colleagues — with the attentiveness of a  
man watching a structure that has been overloaded discover that it is overloaded.
He was not surprised. He had been watching England discover the gap between  
its self-image and its structural reality since 1938. The gap was not shameful. It was  
simply true, and true things did not become less true by being unacknowledged, and  
the sooner they were acknowledged, the less the correction cost. He wrote this in his  
notebook in October 1956 and thought: this is what my father meant by the  
arithmetic. This is the same thing. It runs everywhere.
James went to grammar school in 1956. He was good at mathematics and history  
and indifferent to most other subjects, which his teachers found puzzling because his  
intelligence  was  obviously  more  general  than  his  results  suggested.  Edmund,  
reading his school reports with Dorothy, said: he is bored by things that have been  
settled. He is interested in things that are still open. The school is mostly teaching  
settled things. Dorothy said: most schools are. Edmund said: yes. That is the problem  
with schools.
James came home from school one day in 1959 and said he had been asked in his  
class what he wanted to be when he grew up. Edmund asked what he had said. James  
said: I said I wasn't sure yet, but I thought it would involve working with old things to  
understand current ones, or current things to understand old ones, which amounts  
to the same thing. Edmund said: what did your teacher say? James said: he said I  
should think more practically. Edmund said: what did you say? James said: I said I  
was being practical. Edmund said: yes. You were.
He was fourteen years old, and he was, already, the thing that the name had  
always produced when it was working correctly: a person who attended carefully to  
what was actually there and asked honestly whether it was sufficient, without  
needing the question to have a clean answer, because the Hales had never needed  
the question to have a clean answer. They had only needed to keep asking it.
Edmund did not say any of this to James. He was not a man who narrated things  
that were already apparent. He made tea and they sat at the kitchen table with their  
respective books and the evening came in through the window and Dorothy came  
home from work and said what is everyone reading, and they told her, and she said:  
good. She sat down. The kettle had just boiled.
———

X
On the English of the Mid-Twentieth Century
A Note on the Language of this Volume
The English of this volume is the English after the ornament has been stripped.  
George Orwell wrote its manifesto in 1946 — Politics and the English Language, six  
pages, seven rules, the essential argument being that the great enemy of clear  
language is insincerity, and that dishonesty in politics produces dishonesty in prose,  
and that the recovery of honest prose is therefore also the recovery of honest  
politics. Edmund Marsh-Hale had been practising it since 1933 without having read  
the manifesto, because he had been taught by a father who had learned it under fire  
and who had said, in a letter from France in 1916, that the war had taught him to  
write at things rather than around them.
Edmund's civil service memoranda are the purest expression of this style in the  
archive. Subject. Verb. Object. Evidence. Recommendation. Rationale. The memo on  
tank production in 1941 — do not report an unachievable figure as achievable — is  
thirteen words doing the work that Edmund Hale the Victorian would have taken a  
paragraph to do, and doing it more clearly. The loss is the texture, the density, the  
architectural  pleasure  of  the  long  subordinated  sentence.  The  gain  is  the  
impossibility of misunderstanding.
This is not, it should be said, a comfortable English to inhabit. The Victorian English  
of Edmund Hale senior — the subject of Book VII, the performance, the long periods  
— had the comfort of its complexity. The plain style gives you nowhere to hide, which  
is the point. When Edmund Marsh-Hale writes the less favourable picture is the  
accurate one, he has said everything. There is no further discussion. The Victorian  
version of that sentence would have been two hundred words and would have said  
the same thing in a way that allowed the reader to feel they had not quite heard it.
The language of the next and final volume — Book X — is the English we use now: the  
English of text messages and emails and podcasts and web searches, which is flatter  
than even this and faster and less formal, and which has its own dishonesty problems  
that are different from the Victorian's but not less significant. The performance has  
not disappeared from English. It has changed its costume. James Marsh-Hale —  
fourteen years old at the end of this volume, the subject of the final book — grew up  
speaking the plain style and will encounter, in the years the final book covers, the  
question of what the plain style means when the context is not a civil service

memorandum or a structural report but a life in the twenty-first century, which is  
rather less structured and rather more various and rather less willing to be told what  
the accurate picture is.
He will manage. He has his grandmother's practicality, his grandfather's precision,  
and something that the family has been producing, in one form or another, since  
Ranulf de la Hale stood in a Saxon hall in Worcestershire in 1067 and tried to  
understand what he had been given and what it required of him. The thing has no  
name exactly. The archive calls it by different names in different centuries. Matilda  
called it keeping faith. Thomas called it the arithmetic. Edmund called it the accurate  
picture. Whatever it is called, it is the same thing, and James Marsh-Hale has it, and  
it will carry him through what comes next.
———

From Thomas Marsh-Hale to James Marsh-Hale, 1873 — present
