GLOSSARY
Words, events, and institutions which the text employs and the reader may wish explained
The New Towns 
Act, 1946Legislation enabling the British government to designate and build entirely  
new towns, primarily to relieve the overcrowding of London and other major  
cities and to rehouse populations displaced by bombing or slum clearance.  
The first generation of New Towns included Harlow, Stevenage, Crawley,  
Hemel Hempstead, Basildon, Hatfield, Welwyn Garden City, and Bracknell.  
They  were  designed  according  to  principles  derived  from  Ebenezer  
Howard's Garden City movement, but the postwar versions were more  
pragmatic  —  the  architecture  more  functionalist,  the  planning  more  
administrative  —  than  Howard's  original  vision.  Harlow,  designed  by  
Frederick Gibberd, is widely considered the most architecturally coherent of  
the first-generation New Towns and several of its public buildings are now  
listed.
Ministry of Supply British  government  department,  1939–1959,  responsible  for  the  
procurement and supply of military equipment including tanks, artillery,  
aircraft, and ammunition. At its wartime peak it employed approximately  
forty thousand civil servants and managed an industrial programme of  
extraordinary complexity. The industrial capacity division, in which Edmund  
worked, was responsible for knowing — as accurately as possible — what  
British factories were actually capable of producing, as distinct from what  
they reported themselves capable of producing.
Rationing Introduced in Britain in January 1940 and not fully lifted until 1954, making  
Britain  the  longest-rationed  major  combatant.  Rationing  covered  food,  
clothing, petrol, and household goods, and was administered through ration  
books distributed to every household. The allocations varied over time: in  
1948, at their most restrictive, they were tighter than at any point during the  
war, as postwar economic difficulties — principally a dollar shortage that  
constrained the country's ability to import — pushed allocations below  
wartime levels for many categories. The welfare foods scheme, providing  
supplementary milk, orange juice, and cod liver oil to children under five,  
was among the most enduring legacies of the postwar welfare state.
George Orwell, 
'Politics and the 
English Language' 
(1946)The essay in which Orwell argued that political dishonesty and lazy thinking  
produce dishonest and lazy prose, and that the recovery of honest prose is  
therefore a political as well as a literary act. Its six rules — which include  
prefer the active voice, never use a long word where a short one will do, and  
if it is possible to cut a word, always cut it — became the dominant style

guide of mid-century English journalism and civil service writing. Edmund  
Marsh-Hale's annotated copy was among his effects at his death in 1978.
The Suez Crisis, 
1956A diplomatic and military crisis precipitated by Egypt's nationalisation of the  
Suez Canal Company in July 1956. Britain and France, in collusion with  
Israel, invaded Egypt in October 1956 to retake the canal. Under intense  
pressure from the United States, which threatened to destabilise the pound  
if the operation continued, Britain withdrew within days. The episode is  
generally considered the moment at which Britain's post-imperial decline  
became impossible to deny: the country had attempted to act as a great  
power and had been publicly stopped by its own ally. Edmund's reading of it  
as a structural rather than a political failure — the gap between self-image  
and actual capacity — was widely shared by the generation that had watched  
the war from the inside.
Imperial College 
LondonResearch university in South Kensington, London. Edmund Marsh-Hale's  
BEng  in  Structural  Engineering,  taken  1929–1933,  was  a  four-year  
programme combining theoretical analysis with applied structural design.  
The structural engineering curriculum of the period included classical beam  
theory, elastic analysis, soil mechanics, and the emerging field of reinforced  
concrete design, of which Edmund became a specialist.
The Festival of 
Britain, 1951National exhibition on the South Bank of the Thames and at sites across the  
country, marking the centenary of the Great Exhibition of 1851. Intended to  
demonstrate British creativity and postwar recovery, it was designed by a  
generation  of  young  architects  who  went  on  to  shape  British  public  
architecture through the 1950s and 1960s. The Dome of Discovery, the  
Skylon, and the Royal Festival Hall — the only permanent building on the  
South Bank site — were its most visible products. The Festival was also,  
more simply, an attempt by a country that had been in crisis for twenty years  
to remember what peacetime was for.
———
END OF BOOK IX — THE NAME
The story concludes in Book X:
Hale
1979 — 2026

THE HALE DYNASTY
BOOK X
Hale
Being a Collection of Emails, Archive Searches, Personal Notes,
Journal Entries, and the Documents that were Always
Addressed to You —
pertaining to James Marsh-Hale and one other.
Covering the years
1979 — 2026
*
A Note Before the First Document
This is the last volume in the chronicle of the Hale family. It begins in 1979, when  
James Marsh-Hale is thirty-four years old, and it ends in 2026, when he is eighty-one.  
It is narrated through the documents of the present era: emails, search results,  
journal entries written on a laptop, a scanned letter. The language is the language  
we use now — direct, unornamented, sometimes blunt, occasionally uncertain about  
its own certainty, which is perhaps the defining quality of contemporary English.
The volume has two protagonists, though one of them does not know they are in this  
story until near the end.
The first is James Marsh-Hale himself: structural historian, retired since 2010, the  
son of Edmund and Dorothy, the grandson of Thomas and Rachel, the great-great-
great-grandson — through the irregular line that ran from Edmund Hale's locked  
room to a Camberwell seamstress named Clara to a journalism clerk named Thomas  
— of every Hale who ever held land in Worcestershire and asked whether they had  
done enough.
The second is a woman named Eleanor Voss. She is thirty-eight years old in 2024.  
She is a researcher at the Bodleian Library in Oxford, specialising in medieval land  
records. She has no idea she is a Hale. She has been working with the Hale archive  
for three years without knowing it is, in any meaningful sense, hers.
Matilda's letter ends: keep asking. Keep the land. See to the pig. It was written for  
whoever came after. It was addressed to Eleanor Voss. It was addressed to you.

— The editors of the Marsh-Hale Papers, Oxford, 2026

I
James at Thirty-Four
Harlow and London, 1979–1990
James Marsh-Hale left Harlow at eighteen, in 1963, to read history at the  
University  of  Leeds,  which  was  not  Oxford  or  Cambridge  but  was  where  the  
scholarship took him and which he later said was the correct destination, being a  
university that was interested in what had actually happened rather than in the  
management of an established account of what had happened.
He took a first. He went to the Public Record Office in Chancery Lane in 1966 as a  
junior researcher and spent seven years learning what archives actually were: not  
repositories of the past but active arguments about it, shaped by what had been kept  
and what had been destroyed and who had made the decisions. He wrote two papers  
on medieval land tenure in Worcestershire that were read by approximately forty  
people and cited by four of them, which was, his supervisor told him, an excellent  
result for papers of that kind.
He married in 1972. His wife was named Susan Parrish, a librarian at the British  
Museum who had the quality — valuable in a marriage, he told his father on the  
phone the night after their first date — of finding other people's expertise more  
interesting than her own, which was either genuine curiosity or very good manners  
and either way was useful. They had two children: Anna, born 1974, and Michael,  
born 1977. They lived in a house in Hackney that needed structural work, which  
Edmund assessed on his first visit and pronounced adequate but not ideal, which was  
his highest register of approval for buildings that were not his own.
Edmund died in 1978. James was thirty-three. Dorothy lived until 1994 and spent  
her final years in the same way she had spent most of the previous ones: working,  
reading, being precise about what she saw and direct about what she thought. James  
visited her every two weeks and they talked about everything, the way people who  
have been honest with each other for forty years can talk about everything, and he  
was with her at the end, as Edmund had been with Rachel, and he sat for a while in  
the manner that the family had always sat when the load-bearing element was gone,  
and then he went home and fed the children and made tea and thought: see to the  
pig.
He became, in the 1980s, a historian of medieval property law with a specific  
interest in the relationship between legal instruments and the actual behaviour of

the people they purported to govern. This sounds narrow. It was not narrow. It was,  
as he explained to Susan whenever she asked why anyone cared about thirteenth-
century Worcestershire land tenure, the question of how the gap between the official  
account and the real account gets managed across time, which was the same  
question his grandfather had been asking about factory production figures in 1941  
and his great-grandfather had been asking about wages in 1903 and his great-great-
great-grandmother Augusta had been asking about estate accounts in 1795.
He published three books between 1979 and 1995. Each one was well-reviewed,  
moderately read, and had the quality — which he identified in his father's work and  
which he had apparently inherited — of saying the accurate thing plainly, without  
embellishment, in a way that made the reader feel they had always known it and  
simply needed someone to say it first.
James Marsh-Hale — journal, February 1981
I have been spending my evenings re-reading the archive. Not for work — I know it  
well enough for work — but for the thing it actually is, which is different from what it  
is professionally. Professionally it is a collection of documents relating to land tenure  
and familial succession in Worcestershire from the Norman Conquest to the late  
nineteenth century. Personally it is a family.
I keep coming back to the Civil War letters. John writing to Thomas: I do not think  
you are wrong to serve the King. I think you are wrong about the King. And Thomas  
writing back: some things are settled, and the order of them is not arrived at by men  
sitting in a chamber in London and voting upon it. Both of them wrong in specific  
ways. Both of them honest. Both of them keeping the letters.
My father said once that the archive was a compass rather than a burden. I think that  
is right. But I think what it compasses toward is not a destination but a posture:  
attending, asking, not pretending the question is settled when it is not. I have been  
doing this since I was nine years old and I still do not have a better name for it.
*

II
The Donated Papers
Oxford and Hackney, 1990–2010
Ruth Marsh-Hale died in 1974. She had kept, in a flat in Islington, the box of  
Thomas's notes and the full run of correspondence that constituted the Marsh-Hale  
family's own archive — separate from the broader Hale collection at the Bodleian,  
which was the legal instruments and official documents and formal correspondence.  
Ruth's box contained the letters between Thomas and Daniel, Thomas's journals,  
Edmund's  journals,  Dorothy's  notebooks,  the  photostat  copies  of  the  medieval  
documents that Edmund had spread on the kitchen table in 1954, and Thomas's  
letter addressed to whoever comes after.
Ruth had read the letter in 1969, the year James's account of it appears in his own  
journal. She had sat with it for a long time and then put it back in the box and  
deposited the box with James, who was twenty-four and working at the Public Record  
Office, with the instruction: keep this. He had kept it. He had read it once a year,  
every year, in the manner of something you come back to not because you expect a  
different result but because the result changes as you do.
James donated the Marsh-Hale papers to the Bodleian in 2001, when he retired  
from the University of London where he had been Professor of Medieval Land  
History  since  1988.  The  donation brought  the  total  Hale  archive  — the  legal  
instruments, the Frere catalogue, and now the family papers — into a single  
collection for the first time. He wrote the finding aid himself, which took three  
months and which he considered the most important piece of writing he had done,  
not because it was good writing but because it was accurate.
The finding aid described the collection's contents in the flat, precise language of  
archival description: dates, document types, physical condition, cross-references. It  
noted the existence of Matilda's letter — the original, still mounted between glass —  
and described its condition as fragile, its text as partially legible, and its significance  
as considerable for the history of the family and of Halecroft. It noted that a  
photographic imaging programme might recover more of the damaged central  
section than was currently legible to the naked eye.
Nobody ran the photographic imaging programme for twenty years. This was not  
neglect. It was the ordinary backlog of an archive with more material than it had  
resources to process, and a finding aid that described Matilda's letter as significant

but not as urgent, because James had not wanted to overstate its significance in a  
professional document, because overstatement was the thing he had been trained —  
by his father, by his grandfather's writing, by the archive itself — to avoid.
This was a mistake, though he did not know it was a mistake until later. The  
photographic imaging would have recovered the central section of Matilda's letter.  
The central section contained a name. The name would have changed the shape of  
the story considerably earlier than it did.
But stories do not move at the pace of their most efficient resolution. They move  
at the pace of the people in them, which is always slower and more contingent and  
more susceptible to the ordinary friction of institutional backlog than the stories  
prefer.
From:    j.marsh-hale@bodleian.ox.ac.uk
To:      archive.enquiries@bodleian.ox.ac.uk
Subject: Re: Hale Family Papers — imaging request
Date:    14 March 2001
Dear Archive Team, I am writing in connection with the recent donation of the  
Marsh-Hale family papers (reference: MSS. Marsh-Hale 1–47). I have completed the  
finding aid and believe the collection is ready for catalogue integration.
I wish to flag one item for priority attention when resources permit: the document  
known as Matilda's letter (MSS. Marsh-Hale 3, item 7), currently mounted between  
glass due to its fragility. The visible text has been transcribed (see finding aid), but  
the central section — approximately twenty to twenty-five lines — is damaged by  
water and not currently legible. Multispectral imaging or equivalent would likely  
recover significant portions of this text.
I appreciate that this cannot be immediately prioritised. I note it for the record.
I should also say, for whatever it is worth in a professional communication, that I  
have been reading this archive since I was nine years old. I am glad it is here.
*

III
Eleanor Voss
Oxford, 2021–2024
Eleanor Voss came to the Bodleian in 2018 as a postdoctoral researcher in  
medieval land records, funded by a three-year fellowship from the Leverhulme Trust.  
She was thirty-two years old. She had done her doctorate at Durham on the  
administration of monastic estates in fourteenth-century Worcestershire, which is a  
subject of such narrowness that explaining it at social gatherings had taught her a  
precision of description that served her in every other context.
She was from Yorkshire, originally — a small town outside Harrogate, the  
daughter of a secondary school history teacher and a GP, raised in the particular  
atmosphere of a household where accuracy was expected and excess was gently  
discouraged. She had her father's thoroughness and her mother's practicality and a  
quality  of  attention  that  her  supervisors  had  consistently  identified  in  her  
recommendations as exceptional, which she acknowledged without letting it become  
the central fact of her self-understanding.
She took up a permanent position at the Bodleian in 2021, specialising in the  
digitisation and analysis of medieval property records. She was, by 2023, the person  
the library consulted when they needed someone to look carefully at something old  
and damaged and say what it actually contained.
In the autumn of 2023 her supervisor asked her to look at a backlog of items  
flagged for imaging in the Hale collection, which had accumulated since a donation  
in 2001 and which included a document described in the finding aid as a letter of  
approximately  1390,  partially  legible,  of  considerable  significance.  Eleanor  
requested the item, read the finding aid, read the partial transcription made by  
George Edward Hale in 1858 and preserved in the Marsh-Hale papers, and agreed  
that multispectral imaging was warranted.
She ran the imaging herself in January 2024.
James Marsh-Hale — journal, 12 January 2024
A researcher at the Bodleian has run the imaging on Matilda's letter. Her name is  
Eleanor Voss. She emailed me last week to say the imaging has recovered the central  
section almost completely — twenty-three lines that have been illegible since at least

the nineteenth century and probably much longer. She says the text is remarkable.
She asked whether she could write to me about the contents before she writes the  
formal report. I said yes immediately. I am eighty years old and I have been waiting  
for this since I was nine.
I called Anna to tell her. She said: Dad, are you all right? I said yes. She said: you  
sound strange. I said: I sound moved. She said: same question.
From:    e.voss@bodleian.ox.ac.uk
To:      j.marsh-hale@bodleian.ox.ac.uk
Subject: Matilda's letter — imaging results
Date:    15 January 2024
Dear Mr. Marsh-Hale, I am writing as promised with the results of the multispectral  
imaging of MSS. Marsh-Hale 3, item 7.
The imaging has recovered twenty-three lines of previously illegible text from the  
central section of the document. I am attaching the full enhanced image and my  
preliminary transcription. The text is in the same hand as the legible portions and  
consistent with the late fourteenth century date ascribed in the finding aid.
The  recovered  section  contains  a  description  of  the  Hale  family  across  five  
generations that is considerably more detailed than anything in the surviving parish  
records. It also contains, at line 14, a sentence that I want to bring to your attention  
directly before including it in any formal report, because I think you should read it  
first. The sentence is: and I do set down here, for the keeping of the record, that the  
line does not end with the sons of William Hale but continues also through his  
daughter Margery, who married one Thomas Voss of Pershore in the year of Our  
Lord 1373, and whose line I have followed these thirty years and know to be sound.
Mr. Marsh-Hale. I am Eleanor Voss. My family is from Pershore, in Worcestershire.  
My father's family has been in Pershore since before anyone recorded. I looked at  
the parish records this morning. The earliest Voss entry at Pershore is from 1391.  
The father's name is Thomas.
I do not know what to do with this. I thought you should know first.
James read the email three times. He was sitting at his desk in the house in  
Hackney — the same house he and Susan had bought in 1972, though Susan had died  
in 2019 and it was quieter now in ways he had not fully adjusted to. He read it a  
fourth time. Then he went to the kitchen and made tea, which was what the family  
had always done when it needed to think.

He thought about Matilda's letter as he had known it all his life: the beginning,  
legible; the end, legible; the middle, water-damaged and silent for six and a half  
centuries. He thought about the word she had used in the ending — whoever comes  
after. He thought about Thomas's letter in the box, which he had read every year  
since he was twenty-four, and which ended with the same address to the same  
unnamed person.
He thought: the letter was always addressed to someone specific. We just did not  
know who.
He wrote back the same afternoon.
From:    j.marsh-hale@bodleian.ox.ac.uk
To:      e.voss@bodleian.ox.ac.uk
Subject: Re: Matilda's letter — imaging results
Date:    15 January 2024
Dear Ms. Voss, thank you for writing to me directly. That was the right thing to do.
I have been looking at this archive since I was nine years old. My father spread the  
documents on the kitchen table on a Saturday afternoon in 1954 and explained the  
connections. I have read Matilda's letter — the parts of it I could read — every year  
since I was in my twenties. I wrote the finding aid in 2001 and flagged the imaging as  
needed. I waited twenty-three years.
The sentence you have quoted is not a surprise in the sense of being unexpected.  
Everything in this archive has been building toward some version of it. What is a  
surprise is that it is you. That Matilda was keeping track of both lines. That she  
knew, in 1390, what she was doing.
I would very much like to meet you. I have some things to tell you about this family  
that are not in the finding aid. I also have a letter that was written by my grandfather  
in 1949, addressed to whoever comes after. I believe it was addressed to you.
Are you free for lunch next week?
*

IV
Lunch
Oxford, January 2024
They met on the twenty-second of January, at a pub near the Bodleian that  
Eleanor suggested because it was warm and quiet at lunchtime and had good soup.  
James took the train from London. He was seventy-eight years old and moved  
carefully, the way people who have paid attention to physical structures all their  
lives learn to move when their own physical structure requires the same attention.
Eleanor was already there when he arrived. He had seen her photograph on the  
Bodleian website and she looked as she did in the photograph: contained, precise in  
the way of people who have learned to be precise about what they say because the  
alternative is imprecision about what they mean. She stood up when he came in.  
They shook hands in the manner of people who are observing a formality they both  
understand  is  insufficient  for  the  situation  but  have  not  yet  found  the  right  
replacement for.
They ordered soup. For the first fifteen minutes they talked about the imaging  
process, the specific wavelengths that had recovered the damaged text, the physical  
condition of the original document, the interpretation of certain letter forms in the  
recovered section. They talked about it in the language of their shared professional  
competence, which was the right way to begin, because it established that they were  
both people who looked at what was actually there before deciding what it meant.
Then James said: shall I tell you about the family.
Eleanor said: please.
He started at the beginning. Not at the beginning of the documentary record —  
the legal beginning, the 1214 purchase deed — but at the actual beginning: a  
Norman soldier named Ranulf who crossed the Channel in 1066 with no prospects  
and was given land he had not earned by a king who had not owned it, and who spent  
the rest of his life asking whether what he had done was sufficient, and who died  
without quite answering it, and whose family had been asking the same question in  
different registers ever since.
He talked for two hours. Eleanor listened with the complete attention of a person  
who is not just following a narrative but placing it against the things she already  
knows, testing the fit, noting the places where the fit is exact and the places where it  
requires adjustment. She asked three questions during the two hours, each one

precise and each one landing on something he had not previously articulated but that  
was clearly implied by what he was saying.
The third question was: when Thomas Hale wrote his remembrance in 1382, and  
said we are a family that holds its land and keeps its word and comes home when it  
could go elsewhere — did he know about Margery? Did he know that her line carried  
on?
James said: I don't know. I don't think so. I think Matilda knew. I think she had  
been keeping track of both lines for thirty years and wrote it down so that whoever  
found it would know. She just did not expect it to be unreadable for six centuries.
Eleanor said: she wrote it for me.
James said: yes. That is what I think.
Eleanor said: I have been working with this archive for three years. I have held  
Matilda's letter in my hands. I did not know.
James said: no. But she did.
Bodleian Library, MS. Marsh-Hale 3, item 7 — Enhanced imaging transcript, January 
2024
MATILDA'S LETTER — RECOVERED CENTRAL SECTION
[Lines 1-8 as previously transcribed: To whoever shall read this after I am gone,  
which will be soon enough: I have lived in this village since before any man in this  
room was born...]
[Lines 9-13, recovered:] I have kept these bees through six reigns and two plagues  
and a rising that came to nothing and a hunger that came to much, and I have  
known the sons and daughters of this family since before their fathers were born,  
and I set down what I know here because I am the last who knows it.
[Line 14, key sentence:] And I do set down here, for the keeping of the record, that  
the line does not end with the sons of William Hale but continues also through his  
daughter Margery, who married one Thomas Voss of Pershore in the year of Our  
Lord 1373, and whose line I have followed these thirty years and know to be sound.
[Lines 15-19, recovered:] Margery is a good woman and a careful one and she has a

daughter  named  Alice  who  has  her  grandmother  Aelswith's  eyes,  which  I  
remember though Aelswith has been dead these forty years and the world has  
changed more times than I can comfortably count since then. The blood runs in  
both lines. This is what families do. They go underground sometimes and come  
back up somewhere else and they are still the same thing they always were, which  
is themselves.
[Lines 20-23, recovered:] I write this not for the sons of this house, who know who  
they are. I write it for whoever reads it last, who will not know where they are from  
until this moment, and who will perhaps think it makes no great difference. It  
makes no great difference and it makes all the difference. You are from here. This is  
where you are from. Halecroft, in the county of Worcester, which is a small place  
and an old one and which has been your home since 1066 whether you knew it or  
not.
[Lines 24-27, final section as previously transcribed:] ...a family that keeps asking  
whether it has done enough will keep the land. A family that stops asking will lose  
it, sooner or later, one way or another. Keep asking. Keep the land. See to the pig.
*

V
Thomas's Letter
Oxford and London, January–March 2024
James brought Thomas's letter to Oxford on a Thursday in February. He carried it  
in the manila envelope it had been in since Ruth put it in the box, which she had put it  
in sometime in the 1960s, the envelope now soft at the edges from decades of being  
held and replaced. Eleanor met him at the library entrance. They went to a reading  
room and James put the envelope on the table.
He said: this was written in 1949. My grandfather Thomas was seventy-five years  
old. He had found the archive in 1919, thirty years before, and he had spent those  
thirty years thinking about it and not quite writing what he thought. He wrote this  
instead. He addressed it to whoever comes after. He gave it to my aunt Ruth and she  
gave it to me. I have read it every year since I was twenty-four.
Eleanor said: have you told anyone else about it?
James said: I told my children. I told Susan. I have not shown it to anyone outside  
the family.
Eleanor said: I am outside the family.
James said: I am not entirely sure that is true.
She read it. It took her twenty minutes, which was longer than it took most  
people, because she read it the way she read everything: carefully, going back to  
certain passages, sitting with the ones that required it. When she finished she put it  
down on the table and looked at the wall for a while.
James waited. He was good at waiting. He had been waiting for this for seventy  
years without knowing specifically what he was waiting for.
Eleanor said: he knew about the question. He understood what it was. He did not  
answer it.
James said: no.
Eleanor said: is that — is the not answering deliberate?
James said: I think so. I think the family has always understood that the point is  
the asking. Matilda said it. My grandfather understood it. My father understood it.  
The question is what you do with it.

Eleanor was quiet for a while. Then she said: I have been a historian of medieval  
property records for fifteen years. I have held hundreds of old documents. I have  
read about dozens of families. I have never felt before that a document was written  
for me personally.
James said: she wrote it for you. He wrote it for you. They all wrote it for you, in  
the sense that they all wrote it for whoever came after, and you are whoever came  
after.
She said: what does it require of me?
He said: nothing specific. It is not a legal instrument. It is not a deed. There is  
nothing to inherit in the material sense. The land at Halecroft was sold in 1891. The  
baronetcy is long extinct. What it requires, if it requires anything, is the same thing it  
has always required: attention. Asking whether what you are doing is sufficient. Not  
closing the question.
She said: I already do that.
He said: I know. I could tell from the email. The way you wrote I do not know what  
to do with this. That is exactly right. Not pretending to know what to do with it. Not  
forcing a conclusion. Just holding the question and telling the relevant person.
She said: you were the relevant person.
He said: and now you are.
A Letter
Written by Thomas Marsh-Hale in 1949.
Addressed: To whoever comes after.
I am writing this in Brixton in the spring of 1949. I am seventy-five years old.  
Rachel has been gone eight years. Edmund is in the New Towns. Ruth is at the  
Bar. My body is not what it was.
I found this family's history in a reading room in Oxford thirty years ago and I  
have been thinking about it ever since without quite finding the right form for  
the thinking. I am going to try to find it now, because I think I may not have a  
great many more opportunities.
What I found in Oxford was a family that had been asking one question in  
different centuries and with different tools and in different registers, and that

had never quite answered it, and that had kept asking. The question is: is what  
I have done sufficient? Ranulf asked it on his deathbed in 1115. Thomas Hale  
asked it in 1382 in the form of a remembrance addressed to his son. The Civil  
War brothers asked it every time they wrote to each other from opposite sides  
of a war. Augusta asked it in her journal, precisely and without self-pity, every  
time she calculated what she had been permitted to do against what she was  
capable of. My father asked it in France in 1916, in a letter to my mother, from  
a hole in the ground. Edmund Hale — my father's father in the irregular line  
that neither of them chose — asked it in a locked room in Shropshire in 1882  
and did not answer it, and the not answering was the end of him.
The question has not been answered. I do not think it can be answered once, in  
a way that settles it. I think it is answered daily, by the choice to attend to what  
is actually in front of you, to look at the accurate picture rather than the  
reassuring one, to come home when you could go elsewhere, to ask and not to  
stop asking. My mother called this seeing to the pig, because that is what  
Thomas Hale called it in 1382, and because it is a better description than most  
of the more elevated formulations I have encountered.
I am writing this for whoever comes after. I do not know who that is. It may be  
Edmund's children or their children or someone I cannot imagine, someone  
whose connection to this family runs through a line I cannot trace. Matilda  
knew there were two lines. I did not, when I read her letter. I think she was  
right that both matter.
What I want to say to whoever comes after is this: you are from somewhere. It  
is a small place and an old one and it has never been famous for anything  
except being there, persisting, holding the question open when it would have  
been easier to close it. You did not choose this. It is still yours. The choosing is  
not in the inheritance. The choosing is in what you do with it. The choosing is  
in whether you ask.
Keep asking. See to the pig.
Thomas Marsh-Hale. Written at Brixton, London, in the forty-fourth year of the  
reign of George VI. Spring, 1949.
*

VI
The Archive in Full
Oxford and London, 2024–2025
Eleanor published her findings in the spring of 2024: a paper in the journal  
Medieval  Prosopography  titled  'Two  Lines:  The  Hale  and  Voss  Families  of  
Worcestershire, 1066–1400, with Notes on the Matilda Document.' It was received  
with the respect extended to good archival work: cited by three historians within six  
months, discussed at two conferences, and read by approximately two hundred  
people, which is a large readership for a paper of that kind.
She did not, in the paper, mention that she was herself a descendant of the Voss  
line. This was a deliberate decision, discussed with James over two further lunches  
and one long phone call in which they arrived at the same conclusion by slightly  
different routes: that the paper was better as scholarship than as personal narrative,  
and that the personal narrative could wait for a different form.
The different form was this volume. It was James's suggestion. He said, in an  
email in June 2024: I have been thinking about what my grandfather did not write.  
He had the archive. He had the understanding. He left notes and a letter but not the  
thing itself. I would like to make the thing itself. Not a scholarly edition — there are  
people better placed to do that. Something that reads the way the archive actually  
reads: as a voice, or several voices, across nine centuries. A book that is the archive.
Eleanor said: I will help you write it.
James said: I was hoping you would say that.
They worked on it together throughout 2024 and into 2025. James provided the  
Marsh-Hale papers and the decades of his own reading of the archive. Eleanor  
provided the scholarly apparatus and the medieval sections and the recovered text of  
Matilda's letter, which she had now transcribed fully and verified against the  
imaging.  They  divided the  ten books between them,  with Eleanor writing the  
medieval and Tudor sections and James writing the later ones, and each reading and  
revising the other's work with the precision of two people who have been trained to  
say what is actually there.
James was seventy-nine when they began and eighty when they finished. Eleanor  
was thirty-eight when they began and thirty-nine when they finished. The age gap  
was thirty-eight years, which was the same gap as between Henry Alderton and

Augusta Hale in 1832, a coincidence that James noticed and mentioned and Eleanor  
said: yes, I noticed that too.
James said: this family is very repetitive.
Eleanor said: that is what families are.
From:    e.voss@bodleian.ox.ac.uk
To:      j.marsh-hale@bodleian.ox.ac.uk
Subject: Re: Book X draft — thoughts
Date:    3 September 2024
James, I have been sitting with the draft of Book X for two days and I want to say  
something about it that I find difficult to put precisely.
You  have  written  about  me  as  though  I  am  the  end  of  something.  The  final  
destination. The person the archive was always travelling toward. And I understand  
why — the structure of the ten books builds to this, and Matilda's letter and  
Thomas's letter both seem addressed forward, and I am the forward.
But I am not sure I want to be a destination. I think I would rather be a continuation.  
I think what Matilda was describing was not an endpoint but a resumption: the line  
that went underground in 1373 and has come back up now, not to close something  
but to keep it open.
I am thirty-eight years old. I do not have children yet. I do not know whether I will.  
But the question does not require children to continue. It requires attention. It  
requires someone to keep asking whether what they have done is sufficient, which I  
have been doing professionally for fifteen years without knowing it had a name or a  
history.
I think the book should end with me asking the question. Not answering it. Just:  
asking it. As a continuation, not a conclusion.
What do you think?
From:    j.marsh-hale@bodleian.ox.ac.uk
To:      e.voss@bodleian.ox.ac.uk
Subject: Re: Book X draft — thoughts
Date:    4 September 2024
Eleanor, yes. You are right. I wrote it as an ending because I am eighty years old and  
I have been reading toward this for seventy years and I overcorrected. The error is  
exactly what you have identified: treating the discovery as a destination rather than  
a resumption.

The archive has never ended. Every document in it was written for the next  
document. Matilda's letter was written for whoever came after. Thomas's letter was  
written for whoever came after. The chronicle Brother Eadmer wrote in 1121 ends:  
this chronicle to whoever comes after. The whole thing is addressed forward. It has  
always been addressed forward.
You are not the end of it. You are the next person holding it.
I will revise the final chapter.
*

VII
Halecroft
Worcestershire, September 2025
Eleanor drove to Halecroft in September 2025. It was a warm Thursday, the  
leaves not yet turning, the motorway to Worcester quiet in the late morning. James  
had offered to come with her but she had said: I think this one I should do alone. He  
had said: yes, I thought you might.
Halecroft is not a village that appears on most maps. It is a hamlet now — twelve  
houses, a farm, a church — in the Worcestershire countryside east of Worcester,  
south of the motorway, in the kind of landscape that requires no dramatic adjective.  
Gently undulating fields. A wood on the eastern side. A mill stream running along the  
northern  boundary  of  what  was  once  the  manor's  land  and  is  now  a  private  
farmholding. A church.
The church is St. Wulfstan's. It has been St. Wulfstan's since the Conquest, or  
thereabouts. The Norman core of the building is visible in the arch above the south  
door, its plain rounded stonework unmistakably of the period. The tower is fifteenth  
century. The nave was extended in the eighteenth. The interior is the interior of an  
English parish church that has been in continuous use for nine hundred years: the  
smell of old stone and slightly damp hymn books, the wooden pews worn smooth in  
the particular way of wood that has been sat on by generations, the east window  
catching the morning light.
Eleanor sat in the front pew for a while. She had not planned to. She was not  
religious in any active sense. She sat because it seemed the correct thing to do in this  
space, which was the space where every Hale since Ranulf had sat — had been  
baptised, married, buried — and which was therefore the most continuous physical  
location in the whole story.
She thought about what James had said at their first meeting: she wrote it for you.  
She thought about what it meant to be from somewhere without having known it.  
She thought about her father, the history teacher, who had been interested in local  
history all his life without knowing that his own family was the local history. She  
thought  about  Matilda:  very  old,  very  sure  of  herself,  writing  a  letter  in  
approximately 1390 and hiding it under the lining of a deed-box because she knew it  
would take a long time for the right person to arrive.
She thought: six hundred and thirty-five years. That is how long it took.

Then she got up and went outside and stood in the churchyard, in the September  
light, in the small place that was old and had been hers without her knowing it, and  
she asked the question. Not aloud. But asked it.
Whether what she had done was sufficient.
She did not answer it.
She went to find the Hale graves, which James had told her were in the northeast  
corner, under a yew tree that he thought was probably three hundred years old. She  
found them. The stones were weathered to illegibility mostly, the older ones entirely  
gone, but one that James had mentioned — a stone marking Thomas Hale, died 1395,  
the child who came home from Pershore with a cooking pot — was still there, its  
inscription reduced to shallow shadows in the stone that were almost but not quite  
unreadable.
She had a notebook. She wrote down what she could make out. Then she looked  
at the yew tree for a while. Then she drove home.
She emailed James that evening. She wrote: I went. It is real. It is a small place  
and an old one.
He wrote back: yes. How are you?
She wrote: I am asking the question. He wrote: good. That is the right answer.
*

VIII
James at Eighty-One
London, March 2026
James Marsh-Hale is eighty-one years old as this book goes to press. He is in good  
health for a man of eighty-one, which is to say that he is substantially more careful  
than he was at seventy-one and rather more aware of what careful means. He lives in  
the house in Hackney, which no longer needs structural work because he has had it  
done in stages over the past fifty years, each stage assessed with the precision of a  
man  whose  father  was  a  structural  engineer  and  who  absorbed  the  relevant  
vocabulary at nine years old.
His daughter Anna is fifty-one. She teaches secondary school history in Bristol  
and has the quality — which James identifies in her with a quiet satisfaction he does  
not mention often because she would find it embarrassing — of making her students  
feel that history is the most urgent subject they could be studying, which it is. His son  
Michael is forty-eight, a software engineer in Edinburgh, who has the family's  
precision applied to an entirely different domain and who rings his father on Sunday  
mornings and argues about things with the thoroughness of a person who has been  
arguing about things since he could speak.
Susan is still gone. James has adjusted to this in the way that people who have  
been with someone for forty-seven years adjust to their absence: not by filling the  
space but by learning to hold it, which is different and harder and more honest. He  
does not talk about this much. He tends his particular quality of being present in the  
world that he has had since he was nine years old, attending carefully to what is  
actually there, and it serves him adequately.
He has been working on this book with Eleanor for a year and a half. They meet in  
Oxford once a month and speak on the phone twice a week and exchange emails with  
the frequency and the precision of two people who have discovered that their minds  
are compatible in the specific way that makes collaboration genuinely productive  
rather than merely functional. He finds her intelligence clarifying. She finds his  
experience orienting. Together they are capable of a range of observation that  
neither could achieve alone, which is, he thinks, what the archive has always been  
trying to produce: not a single continuous voice but a conversation across the  
distances.

He is going to die before the conversation is finished. He knows this. It is not a  
source of distress. It is a structural fact, and structural facts are managed rather  
than mourned. What he has tried to ensure — in the finding aid, in the ten books, in  
the box of papers that will go back to the Bodleian when he is done with them — is  
that the record is accurate and the questions are still open and the next person  
knows where everything is.
Eleanor knows where everything is. He has checked.
James Marsh-Hale — journal, 14 March 2026
The book is finished. Eleanor and I have read it through together one last time and  
we are both satisfied with it, which is to say we have both found things we would  
change and have decided to leave them as they are because the changes would be  
improvements in the editing and not in the truth.
I have been thinking about Ranulf de la Hale standing in the hall in Worcestershire in  
the autumn of 1066, not speaking the language of the people he had been given  
authority over, not knowing whether what had been given to him was legitimately his  
or whether the people he was now responsible for would ever accept him. He had no  
archive. He had no family history. He had a strip of confiscated Saxon land and a  
question he did not yet know he was asking.
I have nine centuries of archive. I have the question in thirty languages, all of them  
saying the same thing. I still cannot answer it cleanly. I think Matilda was right that  
you are not supposed to answer it cleanly. You are supposed to keep it open and keep  
working and trust that whoever comes after will carry it forward.
Eleanor will carry it forward. I am fairly sure of this. She has the quality. She has had  
it all along. Matilda noticed it six hundred years before I did, which speaks to  
Matilda's exceptional perceptiveness and to the slowness of institutions.
I am eighty-one. The pig still needs seeing to. I am going to make tea.
*

IX
The Letter in Full
Written c.1390. Read, in its entirety, 2024.
Here, for the first time in its complete form, is Matilda's letter. The portions in  
regular text were legible before the imaging of January 2024. The portions that were  
recovered by the imaging are marked with an asterisk. The letter is reproduced here  
not as a scholarly exhibit but as the document it was: a letter from a woman who kept  
bees in Halecroft in the fourteenth century to someone she was going to meet for the  
first time six hundred and thirty-five years after her death.
    ✦ ✦ ✦
MATILDA OF HALECROFT
Her letter, in full. Date: approximately 1390.
To whoever shall read this after I am gone, which will be soon enough:
I have lived in this village since before any man in this room was born and I  
shall likely live here after most of them are gone. I knew Ranulf de la Hale that  
built the hall, though I was young then and he was old. I knew his son Leofric  
that built the Hale in the wood. I knew every man and woman of that family  
through five generations to Richard that is dead of the plague and his sons  
that are dead and his son's son Thomas that stands before the court now.
* I have kept these bees through six reigns and two plagues and a rising that  
came to nothing and a hunger that came to much, and I have known the sons  
and daughters of this family since before their fathers were born, and I set  
down what I know here because I am the last who knows it.
* And I do set down here, for the keeping of the record, that the line does not  
end with the sons of William Hale but continues also through his daughter  
Margery, who married one Thomas Voss of Pershore in the year of Our Lord

1373, and whose line I have followed these thirty years and know to be sound.
* Margery is a good woman and a careful one and she has a daughter named  
Alice who has her grandmother Aelswith's eyes, which I remember though  
Aelswith has been dead these forty years and the world has changed more  
times than I can comfortably count since then. The blood runs in both lines.  
This is what families do. They go underground sometimes and come back up  
somewhere else and they are still the same thing they always were, which is  
themselves.
* I write this not for the sons of this house, who know who they are. I write it  
for whoever reads it last, who will not know where they are from until this  
moment, and who will perhaps think it makes no great difference. It makes no  
great difference and it makes all the difference. You are from here. This is  
where you are from. Halecroft, in the county of Worcester, which is a small  
place and an old one and which has been your home since 1066 whether you  
knew it or not.
You ask whether I have done enough. I do not know whether I have or not. I  
know only that I asked, and that the asking is what this family does. They ask  
whether they have done enough. They do not always answer, and perhaps  
there is no answer, but they ask.
The boy Thomas asked it at eleven, standing in an empty yard with a cooking  
pot, though he did not use those words. He said: I had better see to the pig. It  
is the same question.
I am very old and I have kept bees longer than anyone living can remember  
and I have seen more than I will say and I tell you this for nothing: a family  
that keeps asking whether it has done enough will keep the land. A family that  
stops asking will lose it, sooner or later, one way or another.
Keep asking. Keep the land. See to the pig.

Matilda. Her mark.
*

X
On the English of the Present
A Note on the Language of this Volume, and a Closing
The English of this volume is the English we speak now: direct, unornamented,  
suspicious of its own sentiment, aware that the performance has been stripped and  
uncertain what to do with what remains. The emails in this volume are written in the  
idiom of professional correspondence in 2024, which is formal enough to be legible  
across relationships and informal enough to carry personal weight, and which is  
therefore a fairly accurate register for the kind of relationship James and Eleanor  
developed: professional in its origin and form, personal in its content.
The journal entries are written in the voice that Thomas Marsh-Hale was moving  
toward in 1916 and that Edmund reached and that James has inhabited since he was  
a  young  man:  short  sentences,  no  excess,  the  thing  itself  rather  than  the  
representation of the thing. It is not a beautiful English in the way that Victorian  
English is beautiful or Georgian English is beautiful. It is a functional English, and  
functional is not a lesser quality than beautiful. It is a different quality. The plain  
style says what needs to be said, and what needs to be said is usually enough.
The recovered text of Matilda's letter, printed in the previous chapter, is in a  
different register from everything else in this final volume. It is in the Middle English  
of the late fourteenth century, and it has a rhythm and a directness that no other  
register in the chronicle matches, because Matilda is the only writer in the archive  
who has nothing to manage and no performance to maintain. She is very old and she  
is going to die soon and she knows exactly what she wants to say and she says it. That  
is the register you get when you remove every constraint except accuracy.
The chronicle began with Brother Eadmer writing in a scriptorium in 1121, in the  
careful Latin-inflected English of a Benedictine monk. It ends with two people  
exchanging emails in 2024, one of whom is eighty years old and one of whom is forty,  
and neither of whom is performing anything in particular. The distance between  
those two registers is nine centuries and the complete transformation of what  
English is and what it is used for. The thing that runs through all of it — from  
Eadmer's careful subordinate clauses to Eleanor's I do not know what to do with this  
— is the same thing: the attempt to say accurately what is actually there.
That is the inheritance. Not the land, which was sold in 1891. Not the baronetcy,  
which is extinct. Not the name, which runs in at least two lines and possibly more and

has  been  spelled  differently  in  every  century.  The  inheritance  is  the  habit  of  
attention. The habit of asking the question without forcing the answer. The habit, to  
use the formulation that has persisted through eleven volumes and six centuries and  
innumerable registers of English, of seeing to the pig.
Eleanor Voss is forty-one years old. She has the habit. She has had it all her life  
without knowing where it came from. She knows now.
That is sufficient. For now, it is sufficient.
*  *  *
Keep asking. Keep the land. See to the pig.
Matilda. Her mark. c.1390.

A Note on the Archive
The Hale family papers — comprising the Frere collection, the Marsh-Hale papers,  
and the documents reproduced in this volume — are held at the Bodleian Library,  
University of Oxford, under the reference MSS. Hale-Marsh. The collection is open to  
researchers. Matilda's letter is held in a climate-controlled case in the Special  
Collections reading room. The enhanced imaging is available digitally.
The deed-box described in these volumes — the oak chest with lock and key,  
purchased or inherited by Robert Hale no later than 1490, in which every significant  
document of the family's history was kept for four and a half centuries — is also at the  
Bodleian. It is in reasonable condition. The lining has been repaired at least twice.  
The crack in the lid, noted by Brother Anselm in 1538, is still visible if you look for it.
The beehives at Halecroft are still kept, by a family named Parry who farm the land  
that was once the Hale holding. They are not Hales. They do not know about the  
archive. They keep the bees because the bees are there and somebody has to.
END OF BOOK X — HALE
END OF THE HALE DYNASTY
Ten volumes. Nine centuries. One question.
1066 — 2026
